# 抱朴子外篇

**Master Embracing Simplicity, Outer Chapters**
*Volume One: "Praise for Reclusion"*
*By Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*

The Master Embracing Simplicity said:

There was a gentleman known as the "Master Who Cherishes Ice." He disdained the restless wandering of the worldly and lamented the bitter toil of those who offer counsel and grasp power. He ceded fertile lands to the wealthy, choosing instead to till the saline wastes himself. He concealed his six stratagems as if sealing a bag, holding jade and gem within yet refusing to speak. His pure voice went unheard, his radiant brilliance unseen by the world. Turning his back on the splendor of vermilion gates, he preserved tranquility within his humble thatched hut. He severed all ties with the grand halls of the Jin and Zhang clans, nurturing his vast and boundless *qi* in the company of recluses. He deemed glory and rank misfortune, regarding jade and silk as mere grass and dust. His noble aspirations soared beyond the clouds, alone defying the present to follow the ancients. Sheltered by towering peaks, he rested upon fragrant beds of orchids. He sipped the limpid essence of rosy clouds, savoring the refined essence of the eight minerals. His thoughts drifted as if dwelling at the edge of the rainbow, his spirit soared as if nearing the inverted reflections of the heavens. The myriad things could not disturb his harmony, the four seas could not agitate his spirit.

Thereupon, the "Lord Who Pursues Power," having heard of him, sighed with emotion and said: "A swift steed hidden in a secluded valley brings shame to Sun Yang; a talent of surpassing excellence left behind in an era of peace is the fault of those who reward the worthy. How can we allow such an outstanding man to preserve his solitary integrity, leaving the sagely court devoid of the service of a great minister?"

He then visited the Master and expounded: "I have heard that those who merely flap their wings and raise their bodies, yet cannot soar through the nine heavens or ascend to the darkest reaches, nor properly order the constant norms—these are not the truly heroic and extraordinary. Now, sir, you wield a blade of decisive judgment, your writings eclipse the most resplendent prose. You delight in rare texts from untrodden marshes, your literary elegance surpasses the flocks of birds and beasts. You display dragon-like patterns in the darkest night, yet sink your jade and gem into the deepest abyss. You lie dormant in the height of summer, conceal your brilliance in the midst of spring. Though you lower your curtain to delve into profound thought, exhaust your brush in dazzling composition, subtly elucidate the Great Ultimate, and expound the primordial origin—your words of joy make wooden figures smile as if with clever wit, your sorrowful speech causes even paired brows to furrow and tears to flow. When you suppress the light, even swan feathers sink in the Weak Waters; when you raise the heavy, even jade and stone float upon the surging waves. When you separate the alike, liver and gall become as distant as Hu and Yue; when you unite the disparate, the ten thousand differences merge into one harmony. Your incisive discourse is as chilling as autumn frost and spring’s severity; your gentle words make ice-bound branches bloom. When you topple the lofty, even the highest peaks crumble; when you elevate the lowly, even deep pools rise in grandeur. When you criticize the pure, you expose flaws in the brightest night-shining pearl; when you rectify the turbid, you instantly clarify the Yellow River.

Yet you cannot bestow great beneficence upon the common people, nor inscribe grand merit for the imperial house. Your name will vanish with the morning dew, your body will perish like the ephemeral mayfly. You scorn the treasures of the sage, forgetting the lament of great old age as the river flows on. I deem this course unworthy of you, sir.

I have heard that the greatest are Heaven and Earth, followed by ruler and minister. The ancient sages, anxious for their times, sought to practice their Dao. Even three months without a lord left them restless. They were ashamed that the sagely ruler of today did not match Yao and Shun, and pitied the common people who could not all be enfeoffed as in ancient times. Thus, some shouldered cauldrons and soared like dragons, others struck their horns and sang like phoenixes. They did not wait for the rush-wheeled carriage before taking action, nor did they require King Wen before rising. They concealed their talents at first, then soared to the five ranks, moving with the times. In advancement, they gained the benefits of their course; in retreat, they avoided the troubles of a wet tail. With wisdom, they preserved themselves; with teaching, they benefited the customs. They enabled those who followed the orchid breeze to incline their branches, and those who bathed in clear waves to wash away filth—as reflections respond to a bright mirror, and squares and circles conform to compass and square. Thus, their merits reached above and below, their beneficence extended to the eight directions. This is why the Tang Dynasty stood so majestically, why Chonghua governed with such reverence, why the Earl of the West divided the realm, why King Fa revolutionized the mandate, why Huan and Wen unified the realm, and why Emperor Gao of Han answered Heaven’s call. None of these were achieved without gathering the worthy as wings or relying on the heroic as boats and oars.

If each were to cling to the loftiness of washing their ears, if all were to hold to the duty of plowing in pairs, then the transformative teachings of antiquity would not be established, the glory of the wise and enlightened would not shine forth, the songs of the wise and good would not be composed, and the net encompassing Heaven would not be cast.

Therefore, those who conceal their talents value adaptability and timely action; the heroic and extraordinary esteem the ability to display their uniqueness and rescue the age from chaos. If the radiant sun hides its light beyond the clouds, the brightness of the heavens will not be manifest. If a roaring tiger sheathes its fangs and claws, its swiftness in seizing prey will not be revealed. If the Grand Lord sword conceals its edge without striking, its power to sever will not be displayed. If the swiftest steeds remain fettered and do not gallop, their wind-chasing speed will not be seen. If all remain silent, then Zigong is no different from the mute; if all close their eyes, then Li Zhu is no different from the blind."

The Master preserved his purity yet neglected the great ethical chaos; he found contentment yet forgot the duty of securing the throne. In life, he bore the burden of worldly mechanisms; in death, he left no enduring renown. Even mediocre men would flush with indignation—how could a man of true refinement remain so indifferent!

"When the marking-line is stretched, the wood grows straight; when rectitude advances, depravity withers. Under the reign of Yu, the Four Fiends were executed; when Confucius was entrusted, Shao Hao was beheaded. It is like thunder’s roar silencing the war-drums, or the morning sun eclipsing the glow of fireflies. Without rescuing those afflicted by the *Zhao Hun* malady, one cannot display the extraordinary skill of the Yue physician. Without fostering a world beset by calamities, one cannot recognize the far-reaching capacity of the exceptional. To sit idly and watch a drowning man—this is not the benevolence of one who refuses to tread danger. To cling to the Dao while the state falls into confusion—this is not the task of a true leader.

"Should one wait for the grasses of Zhongtang to mark the sun’s position, or the phoenix to sing at dawn’s first light; for the unicorn to stand alone in the suburbs, or the interlocked trees to cluster in the wilds; for banners to remain furled and never unfurled, for halberds to be sheathed and never sought—then even Shaobo, poised to retire from his achievements, could not rescue the sunken from their depths. How deeply I wish the Master would not stray so far from the path and return!"

Thereupon, the Master of Embracing Ice gazed into the distance with a serene air, his spirit roaming the celestial thoroughfares, his mind unbound as if nothing else existed. Lowering his gaze, he replied:

"Alas! Are these words truly spoken? The perfected man acts without contrivance, his spirit dwelling in the void. He does not enslave his will to salary and profit, so harm and disgrace cannot touch him. He does not perch on perilous paths, so collapse and ruin cannot threaten him. Though coarse herbs fail to sustain him, his mind delights in a feast of a thousand *li*; though he ranks among the common folk, his heart rejoices in possessing a kingdom. He sleeps in the hut of a humble official, shuts the gate of a reclusive scholar, befriends men like Zhuangzi and Liezi, and tends a dwelling in a narrow lane. Firm as a mountain, unshaken, how could he harbor thoughts of concealment or display?

"Desire expands and muddies harmony, so he relinquishes worldly affairs without a glance. Those at the pinnacle of rank bear profound worries, so he turns from power and profit without hesitation. His nobility does not depend on titles, his wealth not on riches. He soars high with the cloud-peng, untangled by the stench of rotting rats. Heedful of the perils of martial prowess, he finds joy in a simple gourd bowl.

"Moreover, the cosmos is vast and distant, while human life is fleeting—brief as a galloping horse glimpsed through a crack, swift as a shooting star or an arrow’s lightning flight. Why trouble the body with external things when one may wander freely and find contentment?

"The *luan*-bird does not entangle itself in nets, the *lin*-beast does not fall into pits. Even birds and beasts know to avoid peril—how can men of the world fail to grasp this? Those like Yaoli, who destroyed his family to prove his loyalty; Jixin, who burned himself to deceive Chu; Chen Jia, who slit his throat to vindicate his brother; Zhong You, who threw away his life to be minced into pickled meat; Yingmen, who fell on his sword to show his heart; Nie Zheng, who repaid kindness by disemboweling himself; Jing Ke, who severed his own tendons to avenge Yan; and Fan Kuai, who offered his head in sorrow—these are the mad delusions of the foolish, not the choices of the wise.

"For those with ample emoluments come heavy responsibilities; those with lofty ranks endure mental toil. Thus, the fisherman of Qiyuan ignored the prestige of ministerial office, and Baicheng tilled the soil, disdaining the nobility of feudal lords. Yang Shuo found peace in the butcher’s stall, and Yang Zhu refused to part with even a single hair. Yet the petty and ambitious, blind to desire’s pitfalls, flock to any perch without choosing the tree, take office without assessing the times. Greedy for advancement, they do not fear the disaster of overreaching; accepting tasks, they do not weigh their inability to bear them. When speaking of glory, they invoke Yi and Zhou to save the drowning; when warning of regret, they ignore the lesson of the overturned tripod. They wait for the river dragon to sleep before plucking its pearl, bask in the favor of the imperial measure, and hope for safety. They indulge in momentary ease, blind to impending danger; they covet the ephemeral glory of the morning mushroom while aspiring to the longevity of the great *chun* tree. They tread thin ice in anticipation of summer, or climb a rotten branch awaiting a fierce wind. Like fish lured by fragrant bait or pheasants swallowing poisonous grains, they gnaw on spoiled meat to stave off hunger and guzzle poisoned wine to quench their thirst.

"Long ago, Jizi wept at the sight of ivory chopsticks, and Confucius sighed deeply upon hearing of a joint burial—subtle signs foretelling greater truths, beginnings revealing ends. Yet fools step on the trigger without awareness. How difficult it is to foresee the future, and how deeply rooted is the obsession with profit and desire! King Cheng of Zhou was virtuous yet believed slander, and the sage Duke of Zhou fled to the southern lands of Chu. He lamented through the owl’s cry and barely escaped disaster, saved only by the golden casket. How much rarer are enlightened rulers, and how ceaseless the slander of the discontented!

"Virtue does not arise from violent winds that uproot crops, nor do affairs gain merit from bearing jade tablets. Lamentations come too late—so it has ever been.

"Prolonged soaking softens even the strongest glue; persistent dampness severs bone and flesh. The accumulation of dust and feathers sinks ships and snaps axles. Repeated slander turns rumor into a tiger in the marketplace. Thus, Jiang Chong, though lowly and unrelated to the crown prince, fabricated the *juegeng* calumny and destroyed the bond between father and son. The stepmother, though not close to Boyi, wielded the sleeve-bee’s deceit and shattered the love of kinship. How much less can others be certain of their safety?

"Alas!"

The loyalty of Wu Yun led only to his corpse adrift—how tragic! The righteousness of Bai Qi ended but with his throat slit. Indeed, such are the deeds that chill the marrow of those with perfect insight, confounding even the wisest craftsmen.

"Yet some seek to wield meager talent to correct the chorus of conformity, brandishing solitary rightness to censure the many in error. But they fail to see that though metal may overcome wood, an awl cannot fell the forest of Deng; though water may conquer fire, a mere cupful cannot quench a mountain ablaze. A dab of glue cannot clarify the Yellow River’s turbidity, nor a ladle of water dispel the heat of Xiaoqiu. Thus, those who preserve both body and name are exceedingly rare, while those who laugh first and wail after are many.

"Fear of overreaching and regret cannot extinguish the craving for glory; dread of slander and disgrace cannot banish the urge to contend. It is like loathing dampness yet plunging into the deep abyss, hating one’s shadow yet shunning all shade, drilling a boat to stop its leaks, or stoking the flames to halt the boil.

"The seven-foot frame is endowed by those who bore us—it cannot be returned whole if marred. The heart within the square inch is governed by oneself—it cannot be abandoned to drift and flee. Plow the fields to feed oneself, dig a well to drink, don a coarse robe for cover, thatch a hut for shelter, strum and sing for joy, breathe deeply to prolong life. Roam freely among bamboo and silk, entrust the mind to profound brushstrokes, abide in constancy until life’s end—this is more than enough.

"For when the Dao abides, one is revered; when virtue prevails, one is noble. To cast a Sui pearl to shoot a sparrow—none wise would do so. Why must one seek power to gain renown, or wait for stipends to be sated?

"The contented poor count lack of wealth as riches; the willingly humble deem not serving as honor. Thus, Yuan Xian floated upon the sea to still his spirit, while Huzi gladly embraced reclusion in farming. Peng Xian suffered punishment for his noble virtue, and Xin and Wu were condemned despite their great merits. A single branch suffices to shelter the phoenix’s wings—why crave a vast forest? A mere puddle is enough to float the dragon’s scales—why need the boundless ocean? Pulse and greens surpass the eight delicacies; cold springs are sweeter than fine wine. Straw sandals outshine red slippers; a patched robe outshines imperial robes. Grasping a staff brings more ease than wielding an axe; the rustle of leaves delights more than strings and flutes. A thatched roof gleams brighter than vermilion pillars; rough-hewn rafters are more precious than carved beams. Ascending a high peak surpasses pavilions; a rocky ledge with dripping water rivals a painted hall. Piling up writings fills granaries; treasuring profound discourse is like hoarding gold and jade. Forsake the petty attachments of small-minded men, discard the desires of common laborers. Wander the nine marshes in joy, banish worldly wisdom to transcend the vulgar.

"Like the inchworm, bend and hide; conceal your light and guard simplicity. Display clumsiness and feign dullness, know when to stop and be content. Then, savoring the fragrance of magic herbs, soar on the wind like floating clouds; bask in the radiance of the nine suns, ride the wings of the high-flying. Perch above on the wutong tree, alight below on the dark isles. How can this compare to wearing a bridle and crouching in a stable, or being draped in embroidery like a sacrificial ox?"

The Viscount of Opportunism said: "To enter and never depart is to indulge in favor and forget retreat; to go forth and never return is to bear no duty and abandon righteousness. Thus the enlightened know the self is not their own, yielding to whatever they encounter. Whether hidden or revealed, silent or speaking, they hold to nothing fixed. When the time is to stop, they stop; when the time is to act, they act. When silk gifts gather and courtyard torches blaze, the noble Way flourishes, and Heaven’s favor manifests.

Yet when fate drifts into the *yang-nine* era of slander’s triumph, one does not step beyond the gate, seals the sack and refrains from use. Dragons rise and phoenixes withdraw, adapting to the moment’s demand. The ancients sometimes shunned peril and refused to enter, or fled at the first sign of danger before the day was done—fearing the fire on Mount Wu might consume orchid and mugwort alike.

Now the sagely emperor governs the age, the world is level and the Dao at peace. Benevolence reaches the black-haired people, the breeze of grace extends afar, awe subdues the barbarian lands, and blessings moisten the nine distant lineages. Earth’s virtue is modeled in profound bearing, Heaven’s vault emulated in lofty canopy. Divine transformation flows like clouds and rain, dark grace spreads like mist and deep waters. The four gates stand solemn to welcome all, the sovereign’s thought soars with heroic ease to bring order.

This is a rare encounter, a once-in-a-millennium convergence. Yet you, sir, cling to the lopsided pursuit of reclusion, unaware of the harm in excessive restraint. You focus on cultivating the inner like Single Leopard, blind to the perils of facing the tiger unarmed. You hear of one who drowns in crossing the water and conclude all who board boats will fail; you see Shang Chen’s treachery and claim no heir can be worthy."

Master Embracing Ice replied: "The flourishing of sagely transformation is indeed as you describe. Yet the matter of advancing or withdrawing is a matter of individual inclination. Even when Yao and Shun reigned above, the Jishui and Ying rivers had their nest-dwelling recluses; when the Xia sovereigns ruled, the remote marshes harbored plow-holding worthies. Did they fear these dangers? Each simply followed what brought them peace.

Thus they exalted their aspirations, refusing office under kings and lords, preserving the hexagrams’ images—what even common men may grasp. Yan Zhou maintained his integrity, and the sages approved.

As for my own wandering in gardens and marshes, withdrawing into the wilds, it is truly because my talents do not lie in governance, my capacity lacks for ruling the people. With so many scholars rising like clouds, the eminent gathering like scales, civil and martial officials filling the court, and all affairs already well-ordered, I do not wish to raise my flickering light to stand between sun and moon, to strike a jar beside a great bell, to offer a light fan in the season of solid ice, or to flaunt a fur robe in the height of summer—only to be cast aside as useless and swiftly draw the scorn of the times.

If I embrace the classics and compose writings, I may preserve my integrity and establish my name, contributing to the fading transformation. But if I force myself into what I cannot bear, I will stumble and meet blame, like the fox that regrets its smallness. Thus I dwell in my strengths to protect my weaknesses.

Though I lack the merits of standing in court or the toils of military service, I guide the young, propagate the Dao, and nurture uprightness. Different paths lead to the same end—I am no drain on the people. Even the lesser man preserves his single integrity. How can I compare to Xu You? Yet in this sagely age, tolerance is extended, and I am excused like those who roamed in the age of Tang. Is this not acceptable?"

The Viscount of Opportunism suddenly lost composure, his expression turning solemn as he said: "Your words, sir, uphold teaching and aid instruction, your prose exposes deceit and corrects deviation. You champion stillness to curb the restless competition of the age, revive Confucian learning to rescue the fading voice of wisdom. Without those who advance, who would order the constant norms? Without those who withdraw, who would instruct the young? Under the vast sky, all are subjects. Why must only those who wear court sashes and hold tablets be right, while those who delight in hunger and humble gates be wrong?

Those lost in the clouds and mists of the world need a compass to find the Way; those adrift in the vast sea must gaze upon the North Star to return. Now, having heard your noble instruction, I see my own blindness. I shall don my robes and cap, spur my dull steed to follow the swift, and advance with universal love, sparing no effort in selection."

*Records of the Recluses, Scroll Two*

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: Long ago, I wandered among the peaks of Mount Yuntai and visited a recluse, where I encountered a court official. The official said:

"With radiant clarity reigning above, governing the eight extremities, Hua and Yi alike submit, and distant lands offer tribute. Yet you, sir, tread the remote paths of Bo Cheng, mingling with the flocks of birds and beasts. Times have changed, customs differ, and worldly affairs no longer bind as they once did. Thus, those who eat of the wild, dwell in the mountains, and cast off external burdens were once deemed pure and lofty, but now are seen as fugitives. The noble man anticipates peril before it takes shape and severs misfortune at its inception. Would you not flee Zhang Yi’s inner heat only to fall prey to Shan Bao’s external harm? Fearing fullness and resisting care, you forget the near danger of disorder, shun the shallow peril of the ox’s hoofprint, yet plunge into the unfathomable abyss of a hundred fathoms. You turn from the muddy shallows where feet are wet, only to leap into the furnace unwittingly."

The recluse replied: "He who sharpens his ambition on fledgling mice cannot grasp the intent of the *zouyu*; he who busies himself with grains in the courtyard cannot comprehend the distant aims of the *yuan* and *luan*. Just as the cicada and the mite mock the cloud-roving roc, the morning mushroom marvels at the great *chun* tree, the frog in the pit doubts the sea turtle, and the well-dwelling snake scorns the responding dragon—so too do you, in your delight and dread of exhortation and dissuasion, fail to perceive the lofty resonance of the vast and mysterious! I am fortunate to have been born in the age of Yao and Shun—what worry have I of not attaining the aspirations of such men?"

The official said: "In ancient times, the unyielding Hua Shi refused to serve his lord and hid himself in a distant corner of the sea, yet the Grand Duke put him to death. Are you not in peril, sir, in your seclusion?"

The recluse replied: "Lü Shang excelled in warfare but fell short in governance. He could not emulate the *xuan* and *huang* in covering and bearing all, nor liken himself to the seas and mountains in embracing all things. He failed to honor the worthy, esteem virtue, or delight in nurturing talent. Instead, he indulged in punishment and killing, neglecting benevolence and righteousness. Thus, the calamity of his violent rule took root at the very founding of his state. The Duke of Zhou, upon hearing of this, knew his rule would not endure. For attack and defense demand different approaches, and the Way values adaptability. Yet Lü Shang lacked the art of preparing fresh delicacies, the skill of driving afar, or the ability to extend the methods of battle arrays to spare the lives of lofty scholars. He relied on armor and blades to preserve his weapons, then compounded his folly by swimming in treacherous waters, aiming at fleeing targets with the precision of a marksman—how could he hope to hit the mark? When the nests of common birds are overturned, the divine phoenix will not alight; when the pools of fish and turtles are drained, the sacred *qiu* dragon flees afar."

"When the wombs of common beasts are ripped open, the unicorn will not pause even at the outskirts of his domain; when a single humble scholar is harmed, heroes of renown will not set foot within his borders. Lü Shang founded a dynasty to bequeath to posterity, yet he spread the seeds of cruelty and laid the tracks of tyranny—just enough to drive the talented to serve other states and chase the worthy into the arms of his enemies. How far removed is this from those who bought the bones of a steed to attract swift horses, or bowed in humble lanes to turn back the armies of Qin!

You ask how Lü Shang compares to the Duke of Zhou." The courtier replied, "I cannot judge." The recluse said: "The Duke of Zhou, that great sage, humbled himself before the lowly, gulping down his meal and grasping his hair in haste, fearful of losing even one man. He followed seventy scholars from thatched cottages, and personally carried gifts to ten men of common cloth whom he treated as teachers, while twelve others he counted as friends—yet he never forced them to serve at court. Had Lü Shang stood in the Duke of Zhou’s place, these men would have become corpses exposed in the marketplace, their flesh rotting in ditches and gullies.

Emperor Yao did not compel Xu You or Chao Fu to serve, nor did Emperor Shun coerce Shan Zheng or Shi Hu. Yu the Great did not pressure Bo Cheng Zi Gao, nor did Tang the Victorious press Bian Sui or Wu Guang. King Wen of Wei did not bend Gan Mu to his will, nor did Duke Ping of Jin force Hai Tang into office. Yet they served these men as teachers and honored them as masters—were these six rulers so weak? Truly, the hundred virtues differ in their excellence, and stillness is hard to equal. They cherished the noble reputation of revering worthies and shunned the shame of harming the good. To seize them would not have added to their authority, while letting them go would not have left their offices vacant. Following their will could have spread the influence of humility, fostering yielding and restraint, shaming the greedy who rush forward, and moving the reckless who act without thought. Though such men might not immediately fill a need or match the talents of those standing in court, is this not better than bowing and scraping, flattering the powerful, clutching gifts and bribes, hurrying through the night in the dust of the world, competing for passage and vying for rank, abandoning the roots of virtue and learning for the trivialities of conformity and faction? Even these six rulers would not have harshly pressed recluses—how much less would they have wielded blades against them! Truly, the sages are worthy of emulation; Lü Shang was clearly mistaken.

Though Emperor Gao of Han had many flaws in his conduct and little knowledge of the classics, his magnanimity and generosity were vast. He harbored no petty grudges, his mind was open and free. Though he hungered and thirsted for the Four Graybeards, he did not force them. Only when the Crown Prince humbly invited them, making them his wings, did he respect their virtue and correct his own feelings. He lamented in the *Yellow Swan* song and put an end to his concubine’s ambitions—so deeply did he treasure the worthy and honor the reclusive. No wonder he rose from commoner to rule the realm; his capacity surpassed others.

As for Lü Shang’s killing of the principled and pure, it was out of fear they would obstruct the masses. Yet the true affliction of the age is not that too many embrace poverty and delight in lowliness, but that men are restless in their pursuit of advancement and neglect their conduct. Even if recluses were everywhere, those entangled in power and profit, whose ambitions know no bounds, could never sever their usual desires to admire such withdrawal. Since the dawn of time, men have been many, yet those who forget wealth and abandon rank remain fewer than one in ten thousand. Confucius himself studied under Laozi yet could not practice his non-action; Zigong and Yuan Xian were disciples of the same master, yet could not emulate his austerity. The Four Fiends lived alongside Chao Fu and Xu You, Wang Mang shared an era with the Two Gongs—yet none could imitate them. Even if the common folk were whipped and shamed, humiliated and coerced into following the principled and pure, they would still refuse. How, then, could Lü Shang fear they would corrupt the customs? His failure to grasp this, governing a peaceful age with military law and wantonly harming the worthy, was a grave error indeed! Only his great achievements prevented his immediate downfall.

Before Lü Shang met King Wen, he too had lived in obscurity and poverty. Ordinary people scorned him, an old woman drove him away, his labor went unsold, his butchering and fishing brought no gain—yet not a single person admired him. In his seclusion, why did he alone fear the principled and pure would obstruct the masses? Had King Zhou of Shang captured and imprisoned him as he fled, would Lü Shang, facing death, have claimed his punishment was deserved?

Cao Cao of Wei also played with harsh laws and was swift to kill, yet in his heart he wished to employ Kongming. Kongming himself declared he took no joy in entering service. Cao Cao sent him away, saying, 'It would be wrong to humiliate a man of lofty virtue in the court of a corrupt ruler.' Though he lashed the Nine Provinces and laid the foundations of an empire, he did not act recklessly.

As the turmoil of the world drags on and competition becomes custom, some thrust bribes forward to leap like dragons, others climb through factions to rise like phoenixes. The prevailing winds shape habits, the great Way grows overgrown, and later generations, benighted, let Confucian teachings fade. To establish oneself, nothing but wealth will do. If there are those who stand aloof from the crowd, who do not leave their gates but silently savor the Way, they should be sought out with respect to promote humility and restraint. For if Sun Wu and Wu Qi bear arms, it is the strength of one man; but if their strategies are employed, they surpass ten thousand. Today, if great scholars are made officials, they need not attend to every matter. Left in the mountains and forests, they can mold the young and spread the teachings of ritual and respect. Why must one harness a great elephant to catch a mouse or a simurgh to hunt a quail?"

[The courtier said,] "But if they wither in the marshes and bogs, living and dying in vain, how can they be considered worthy?" The recluse replied: "You may cling to anthills and toy with fox mounds, but you have not yet ascended the Tower of Langfeng to gaze upon the clouds."

Those who amuse themselves on gleaming shores and wander through stagnant pools have yet to float upon the Southern Sea or ford the Celestial Han. The so-called men of resolve need not dwell in office or rank, nor must they seek merit through conquest. The highest forget themselves entirely; the next forget their names. They spread their wings to surpass the crowd, gallop beyond the beaten track, do what ordinary men cannot do, sever what lesser talents cannot sever. Their measure defies the common scale; their purity remains unstained by fame or position. Their unsullied virtue is enough to wash away the filth of a hundred generations; their lofty conduct is enough to rouse the turbid men of ages to come. Why must one don crimson and trail purple, wear the crown and ride the light carriage, be clad in the embroidered robes of sacrificial oxen, or swallow the fragrant bait of Zhan He—only to burn in the scorching heat of Zhang Tian by morning and turn to frigid ashes by evening?

The shrike does not trade its thorny thicket for the far reaches of the clouds; the sturgeon does not barter its deep grotto for the vastness of the sea. Tigers and leopards enter grand halls and grieve; swans and cranes alight upon lofty peaks and mourn. Each thing has its heart and thrives in its own strength. All are at peace when their desires are fulfilled, and all grow desolate when they lose their place. Men of the world, in their multitudes, are like this: a single day without a lord, and they are filled with dread. They are like the jade piled in Lantian or the timber amassed in Deng Forest—skilled craftsmen and master builders may use them as they please. Why must one drown fish or sink birds?

To withdraw in noble retreat is what the ancient sages approved. Some go forth, some stay—each follows his own inclination. What men of learning truly value is the establishment of virtue and the creation of enduring words. To practice filial piety, friendship, benevolence, and righteousness with pure and lofty conduct—this is to establish virtue. To exhaustively study the *Classic of Mountains* and the *Classic of Seas*, leaving behind brilliant writings—this is to establish enduring words. Even if Zheng Xuan had no success in governing the people, he cannot be said to be inferior to common officials; though Confucius had no military conquests, he cannot be deemed lesser than Han Xin or Bai Qi. To preserve both body and name intact is the highest achievement. To live in seclusion and fulfill one’s aspirations was praised by the ancients. Boyi and Shuqi, though unyielding in their principles, were lauded by the ancients for neither descending to disgrace. To "not descend" is to affirm the loftiness of reclusion; to "not be disgraced" is to know the defilement of entanglement. The purity of the sages was what Mencius admired, declaring that Heaven’s nobility surpasses that of seals and sashes. To cultivate one’s aspirations and forsake glory was what Xunzi esteemed; once the Way and virtue are complete, kings and dukes may be held in light regard.

Yet the world fears only power and values only profit. Those of great virtue who remain obscure are dismissed as mediocrities; those of small talent burdened with great tasks are hailed as lofty gentlemen. Some risk peril and court danger, casting aside their lives and abandoning their bodies in the abyss, all to seize fleeting glory in a single morning. Compared to those who hold the four seas in light regard and cherish even a hair on their shins, how distant they are!

The courtier said: "Those who withdraw into seclusion, content in mountains and marshes, bearing no weight of worldly honor, unrestrained and unbridled, useless to their times—sniffing at emoluments and profit, they are truly of no benefit to the world. How can this be?"

The recluse replied: "The qilin does not bark to guard the gate; the phoenix does not crow to herald the dawn. The divine steed does not pull the plow; the temple priest does not manage the kitchen. Moreover, the sun illuminates all beyond the four seas and nurtures the world with its gentle, warming breeze; fire shines like a brilliant lamp in the dark night and works metal and stone to serve its purpose. The world cannot go long without the sun, nor can it do without fire for a single day—yet their greatness and smallness cannot be equated. Beyond the rivers and seas, encompassing the two poles, rising as clouds and rain, descending to form the hundred streams—yet for daily use, they do not compare to a well of several fathoms, nor do they surpass ditches and canals in irrigating fields and gardens. When weighed in their vastness and minuteness, which is truly boundless?

Jie and Zhou were emperors; Confucius was a minister of state. Yet when compared to Jie and Zhou, none do not grow angry; when likened to Confucius, none do not rejoice. Thus, nobility and baseness truly do not lie in rank. Therefore, Mencius said that Yu, Ji, and Yan Hui would have been the same had their positions been exchanged. Zai Yu also declared that Confucius was far worthier than Yao and Shun. When a commoner is equated with a king, or a scholar is exalted above Tang and Yu, it is virtue alone—what need is there for office?

Moreover, to commune with the numinous and ascend to the workings of creation, to hold heaven and earth within one’s embrace, to be so vast that worldly affairs do not lodge in one’s heart, so boundless that favor and disgrace do not disturb one’s purity—what the vulgar desire cannot stain one’s spirit; what confounds ordinary men cannot sway one’s resolve. Glory and splendor are but excrescences; the ten thousand things are but the wings of a cicada. How could one of such a mind bend his limbs, bow his bearing, sip from what he dislikes, or seek what he has discarded—smiling to advance, stooping to retreat, fearing the displeasure of the vulgar, lamenting the decline of his body? Would he debase the Dragon Abyss sword for use as an awl, suppress the mighty *lingfei* drum for the sound of a battle drum, wield the yellow battle-axe only to grasp a sickle, or lower the embroidered banner to enter the shade of the scholar-tree?

King Tai of Zhou abandoned his staff and cast it aside; Yue Yi fled into a cave to escape; Ji Zha withdrew to till the fields; Lao Lai irrigated his garden to distance himself. They followed their inclinations, and none could sway them. To be pure and unadulterated is true wealth; to remain unburdened by service is true nobility. As for those who receive fiefs and lands—these are but emoluments and profit, not true wealth or nobility. Moreover, the higher the office, the heavier the responsibility; the greater the achievement, the more others envy. Only in poverty and lowliness is there none to contend with me, allowing one to treasure it forever without care.

To wash one’s furs and wear coarse cloth, to uproot mallows and abandon weaving, to have only beans to cover the pork, to dine on coarse grains and rough vegetables—one may yet incur the scorn of the base and deceitful. But to build screens and block the threshold, to enjoy three returns of jade feasts, the wealth of the Marquis of Rang, the ease of Anchang—these bring the burdens of usurping the superior and defiling purity."

Better still is to wander in spirit through the classics, to exhale the old and inhale the new, seeking sustenance at the tip of the plowshare, drawing warmth from the threads of the loom. With belly upturned to the river, one is already full; with body gathered on a single branch, one finds ample ease. The teeming ten thousand things dissolve into dust and ash.

To gnaw on meager gruel for sustenance, to wear a patched hempen robe, to roam freely in simplicity, untroubled by joy or sorrow—this is true honor and joy, likened to having nothing at all.

As for office: if one seeks fame, a fine brush can vent one’s frustrations, and written works can preserve one’s name. Why rely on illustrious historians? Why trouble with inscriptions on tripods? Mencius did not count arrows and stones as merit, nor did Yang Yun consider governing the people a benefit to the age. To seek benevolence and attain it—is this not sufficient?

The official then says: “Those who withdraw from the world are disloyal subjects. How can they rightfully dwell in the ruler’s land or eat the ruler’s grain?”

The recluse replies: “How can you say such a thing? When Yan Hui died, Duke Ding of Lu was about to offer condolences in person and sent someone to consult Confucius. Confucius said: ‘All within the state are subjects.’ Thus the Duke ascended by the eastern steps and performed the rites of a lord. From this, we see that ‘within the shores of the land, none are not the king’s subjects.’ Those at court exert their strength to manage state affairs; those in the mountains and forests cultivate virtue to curb greed and corruption. Different paths, same destination—both are subjects. The king has no bounds; the world is his home. Where the sun and moon shine, where rain and dew fall—all is his domain. How can one suspend oneself in empty space, feeding on morning mist, refusing to dwell on the earth or eat its grain?

The gold and jade of the mountains, the pearls and shells of the waters—though not stored in the treasury or used for daily needs—are still the ruler’s wealth. The retired scholar, not ranked among the meat-eaters, is like the treasures of the mountains and rivers—are they not also part of the realm? Xu You did not flee beyond the four seas, nor did the Four Hoary Ones escape to the farthest wilds. Thus it is said: ‘The wise of all lands are all the emperor’s subjects.’

Gan Mu did not bear arms to defend the border or build fortifications on the frontier, yet he rendered great service to Wei. Now the recluse purifies his conduct within his thatched hut, chanting the Way of the former kings, teaching the people to yield and defer, ensuring the traditions of the Ru and Mo endure—this too is what Yao and Shun would approve. As for Boyi and Shuqi refusing the grain of Zhou, or Bao Jiao dying beneath a bridge—their stubbornness is hardly worthy of emulation.

In the past, Emperor An sent dark silk and jade to summon Zhou Yanzu. Emperor Huan sent dark silk and jade to summon Wei Xiuming. Emperor Shun sent dark silk and jade to summon Yang Zhongxuan, who was appointed Attendant-in-Ordinary but did not go. Emperor Wen of Wei summoned Guan You’an, who did not come; he was then appointed Gentleman of the Palace, but still did not go. The emperor decreed that his locality provide him with one sheep and two *hu* of wine every eighth month.

Emperor Huan sent dark silk and jade to summon Ping Jieru, who was appointed Governor of Taiyuan and Chancellor of Donghai but did not go. Emperor Shun sent dark silk and jade to summon Fan Jigao, who did not come. The emperor decreed that his locality provide him with one sheep and two *hu* of wine every eighth month, and also bestowed a stool and staff, treating him with the rites of a teacher.

In the time of Emperor Xian, Zheng Kangcheng was nominated by his province as Filial and Incorrupt, Outstanding Talent, and Excellent and Upright; he was summoned fourteen times by the Three Ducal Ministers but declined all. He was summoned by the imperial carriage as Left Gentleman, Academician, Attendant-in-Ordinary, and Grand Minister of Agriculture, but did not rise. Emperor Zhao summoned Han Fu by the imperial carriage; he came and was given fifty bolts of silk and sheep and wine.

Fa Gaoqing was nominated twice as Filial and Incorrupt, five times by his province, eight times by the Three Ducal Ministers, and nine times as Excellent and Upright and Academician; he was summoned three times but declined all. Emperor Huan sent dark silk, jade, a covered carriage, and a light chariot to summon Han Boxiu, who did not come. He sent dark silk, jade, a covered carriage, and a light chariot to summon Qie Boya, who was appointed Palace Grandee and Governor of Jianwei but did not rise. Yet all were treated with esteem and not subjected to coercion.

If these emperors rightly honored the recluses, then King Tai’s execution of Hua Shi was cruel and excessive—this is plain to see.”

The official, chastened and lost in thought, sighed deeply and said: “Now I understand the principle of transcending the common—it is beyond the grasp of the mediocre.”

*Admonitions on Learning, Scroll Three*

Bao Puzi said: Learning serves to clarify and settle the nature and principle, to winnow away dust and filth, to carve and forge raw ore, to grind and temper dullness and bluntness, to awaken and guide intelligence, to adorn and dye plain substance, to examine the past and foresee the future, to broadly traverse exhortations and admonitions, to look up and look down—all these lie herein. Matters of human affairs and the kingly Dao are perfected herein. Advancing, one may serve the state; retreating, one may preserve oneself. Thus, none among the sages and worthies fail to pursue it diligently, striving day and night, laying down their tools only when the sun stands at midday, persisting even through hunger, cold, peril, and hardship. How could this be for seeking gain in the present age? Truly, it is the natural joy of the endeavor.

Even the shallow skills of carving and painting, the simple arts of archery, charioteering, and horsemanship, still require practice and familiarity before mastery. How much more so the vastness of human principles, the profundity of the Dao and virtue, the transformations of yin and yang, the dispositions of ghosts and spirits—so distant, abstruse, and mysterious, truly difficult to grasp by innate knowledge. Though one speaks of white color, without dyeing it will not be radiant; though one speaks of sweet flavor, without blending it will not be fine.

Thus, the luminous jade remains unpolished, and its glow by night will not emerge; cinnabar and azurite go unrefined, and the pure hook’s strength will not be achieved. Fire will not kindle without drilling, nor blaze without fanning; water will not flow without channeling, nor deepen without accumulation. Therefore, though the raw essence lies within me, its fulfillment depends on what lies beyond.

Ascend the Towering Wind, grasp the Pillar of Dawn, and only then does one know the darkness and narrowness of the well and valley. Peruse the Seven Classics, ponder the Hundred Schools, and only then does one realize the utter confinement of facing a wall. To seek knowledge without study is like desiring fish without a net—though the mind toils, there will be no harvest. To fathom principles through broad learning is like riding the wind—though the body rests, one attains the distant.

When powder and rouge are applied, Xi Shi gains yet greater beauty, while the old wen is concealed; when the arts of the classics run deep, the gifted achieve penetrating insight, and the dull-witted awaken. The *wen* catalpa soars to the clouds, yet cannot be called a pavilion—it lacks the joinery of adze and wheel. One may be naturally bright and forthright, yet cannot be called a noble person—one does not discern the right and wrong of great moral principles.

To surpass a thousand *li* in a single morning, one must rely on the fleetness of the shadow-chasing steed; to cross the vast waves and reach distant shores, one must depend on the vessel of boat and oar. To see all that lies beyond without descending from the hall, one must turn to the written records; to plumb the abyss’s subtleties without wearying the spirit, one must find a luminous teacher. Thus, vermilion and green transform plain silk, and instruction dispels ignorance and obscurity.

Part the dark clouds to raise the great light, and the forms of the ten thousand things will have nowhere to hide; unfurl the bamboo and silk to examine past and present, and heaven and earth will have nowhere to conceal their truths. How much more so for ghosts and spirits—and how much more for human affairs!

Can mud and clay be made as firm as metal and jade? Can crooked wood be worked to meet the inked line? Can the hundred beasts be taught to array in battle? Can livestock be trained to advance and retreat? Can deep-dwelling fish be moved by sound? Can the trigger-stone be stirred by sincerity? How much more so for those who bear the Five Constants and are endowed with the most numinous essence!

The lowly and lofty steeds of the team—this is the work of instruction. The fierce and striking birds—this is the result of habituation.

Those common horses and wild hawks are by nature of the same kind—these are esteemed for their refinement, while those are despised for their crudeness. If one advances through the merest trickle without ceasing, one will surely merge with the vast sea; if one piles but a single basket of earth without rest, one will surely reach the towering summit. When great rivers surge and swell, the dragons and serpents swim in throngs; as days and months progress, virtue is established and the Dao perfected. Only then may one be called upright.

O to dream like the Great Yu or the Duke of Zhou—how could this merely loosen the fetters of ignorance! Of old, Zhong You wore a rooster’s crest and boar’s tusk, dangled cicada-shaped jade ornaments, and danced with a sword, boasting of the strength of southern mountain bamboo, wishing to rely on the unyielding force of nature. Yet the Master, with his skillful guidance, steeped him in virtue and teaching, and thus he became a disciple who ascended the hall and ranked among the four sages. Zizhang was a man of low birth, once a bandit of fierce cunning, but through gradual immersion in the Dao’s instruction, he became a renowned scholar, exchanging courtesies with kings and nobles—how could this merely free him from vulgarity!

Thus the worthy grieve at the fleeting nature of life in the world and lament the nameless oblivion of those who vanish without renown. Moved by the great teaching of “morning’s understanding,” they awaken to the boundless reach of the subtle Dao. Fearing the warning of “falling into brightness,” they realize how wayward thoughts breed madness. They do not idle the day away in gluttony, nor squander even an inch of time. Gazing at the flowing river, they steel their resolve; mourning the lightning speed of a passing crack, they dread the swiftness of time. They cut away frivolous desires and reduce worldly trivialities, cleanse the heart of anxiety over poverty, cast off the filth of grand ambitions, cease the diversions of hunting, gambling, and gaming, and correct the indolence of daytime naps and idle slumber. Knowing that mere thought brings no benefit, they spur themselves onto the sagely path.

They learn to accumulate knowledge, question to refine understanding, advance virtue and cultivate their work, review the old to grasp the new. The Duke of Zhou, though a supreme sage, read a hundred scrolls each day. Confucius, though divinely endowed, wore through three bindings of his bamboo texts. Mo Di, that great worthy, carried cartloads of writings. Dong Zhongshu, peerless in his generation, never glanced beyond his garden gate. Ni Kuan weeded with his books in hand; Lu Sheng cut reeds to copy texts. Huang Ba studied while shackled in prison; Ning Yue doubled his efforts through day and night. Thus they delved into the profound mysteries of the Dao, plumbed the depths of subtle words, beheld ten thousand ages as a single day, and knew the eight wilds as their own courtyard. They examined the waxing and waning of the seven luminaries, traced the transformations of the Three and Five, discerned the signs of rise and decline to come, verified good and ill in what had passed, grasped the workings of yin and yang as if in their palms, and perceived incipient omens as though already fulfilled. Thus their great virtue and towering achievements crowned their age, and their pure radiance and illustrious reputations spread without end.

Moreover, one hears of the *shangyang* bird and prepares for floods, consults the *niaonu* oracle and brings order to the east, inquires of the floating fruit to know the taste of its flesh, questions the mole cricket to recognize the *fenyang* beast, unfolds the *Lingbao* scriptures to understand mountain mysteries, or breaks apart a sacrificial platter to expound on the axle-load of a war chariot. Gazing at the *li* and *bi* constellations, one distinguishes the signs of yin and yang; observing the winter katydid, one grasps the error in intercalary months. What is so wondrous about this? It is merely learning.

Even children’s rhymes aid the sage’s ears and eyes—how much more the vastness of the *Classic of Mountains* and the *Search for the Dao*! Yet talents and natures differ in excellence, and thoughts vary in depth and brevity. Some possess innate knowledge and mature early; others must be led by the ear before they understand.

Swift comprehension and timely practice are the hooves of the *ji* and *lu* steeds; delayed understanding and late awakening are the wings of the quail and magpie. Though the former may soar to the ends of flight and leave the world behind, if they halt and do not advance, their steps will not surpass others. Though the latter may progress but a foot at a time, if they press forward without cease, they may yet cross mountains and marshes. The learning of the bright and the dull is much like this—youth is single-minded and hard to forget, while maturity scatters the spirit and loses focus. Thus, one must devote oneself to study early, and when mastery is attained, practice becomes one’s nature, no different from the natural Way.

Yet there are those of exceptional talent who, though hindered in their prime, may lose the dawn in *Yanggu* but reclaim it in *Yuyuan*. One then realizes that late sowing in fertile fields is still better than barren years of neglect. The parable of the candle’s light holds true.

The world’s path is fraught with peril, Confucian teachings have declined, and the ways of civil and martial virtue are on the verge of collapse. Some drown in sensual pleasures, others race down the path of rivalry. The poor and solitary who master the Six Arts, though endowed with the gifts of *You* and *Xia*, are suppressed beneath the Nine Springs; those who ride the wind and cling to the wings of the phoenix, though of mediocre talent, still soar beyond the clouds. Those who abandon fundamentals for trivialities call it diligent cultivation; those who embrace the classics to refine themselves are dismissed as obscure and impractical. Thus, all rush headlong through wind and rain, bearing frost and treading ice, clutching gold and grasping jade, carrying purity and hefting fat, to chase the easy shortcuts of crooked paths, seeking to sow in the morning and reap by evening.

As for those who lower their curtains and rest on high pillows, roaming their spirits through the Nine Classics, delving into profound meanings and savoring the Dao in stillness, steadfast in unshakable resolve, displaying their brilliance after the frost of winter—those who neither measure the world to follow its traces nor drift with the crowd like floating duckweed—such men are rare indeed. Those who fret over advancement or chafe at stagnation—how could they abandon the smooth, swift thoroughfares to cling to the difficult, dead-end paths? This is why no one lingers by the riverside, and why the *Zijin* was composed. Those who lament the customs of the age grieve deeply and sigh long; those who mourn the decline of the Dao harbor sorrow and sink into despair.

Yet cold and heat alternate, adversity ends and prosperity returns; civil and martial virtues rise and fall in turn—this is the constant pattern of the world. May the bandit hordes be swept away, the restoration come now, the Seven Luminaries follow their courses, and the old state be renewed. Sweep the heavens with the comet’s broom, stoke the great furnace of the Nine Suns, turn the great potter’s wheel of the imperial standard, and open the dark model to guide all things. Refine the masses, forge the finest, wash away accumulated dust, and return from error to rectitude. Sheathe the weapons of war, store the bows and arrows, revive the academies of the *Biyong*, gather the sons of the state, cultivate civil virtue, let the golden bell resound, and the jade chime vibrate. Summon wind and cloud from their hidden beginnings, present silk bundles to the recluses of the hills. Let the winged phoenix spread its pinions in the clear void, and the sturdy steed of mighty neck gallop a thousand *li*. Let those who harbor hidden brilliance, who are learned and widely versed—disciples like *Shen Gong* and *Fu Sheng*—unfurl their dark silk, ascend the rush-wheeled carriage, exhale pent-up breath, expound their pure principles, manifest their persons, and practice their Way. Thus may the sagely age follow the lofty tracks of *Tang* and *Yu*, race along the broad paths of grand peace, let the dark currents moisten the Nine Realms, and the breeze of kindness spread beyond all bounds. The Five Punishments set aside, odes of praise arise; harmonious *qi* pervades, and fine grain flourishes—is this not bliss?

In the days of Qin’s Second Emperor, Confucian learning was not esteemed; the Way of the former sages was abandoned, and the laws of punishment were practiced. The people saw no virtue, only execution. Thus, bewildered, they knew not the path to return from error; defeated, they knew not the means of self-rescue. They plummeted from the heights of the clouds into the abyss below, crushed like leeks into powder. From the exalted to the lowly, can there be no mirror?

**Scroll Four: On Exalting Learning**
*Baopuzi* says: Those who fix their gaze on autumn’s down do not see the splendor of the heavens; those who indulge their minds in trivial matters do not perceive the vastness of the Confucian and Daoist Ways. He who savors the salted fish forgets the orchid and iris; he who is lost in the great cannot return. Those who receive the carpenter’s line produce no crooked wood; those who are steeped in the Dao’s teachings become no perverse men. No method of cultivation surpasses learning. The breadth of learning lies in tirelessness; tirelessness lies in steadfast resolve. If resolve is not firm, the poor and lowly will be consumed by the struggle for survival, while the rich and noble will drown in ease and pleasure. Thus, those of vast learning and deep insight appear but once in an age, while those who face the wall tread shoulder to shoulder.

If the common scholar tills the soil by day to feed himself and studies by firelight at night; if the official, in the intervals of feasting, occasionally turns to admonition and reflection—then the world would have no blind men, and *You* and *Xia* would not be lacking. Yet there are those pressed by hunger and cold, whose coarse fare is insufficient, whose skin is ravaged by wind and frost, whose mouths lack even chaff and bran. They have no means to seek a teacher, and their families face urgent needs from dusk to dawn. If they set aside the plow, farming suffers; if they take up the scroll, sustenance fails. Though their studies are incomplete, they are not to blame. This is like the thousand-*li* steed trapped beneath the salt cart, or the red ore of the *Chi* blade that never enters *Ou Ye*’s forge.

As for the sons of nobles and princes, who idle in luxury and pleasure, who saunter among silks and fine fabrics, ignorant of the hardships of sowing and reaping, whose eyes tire of black and yellow, whose ears weary of *Zheng* and *Wei* music, whose noses are sated with orchid and musk, whose mouths are dulled by rich meats, who in winter layer fox and sable in splendid warmth, and in summer drape themselves in sheer gauze and fluttering silk; who ride forth in the light carriages of *Qing Feng*, and feast within the radiant chambers; who adorn their pillars with kingfisher and jade, and hoard without end in their chests and coffers; who display alluring beauties to amuse their hearts, and drown in fine wine to the point of stupor; who lead in revelry and preside over gambling—such men do not understand literature, regard scholars as worthless as weeds, lack classical references in speech and writing, and err in their allusions to matters and categories.

Those who engage in heated debate are defeated before the battle is joined; when faced with doubt, they wither before their time. Even one as eloquent as Shu Mai would be no different from the blind—what could he discern?

The *Baopuzi* states: It is said that the emperor’s eldest heir must enter the Grand Academy to receive instruction from a teacher and inquire into the Dao. Those who study among the state’s youth must first understand how to be a minister before they can become a ruler, and first understand how to be a son before they can become a father. Thus, one establishes learning before taking office; to govern without study is to wield a blade and wound oneself—just as Zheng Qiao lamented. To indulge emotions and follow desires is to be no true man.

Yet the sons of the nobility, born within the depths of the palace and raised by women’s hands, have never known the toil of anxiety or fear. Some are still swaddled in infancy when they are granted offices of blue and purple; their talents barely suffice to wear robes and caps, yet they occupy eminent positions. They wield the power over life and death, grasp the handle of promotion and demotion, decide honor and disgrace through bestowal or deprivation, and sway benefit and harm with a word. Love and hatred never cease for a moment; praise and slander assail their ears. Suspicion and resemblance, seeming right yet wrong, give rise to groundless accusations through opportunity, or crafty words through false trust. The permutations of intrigue are endless, beyond the reckoning of the shrewdest calculation or the scrutiny of brush and ink.

Without the study of method, how can one discern the true from the false in right and wrong, or grasp the principles of past and present conduct to awaken to truth? Without such clarity, how can one avoid the calamity of a toppled nest or an overturned chariot? The sages of old, though exalted, never forgot the peril of their position. Loving their children, they desired to teach them the proper way, carving and polishing them, keeping them far from deceit and falsehood. They chose enlightened teachers to shape them, selected worthy friends to gradually influence them, urged them to broad reading, and showed them success and failure. They made them examine the past to understand the future, observe others to know themselves, drove them onto the straight path, and restrained them within bounds—so that they stood in awe as if treading a precipice of ten thousand fathoms, or trembled as if riding a galloping steed across thin ice. Thus they could avoid many regrets, preserving their integrity and good fortune.

In former times, the Dou clan enjoyed the blessings of inherited instruction, while Huo Yu suffered the disaster of willful conduct. Zhongshan and Dongping found security in their love of antiquity, while Yan Ci and Liu were imperiled by their ignorance, like men facing a wall. The past is not forgotten—it is a fine mirror for the present. Tang and Wu were influenced by Yi and Lü, and their rise was swift; Xin and Gui were swayed by flatterers, and their fall was sudden.

Friends and teachers must be chosen with the utmost care. One should seek those of humble origins and virtuous conduct, who stand firm in hardship and are feared for their integrity. Those whose classical learning rivals Zhongshu or Huan Rong, or whose uprightness is as strong as Gong Sui or Wang Ji, can discourse day and night on the ultimate Way of loyalty and filial piety, sternly admonish with the patterns of survival and ruin, wash away filth and stain, guard against wickedness and correct crookedness. Such men can surely restrain their passions and uphold the law, guiding one into the teachings of virtue.

But in the late years of the Han and the final days of Wu, it was not so. Appointments were made by gazing at the canopies of carriages, relying on the flowery praise of cliques. They bore the name of teachers and friends, but lacked the substance of offering counsel. Far from being beneficial, they were instead harmful. What they expounded was not the Dao or virtue; what they recommended was not loyalty or benefit.

Only the pursuit of novel melodies and seductive beauty, of lithe bodies and skillful hands, occupied their days—judging the purity or huskiness of songs, adjusting the length of pipes and strings, comparing the swiftness or sluggishness of hounds and horses, debating the finest locations for excursions, weighing the merits and flaws of divergent paths, measuring the refinement or coarseness of carvings, testing the dexterity or clumsiness of board games, calculating the victories and defeats of hunting and gambling, ranking the beauty or plainness of courtesans and concubines, critiquing the vulgarity or elegance of attire, disputing the quality of mounts, and arguing the density or sparseness of bows and swords. They gathered the strange and assembled the rare without limit, their excesses swelling day by day, month by month.

When they spoke of palaces, they likened them to distant jade terraces and crystal chambers, or modeled them after the nearby grandeur of Epang and Linguang. A thousand gates and ten thousand halls were deemed cramped; Kunming and Taiye lakes, shallow and crude. They scoffed at thatched roofs as unworthy and derided earthen steps as rustic.

The people’s strength was exhausted by labor, their stores depleted by frivolities. They heaped earth into mountains to rival Song and Hua, diverted rivers to mimic the Nine Streams. They ascended towers that pierced the clouds, opened lattice windows that brushed the heavens. Lewd music clamored to bewilder the ears, fluttering sleeves dazzled to confuse the eyes. The licentious quarters of Pu and Beili echoed with ceaseless performances—cries and shouts blurring day into night. Revelers lingered over cups of wine, drowned in the strains of strings and pipes. Some erected canopies of emerald plumes, others hunted fierce birds in the suburbs, galloped swift steeds over treacherous peaks, or exposed servants to the blazing sun. They set out at dusk and returned by starlight, neglecting urgent affairs, abandoning rewards and punishments.

Some launched ornate barges on shimmering waters, cast dense nets in green rivers, dangled fragrant bait in rippling pools, or unleashed songs in clear depths. They loosed high-flying arrows to bring down light geese, drew submerged lines to snare hidden fish. Others set snares in forests and foothills, encircled mountains and marshes with layered nets, arrayed crimson banners amid lush grass, or raced fleet horses across the plains. They unleashed hounds to devour cunning beasts, flew falcons to strike soaring birds, felled fierce rhinos with powerful crossbows, and slew bears and tigers with long halberds. Such pursuits, far from sating them, stretched on for years without end.

To this were added seasonal gatherings, farewell feasts, and celebratory banquets, where they entertained favored guests and welcomed distinguished visitors bearing gifts. The affairs of the world pressed upon them without respite. Thus, refined propriety receded, and indulgence deepened. Their estrangement from Confucian learning grew ever more distant. Few indeed were those who could discern the principles of rise and decline, extricate themselves from this morass, abandon the perilous path of moral decay, and tread the enduring Way of eternal order.

Alas! This is why those who preserved their states and families were so rare, while those who collapsed in ruin and wept blood were beyond counting.

Now, with a sagely and enlightened ruler on the throne, who examines antiquity to aid the world, strengthens dikes to prevent breaches, and clarifies praise and censure to encourage virtue and discourage vice, it is hoped that the imperial clansmen, noble houses, and wealthy youths will vie to esteem Confucian arts, moderate their pursuit of frivolous arts, set aside the nonessential teachings of Laozi and Zhuangzi, and refine their mastery of the Six Classics’ true Way.

**Chapter Five: The Way of the Ruler**
*Baopuzi* states:
When the pure and subtle *qi* ascended to form the heavens and the turbid and heavy *qi* descended to form the earth, the hierarchy of honor and awe became manifest. The ancient sages drew from the Two Modes to establish the Way of ruler and minister; they instituted offices and divided duties, and thus the transformative harmony of governance flourished. Those who would rule must first cultivate themselves to lead the realm, cast aside factionalism to uphold the royal Way, discard personal bias to exemplify perfect impartiality, and embrace the cosmos to encompass all things.

Having already discerned truth and falsehood beyond the material world, they further examine their own perceptions. Having already sharpened their hearing to receive all voices, they further refine their own understanding. They emulate the level of water to advance the good, sever the string of a broken lute to dismiss the wicked, illuminate virtue and block transgression, employ the near to cherish the distant, and honor the worthy. They ensure that compasses exhaust their circles, squares fulfill their angles, plumb lines extend their straightness, and axes perfect their cuts. No vessel is burdened beyond its capacity, no talent misapplied to its task.

They scrutinize names to demand substance, frequently inspect and relieve hardship, establish canonical teachings to set the people’s standard, and clarify praise and censure to encourage virtue and discourage vice. They enforce uniform standards to prevent presumption and excess, and carefully distinguish right from wrong to dispel obscurity and stinginess. In bestowing rewards, they avoid undeserved favor; in meting out punishments, they ensure no grievance remains unaddressed. They mold their people with the Six Arts, guide them with loyalty and trust, govern with kindness and harmony, and regulate with ritual and law. They elevate the obscure and hasten the suppressed, stir the clear currents to purify the turbid.

Thus, no one follows devious paths, and no affair exceeds its proper bounds. Those who serve at court or govern the people dare not encroach upon others’ offices or overstep their roles. Those who lead troops into battle dare not shrink from danger or cling to life. They delight the near to embrace the distant, cultivate culture to attract the estranged. They enrich the people’s wealth and grain, broaden the path of moral advancement, and curb the proliferation of deceitful schemes. They then clarify punishments and rectify laws, adjudicate cases with compassion and reverence. When governance is pure and transformation complete, they conceal flaws and overlook faults, and the Five Teachings prevail in magnanimity.

Externally, they unite the many talents in civil and military affairs; internally, they strengthen the imperial lineage as a bulwark. They ensure that the close and distant support one another, the tail serving as the body’s trunk. Though branches may flourish, there is no fear of harming the root; though streams may swell, there is no risk of forsaking the source. Like mountains standing firm as stone and peaks, they deter covetous ambitions.

Observing the annihilation of the Sanmiao, one realizes that even great rivers cannot be relied upon. Witnessing the fall of Youyu, one understands that even sheer cliffs cannot be trusted. The Yangtze and Han rivers remained, yet mighty Chu suffered humiliation. Jian’ge stood unchanged, yet Ziyang’s clan was exterminated. The Four Peaks and Three Hills have never belonged to a single family; golden cities and boiling moats are no match for human harmony. Security lies beyond the seas, not in mountains and rivers.

Thus, wise rulers hold fast to their fears of insufficiency and hasten to correct even the slightest faults.

Even when plans prove sound, one must still ponder dangers without respite; though battles are won and lands expand, one must still guard against complacency and remain vigilant by night. Emulating the vastness of the celestial dome, one models oneself on the earth’s boundless capacity to bear all things. Radiating profound brilliance to illuminate the subtle, one extends foresight to what has not yet shown signs. Drawing warmth from spring’s sunlight to nurture and nourish, one tempers all things with autumn’s frost to instill discipline.

One weighs words from the provinces to compare differences, balances scales to assess the multitude of voices. Emptying oneself to fully grasp the sentiments below, one defers credit to encourage future merit. Govern with technique, and the beginning and end may be exhausted; order with measure, and disparities may be aligned. Lofty as the windswept heights of Langfeng, none below can gauge one’s weight; profound as the abyss of ten thousand fathoms, even close attendants cannot fathom its depth. Thus, the ruler’s flowing source knows no end, and the talents of all officials are fully displayed; one’s own bounds extend without limit, while others’ strengths and weaknesses are clearly defined.

When decrees are issued and commands proclaimed, the rumble of chariots resounds like thunder, unshaken by specious arguments. When laws are drawn and systems established, they shine as brightly as the seven luminaries adorning the sky, unwarped by favor or resentment. Grand strategies cast a far-reaching net, dense as clouds gathering on high. Abiding in steadfastness to accomplish tasks, one stands as firm as Mount Song and Mount Tai rooted in the earth.

One anticipates fortune and misfortune before they sprout, scrutinizes praise and blame from the mouths of clever speakers. One does not allow simplicity to scatter into artifice, nor let unity dissolve into duplicity. Though capable of independent judgment, one must broadly accept advice from even the humblest; though devoted to magnanimity, one must purify one’s ears against insidious slander.

When the people suffer cold and hunger, one pities them and blames oneself; when the common folk commit offenses, one declares the fault lies with oneself. When auspicious omens arrive, one reflects on the blessings of divine favor; if one encounters Heaven’s wrath, one recalls the self-reproach of the mulberry grove. One does not hesitate to change the strings when the tune requires adjustment, nor feel shame in retracing one’s steps after morning’s errors.

With the vigilance of a tiger’s gaze, one guards secrets; with the poise of a qilin, one receives the distant. If no elder sings the *Hit Wall* song on the road, one feels ashamed to hear harmonious music; if the people suffer famine, one feels humbled before a feast of a hundred dishes. Dwelling in soaring pavilions that scrape the sky, one fears the exhaustion of laborers; savoring fine delicacies beneath the moon’s glow, one worries over missed planting seasons. Listening to the indulgent strains of pipes and strings, one grieves over excess in pleasure; gazing upon ornate splendor, one frets over the cruelty of levied taxes.

Following Yao’s coarse robes and Shun’s plain garments, emulating King Wen of Wei’s simple silks; recalling the humble palace of the Xia, recognizing the unbuilt Terrace of the Dew; heeding the disaster summoned by Zhanghua, awakening to the swift ruin of Epang. In proclamations and oaths, one remembers the failures of timely trust. In indulgence, one senses how Daji and Baosi bewitched rulers. In campaigns, one measures strength and timing, ensuring no wailing resentment arises from a hundred *li*; in executions, one leaves no room for sentiment, sparing no wronged soul like Chi Yi’s grievance.

One takes warning from Du Bo’s fate at the hands of King Zhou, and the tale of the man who mistook a pig for a man. In disinheriting the rightful heir, one heeds the grave error of Duke Xian of Jin; in elevating a concubine’s son, one recalls Liu Biao’s extinguished lineage. In hunting, one rejoices in losing game to gain worthy men, recognizing the wisdom of casting wide nets to attract the distant; in partiality, one fears the slander of the sleeve-bound bee and the monopolizing favor of Flying Swallow. In relying on a single minister, one recalls the tyranny of the deer and horse, and the enmity of Gong and Xian toward the upright.

In accepting strategies, one remembers Gaozu’s haste to receive counsel and Emperor Jing’s execution of Zhou Yafu. When fine wines are offered, one distances Yi Di; when flattery and indulgence arise, one recalls the downfall of Luan Bu and Ji Shen. One removes the sycophancy of Zheng’s son, befriends the kindness of Fang Mi. Learning from the white dragon’s lesson, one ceases reckless behavior; observing the emaciated horse, one curbs insatiable desires. One prevents the transformation of humans into swine within the six palaces, halts the quest for blood-sweating horses from distant lands.

One removes vicious dogs to quell the scourge of drunkenness, purchases horse bones to attract steeds of swift wind. One honors the frog to encourage bravery, avoids the mantis to inspire martial valor. One heeds the candid words of Gonglu, embraces the integrity of armor-clad soldiers. One plucks useless hairs from back and belly, gathers the six pinions for soaring flight. One silences flatterers like reed pipes to heed the admonitions of the Minister of Works, breaks the luxuriant branches to emulate the virtue of the Lord of Liang. One banishes Dan Ji to end the allure of seduction, retires Zi Xia to dispel the bewitchment of the "leftover peach."

One conceals the fish in the depths, wields the handle of the sharp blade. One does not shrink from the trouble of moving firewood to prevent the cost of scorching. One fosters the cultivation of integrity and shame, clarifies the standards of examination. Anger does not exceed the law to inflict cruelty, joy does not transgress statutes to bestow lavish gifts. One severs affection for the beloved when they transgress, granting no pardon; one discerns goodness in the despised, leaving no merit unrecognized.

One bends low to welcome loyalty, hears harsh words without concealment. One widely solicits criticism, though it may be demeaning, without rejection. One overlooks minor flaws to employ great talents, forgets recent faults to remember distant achievements, enabling men like Cao Gui and Meng Ming to redeem themselves, and Wei Shang and Zhang Chang to erase their disgrace. One punishes the treacherous minister who bore the hook, yet records his great merit in restoring order; one unbinds Li Zuoche, who offered the lofty strategy to cease war.

Then the owl and kite transform into mandarin ducks and simurghs, deceit and falsehood turn to loyalty and steadfastness. The sharpest blades emerge from barren salt flats, luminous pearls rise from the mire. Sharpened points and whetstones stand ready, the nine virtues harmonize in perfect accord. Turning westward, one hesitates in deference to seek the counsel of teachers and friends; honoring the elders, one fosters filial piety and brotherly respect. Thus, those who dwell in the depths lift their gaze to ascend, those who nest in the mountains bow to gather.

Brilliant ministers assist within, the Nine Tigers guard without. Governance is sound above, and all things incline below; kindness flows near, and grace extends afar. The wise and sagacious exert themselves in their posts, the common folk yield their boundaries in the marshes. Then, one abolishes the harshest laws, revives the pure breeze of great harmony. With carriages of rushes and jade silks, one draws forth the hidden talents of the countryside; the finest minds assemble to exhaustively debate the gains and losses of the Dao.

One reduces the multitude of shepherds, restores the purest unfermented wine. One expands the gates of benevolence and yielding, blocks the ford of ostentation and rivalry.
