One commends the conduct of righteousness, upholds the standards of the Dao and simplicity. Those who cleave to virtue rejoice as sprouts delight in sweet rain; those who return are like flowing streams rushing to the great river.

The common folk rest secure, as green leaves cling to long branches; the barbarians gaze up, as all stars revolve around the North Star. Thus, the seven celestial bodies do not disorder the patterns of the dark pole, cold and warmth do not err in their seasons. The four auspicious beasts appear in full, orchid blossoms and magic fungi glow resplendent. Sweet dew drips and gathers in the clouds, fine grain sways and fills the granaries.

The red drought demon flees to the celestial river, the black pestilence is bound in the northern wastes. The hundred rivers know no boiling tumult, the Southern Dipper silences its blight upon the crops. Things do not wither untimely, people utter no sighs of lament. Prisons stand empty, the five punishments lie dormant.

Where the royal calendar does not reach, where the gentry’s cap and sash do not extend—those in felt and fur, dwelling in mountains or exiled by the sea—none fail to rejoice and reform, moved by harmony and crossing countless tongues. Rare birds offer tribute at the vermilion court, jade rings are presented from the westernmost reaches. The round-headed swiftly reform, like mist drifting with the strong wind; distant frontiers obey commands, as echoes answer the purest sound.

Truly, this is the pinnacle of flourishing achievement, the path trodden by the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors. Thus, one secures the ancestral temple without end, multiplies the branches of the lineage for a hundred generations. For when roots run deep, the branches flourish; when those below are joyful, those above are at peace.

If a horse is untrained, even Zaofu cannot urge it a thousand *li*; if the people do not submit, even Yao and Shun cannot achieve the beauty of heaven’s harmony. When a horse is pushed to its limit, unruly behavior arises, and collapse is the only fear; when the people are oppressed, rebellion multiplies, and disaster must strike. How can one not tremble with vigilance, awaiting the dawn? How can one not quake with caution, ever mindful of peril?

If a ruler does not reflect deeply on order and chaos, does not keenly observe the signs of ruin—though his gaze discerns the autumn down a hundred *xun* away, though his ear distinguishes the purity of the eight sounds, though his brush scatters jade-like prose, though his grasp shatters halberds and hooks, though his heart recites ten thousand verses, though his tongue deploys waves of eloquence—it is still of no use when the earth crumbles, no remedy when tiles shatter. Why? Because he neglects the great and attends to the trivial, mires himself in the work of subordinates, and remains blind to the root of the matter.

If one can truly act beyond the crowd, tread as if over deep ice, remain mindful of the warning against reckless speed even in peace, and never abandon the fear of ruin even in prosperity; if one grasps the main threads to order the details, wields the principles of the Dao to guide all talents; if Han and Bai exert their strength to repel enemies, Xiao and Cao exhaust their abilities to govern the state; if the ruler’s resolve is firm and decisive, and his advisors unite in offering their wisest counsel—then, though the ruler may recline in the jade chamber, wander the cloud-topped pavilion, let wine spoil in the goblet, and weary musicians with endless dance, he may still govern with folded arms and rely on the worthy, sleep soundly and hold them accountable.

Why must one dwell in a humble thatched hut, eat plain broth, endure the toil of guarding the gate, or bear the hardship of exhausting one’s mandate—only then to bestow kindness upon the common folk and extend the Dao beyond the seas?

The benighted ruler, however, does otherwise. In governance, he may be benevolent yet indecisive, allowing red and purple to blur, rewarding no uprightness, punishing no wickedness. Or he may be harsh and cruel, all might without mercy, his punishments excessive, unforgiving, and unjust. The roots are exposed, the foundation crumbles, perilous as a nest on a curtain—yet he compares himself to the sun and moon, likens his stability to Mount Tai, believing that emulating the enlightened and virtuous is no great challenge, and that caution and reverence are beneath consideration.

Thus, the innocent and blameless suffer wanton punishment, the people see no virtue, only the sword. In appointing officials, he deems those who flatter his will as worthy; in promoting talent, he favors those close to him. The highest ministers are chosen from the empress’s kin; decisions on vital matters rest with sycophants. Those he elevates are distant relatives beyond the nine clans, their abilities unexamined; those he trusts are petty flatterers, while he detests the upright and forthright. Those he suppresses are mere echoes, judged without true discernment; those he favors are praised without regard for merit.

Officials entrusted with key posts and the governance of the people, generals charged with military command—some grow corrupt and ruin their domains, some scheme for private gain and disrupt the court, some are weak and fail in their duties, some are timid and lose military advantage. Yet the ruler remains oblivious, unwilling to demote or dismiss them, instead entrusting them further, hoping for late redemption. But small vessels cannot bear great burdens, and disaster soon follows.

Talented and far-sighted men without patronage—some don humble garb and withdraw from court, some languish in obscurity and hardship, holding their wisdom in silence. The people’s strength goes untapped, and the decline of the state becomes inevitable.

Moreover, he neglects the classics and their warnings, never reading or heeding them, indulging instead in frivolity and revelry, lost in pleasure and distraction.

Lofty towers are built to gaze down upon virtue and power, vast parks and gardens expand while the embrace of worthy men narrows. Deep pools and ponds are dug while trust and good faith grow shallow. Delight is taken in hounds and steeds, and honest remonstrance is despised. Pearls and jades are prized while wisdom and strategy are held cheap. Luxurious silks abound, but benevolent grace is stinted. Relief is delayed while exactions are hastened. Diligence is spent on the hunt, and agriculture is neglected. Land consolidation is favored, and the people’s lives are made light. Entertainers and performers are advanced, and the refined and learned are set aside. Favorites are enriched while warriors are slighted. Sensual pleasures flow unchecked, and state affairs are forgotten. Intoxication and diversion come first, judgment and governance afterward. Corvée labor is multiplied, and rewards and gifts grow sparse. Craftsmen are set to fashioning costly but useless objects, and granaries are filled with grain-fed livestock.

Thus, peril and ruin cannot be blamed on Heaven, nor weakness and decline attributed to others. For good and ill arise from oneself—were Tang and Wu exceptions? Of old, King Wen of Zhou covered the unburied bones of the dead, and all under Heaven praised his benevolence. King Zhou of Yin carved out the heart of Bi Gan, and the Four Seas reviled his cruelty. The gaze of the realm is fixed upon the ruler, and praise or blame spreads swiftly. The difference between success and failure does not lie in great deeds alone.

When a ruler’s reputation is weighty, even distant tribes submit in allegiance, yet this cannot be feigned. When reproach accumulates, even the Central States turn away in disaffection, and no words can restore their loyalty. Therefore, though small virtues may bring no great benefit, they must not be neglected; though minor vices may bring no immediate calamity, they must not be indulged.

If one gives free rein to passion and desire, refusing to share joy with all under Heaven, then when distress comes, none will offer solace. If the foundation is eroded and the heights resented, one will not perceive the collapse below until the summit crumbles—and then none will come to prop it up. Thus, the reins slip from one’s grasp, divine treasures are lent and not returned, strength is exhausted, wealth is depleted, and the people can bear the burden no longer. Resentment seethes below, Heaven’s wrath gathers above. Tian Cheng and Dao Quan plotted rebellion within the palace tents; King Wen of Zhou seized two-thirds of the land from his western neighbor. Men like Chen Sheng and Wu Guang brandished their swords and raised their voices; Liu Bang and Xiang Yu wielded their halberds and stirred the storm. Scaling ladders ascend the hundred-zhang walls, gleaming blades clash beneath the palace gates. Flying arrows rain down within, the palace guards scatter without. Only then does the ruler grieve and long for the great worthies of bygone ages, sweep the path to welcome the wisdom of recluses, yearn for Yi and Lü in their mountain retreats, and summon Sun and Wu from the wilds. He bows to wise counsel but finds no one to heed it, seeks sound strategies but receives none. It is like hauling water from the sea to douse a burning mansion, or building a boat on the islet when the floodwaters have already risen to the rafters.

The lofty title of "August" cannot be built on arrogance and stinginess, nor can the investiture of the Eastern Peak be maintained through wanton indulgence.

The supreme sage, even when spurring his chariot at full speed, still fears falling short of those who came before; yet the mediocre ruler, ambling along with reins in hand, believes himself their superior. Some, in times of peace, reflect on peril; others, amid peril, indulge in ease. Some, having achieved order and stability, remain neither idle nor dissolute; others, teetering on the brink like eggs stacked precariously, remain oblivious and unmoved. Were it not for the drowning folly of tyrants like Xin and Gui, why would we prize the lofty wisdom of those enlightened and discerning? Ever mindful of this truth—oh, may it ever be so!

**On the Integrity of Ministers, Scroll Six**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity states: In the age of Tang and Yu, though they revered antiquity and governed with wisdom, they still relied on the assistance of their ministers to achieve their towering success. Thus, the reign of Emperor Yao flourished, all affairs coalesced in harmony, the four gates stood solemn, the hundred offices followed their seasons, the barbarians caused no disturbances to the Central Kingdom, and the phoenix nested in the palace pavilions. Like the head and limbs, though differing in rank, they were as one, mutually dependent. When the ruler measures ability before appointing, he avoids the calamity of overturned food baskets; when the minister assesses his own talent before accepting office, he escapes the disaster of exile. Those who, like shadows and echoes, bow and obey without question are but hollow vessels of idleness. Those who defy commands and remonstrate with unyielding integrity are the pillars of the state. Those who anticipate and comply with every whim are flatterers; those who correct errors and dissent are the state’s stalwart defenders. They must prostrate themselves before the axe and offer their lives to speak truth, standing firm even before the cauldron’s searing heat. If loyal yet suspected, slandered yet unheard, they may withdraw; if their death would serve no purpose but only compound the ruler’s faults, they may depart.

In action, they rely not on unorthodox precedents; in stillness, they follow not unprincipled models. They tolerate no favoritism in requests, nor do they bend the law for personal gain. They mete out punishment justly, without excess toward those they resent, and bestow rewards fairly, without bias toward those who flatter them. They do not usurp authority to seize power, nor feign purity while harboring corruption. In office, they strive to expose flaws with forthright counsel; in retreat, they promote the worthy without obstruction. Rising early and retiring late, they grieve over the people’s unrest; living frugally and restraining their desires, they tread as if upon thin ice. They offer strategies and recommend scholars without boasting of their deeds; ill-gotten gains never lodge in their hearts. In court, they hold fast to the integrity of an arrow; in private, they embody the purity of a lamb. Facing peril and hardship, they forget their families and disregard their lives. Wielding the scales of office, they judge with impartiality, free of partiality. They emulate the guidance of Xiao and Cao, admire the brilliant schemes of Zhang and Chen, follow Zhou Bo’s unwavering loyalty, model themselves on the forthrightness of the two Baos, uphold the steadfast resolve of Yuan and Hong, and maintain the unyielding resolve of Tian. They accept the fate of being roasted alive for their principles, while guarding against the fate of Han and Ying, who lost their loyalty. In service, they do not shirk labor; in return, they do not boast of their merits. They ascribe achievements to others and take blame upon themselves, yielding precedence to all. Ever sincere and reverent, they stand as if the awe-inspiring majesty of Heaven were before them; day and night, they remain vigilant, as if the seething cauldron were at their side.

Bearing the weight of their charge, they take Yi and Zhou as their exemplars; serving the realm, they follow the path of Ji and Shao. In mourning the departed and caring for the living, they devote themselves to loyalty without wavering. When seizing traitors who violate the law, they strike like hawks and falcons upon sparrows. Defending the frontiers, they emulate the illustrious footsteps of Wei Jiang and Li Mu; governing the people, they aspire to the virtuous governance of Wen Weng and Xin Chen. The utmost loyalist would sacrifice all for the state—how much less would he harbor cunning to deceive his ruler? The righteous, when duty demands, would face annihilation without hesitation—how much less would they shrink from demotion or disgrace? Thus, they preserve their honor, ennoble their kin, and entrust their legacy to the people’s songs. Like the ritual vessels of Kunwu, the capable engrave their merits. What manner of men were Gaoyao and Hou Ji? Truly, they were none other than this!

The Master of Embracing Simplicity states: If a minister’s achievements are not grand, he feels shame at the emptiness of his emoluments; if his merits are not flourishing, he feels humiliation at the undeserved height of his rank. Upholding trust and reflecting on compliance, Heaven and man lend their support; fearing excess and dwelling in humility, one ensures lasting blessings. Every step adheres to the Dao and the law; every gesture follows the plumb line. Though honors may be delayed, disgrace will surely be averted. But those who harm their superiors to curry favor below, who abandon the public good for private gain, who flatter and fawn, adding water to water—even when the ruler’s actions are misguided, they smile and praise them. They repeatedly offer luxuries, leading the ruler into depravity. With cunning words, they undermine governance; with obsequious smiles, they ingratiate themselves. Above, they blind the ruler’s clarity; below, they block the path of worthy men. Externally, they forge alliances with foreign powers; internally, they cultivate factions against the public good. Though their talents may adorn falsehoods and their words gloss over faults, those who wield power like Zhao Gao or dominate the court like Dong Zhuo have never escaped being butchered by the sword or their families consumed by fire. Yet the foolish and blind abandon the upright for the crooked, forsake truth for falsehood, cling to ruinous paths, and refuse to change. The calamities that befall them do not descend from Heaven.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity states: Ministers are likened to limbs—they cannot refuse the tasks of treading on ice or grasping fire. Thus, the ancients compared them to the earth: dig into it, and springs of water flow forth; plant in it, and a hundred grains flourish. The living stand upon it; the dead return to it. Though their merits are great, they do not seek reward; though their toil is exhausting, they dare not complain. Those who truly understand this art grasp the key to self-preservation.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity states: When a minister’s duties are limited, governance proceeds smoothly; when his responsibilities are vast, delays and obstructions multiply. Without the strength of Meng Ben or Xia Yu, one cannot shoulder the weight of multiple men; without the prowess of ten thousand, one cannot oversee diverse offices. This is why the Marquis of Han condemned the encroachment of his subordinates, and why Ziyuan feared the disaster of being unequal to his task. Yet if one’s talents are unparalleled, excelling in both civil and martial arts, possessing the profound strategies of a trusted advisor within and the far-reaching tactics of a conqueror without—even if affairs are numerous, they will be managed with greater ease, and both paths will be traversed successfully. To abandon such a one would disrupt the proper order; to entrust the task to another would be impossible. In such cases, holding multiple offices is permissible.

If one lacks such capacity, one ought to reflect and withdraw, for like a cart bearing too heavy a load, few will not fall short. Ordinary men, greedy for glory, fail to consider the calamities that follow; once their own ruin is complete, disaster befalls their lord and kin—how lamentable! All recoil from the axe before the unhewn timber, yet none decline offices they cannot bear.

Alas! This is why Chen Ping and Li Yiji warned against overtaxing one’s strength, and why Zhang Liang loftily stepped back to avoid overreaching.

**Chapter Seven: Sound Principles**
The Master Embracing Simplicity says: Birds that alight without choosing their perch will surely be those ensnared by nets. Those who enter service without assessing the times will surely be men who meet with peril and disgrace. When the times favor one, one soars like a responding dragon gazing upon auspicious clouds; when the times turn against one, one is cast adrift like a great fish stranded on dry land. Thus the wise conceal their talents to await the right moment, and hide themselves to act when the time is ripe. If a lofty crag is about to collapse, no slender thread can hold it; if the Dragon Gate seethes, no handful of earth can dam it. Then one does not recklessly risk ruin, nor gamble on luck.

The Master Embracing Simplicity says: When the Duke of Zhou assumed the royal regency, when Yi Yin deposed Tai Jia, when Huo Guang deposed the Marquis of Changyi, and when Sun Chen deposed the young emperor, these were cases of setting aside the Dao to employ expediency in order to stabilize the altars of the state. Yet the Duke of Zhou was exiled amid the clamor of slander filling the roads; Yi Yin ultimately suffered execution, and a great fog lasted three days; Huo Guang barely escaped harm in his own lifetime, and his family was soon destroyed; before the shade of the mulberry tree had shifted, Sun Chen’s head and feet lay in separate places. Their laughter had not yet faded when their wailing began.

If one does not uphold what is precarious, of what use are the seven contending ministers? Without the Dao, nothing can be saved. This emboldened men like Wang Mang to hatch their treacherous schemes, citing past precedents to justify their wrongs while harboring the hearts of wolves and jackals. The disorders of later ages stem from the examples of Yi and Huo. Future gentlemen should take heed.

Matters of deposition and enthronement are minor compliance masking great rebellion—they cannot be prolonged. Even the cunning of King Zhao of Yan was met with censure; how much worse, then, to depose one’s sovereign! Such men may carry out their plans and succeed for a time before suffering calamity. Yet if, from the outset of their schemes, greedy men betray them, their entire clans are exterminated and their sacrifices obliterated—while others are enfeoffed in their stead—such cases are not few.

If treacherous flatterers abet tyranny, like Jie’s Gan Xin and Tui Duo, or Zhou’s Chong E and Lai, or Li’s faction, replacing them with loyal and capable men is no difficult task. To purge the many evils at the ruler’s side, to banish the wicked clans to the four frontiers, to command troops and guard the borders, to uphold the straight Dao and abide by the law, to wield the axe with severity, to maintain a stern demeanor and adhere to the plumb line, to reward clearly and punish without fail, to spare none who transgress, to appoint the worthy and employ the capable, to ally only with the loyal, to act without presumption, to proceed only after consultation—if the ruler errs, to correct him with reasoned remonstrance; to remain vigilant and reverent, never forgetting caution—thus may the altars of the state be eternally secure above, and one’s own person remain free from harm below. When the task is accomplished, one does not linger but petitions to retire, tenders one’s bones, and recommends a worthy successor. Is this not a course of ample ease? Why seize the sovereign’s seals and endanger the lord one serves?

The ruler is as Heaven, as a father. If the ruler may be deposed, then Heaven may be altered, and fathers may be replaced. Those whose merit overshadows the age receive no reward; those whose might threatens the ruler’s person court danger. Even those who have conquered and subdued, whose achievements are unmatched, are cast aside like bows when the birds are gone, or cooked like hounds when the hares are caught. How much more so for those who depose their rulers and yet expect the favor of their successors? This is no different from a son who abandons his own parents in the mountains, takes another to raise him, and claims, “I am as filial as Boyu or Zeng Shen, but my parents were unworthy of my service, so I left them.” Though he offer the three sacrificial beasts, attend them morning and night, how could he be deemed sincere?

Men like Huo Guang, though showered with ranks and boundless rewards in their time, were honored only for their schemes—was this their true devotion? To cast aside a wife and slander her former husband, to buy a slave and defame his former master—even common men find this unjust. How much more so when one wounds one’s own kind, for such is the natural sentiment. Thus Yue Yang was estranged for his ruthlessness, while Qin Xi was favored for his excess of kindness.

Yet the world praises Tang and Wu as right while lauding Yi and Huo as worthy—this only encourages rebellion. Moreover, deposed rulers are not always entirely at fault. Some, entrusted with aiding young sovereigns, wield power tyrannically, accumulating great crimes and posing future threats. While they still hold sway, they are replaced to avert imminent disaster. The merit of such a settlement lies in self-interest, not in service to the state. Once power is seized, life and death are decided by a word. When the deposed ruler’s divine mandate is lost, all the sins of the realm are laid at his door. Though men know this, who dares speak it aloud? Without a Dong Mou or Zhu Xu to devise a plan, without a Nan Shi or Dong Hu to record the crime, who in later ages will vindicate them? Only those who see clearly can perceive that Jie and Zhou were not so evil, nor Tang and Wu so virtuous.

The annals all exalt the ruler and humble the minister, strengthen the trunk and weaken the branches. The *Spring and Autumn* holds that Heaven cannot be opposed. The great sages’ scriptures enjoin serving father and ruler as one. Of the three bonds of human life, all are to be honored equally. Yet to permit the deposition of rulers opens the door to lawlessness, subverting the proper order and making it impossible to instruct. Vulgar scholars, mired in the butcher’s shop of sophistry, argue that Tang and Wu were like those who ate horse liver, condemning those who criticize such acts as ignorant of expediency’s adaptability, prizing the initiation of good without violating propriety—yet they do not call it a reversal of principle and a betrayal of righteousness.

Yet those who established discourse in former ages failed to dissect these matters by the great Dao, thereby emboldening men of such inclinations to sharpen their blades and ascend crumbling peaks—methods ill-suited to prolonging life or securing distant safety. Though decrees may briefly exalt, and lavish rewards suddenly amass, it differs not from adorning sacrificial oxen with embroidered silks, cherishing deep-water fish with buckwheat, quenching thirst with wine from clouds and sun, or sating hunger with rancid meat and moldy jerky. Yet those who wield the brush all join in praising such acts, turning them into tales of beauty, recasting crimes beyond execution as wisdom in adaptation—leaving men stifled in eternal regret.

Some admonish me, saying these words wound the sages and will surely invite ridicule and censure. I reply: "Shun and Yu underwent trials within and without before receiving the mandate at the Hall of Wen’s Ancestors. Even if one wished to wound them, how could the sages be wounded? In former times, Yan Yannian indicted Huo Guang in court for acting against the Dao, and all above and below stood in solemn awe, unable to refute him. How much less should I fear common speech, when my words serve as a warning to the age and name no one in particular?"

Bao Puzi says: To fulfill one’s duty without concealment is possible. Yet to ensure that one’s words are always accepted and one’s person always secure—this requires the right time. When the time is inauspicious, the treacherous and wicked, envious of what they cannot attain, will suppress their superiors and oppress their subordinates, loathing the upright and reviling the just. Fearing the censure of the impartial and the exposure of their crookedness, they strive to eliminate those who surpass them, thereby averting their own punishment. Enlightened rulers do not appear in every generation, and mediocre sovereigns, confused between black and white, not only fail to heed loyal counsel but may even divulge the most earnest admonitions. Thus, men like Hong Gong and Shi Xian adorn their cunning words to fabricate deceptive likenesses, feigning utmost impartiality to peddle their private treachery. Those who offer arts of longevity may instead incur the penalty of immediate execution; those who present plans to secure the throne may swiftly suffer personal ruin. Therefore, it is not the speaking of words that is difficult—it is the timing of speech that is hard.

To advise the worthy with wisdom does not guarantee acceptance, as Yi Yin petitioned Tang seventy times before success. To instruct the foolish with intelligence ensures rejection, as King Wen’s remonstrances to Zhou were never heeded. That one’s words go unheeded is still tolerable. Yet Li Si’s execution of Han Fei, Pang Juan’s mutilation of Sun Bin, Shangguan’s slander of Qu Ping, and Yuan Ang’s betrayal of Chao Cuo—such cases are too numerous to record. To be a minister is no easy path!

Those who advance without turning back achieve posthumous renown, while those who conceal their talents and await the right moment may never find it. Ministers of towering merit appear but once in ages, while those who perish in the ice of calumny fill the pages of history. Alas, how exceedingly rare it is to encounter the right time! From this, we know that men like Lü Shang of the Wei Riverbank and Fu Yue of the cliffs, though they bore the talents of kingly counselors and the abilities to transform an age, spent their lives holding fishing rods and wielding tamping mallets among the common folk, dying without ever encountering a King Wen or a Gaozong. Such men cannot be blamed.

---

Bao Puzi says: The swift gallop of Lü’er and Ji is due to Zaofu’s mastery of the reins; the orderly governance of Yu and Ji stems from the sage rulers of Tang and Yu. Thus, they covered a thousand *li* without toil, and their abdications gave rise to hymns of praise. Yet when mediocre rulers mount fine steeds like Suoshuang, or when King Xin of Yin faces the Three Worthies, the desire for a swift, effortless ride results in taut reins and cramped shafts, while the urge to offer loyal counsel and exhaustive advice triggers disaster as swiftly as a loosed crossbow. Hence, the chariot overturns on treacherous roads, and the state collapses beyond recovery. Noble steeds fail under clumsy drivers, and wise men stumble in benighted times. Confucius could not prevent the Duke of Lu from going forth, nor could Yan Ying halt Cui Zhu’s rebellion. Their talents were sound, but their lords were not. The lord is like a vessel, the minister like its contents: if the vessel is small and the contents large, they cannot accommodate one another. A child may spurn a thousand gold pieces to chase a butterfly, and the people of Yue may forsake the eight delicacies to relish frogs and toads—such is the calamity of not valuing what is good, compounded by the affliction of not recognizing what is bad. If one’s abilities go unused, even the most precious remain worthless; if no one supports them, harm is sure to follow. In the past, Duke Ling of Wei heard sagely words and was repeatedly startled, while Duke Xiao of Qin listened to lofty discourse and fell asleep. To expect the cultivation of grand peace and the achievement of meritorious deeds under such rulers is like retreating to pursue a galloping steed or heading for Yan while intending to reach Chu.

---

Bao Puzi says: The Southern Sea draws the tributaries of the world to form its unfathomable depths; the Dark Garden piles its foundational stones to reach the heavens’ heights. The great hall soars to the clouds, relying on the accumulation of countless rafters; the wheel’s curve and the shaft’s straightness leave no part wanting. Thus, when the finest ministers assist, the transformative power of governance reaches Heaven; when talents of strategic defense are fully employed, the schemes of usurpers lie dormant. This is why Shun and Yu possessed the realm yet did not cling to it, and why Duke Ling of Wei, though arrogant and self-indulgent, never faced peril. When many strengths combine, even ten thousand *jun* are not too heavy to lift; when the wisdom of the multitude is applied, even myriad tasks are not too burdensome to complete. Thus, the many-legged perish without exhaustion, and the land of many scholars does not fall to ruin.

Yet swords and halberds are not suited for sewing, nor can awls and drills be used to strike and sever. Oxen and horses cannot bark to guard, nor can chickens and dogs be harnessed to pull. Employ each for its strengths, and no task will fail; avoid their weaknesses, and no talent will be discarded in the world.

---

Bao Puzi says: To abandon a light boat and cross boundless waters is to see no certainty of reaching the other shore; to admire grand peace without worthy assistance is to have never heard of its achievement. The soaring of the great roc and phoenix relies on the power of their six pinions; the flight of the deep-dwelling dragon depends on the company of clouds and mist. Thus, the recruitment and employment of the worthy is the paramount duty of a ruler; the establishment of merit and accomplishment is the aspiration of the eminent. To delight in order and stability yet neglect the wise is no different from desiring a long journey while discarding fine steeds like Qiji and Lü’er.

To lift the obscure from their rustic obscurity, to raise the forgotten from their hidden depths, to assign offices commensurate with their talents, and to grant emoluments befitting their merits—this is how a lord treats the worthy. To toil day and night in public service, to exhaust heart and strength in governance, to advance the good and expel the evil, and to leave nothing undone in one’s knowledge—this is how a minister repays a patron.

The world may have reclusive citizens, but it cannot have a solitary ruler. Scholars may rejoice in seclusion without worry, but a lord cannot govern without ministers. Thus, Fu Yue and Lü Shang did not eagerly seek fame and position because, with their virtue complete, they held kings and dukes in light esteem. Yet Kings Tang of Yin and Wen of Zhou dreamed of obtaining worthy men, for the establishment of great merit requires fine assistance.

The peril lies in being born within the deep palace, reared in the hands of women, ignorant of the hardships of farming and weaving, unaware of the principles of vigilance and fear. Inheriting a legacy and continuing a lineage, they are blind to the rise and fall of states. Their urgent concerns lie in extravagance and waste, their paramount duties in leisure and feasting. They indulge in hunting and revelry, drown in wine and music, startle at the sound of lewd songs, and shift their gaze at the sight of alluring beauty.

To employ wisdom and apply insight—these are but two matters. In scrutinizing men and things, they do not strain the spirit; they know only that playthings may delight the heart and mind, yet fail to recognize that extraordinary men can secure the altars of state. Rhinoceros and elephant, pearls and jade, though footless, arrive from ten thousand *li* away; vessels that steady the tottering, though capable of action, sink within the four borders. When the illness of the two youths has already taken hold, they seek a skilled physician; when the disaster of a sagging ridgepole has already gathered, they think to consult a planner—why wait until fire breaks out to dig a well, or grow hungry to measure fields?

Even the lowly servant cannot be left without soothing care if one expects his full strength; how then can one hope to employ men suddenly, without prior cultivation?

**Chapter Twelve: Employing the Capable**

Or it is said: “That whose tail is greater than its body cannot be turned; a minister more worthy than his lord cannot be entrusted. Thus, what the mouth cannot contain, if forced down, will surely choke; one whose talent is unmatched, if rashly relied upon, will be held in contempt.”

The *Baopuzi* says: “How perverse these words!”

In ancient times, Prince Jing of Jing assumed the regency in his youth, yet relied upon the Twenty-Five Elders to attain prosperity and well-being. Zijian rose from common origins to govern a great state, for he was surrounded by many who surpassed him, and thus reaped their vast benefits. Duke Huan of Qi murdered his brother to seize the throne, his conduct bestial, his hair unbound in drunkenness, his harem numbering three hundred; yet by entrusting power to Guan Zhong, he became the foremost of hegemons. After Guan Zhong’s death, however, calamities and rebellions arose in rapid succession. For over twenty years, Lu employed Jizi, and within its borders there were no flawed policies, nor beyond them any encroachments; yet when he passed away, ruin and distress swiftly followed. Was this not because, though his own talent fell short, his achievements were so great when others’ strengths were employed, yet when he took all matters into his own hands, disaster struck in this way?

Emperor Gao of Han made decisive plans within his dark tent, determining victory a thousand *li* away—yet in this he was not the equal of Zhang Liang and Chen Ping. In commanding troops, the more he fought the better he fared, and none could withstand him—yet in this he was not the equal of Han Xin and Peng Yue. Only by employing all of them together did he succeed in establishing his imperial enterprise. Thus, hastening on foot, step after weary step, is not so good as riding a swift steed; chasing flying birds and running beasts is not so good as enlisting the skill of hawks and hounds. A mighty crossbow is hard to draw, yet it can shatter the sturdiest armor and reach distant targets; a great ship is hard to board, yet it can carry heavy loads and cross deep waters. A fierce general is hard to control, yet he can repel invaders and expand the realm; a lofty sage is hard to command, yet he can set right the constant norms of human relations.

In the past, Duke Ai of Lu was a mediocre ruler, yet Confucius, the supreme sage, dared not withhold his utmost loyalty. Duke Jing of Qi was of modest ability, yet Yan Ying, a man of great virtue, dared not withhold his complete sincerity. How could a minister presume to compare wisdom and strength with his lord, or weigh their respective merits and demerits, insisting that only a Yao or Shun is fit to serve? What task is not the lord’s? What service is not the people’s? To feel shame that one’s lord does not measure up to Tang and Yu—this is the mindset of one who truly understands the Way.

**Chapter Thirteen: Revering the Worthy**

Bao Puzi said: When You Yu remained among the Rong tribes, Duke Mu of Qin was filled with anxiety. When Chu executed Dechen, Duke Wen of Jin rejoiced. When Yue Yi departed, Yan fell into ruin; when Fan Li and Zhong entered, Yue rose to hegemony. Ruined states and lost houses—these were the consequences of losing their men of talent. How could it be merely that those who possess such men are esteemed, while those who lack them are held light?

The tomb of Liu Hui still blunted the edge of the Yuan invaders’ assault—how much more would his presence in court have achieved? The reclusion of Ganmu still repelled the attacks of the border raiders—how much more would his placement at the right hand of the throne have accomplished? The mere image of Zhi Du struck terror into the hearts of fierce foes. The corpse of Kongming still made a great state sheath its blades. With such men to repel invaders, territory would surely not be encroached upon; with such men to lead armies, the lord would surely not be humiliated.

Thus, enlightened rulers send bolts of silk to the humble lanes, raise forgotten feathers from the withered groves, dispatch carriages across the river bridges, and tirelessly open the four gates. They spare no gold or jade, scruple not at a thousand *li*, shrink not from lowering themselves, nor feel shame at humble words—all to make the recruitment of the worthy their foremost task and the acquisition of men of talent their most precious treasure. Those who recommend such men receive the highest rewards; those who obstruct them are deemed usurpers of office.

Thus, the Duke of Zhou bore his gift to the thatched cottage; Qin Shao bowed to Chang at Zhang Sheng’s gate. When Master Zou crossed the border, the Lord of Yan took up the broom; before Zhuang Zhou had even eaten, King Hui of Zhao rose in reverence. Duke Ping of Jin received Hai Tang though his legs were crippled, and dared not sit upright; Ren Zhi of Qi visited Ji Qiu repeatedly, undeterred by the frequency of his toil. The King of Chu received the rod from Bao Shen; □ Jian removed his armor before Gong Lu’s hut. Though they lowered their lofty status and humbled their fullness to honor the lowly, in the end they sharpened their vision to see afar and lent their ears to hear broadly. Like dragons soaring and tigers crouching, it was only fitting.

**Chapter Fourteen: The Use of Punishment**

Bao Puzi said: None fail to esteem benevolence, yet none can govern purely by benevolence; none fail to disdain punishment, yet none can order the people without it. Some say: “When a sagely lord rules, customs turn like grass in the wind. When the Way is pervasive and transformation complete, where is there need for punishment?”

I argue thus: Virtue and teaching are the embroidered robes of sacrifice; punishments and penalties are the armor and blades of defense. To govern the cunning and violent with virtue and teaching is like facing sharp blades in embroidered robes; to apply punishments in a peaceful age is like wearing armor in the ancestral temple. Thus, benevolence is the instrument for nurturing all things, while punishment is the tool for correcting wrongs. If I wish to benefit them, but they persist in harming, then without punishment, they will not cease. Punishment is the assistant of benevolence—this much is clear.

Consider those who preserve the mysteries of embryonic breathing, exhaling and inhaling, absorbing the radiance and gazing inward, imitating the bear’s climb and the bird’s stretch—these are the arts of long life. Yet they are arduous and slow, few who practice them succeed, and those who attain them are one in ten thousand. When illness is grave and pain severe, when body and life are in peril, one cannot but attack with needles and stones, treat with potent medicines. If one were to abandon the methods of Bian Que and instead aspire to the Way of Master Red Pine, the dead would be legion.

Governance by benevolence is not without its beauty. Yet the common people are crafty and deceitful, chasing profit and forgetting righteousness. If one does not align them with awe and correct them with punishment, then even if one longs for the customs of Fuxi and Shennong, chaos will be irrepressible, and the disaster profound. To use killing to stop killing—is this done with joy?

The creation of the Eight Trigrams exhausts principle and fulfills nature; the use of punishment and imprisonment is set forth in *Shi Ke*; binding with ropes is found in *Xi Kan*. Thus, the use of punishment is ancient indeed. Even in the time of the Yellow Emperor, though his sagely virtue was lofty, he personally led expeditions, fighting a hundred battles. Corpses piled up at Zhuolu, blood flowed at Banquan, yet he could not prevent rebellions in his time. How could he make all the people good, ensure none committed crimes, and yet not govern? In the flourishing age of Yao and Shun, they emulated Heaven in applying punishment—exiling, executing, banishing—and the world submitted. Though Han Wen was profound and serene, rivaling the glory of Cheng and Kang, he still executed four hundred, and many died under the lash.

The master carpenter does not abandon the plumb line, and so no wood is crooked. The enlightened lord does not abolish execution and punishment, and so no governance declines. The Way of Heaven and Earth cannot be purely benevolent: the Green Yang unfolds the harmony of nurturing and growth, while the White Autumn wields the might of stern killing. When the melting wind blows, withered plants and withering algae flourish; when the white dew congeals, luxuriant blossoms wither and fall. Thus, the myriad things thrive, and the year’s work is accomplished. Without cold, creeping things would not hibernate, and roots and plants would bloom in winter. Without severity, treacherous villains would arise together, and sharp weapons would be kept sheathed for long.

Thus, clear rewards preserve the upright, and certain punishments restrain the wicked. The instruments of encouragement and deterrence—none are more essential. In observing the people and establishing teachings, one tempers leniency with strictness, so that the timid are not trifled with, and the resolute do not injure kindness. The crimes subject to the Five Punishments number three thousand—this is to show that the plumb line cannot be bent. When the Minister of Justice carries out an execution, the lord does not hold court—this is to show that the law cannot be abolished. If the plumb line is bent, treachery will sprout; if the law is abolished, calamity and chaos will multiply.

A ruined state is not without decrees—its trouble lies in decrees being numerous yet unenforced. A defeated army is not without prohibitions—its trouble lies in prohibitions being set yet unheeded. Thus, the multitude of evils spreads, and inferiors grow contemptuous of their superiors. Rewards are valued for matching merit, not necessarily for being lavish; punishments are valued for fitting the crime, not necessarily for being harsh. If the rod is abandoned in the household, servants and slaves grow lazy; if military expeditions cease in the state, the masses below grow irreverent.

Affection depends on respect to avoid ruin—thus rites are established to exalt it. Virtue requires awe to endure—thus punishments are created to enforce it. If Ban Chu were to discard the compass and square, then squares and circles would not conform to things. If an enlightened lord were to set aside laws and measures, then schemes and deceits would give free rein to their cunning.

The sage-kings Yao and Shun were as benevolent as Heaven itself, yet they did not pardon the Four Criminals. The Duke of Zhou cherished his brothers, yet he did not spare the Two Uncles. When Confucius executed Shaozheng Mao and Emperor Wu of Han put his nephew to death, their tears fell as they lamented the necessity of the law—such was their inescapable duty.

Thus, to punish one is to warn ten thousand; to sacrifice the few is to preserve the many. Compared to combing one’s hair, the benefit reaches the multitude; likened to excising a carbuncle, the preservation is of the greater whole. Acupuncture and moxibustion are cruelly painful yet cannot be halted, for they cure disease; punishments are grim and harsh yet cannot be abolished, for they remedy corruption.

Though the six armies stand dense as a forest, not all are truly brave. To charge through blades and plunge into fire is what human nature dreads. Yet if one urges with gentle countenance, few will risk their lives; if one enforces with decisive execution, none will fail to fight fiercely. Thus, those who labor with smiles do not match the speed of those roused by shouts; those who govern with inducements do not equal the order imposed by punishments.

Therefore, even the safe and contented in deep valleys must uphold strict laws, just as Wei Zi hastened to punish those who discarded ashes, enforcing severe penalties. To use what men fear to restrain what they trifle with—this is the way to ensure sternness without transgression, the art of preserving the people.

The skill of a master physician lies in preventing illness before it arises; the insight of one who grasps order and chaos lies in averting future calamities. Yet if light punishments are used to forbid grave crimes, or lenient laws to protect great benefits, and these are proclaimed with ever greater clarity, offenders only multiply—like digging a pit to block the road, a course no humane ruler would embrace.

The wise governor must first correct himself to lead others, discipline his kin to order the distant, never bend the law to follow whim, and punish all crimes without pardon. Like Shi Shi of old, who cut off affection to execute his kin, or Duke Wen of Jin, who stifled sentiment to behead Jie Zitui.

Thus, benevolence is but the cosmetics of governance; punishments are the reins and bridle of the age. Cosmetics are not the most urgent need of the body, yet reins and bridle cannot be absent even for a moment. If solemn reverence slackens but slightly, indolence and disrespect soon follow; if awe and severity falter for an instant, the multitude of evils stirs in the heart.

When anger is right but not expressed, treacherous ministers become tigers; when killing is due but not carried out, great bandits arise. Water, long neglected, breaches the river; mountains rise from a span’s height. A towering tree of a thousand feet begins with a hair’s breadth; the fire from drilling tinder can be quenched with a ladle of water; the egg of a swan, unhatched, can be crushed in the palm. But when it rides the whirlwind and scorches the great plain, or spreads its six wings to soar above the morning clouds, then even the wise and brave cannot restrain it.

Therefore, the enlightened ruler resolves difficulties while they are easy, eradicates evil while it is small, does not boast of virtue to foster disorder, nor hesitates like one grasping an axe handle. Thus, punishments are the sacred instruments of the state, to be wielded by the ruler alone, never entrusted to others—like a great sword that cannot be grasped by the blade, or a giant fish that cannot be removed from the deep.

Here lies the origin of rise and decline, the source of safety and peril. Tian Chang’s usurpation of Qi, the Six Ministers’ partition of Jin, Zhao Gao’s assassination of the Qin emperor, Wang Mang’s usurpation of Han—each followed the path from frost to ice, a gradual accumulation. Some sighed forever by the sea, others clutched their hearts at Wangyi Palace; their disasters extended to their ancestral temples, serving as warnings to later ages. All stemmed from admiring empty reputations of antiquity while forgetting the real calamities at hand.

Someone may say: “The rise of penal laws belongs to the declining age.”

The Way of establishing humanity lies solely in benevolence and righteousness. When I am serene, the people naturally rectify themselves; when I am without desire, the people naturally return to simplicity. The caution against overcooking delicate fish is to avoid excess. Govern with leniency and love, and the masses will gather; employ joy in command, and subordinates will submit. Thus Mencius deemed embodying benevolence as peace, while Yang Xiong called Shen Buhai and Han Fei butchers. Excessive reins and tight bridles are not the driving of Zaofu; harsh punishments and severe penalties are not the Way of the Three Dynasties and Five Emperors. Therefore, in the time of Youyu, the ruler did not point or issue verbose commands—merely sitting in reverent dignity facing south, and governance flourished in harmony. Fu Sheng led by example, strumming the zither and chanting poetry without leaving his hall, yet fishermen and hunters observed propriety by night. If one can bestow generous favors and lighten taxes, relieve the destitute and promote the overlooked, appoint the worthy and employ the capable, encourage agriculture and reduce expenditures, attract the estranged with ritual and embrace the distant with virtue, cultivate them through education and govern them through schools—then those who inspire goodness from above will be like grass bending before a sudden wind, and those who wash their hearts and reform their countenances will be like clear waves cleansing light dust. The court will be filled with lords who yield in virtue, the countryside will know no transgressions against propriety. Prisons may lie empty, whips may forever be set aside—why must rewards and punishments alone govern the state?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: “The *Book of Changes* speaks of ‘clarifying punishments and rectifying laws,’ while the *Book of Documents* urges ‘compassion in adjudicating cases.’ Conferring ranks at court and executing criminals in the marketplace have been practiced since antiquity—how can this be confined to a declining age? Excessive benevolence prevents laws from being established; insufficient awe allows inferiors to encroach upon superiors. If laws are not established, all affairs become chaotic; if inferiors encroach upon superiors, rebellion becomes manifest. The purest virtue was already diluted by the Three Dynasties, and the great simplicity further scattered under Qin and Han. The Dao has declined since ancient times, customs have grown shallow in the present age—yet one seeks to govern deceit with knotted cords, transform cunning with silence, abandon reins and whip to ride a galloping horse on treacherous paths, discard rudder and oar to navigate a drifting boat through waves, chase thieves in circles, extinguish fires with bows and courtesies, execute Chao Cuo to repel the Seven States, or brandish weapons to pacify the Red Eyebrows—such measures are clearly unfeasible!

For the Three Sovereigns walked while the Five Emperors galloped; from the age of hegemons and kings onward, the pace has only quickened. When governance decays, officials deceive and the people scheme, bandits and thieves operate openly. Shaving heads and applying shackles fail to deter the shameless; exterminating clans cannot curb ambition. Eyes are multiplied to broaden vision, ears are piled to extend hearing, candles are raised to resolve backlogged affairs, hearts are scorched to stem the source of treachery—yet the marketplace and court still echo with sighs, the frontiers still harbor unheard injustices. Those who wield power arbitrarily may act beneath a single glance; those who harm the state and endanger the realm may plot within the palace walls. To govern a shallow and degenerate age with the Way of Taihao, or quell a boiling cauldron’s chaos with a single unchanging song, is to fail to recognize the need for adaptation with the times and the necessity of adjusting measures to circumstances. It is like carving a mark on a boat to retrieve a lost sword, shooting an arrow three days’ flight to strike a target five paces away, discarding rhinoceros-hide armor to wade into unfathomable depths, or casting off a fur coat in sweltering heat—such confusion is unparalleled!

One should first issue commands and only then punish, secure the truth without delighting in it, so that the Lord of Bo feels no resentment over his lost fief, and the men of Yu and Rui feel shame and abandon litigation. But if the violent conceal their nature, grasping the executioner’s rope without fear; if one is lured by tolerance into letting corruption spread unchecked—relying on grand words to conceal deep illness while forgetting the urgency of one’s fatal condition—how is this different from one parched with thirst pointing to a distant sea a myriad miles away, or drowning in a deluge while building a boat in the forest of Changzhou? How can one escape the fate of Kuafu or evade the disaster of the Flood?

The world scorns the practical methods of Shen Buhai and Han Fei while praising the fanciful discourses of Laozi and Zhuangzi. Yet in governance, they cannot do without punishments—pardoning killers, absolving assailants—like offering half a tile or a shard of pottery to a starving man at dawn, utterly useless. The words of Daoists are lofty indeed, but their application leads to ruin. They are as impractical as using a *ganjiang* sword to sew thread, an elephant to catch mice, a golden boat to brave towering waves, or a jade horse to gallop a thousand miles. To follow their teachings would require burning shackles and razing prisons, abolishing officials and erasing penal codes, melting down weapons and leveling city walls, dispersing treasuries and destroying tally tokens, dismantling barriers and toll stations, breaking scales and measures. One would blind Li Zhu’s eyes and deafen Zi Ye’s ears, drifting untethered, returning to the governance of Heaven—without instruction or management, forgetting all in rivers and lakes. The court would be as silent as if empty, the people would never interact even unto death. Such theories may be discussed, but they cannot be practiced.

Vulgar scholars merely hear that the Zhou rose through benevolence and the Qin fell through severity, yet fail to realize that the Zhou’s success was not purely benevolent, nor the Qin’s downfall solely due to harshness. In ancient times, the Zhou employed corporal punishments—amputating feet and slicing noses. At the covenant of Mengjin, latecomers were beheaded; rewards and punishments were enforced with utmost rigor, oaths included the slaughter of families. Examining their deeds, they were not entirely benevolent. By the late Zhou, laws were disregarded and texts manipulated, rulers grew tyrannical, edicts failed to reach beyond the cosmos, rites and music, punitive expeditions—all were no longer decided by the throne. Officials vied for power, turning into a coiling serpent. Cutting off the root and blocking the source, they destroyed crowns and rent robes. Some were drowned in the Han River, others exiled to Zhizhi. The collapse of the Zhou stemmed from insufficient severity. When the Qin first rose, officials were appointed based on talent. Men like Shang Yang and You Yu implemented laws within; generals like Bai Qi and Wang Jian conquered without.”

Uniting the weak and attacking the benighted, seizing might to establish hegemony, they devoured neighboring states and crushed rival lords, expanding territory and repelling the Rong. With the dragon’s transformation and the dragon’s gaze, they relied on clear rewards and certain punishments to lay the foundation of imperial enterprise.

Yet as the dynasty declined into its final years, rulers grew arrogant in triumph, indulging in boundless extravagance. They compounded this with tyranny: building walls a myriad *li* long, constructing over a thousand detached palaces, and amassing bells, drums, and female musicians without restraint. The labor at Mount Li, the levy of two-thirds taxation, the conscription of the poor, the brutality of burying scholars alive, the northern campaign against the Xianyun, the southern conquest of the Baiyue—armies of a million were mobilized for decades on end. The realm mourned with the sorrow of separation, every household sighed with resentment and neglect. White bones piled into mountains, empty sacrifices spread across the wilds. When Xu Fu departed, he left behind a legacy of countless foes; when Zhao Gao entered, he gathered a pack of jackals and wolves. The world yearned to rebel, and nine out of ten homes stood empty. Yet its downfall was not due to severity—this was how Qin had gained power, not how it lost it.

“For punishment arises from the blade: the skilled wield it to perfect themselves, while the clumsy wound themselves with it. In governing the state by the Dao, when punishment is applied as an aid, it can suppress deceit and falsehood, turning the wicked from their ways. But if the net’s ropes are severed and its mesh disordered, offending Heaven and misapplying punishment without reason, ruin will come swiftly. It is like water and fire, which sustain life yet also take it—it depends on whether one can use them or not.

“If tumors and growths are left untreated and one does not practice the arts of Bian Que, how can one hope for the longevity of Lao Peng? If treacherous factions multiply yet no strict system is enforced to punish transgressions, how can one expect enduring prosperity? One must select men like Zhang Tang to administer the law and govern the age, and choose officials like Zhao Yu and Chen Chong to investigate and impeach. The enlightened ruler remains vigilant above, while loyal ministers devote themselves below. At the sight of wrongdoing, they strike like hawks and falcons seizing sparrows; upon spying the first signs of disorder, they clear it like pheasants and pheasants weeding the fields. Rewards are bestowed without error, and executions are meted out only to the guilty—then the path to Great Peace need not be sought elsewhere. If decrees are obeyed without violation, one may nearly abolish punishment and achieve order, though I dare not assert this with certainty.”

Some asked, “If punishments truly aid instruction and foster goodness, why do they sometimes deviate from the proper standard? And as for the corporal punishments of old, could they be revived?”

The *Baopuzi* replied, “Why not? In ancient times, the Zhou employed corporal punishments and endured for seven hundred years. The Han abolished them, yet its reign was shorter. Later, they were replaced with flogging and caning, which resulted in many deaths. Outwardly, this appeared as leniency, but in truth, it was a means of killing. When crimes did not warrant death, the only alternatives were exile, flogging, or caning. If an amnesty was granted, the offender suffered no lasting harm—shaving the hair of one who had just begun to regrow it, or striking wounds that had only just healed, hardly sufficed to punish crimes deserving of death. Now, with corporal punishments abolished, there is no intermediate penalty between death and lesser punishments. Thus, crimes deserving of death short of execution are left unaddressed by anything but exile, flogging, or caning, creating an imbalance in severity. Moreover, while capital offenses are rare, those deserving of death but not execution are common. If corporal punishments are not used, such crimes often go unpunished.

“If, however, crimes other than treason, rebellion against ruler or kin, military offenses in the face of the enemy, or murder by one’s own hand were punished by corporal penalties instead of death, this would suffice to deter the wicked. The punished could still perform labor, contribute to society, and preserve their lives, while their lifelong disfigurement would strike fear into the hearts of all who saw them, deterring future offenses more effectively than execution. Killing is no light matter, yet after three days, the condemned are buried and forgotten—few witness it, and fewer remember. But corporal punishment serves as a lasting warning.

“In the Wei dynasty, this matter was frequently debated. Eminent scholars and learned men well-versed in governance all agreed that corporal punishments should be restored, though dissenters opposed them—yet their objections were unconvincing. Even Emperor Wu of Wei concurred. The only reason it was not implemented was that the frontiers were not yet pacified, and distant peoples, upon hearing that China severed limbs and sliced off ears and noses, would have taken it as proof of cruelty. Thus, it was temporarily suspended until the realm was unified. The erudite Yang Xiong also believed corporal punishments should be revived, but as they had been abolished for so long, those who sat in judgment did not consider it urgent.”

*Careful Selection, Scroll Fifteen*

Bao Puzi states: The towering heights of Mount Hua and Mount Heng reach the heavens precisely because their foundations run deep. The resplendent achievements of Yao and Shun were made possible only through the excellence of their ministers. Though one may possess the skill of Sun Yang, without the swift hooves of a *qiji*, one cannot traverse a thousand *li*. Though one may have the talent to delve into antiquity, without capable aides to put it into effect, one cannot consolidate the manifold tasks of governance.

Though a ruler’s wisdom may rival the sun and moon, and his divine insight discern even the faintest signs, the myriad affairs of state cannot be managed alone, nor can every minute detail be personally overseen. He must rely on eyes to survey distant matters and ears to extend his hearing, truly requiring officials to uphold and assist the realm. Thus, no sagely ruler fails to seek worthies from the depths of his heart, making the recruitment of talent his foremost duty. He bestows jade and silk upon recluses in their rustic retreats, dispatches carriages to the wilds and marshes, toiling in the search for men while reaping ease in their employment. From the highest ministers beneath the scholar-tree to the lowliest gatekeepers, all charged with governing the Dao and ordering the state are entrusted with their duties.

Through reverent self-cultivation and non-action, order prevails and punishments fall into disuse; transformation spreads without limit, and the myriad states enjoy peace.

Establishing offices and dividing duties is like constructing a hall—if a single component is unfit, it becomes the cause of collapse. Now, those recommended for office are measured by the four standards, and from the three offices and nine ranks they emerge. One must select the most outstanding and exceptional talents. Yet in the late years of the Han, during the reigns of Emperors Huan and Ling, power departed from the imperial house and rested with treacherous ministers. The net of governance leaked, defenses crumbled, moral winds declined, and instruction faltered. They suppressed those of pure virtue and promoted flatterers, demoted those who trod the Way and advanced the wealthy. Striving for advantage became the custom, and shameless pursuit of gain went unchecked. Some offered bribes of self-promotion, others sold letters of recommendation from influential men. Some, by virtue of their fathers’ or elder brothers’ prominence, received appointments merely by standing before the gate; others, through bowing and scraping, were repeatedly recruited after long practice.

When the scales of assessment are unbalanced, weights and measures become confused; when measuring vessels are untrue, quantities are muddled; when the marking line is not laid out, straight and crooked cannot be distinguished. If standards are skewed, impurities proliferate. Ruling the people in this manner leads to cruelty and rapacity—officials extort and amass wealth to recoup the cost of buying their posts. Placed in the court, they bring chaos like tangled silk. They appoint the incompetent and mediocre as their clique, yet expect the wind to bend the grass and all affairs to prosper. How is this different from hanging shards of tile and demanding luminous pearls, or plucking an untuned string and seeking pure sound?

How can one not purify the turbid and elevate the sunken, sift the worthy from the unworthy, enforce rigorous examination procedures, and heighten defenses against the greedy? The roots of ruin and suffering—how can they not be feared?

In ancient times, when the feudal lords presented scholars, those who were suitable were deemed meritorious, and the meritorious had their ranks advanced and titles elevated; those who were unsuitable were deemed at fault, and the faulty had their positions demoted and lands reduced. Yet even then, they could not prevent the *Shi* poets from composing satires like *"The Great Cart"* and *"Eating Without Labor,"* or the worthy from the mountains and forests from lamenting *"Felling Sandalwood"* and *"Trapping Hares."* How much worse, then, is the crime of recommending the unworthy and the calamity of appointing those unfit for their posts?

Once the scales of assessment lose their standard, how can the many and the few be adjusted? The solitary and outstanding conceal their talents, awaiting a fair price; the petty and frivolous curry favor through connections to hasten their advancement. Only by awaiting their true worth do the former sink into poverty and distress; only by rushing do the latter leap forward through deceit.

When kites and owls flock together, phoenixes retreat into seclusion; when jackals and wolves block the path, qilin flee afar. Promote the good and instruct the people, and the unvirtuous will distance themselves; let the treacherous and false gain honor, and the heroic and outstanding will vanish. Those of lofty integrity disdain association with the base, and the pure of conduct are ashamed to join the ranks of the gluttonous.

When appointments are uniformly mistaken, the worthy withdraw in silence; when the worthy withdraw in silence, the wicked draw near to one another; when the wicked draw near, the path of petty men lengthens; when the path of petty men lengthens, *Taowu* monsters stand shoulder to shoulder. This is why odes of praise are not composed and why cries of resentment rise in a clamor.

Those of towering talent, relying on abilities surpassing their own, bend or stand, speak or remain silent, submitting to Heaven and entrusting their fate to nature. In success or failure, gain or loss, they yield to spontaneity. How, then, can they not fall behind the faction-ridden and stand beneath the powerful? The reclusive and exceptional, who move only in accord with ritual, stand firm as mountains and deep as abysses—few recognize them. The ambitious and scheming slander and obscure them. Thus, even a ruler who seeks the worthy may never learn where extraordinary talent resides, and those who embrace the Way may wish to offer their service but find no path.

Though possessing the talents of Hou Ji and Xie, and the capacity to surpass the age, they sink into obscurity until death, never gaining rank or position. Meanwhile, those with factions and influence gather like scales in abundance. With men scarce and offices vacant, and those appointed deemed excellent, how can one not gather and employ them?

In the reigns of Emperors Ling and Xian, eunuchs held power, and a cabal of villains wielded authority, endangering the loyal and good. The central court failed in its selection of officials above, while the provinces and commanderies treated recommendations lightly below. When selection fails above, governors and prefects are not the right men; when recommendations are treated lightly below, the worthy and filial are not chosen.

Thus, the people of the time said: *"Recommend a Filial and Incorrupt, and he does not know his books; examine a Filial and Upright, and his father lives apart. The poor and pure are muddied like sludge, while the high-ranking and capable are timid as chickens."* They also said: *"In ancient times, one studied the classics diligently to advance; in this age, one seeks office to avoid labor."* Such was the depth of their indignation.

At that time, offices were hung out for sale like goods in a shop; those competing for passage bought them like market-goers. The upright, though without connections, advanced directly; the empty-handed, gazing at the road, were swept into the ranks. Those who paid more gained higher office; those who paid less received lower posts. Thus, the Eastern Gardens amassed money from selling offices, and Cui Lie was mocked for the stench of copper.

The superior set the example, and the inferior followed with greater zeal. Flatterers and favorites monopolized favor, while discussions of local worthies, the Impartial and Just, and the Ministry of Personnel all became brokers, each demanding their price. How could the pure and poor have any hope? Such was the state of affairs.

Moreover, the crooked and the upright are as incompatible as ice and charcoal; those who detest integrity despise those who refuse to join their factions. Those who wield power with perverse measures fear others’ criticism of themselves; those who attain success without following the Dao dread that their reputation may not be fair. Thus, they conspire to fabricate false accusations, slandering the pure and virtuous, heaping baseless flaws upon them, so that none dare to rescue or promote them. As a result, the virtuous like Zeng and Min endured the slander of Shang Chen, while the wise like Confucius and Mozi bore the stain of Robber Zhi. Those who uphold integrity and abide in constancy are mired in the mud and mire, while the cunning and deceitful soar like rainbows in the sky.

Yet the shallow-minded, unable to distinguish between the crooked and the upright, call those who guard the Dao "sunken in obscurity" and praise those who tread crooked paths as "adept at adaptation." When customs sway with the wind and drift with the current, how can men ever return to virtue and discipline, or devote themselves to learning? Thus, all abandon the toil of self-restraint and hasten toward the swift gains of bribery. This is indeed the reason for the Han’s downfall—a lesson future generations must deeply heed.

Someone asked: "Your critique of the Han’s late imperial examinations is indeed accurate in diagnosing its ills. If we now wish to guard against past mistakes, avoid the path of a collapsing cart, change the tune of the present age, and prevent the erosion of laws, so that the rustic tunes of Pu Shang give way to the proper music of *An Le*, and that minor ailments do not fester into chronic afflictions—is there a method for this? Some scholars possess noble bearing and abundant grace, yet harbor emptiness within, lacking substance in their aspirations. If we judge by appearance alone, we may not necessarily select the worthy; if we proceed slowly with preliminary trials, we cannot act in haste. What, then, is to be done?"

The *Baopuzi* replied: "To know men is wisdom—even the greatest sages find it difficult. If we now require all governors and prefects to discern true talent before employment and ensure the integrity of their conduct from beginning to end, this is no easy task. Yet if they set aside personal bias, exhaust their wisdom, remain unmoved by profit or desire, and refuse to yield to favoritism or pressure, then for those they wish to recommend, they must carefully observe and thoroughly investigate, examining their reputation and scrutinizing their conduct, comparing accounts to guard against deception. Let their kinsfolk praise their filial piety and brotherly love, and let their villages and towns attest to their trustworthiness and righteousness. If they have held minor office, let their loyalty, purity, and administrative skill be proven—then even a scrap of brocade reveals their craftsmanship, and a mouse caught in a trap demonstrates their courage.

Furthermore, all candidates for *xiucai* and *xiaolian* should, as before, be tested on the classics and policy questions to prevent deceit in their responses. Those who fail must be rejected outright, and the officials responsible should be punished with demotion and prohibition from office. If the candidates they recommend fail, the prefects and governors should be dismissed; if they pass in small numbers, the officials should be demoted. Those who pass in greater numbers may not be promoted beyond their previous rank. If one accepts bribes and recommends the unworthy, and this is discovered and verified, they shall be stripped of office and barred for life, with no pardon by imperial decree. The recommended and the recommender shall share the same punishment. If this law is enforced for one or two years, the number of *xiucai* and *xiaolian* candidates will surely decrease, and this will suffice to reveal how long the empire’s selection process has been imprecise. After this, those who cultivate virtue and diligently study will surely increase.

Moreover, for all officials in office: if they commit public offenses, let the law take its course. If they are guilty of corruption or graft but not deserving of death, upon completion of their sentence or pardon, they should be barred from office for life—twenty years for lesser offenses. Thus, even the most corrupt officials will surely transform into Boyi and Shuqi. But if those who take bribes while in office amass wealth like mountains, and upon exposure merely plead for leniency, or if they are dismissed only to be reappointed within days, then even Zeng and Shi will turn into Robber Zhi. In such a case, even if all recommended scholars are qualified, they will have no recourse against unworthy superiors."

Some may ask: "One who speaks well may not necessarily act well. Even if candidates pass the examination and policy response, how can we be certain they possess administrative talent?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "In ancient times, archery was used to select men—how much more should we value mastery of the classics? If we abandon this method, I have yet to see a better alternative. Lush grasses do not grow in barren soil; giant fish do not spawn in shallow streams. Profound words do not fall from the mouths of mediocre men, nor do lofty writings flow from the brushes of the dull-witted.

Thus, perusing the *Hong Fan*, one recognizes that Jizi possessed the capacity to order the world. Reading the *Nine Strategies*, one sees that Fan Li harbored the art of governing a state. Examining the writings of Guan Zhong, one discerns his talent for restoring order from chaos. Studying the texts of Shen Buhai, one perceives his mastery of the Dao of hegemony and kingship.

Today, if candidates for filial piety and incorruptibility must pass the classics without error, and those recommended for exceptional talent must respond to policy questions without misinterpretation, then they cannot remain obscure or concealed. Even the finest generals, selected for their courage and martial prowess, are further tested through policy responses—how much more should this apply to scholars?

Granted, this method may not guarantee the selection of all worthy and capable individuals, but it is surely superior to no testing at all. At present, let this single measure be applied to all those eligible for recommendation and examination: none will dare neglect their studies. This alone will greatly benefit the cultivation of customs and education.

If the realm fears the consequences of reckless recommendations, if all men abandon their pursuit of undeserved fortune, turn away from petty competition, and return to the foundation of learning, then the Dao of the Ru will flourish, private bribery will gradually cease, extraordinary talents will be employed, and official posts will no longer lie vacant."

Another objects: "Sir, you advocate strict examination laws, yet the punishment of lifelong disbarment is harsh and severe, striking fear into many. Tight reins and frequent spurring are practices even Bo Le would avoid; stringent laws and rigid prohibitions are a disgrace to virtuous governance."

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "Medicines that strengthen bones and nourish flesh excel at sustaining the body and prolonging life, but they cannot rescue one drowning in imminent peril. Policies of leniency and tolerance may suit the governance of the honest and simple, but they cannot remedy the decline and decay of an age.

When beset by tigers and wolves, if one does not brandish a spear or unsheathe a sword but instead strums the zither and recites poetry, I have yet to see how such a person can preserve their life. When flames engulf a house, if one does not rush to pour water but instead bows and defers in ceremony, I have yet to see how the fire will extinguish itself.

To discuss this with those who knowingly sell their schemes is like debating the capture of thieves with Robber Zhi."

Ge Hong replies: "Now that all under heaven is unified and the nine regions share the same customs, the royal institutions and government decrees should truly be uniform. Even with small measuring tools like scales and measures, one cannot allow them to vary from place to place—how much less can the standards for scholars and officials be left unchecked and inconsistent? Though the lands south of the Yangtze lie distant, bordering the sea’s edge, they have long been steeped in the Dao and transformed by culture, following ritual and teaching for over a thousand years. In the past, though briefly separated for less than a hundred years, the pursuit of Confucian learning was never entirely abandoned. Only because their territory is smaller than the Central Plains are the numbers of their scholars not equal in size. Yet among those of lofty virtue, talent, and learning, figures like Ziyou and Zhongren are by no means inferior to those of the central states. When the lands of Wu first submitted, their tribute scholars were dismissed without examination. Now, with peace nearly forty years established, they are still not examined—this is why the Confucian learning of the southeast has declined compared to former times. This is to treat them as akin to those in left-lapped robes, not to distinguish them. Moreover, even a gentleman treats others with ritual—how much more should those who are their joyful and kind parents do so! Laws that invite trouble and decrees that harm transformation—this is precisely what they do.

If tribute scholars are no longer examined, they will surely all engage in ostentatious pursuit of empty reputation—who would still open books to study?
