This is what is meant by ‘enriching them only to ruin them.’ Some are born with a love for antiquity, their hearts delighting in arts and letters. They study not for salary, savoring the Dao and forgetting poverty—like Fa Gaoqing and Zhou Shenglie. Those whose learning is profound yet do not serve (text may be missing) for the sake of glory and profit are one in ten thousand. As for figures like Ning Yue, Ni Kuan, and Huang Ba, their diligent self-exhortation in the classics was not due to innate nature but arose from hardship and suffering, seeking to elevate themselves through classical arts. Had it not been for the age of Emperor Wu of Han, even men like Zhu Maichen and Yan Zhu would not have read books. Now, if the path to wealth and honor happens to be easier than scholarship, and one lacks a natural inclination for learning, who would willingly endure hardship, sweeping floors as a student, traveling far to seek teachers and inquire of the Dao?

In times of war, the martial is prized and the civil declines. Common folk view scholars as servants and slaves, seeing the classics as worthless—why? Because fame and reputation have turned away from them. To do so is like selling *zhangfu* hats to the Yi and Yue or offering beards and snakes to the Central States. Now, if distant and near are treated equally, with clear examinations and assessments, many will surely carry their satchels a thousand *li* to seek teachers and friends, redirecting the costs of gifts and bribes to purchase books and records—this will not take a full day.

Ge Hong says: Talented and learned scholars capable of passing the *xiao* and *xian* examinations are already rare. Even if, as in the reigns of Emperors Huan and Ling, officials were not selected first by wealth but by those who preside over the imperial secretariat, then even if one mastered all the classics with full comprehension and answered every question flawlessly, they would still be slandered and obstructed, unable to advance. I have long regretted that those in power did not take greater precautions. My opinion is that for those who will be examined in the new year, this year the court should have erudite officials and talented scholars prepare examination questions in advance, ensuring they are sufficient for use. Gather them in the palace, forbid drafts from being kept, and seal them. When the time comes for the examination, distribute them swiftly. Thus, personal connections and favors will be cut off. Those answering the questions should all be assembled in one place, with high-ranking officials from the central offices personally overseeing them. Strictly prohibit any communication or movement, and only dismiss them when the examination is complete. Violations should be punished without pardon. In this way, the wings of favoritism and nepotism will be clipped.

A wise ruler relies on his own incorruptibility, not on others not deceiving him. Why should he feel shame in strictly enforcing such measures? If the examination system is established, then the empire need not establish official schools, and people will naturally strive and delight in learning. The four categories of officials also include those who clearly understand laws and decrees. Yet today, those in office, regardless of rank, are entirely ignorant of the law. Some legal terms are obscure and difficult to grasp, while petty officials are often dull-witted. To entrust them with deciding cases is to entrust life and death to them, to treat the lives of the common people lightly, and to hand them over to the ignorant. If an official does not know the law, he will be deceived by his subordinates without realizing it. When rendering judgments, if he is muddled and unable to understand the law, or if he does not apply it and leaves it to others, or if he decides cases by whim and errs by not carefully following the law, then even upright and capable officials should be tested on their knowledge of laws and decrees, as with the examination of the classics. Those who score highly should be appointed according to their talent and rank. In this way, the empire will surely have fewer officials who manipulate the law and fewer unjust cases.

Friends and Acquaintances, Scroll Sixteen

Ge Hong says: In friendships, one should not be indiscriminate. To be insincere in heart while maintaining appearances—this is what Yang Xiong criticized. Thus, even if one holds a high position, enjoys a fine reputation, shares the same gate, or is of similar age, if their aims and conduct differ, if their pursuits and values diverge, I have never formed ties with them. Some boast of their seniority, striding high and gazing far, or neglect and slight old friends who have declined, or dismiss those of humble origins and lesser status. Some judge others by rank rather than virtue. Those who do not encounter a true understanding friend, who languish in obscurity, even if their talent runs deep, their wisdom far-reaching, their conduct pure, and their integrity lofty, cannot succeed. Meanwhile, those whose advancement is merely coincidental, whose positions are prominent and offices prosperous, even if they are as ignorant as a wall or as mediocre as a fool, will surely rise. Such people, though they may cause insects to soar like clouds or sparrows to reach the heavens, wielding power like a blade and deciding fortune and misfortune with their words—when they favor you, they bloom like ice melting into flowers; when they abandon you, they wither like spring’s end. I would rather silence my tongue and turn my back on them, ashamed to share the world with such men. Whether one is impoverished or successful, one cannot seek them out."

Yet these frivolous and unworthy men, those without distinction, lacking even the fleeting resolve to correct their faults, hasten through the night as if chasing stars, lingering at the gates of the powerful. With obsequious smiles and fawning words, they lower themselves in countenance, bearing jugs of wine and gifts of silk, ever seeking favor. Though they toil long and endure hardship, they are still met with disdain and rejection. Thus, they resort to currying the favor of elders to forge connections. When their advances are accepted, their joy surpasses even that of a prisoner granted amnesty; when rebuffed, their despair exceeds the grief of one struck by illness or bereavement.

Success and failure are decreed by fate, and the Dao prizes uprightness. Prosperity and adversity are left to nature—why then obsess over the paths of advancement? Alas, such petty men! How base they are! Human nature differs so widely—how far apart we stand! Each time I witness this, I cannot help but feel indignation on their behalf.

Once, Zhuang Zhou, seeing the vast retinue of Hui Zi, abandoned the fish he had yet to eat. I, too, lament that worldly men do not strive earnestly for true attainment. Beholding those who gallop through the dust of the world, I see them not laboring to establish virtue or cultivate their own foundations, but instead chasing high connections to form cliques, believing no principle more vital, no urgency greater in this age. Those who stand tall and independent like a mountain are dismissed as stingy and inept; those who grovel with the demeanor of slaves are praised as astute and worldly-wise. Such customs, once formed, spread like wind—none pursue the root, and all drift ever further. How lamentable!

Some, though of meager virtue, occupy lofty positions, their capacities overflowing with ambition. At the mention of wealth or profit, they startle as if shaken; at the sight of extraordinary men, they feign sleep. Yet those who lean on staffs in tattered robes, bearing satchels of books—though their words shine like jewels and their learning delves into the profound—are treated as dust or weeds, never welcomed. But when the likes of Cheng Zheng or the scions of Wangsun, clad in fine robes and riding sleek carriages, their hands heavy with gold and jade, appear—though they cannot wield a brush to compose a letter or distinguish beans from wheat—they are met with haste, shoes discarded, meals spat out, hair grasped in urgency.

I only regret that I do not hold office, for though I have an axe, I lack the handle—powerless to purge the filth of the state, to cast its corruption to the farthest reaches or banish it to the northern wastes. Those men, though resplendent, wield blades that decide all—with power enough to move mountains and uproot seas, their breath alone can lift clay idols to the clouds. Yet when they stand at my gate, I have no time for them. And still, many lower themselves, forcing smiles, crawling forward on their knees, seeking their favor for some imagined gain. How tragic! The hearts of men are so uneven—how vast the gulf between them and true worth!

This is why, though born in a sagely age, I remain mired in obscurity and lowliness. Those I once looked back upon and saw no more, I now gaze after in vain—how could it be otherwise? Yet if my nature cannot endure it, I shall follow my own inclinations. In this, I cannot be swayed, whether it leads to survival or ruin.

Some may object: *"Times change, and the world with them; the duties of past and present differ. Conduct is established by oneself, but reputation is shaped by others. Gold and jade endure the unfathomable because they rest in a light boat; the sacred bird alights in the dark heavens by the power of the whirlwind; the fragrance of orchids is spread by the pure breeze; and the recluse rises from obscurity through the aid of a true friend. Now, sir, you choose friends only from those whose conduct is pure and whose deeds are clear, favoring only those whose hearts have been sifted and refined. Alone, silent, and secluded, you cast aside your peers, make enemies of the age, and provoke the scorn of common custom. Is this not at odds with the lofty principle of harmonizing with the world while preserving one’s light? If one were to befriend only the wise, then even the humble cottage would not welcome the noble Duke of Zhou; if one were to associate only with equals in talent, then Confucius himself would have no disciples to enter his chamber."*

Bao Puzi replied: "I have heard that those who choose friends with care do not lose true companions, while those who form bonds indiscriminately often come to regret it. Thus, the sages of old first selected and then befriended, never first befriended and then selected. Your argument, sir, is a calculation for worldly success; my resolve is the aspiration of a retiring scholar. You say that jade floats and birds soar only by some agency—this is indeed a separate doctrine of its own. Yet I would rather be an untransported treasure, an earthbound roc, an unscented orchid, a man without faction—what loss is there in being a luminous gem, a creature of boundless wings, a fragrant blossom, an undying orchid?

Moreover, when a man’s reputation exceeds his substance or his rank surpasses his talent, even he who holds such a position seldom escapes calamity and disgrace—how then can befriending him bring honor or fortune? By this reasoning, to associate with one who prospers by chance is no true achievement, even if he attains success; while to cling to one who errs is like seeking shelter beneath a rotten tree—when it collapses, how can it save me from a shattered skull?

I have heard that the true gentleman, content in himself and indifferent to externals, regards the common run of men as though forced into fleeting contact with them. Though they number in the thousands, they are but fleas and lice clinging to his robes, warts and growths upon his body. Though they depart by the tens of thousands, they vanish like dust blown from Mount Song, like withered branches falling from Deng Forest. How can their presence bring gain, or their absence be felt as loss?

As for friendship, it must be founded upon those who are upright, trustworthy, and learned—who correct one’s errors and rebuke one’s faults, who speak no flattery in life and make no excuses in death, whose bond remains unbroken through all seasons. Yet such men are not easily found. Some diverge in speech and silence, others differ in love and hate; some unite in prosperity and part in adversity, while others forget loyalty when profit beckons. In their present state, they are like jade and fish forming a fellowship, like ice and charcoal sharing a vessel—how can such unions endure?

Even father and son, bound by nature and sharing the same inclinations, may yet disagree: Zizheng and Zijun held opposing views, while Boqi of Nanshan was forced to plead his case. That men’s faces differ while their hearts diverge is an ancient truth. To lump them all together—is this not difficult indeed?

The common run of men form friendships without regard for shared purpose, chasing fame and following power, warm in approach and cold in departure. They overlook faults and ignore delusion; when profit arises, they seize it alone without sharing; when harm comes, they flee without a thought for others. When opportunity favors them, they take precedence without yielding; when crisis strikes, they betray others to save themselves. In such cases, having such friends is worse than having none.

Not all in the world are unworthy of friendship—yet those who bring benefit are few, while those who bring trouble are many. The discernment to know men is a gift even the greatest sages find difficult. Yet those of limited talent, lacking in judgment, strive to broaden their circle of friends, hoping all will prove steadfast through peril and adversity, bearing each other’s burdens through hardship. I have never seen such abundance. It would be easier to seek flawless jade upon an anthill or phoenixes in the nest of a wren.

I do not dare claim that in the southern slopes of Lantian or the depths of Danxue, no such men exist—only that they are rare indeed. When principles differ, gold sinks while feathers float; when aspirations clash, fire rises while water descends. If they cannot be aligned, not even the Creator or the Craftsman of Nature can make them so—how then can they be forced?

My nature is as dull as a plodding horse, compounded by a heaven-sent indolence. Games of skill—board and dice—I despise; swift rides, hunting, and idle roaming—I do not pursue. I have no taste for jest or vulgarity. These are the very things the age delights in, yet I lack them all, which is why my close friendships are so few. Moreover, I cling to honesty and freely speak my loyal counsel, drawing the bitter medicine of reproof, which few willingly accept. I urge others to study and warn them against reckless ambition, discourage gambling, and curb their excesses—these are things most men cannot abide.

To cast aside one’s true nature to conform to the world, to force oneself to please others—this is a burden I cannot bear. Yet if one knows this and does not change, can one not be called blind to the times and inept in great matters? Friendships that do not endure, alliances that dissolve—both parties earn a reputation for inconstancy and lose the beauty of a perfect end. Where there is depth, affection arises; where there is shallowness, resentment takes root. This is the natural principle—can one not choose with care? To clap a man on the back over wine, to clasp his hand for a moment, to seek quantity while demanding perfection—this is what I fear."

Someone asked: "Then should one have no friends at all?"

Bao Puzi replied: "How can this be so! Those who fear water need not abandon boats and oars, nor must those who dread injury forsake the adze and axe. The Way of friendship has endured since antiquity. If heaven and earth do not commune, they cannot achieve harmony; if superiors and inferiors do not commune, their aspirations diverge. Without harmony, the two qi remain divided; with divergent aspirations, the world has no states. Yet while beginnings are easy, endings prove difficult. The danger lies in forming bonds with the wrong person, or ruining great matters over petty disputes.

The *Book of Changes* praises the multitude of orchids, and the *Book of Songs* extols a hundred strings of cowries. Though one may have brothers, they are not equal to friends. Earnestly seeking the Three Benefits—praised by the great sages—strengthens the bonds between disciples and keeps slander at bay. It was through friendship that Guan Zhong avoided execution and established hegemony, and that Zi Yuan left his post as pavilion chief to ride in a vermilion carriage. Such is the power of fellowship.

A single string cannot produce the harmony of the *Shao* or *Xia* melodies; a lone hue cannot achieve the brilliance of dragon-scale brocade; one flavor cannot blend the sweetness of Yi’s tripod; a single tree cannot attain the lushness of Deng’s forest. The heights of the Dark Garden are built by the accumulation of countless stones; the vastness of the Southern Sea arises from the confluence of many streams. When a bright mirror is raised, a crooked cap is revealed; when Xihe’s light shines, a bent shadow is perceived; when the straightening board is applied, the ailment of crookedness is cured; when good friends unite, the Way of assisting virtue flourishes. The wise understand this: in their aspirations, they carefully choose those superior to themselves; in their associations, they deliberately seek those of like mind. At home, they discuss the Dao and cultivate virtue; abroad, they align their hearts and soar as one. Otherwise, they engage in the pursuits of fishing and hunting; at best, they collaborate in worldly affairs. In peace, they refine their understanding of principle; in peril, they support one another. They feel shame if Tan Ken monopolizes the virtue of steadfastness, and do not allow Wang and Gong to claim sole beauty in dusting their caps. Thus, the Way of friendship is to be cherished.

Yet true discernment is not easy. Power and profit breed fickleness; accumulated slander severs even neck-sworn pacts; gradual erosion dissolves the firmness of glue and lacquer. Hence the lament for forgotten bonds of old, or the refusal to overlook even a glance of resentment—so that the friendship of Yuan Bo and Ju Qing shines only in the past, while the betrayal of Zhang Er and Chen Yu repeats endlessly in the present. Reflecting on the past and examining the present, one cannot but sigh. The phoenix does not share a branch with the owl, the unicorn does not herd with wolves, clear springs do not mingle with muddy torrents, and the benevolent and wise do not dwell with the cruel and benighted. For gradual contamination corrupts the straight path, and coercion breeds harm."

Someone asked: "May I inquire about the Way of perfecting friendship?"

Bao Puzi replied: "When a gentleman severs a bond, he does so without malice—how could he then speak ill of a former friend? He would sacrifice himself for a friend’s sake—how could he compete over rank or status? In good fellowship, there is intimacy without disrespect, harmony without conformity. If a friend errs, he admonishes with solemn countenance; if told of his own faults, he swiftly corrects them without hesitation. He does not withhold counsel to avoid offending, nor reject advice because it grates on his ears. He does not use clever words to conceal wrongs, nor embellish faults with flowery speech. He does not feign agreement while his spirit rebels, nor pretend affection while his heart disagrees. He does not flatter to one’s face and resent behind their back, nor resent another’s superiority. He guards their shortcomings while drawing out their strengths, concealing their faults while proclaiming their virtues. Externally, he avoids petty disputes; internally, he sheds the burden of rivalry. Only then is the bond of the *Deer Cry* preserved, and the satire of the *Felling Trees* silenced.

But if one treats bonds lightly, valuing union but not separation, prizing depth but not difficulty in parting—beginning like shadow and form, ending like sun and moon—then the deepest joy turns to bitter enmity, and clasped hands become sworn foes. The regret of carelessness is beyond remedy.

In the past, during the decline of the Han, the imperial reins slackened, and the duty of public service faded while the custom of contention flourished. Defying the times was deemed lofty, while saving the world was seen as humiliation. The rites between high and low collapsed, and the great ethical order descended into chaos. Those in office did not strive to fulfill their duties but abandoned fundamentals for trivialities, forsaking substance for reputation. Those who neglected state affairs won praise, while those who amassed transgressions gained merit. All spurred their carriages and whipped their horses, traveling by starlight and resting only briefly, braving cold and heat to rush to the gates of the powerful. They traded empty glory for the words of the influential, and bought undeserved rank from those who sold office. Some, vying for their desires, turned to mutual destruction. Gongshu Wei, distressed by such a world and unable to set it right, could not bear to witness it—thus, in indignation, he wrote his treatise, shut his door, and severed all ties. This was indeed a passionate response to circumstances, but in overcorrecting, he strayed from the eternal lesson. One should merely distance oneself from unworthy factions and be cautious of the roots of flattery and corruption. Why go to extremes—baring one’s body to prove a point or starving oneself to rebuke luxury?

If friendship turns to harm despite earnest admonition, leading to great disorder, then it is right to abandon what rites and justice have already forsaken.

*Baopuzi*, Scroll Seventeen: "The swift steed can gallop across the plains with sinews like orchids, yet cannot tread upon ice to cross the deep; the fierce tiger can roar like thunder and rend its prey, yet cannot soar through clouds and mist to ascend the void. The great roc cannot flap its wings within a cage, nor can the nimble sparrowhawk strike like lightning beneath the banquet table. Things are thus, and so too are men. Therefore, one who can harmonize yin and yang may not necessarily master the hundred virtues or govern documents; one who can expound the Five Constants and Nine Virtues may not necessarily perfect minor purity or meticulous details. Hui Shi, paragon of prime ministers, could not command boats to brave the waves; Han Gaozu, peerless in martial spirit, could not manage his estate or maintain discipline."

Han Xin, foremost among fine generals, could not manage farming or commerce to stave off hunger and cold; Zhou Bo, stalwart pillar of the altars of state, could not account for grain and coin or adjudicate legal pleas. If one were to discard the long for the short, then talents peerless and preeminent would go unused; if one were to demand perfection and scrutinize petty proprieties, then the achievements of those who craft eras and succor the people would never be manifest.

Heaven cannot level its northwest, nor can earth raise its southeast; the sun and moon cannot cast their light into crooked caves, nor can the surging wind stir waves in the depths of a well. To pick one’s teeth, the finest pine or catalpa is no match for a single-inch toothpick; to clean one’s ears, the greatest beams are no better than a wren’s feather; to shoot birds, a thousand gold is no match for a pellet of mud; to sew and mend, a long sword is no match for a needle of a few inches.

Why must one harness a great elephant to catch a mouse, or fashion a roc to herald the dawn? Thus, Jiang Ziya sold warm broth yet found no buyers, yet was summoned as teacher by Kings Wen and Wu; Jiang Sheng was indolent and insolent in a hundred leagues, yet stood peerless beneath the Three Sophoras.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: Ornate compositions and florid prose are not for the blind to appreciate; talents of heroic and transcendent quality are not for the shallow and narrow-minded to recognize. If one’s gaze cannot reach the object, then a dragon’s robe and plain hemp will fetch the same price; if one’s discernment cannot penetrate the matter, then the outstanding and the mediocre will be judged alike. When the eye does not see, beauty does not enter the spirit; when none befriends, those who would harm draw near.

Moreover, love and hate, approval and disdain, have never been uniform across the ages. As times change and customs shift, the same things command different values. Take the jade tablet of the Xia sovereign: once it was worth several cities, yet if sold today, it would be cheaper than bronze or iron. Thus, in former times, those who withdrew to seek their Dao were deemed lofty scholars, while now those who dwell in mountain forests are deemed unworthy. Therefore, the worthy pillars of a sagely age become the criminals of a benighted generation; the integrity of the ancients becomes the feebleness of the latter days.

The man of grand and towering spirit, the life that treads the Way—his towering trust is not merely a wall of fathoms high, his profound beneficence is not merely the depth of Lüliang Gorge. Thus, the shortsighted and near cannot appreciate him, the shallow and hasty cannot fathom him. They despise him for being different from themselves, resent him for not seeking their favor, and so they neither value nor employ him—what more need be said?

Some cast dust upon white jade, raise sores upon jade-like skin, slander in unison, and attack the independent. They heap upon the worthy the filth of Meng Ben and the plunder of Jie Zitui, and slander Chao Fu and Xu You as thieves and burglars. Unless one possesses the luminous insight of the sun and moon, the dark discernment that reveals the subtle, how can one sift jade from mud, draw pearls from the depths of the river?

When fine jade lies unsold in the marketplace, how much less will it be recognized when veiled in uncut stone? When a remarkable man is halted at the sound of his zither, how much less will he be discovered when hidden in the thickets of the Four Rams? Sun Bin longed to deploy his secret strategies, yet Sima Qian had his feet cut off; Han Fei wished to establish governance, yet Li Si had him killed; Jia Yi was impassioned, harboring the art of ordering the state, yet was pushed aside by warriors; Liu Xiang was loyal and good, possessing the means to save a nation in peril, yet was ensnared by Hong and Xian.

This is why Bian He held his uncut jade and wept blood, why Qin Xi smashed his head in despair. Jade and stone are easily distinguished, yet the love for treasures is not as deep as the love for the worthy. Even when a treasure that is easy to distinguish is paired with a love that is profound, one still incurs punishment and mutilation, suffers slander for generations. How much more so for the worthy, who are difficult to know and not urgently sought after? Slanderers add feet to a snake that has no form; treacherous ministers fear the harm that loyalty and integrity bring to themselves; the crooked resent the straightness of the marking line; those who strip at night hate the coming of the bright candle.

Thus, lofty reputations and fine conduct are suppressed and not extolled, while groundless slander takes shape before its shadow. Without a man of Chu to wail and recommend, there is not one chance in ten thousand of encountering recognition—this is only to be expected. He who takes jade for stone will also take stone for jade; he who takes the worthy for fools will also take fools for the worthy. To take stone for jade does no harm; to take fools for the worthy is the diagnosis of ruin.

For ruin, though it may seem to exist, is already doomed, just as a pulse that is dead, though the body lives, is already fated to die. Can one not be cautious? Alas, how lamentable!

Those who do not ponder this are such. Of old, Confucius, the supreme sage, was entangled by the men of Qi in the east and blocked by Zixi in the south. Wen Zhong, a man of great worth, was at first not esteemed by the customs of Jing, and in the end was leisurely executed like a common criminal. To establish merit in the span of a lifetime—is this not difficult indeed?

Jade like *jielü* and *xuanli* cannot be purchased without the wealth of Tao or Yi; a weight of a thousand *jun* cannot be lifted without the strength of Meng Ben or Wu Huo. The strings of *Bai Xue* cannot be tuned without the skill of Ling Su; talents like those of Mao Lun cannot be employed without an enlightened lord.

Yet luminous treasures of the night do not diminish their essence to sell cheaply for want of buyers; great bells and Zhou tripods do not lighten their weight to be lifted by dwarfs though cast aside; the zithers of Yiyang and Yunhe do not cease their song to vie for fame in lewd tunes; and virtues that surpass the multitude do not tread crooked paths in obscurity nor sever their integrity to follow vulgar ways.

Thus, the *he* jade becomes unsellable goods; supple wood is discarded as useless; the ore of the Red Blade never enters the furnace of Ou Ye; and the company of the Eight Elemental Worthies never gains entry through the Four Gates.

Gezhu said: Among my friends there was one known as Master Juling, serene and untroubled, his form and spirit in perfect forgetfulness. Outwardly, he did not adorn himself with the guise of startling the foolish; inwardly, he harbored no mind to accommodate others. His spirit roamed the ancient canons, finding joy that banished care. By day, he pursued the last rays of Xihe; by evening, he basked in the lingering glow of Wangshu. The Dao, however distant, he exhaustively explored; no word, however subtle, did he fail to scrutinize.

Yet his carriage left no tracks in the lands of the powerful, and his letters never crossed the thresholds of the eminent. Thus, his name never passed beyond his thatched door, nor did his person stray from the furrows of his fields.

Then Yiliang the Grand Master approached and challenged him, saying: “I have heard that when the coiled dragon stirs, dark clouds gather; when the Dao rains transformation, hidden talents arise. Thus, the broad streets resound with the songs of unicorns, and the tripods and cauldrons reveal the traces of soaring winds. To sell such gifts is to reap boundless reward; to leap with them is to ascend beyond the heavens. Their radiance illumines the Valley of Sunrise, their brilliance spreads through the Nine Realms. Their merit shades the age, their fame echoes without end.

Yet he who does not tread the path of a mere fathom’s length, though swift as the wind, cannot reach its end; he who does not stir from beneath the rafters, though his wings be those of the great roc, cannot attain the heights. How much less can one who buries his feet in the outer wilds, who folds his wings on the branches of secluded Wu, hope to match swiftness with the sun’s shadow or soar lightly through the azure vault?

The years pass swiftly and do not return; life in this world is fleeting and never repeats. The wise, treading frost, know that solid ice must follow; dwelling in beginnings, they perceive that all living things must end. The Six Dragons hasten their course through the great chaos; hoary heads swiftly announce the approach of dusk. This is why the ancients, gazing at the flowing stream, sighed in reflection, and casting their eyes upon the passing crack, grieved.

You, sir, possess a talent surpassing the age, containing within you a grandeur that transcends the vulgar. Your brush flows vast and deep as surging waves; your sixfold marvels lie pent in profound reserve. Yet you cannot ride the whirlwind to towering heights, nor display your purity in the Nine Mysteries. Your vessel remains unpresented at the ends of ritual vessels; your person is not spared the toil of bearing firewood. You offer harmonious notes in a land of the deaf, peddle ceremonial caps among the bareheaded. In vain do you forget sleep in the forest of brushes, striving to fathom the divine; in vain do you cherish jade within your breast, pour forth gem-like words from your brush’s tip. Your body suffers the hardship of repeated want; your spirit labors amid lofty and unyielding heights. It is as if you buried a foot-long jade beneath deep soil, or sealed brocade in a sealed chest—never to gain wealth through exchange, who will appreciate such buried treasures?

When the dragon and the steed are fettered, how do they differ from the lame donkey? When the Red Blade sheathes its edge, how does it surpass a leaden knife? The great sturgeon does not dwell in ox-tracks; the roc does not linger in the brushwood. I hope, sir, that you will forsake the dragon’s den, ascend the path of clear advantage, abandon the solitary purity of your door and courtyard, observe the two mice and awaken to distant truths, cross the barren valley to climb the heights, don the crimson robes and cast off plainness. Compete with the morning’s swift winds, do not linger or err in your course. Gather your name and vessel in the exalted, let your echo resound in the felicitous cauldrons. Even Bo Cheng, a mere peasant, could distinguish the taste of fern—is that truly worthy of admiration?”

Master Juling replied: “I have heard that the subtle workings of the spirit are profound and obscure, the primordial chaos faint and indistinct. Fortune and misfortune intertwine in the interplay of hidden and manifest; rise and fall are entangled in the cycles of fullness and emptiness. The swift traveler cannot escape the shadow that pursues him; the joyful achiever cannot avoid the ruin that follows success. To correct the flow at its end is not as good as establishing order before signs appear; to mend what is already done does not compare to returning to the simplicity beyond the primordial.

Thus, the wise mantis willingly bends to preserve its extension; those who understand the cycles of opening and closing do not grieve or rejoice over adversity or prosperity. Moreover, the great potter shapes things, the great image flows into form—restlessness and stillness have different inclinations, soaring and sinking diverge in nature. Gold and jewels are prized for their weight; feathers are valued for their lightness. The narrow-minded cling to the mud and filth; the enlightened roam freely in the mysterious and pure. The stagnant pool receives the runoff and overflows; the vast sea swallows a hundred rivers yet does not fill. The gudgeon and shrimp leap joyfully in the mire; the red dragon soars mightily in the high heavens. Those who chew fragrant bait rush to their deaths in pursuit of desire; those who savor emptiness and blandness embrace the harmony of heaven and hasten toward life. Those who perceive the subtle workings of the spirit gaze upon the formless and are not deceived; those who are blind to good and ill blunder into the drawn crossbow and are not startled. Each follows his own inclination—how could they willingly change their course?

I have heard that the five jades cannot carve themselves from the mountains of Song; the soaring serpent cannot streak like lightning without mist; the Dragon Abyss cannot cleave rhinoceros and buffalo without being grasped; the great bell cannot resound without being struck. Golden mushrooms require the autumn wind to shine; the oriole awaits the smoke and fire of the sun’s vessel to sing. The fine steed does not recklessly gallop into danger; the gentleman does not compromise his name for a chance encounter. When the times are adverse, one sinks into obscurity; when the times are favorable, one towers in the celestial court. The scholar who flaunts himself is not esteemed; the woman who offers herself is not chaste. Why must one emulate the lofty standards of those who washed their ears, or imitate the vain glory of those who carried sacrificial meat?

In adversity, Yu wandered and fished, Jiang Taigong was driven away by a foolish woman, Fan Li endured humiliation in the privy, and Hong Yi concealed his marvels while herding. Yet in prosperity, Han Xin cast aside his fishing rod to become a lord, Wen Zhong left his corpse behind to don the blue robe, Fu Yue put down his trowel to discourse on the Dao, and Guan Zhong escaped his shackles to become chief minister. The gentleman conceals his vessel to await his time, accumulates virtue to act when the moment comes. If the time is not right, he does not appear; if the lord is not worthy, he does not serve. Obscurity or prominence depend on circumstance; action or withdrawal are not fixed. In stillness, he is the paragon of recluses; in motion, he is the foremost of the Eight Elemental Worthies. Some apply their thoughts to establishing words; some inscribe their merit on the vessels of state. Different paths lead to the same end; their purpose is one.”

“The scholar may perform deeds of noble worth, yet cannot compel the vulgar to esteem them; may possess talents of great utility, yet cannot force the age to employ them. Clad in coarse cloth, nourished by wild herbs, snaring hares in the thickets—his heart rejoices, his will is content, as if he would spend his life thus. Arrayed in court robes, riding a light carriage, draped in crimson and layered purple—he remains as he was in homespun. Ever like one in plain dress, this is the bearing of the perfected man.

If the treasures of the banquet table are not amassed, if the integrity of the four-walled hut is not pure, the fault is mine. That few recognize it, that rank and renown do not attain, that jade is taken for stone, that the phoenix is called a quail—these are not my failings.

To fret over being known, to chafe at obscurity—these are the feelings of the common folk. To nurture qi with boundless ease, to remain indifferent and free of desire—this is the resolve of one without vexation. When the time comes, the Dao advances; the vessel of great capacity is not elated. Between heaven and earth, the one who knows fate does not grieve.

As for chasing after ten thousand gold in goods to seek a hundred or ten in return, losing flesh and bone in the bargain—I have no leisure for such.” — *On Names and Realities*, Scroll Twenty

The disciple asked: “I have heard that in the late Han, during the reigns of Emperor Ling and Emperor Xian, evaluations were perverse and reckless, the talented and reclusive languished in obscurity, the gluttonous and rapacious prospered, names did not match realities, prices were unmoored from goods, the well-connected were deemed worthy, and the poor were called fools. What was the reason for this?”

Master Baopu replied: “The thunder chariot crashes and rumbles, yet some do not hear it; the seven luminaries traverse the heavens, yet some do not see them. Is it only physical senses that suffer deafness and blindness? The obscuration of the mind and spirit is likewise. Thus, some hear profound words yet do not recognize them—not for want of ears; some see extraordinary talents yet do not know them—not for want of eyes. It is because their hearing does not reach the subtle, and their vision does not attain the marvelous.

Those of great wisdom and far-reaching capacity stand firm as mountains; those of small capacity and narrow ambition drift like tumbleweeds and duckweed. Only because they stand as mountains can nothing move them; only because they drift as duckweed do they flow without obstruction. In terms of goods: those sealed and stored to await buyers are only the rarest and hardest to sell; those drummed and hawked in the marketplace are the common and easily exhausted. In terms of talent: those rooted in the heights of Song and Tai may tower a thousand *ren* and shade ten thousand *mu*, yet none know them; those planted along the roadside may be crooked and stunted, yet they are quickly used. Thus, the ancestral temple holds sacrificial vessels of withered poplar, while the remote valleys abound with uncut catalpa and oak.

Those who usurp hollow fame soar like lizards into the clouds; those who lose true value sink like jade dragons into the nine springs. Thereupon, the quail soars on the wind in proud flight, while the divine phoenix folds its wings in seclusion. Lead and tin pass for the treasures of Tai’e; dogs and sheep are reckoned the equals of tigers and wolves. When flatterers drum up bribes as mighty wings, there is no height they cannot reach; when they ride cliques as boats and oars, there is no distance they cannot cross. With early rising and deferential attendance, anticipating desires and obeying commands, their glib tongues and flattering words seem eloquent, their hearsay and gossip seem learned, their treacherous hearts and gentle demeanors seem benevolent, their sordid deeds and fair words seem upright, their delight in others’ faults seems loyal, their disregard for taboos seems forthright—thus they often succeed.

Moreover, those who look to me seek my favor, while those who do not seek me, I cannot love—this is the natural principle. The worthy are always few, the foolish always many. The many band together to conceal flaws; the few stand alone and weak, without support. Flatterers draw one another upward and block the straight path; the outstanding and wise remain in low positions, unseen. The principle of ‘pulling the roots of the *mao*’ collapses, while the mob of ‘riding the wrong carriage’ rises. The dragon soars too high and falls, weeping tears of blood. Thus, Zixi drove away the great sage Confucius, and Zang Cang slandered the epochal Mencius. If even these two could not escape such misfortune, what need to speak of those below them? This calamity has existed since the dawn of time, and its bitterness is not confined to one age. Looking back through history, this malady is ever the same.

As for the slow and lame lifting their heads in the royal entourage, while swift steeds are left to pasture in the wilds—those who hold office yet do nothing, the state and people wither and decline, inferiors oppress superiors, and the foundation crumbles—this is the cause. Some rot like maggots without burial, some flee in exile like Shen and Hai, some have their sinews torn out in the temple beams, some meet their end at Wangyi. All because those promoted are not genuine, and the loyal and capable go unused.

Thus, the enlightened ruler is diligent in seeking the worthy and eager to elevate the extraordinary, guiding the stagnant to advancement and strictly guarding against obstruction. If talent is truly sufficient for the task, he does not scruple over butchers and fishermen; if words are truly applicable, he draws them from the ranks of soldiers. Some are raised from beneath the ox’s mouth and placed above the assembled officials; some are plucked from shackles and entrusted with the weight of the state. Thus, meritorious deeds flourish, borders expand, distant lands submit, might is established, achievements are secured, and the lineage endures for generations.

The straight plumb line is hated by crooked wood; the upright and impartial are loathed by the wicked and treacherous. If the ruler cannot wield profound insight to seek the hidden, but must rely on those in power to make recommendations, then observe: in every age, those who monopolize power invariably recommend those who attach themselves to them and advance those who benefit them. They slander those they fear and promote those they love. Those they fear are the most impartial; those they love are their private allies. When the most impartial are employed, cliques are broken; when private allies prevail, the ruler’s authority is usurped. The breaking of cliques is the path to prosperity; the usurpation of authority is the beginning of danger. When slandering those they fear, they fear their words are not harsh enough—even if they mutilate them, it still does not satisfy their intent, so they must eliminate them to be content. When advancing those they love, they lament that their praise is not effusive enough—even if they elevate them, it still does not gratify their hearts, so they must endanger others to secure their own.

Thus, those who die bearing false charges and those dismissed without fault have always existed.

Therefore, those who embody the Dao and align with truth stand tall and alone, their talent vast and capacity boundless, bearing frost and treading ice, their thoughts spanning heaven and earth, their vessels equal to the great ministers of old. They hold the classics within humble gates, profound and steadfast as mountains. They would rather preserve their purity in obscurity than cringe and fawn for fleeting gain. They delight in hunger and the narrow lane, honing their lofty integrity; they conceal their abilities and preserve their truth, awaiting the end of their natural years. They do not emerge out of season, nor move without propriety. Clad in coarse cloth and chewing vegetables, they are not discontent; though their hair turns yellow and their fortunes remain unfulfilled, they are not resentful. How could they crumple the towering heights of Mount Tai to fit a square peg, or gather the wings that span the sky to weave a morning alarm for the ranks of officials, only to be stifled beneath the mediocre? They would not leave the forests of the phoenix to enter the brambles of the thorn bush, compete for rotten mice with crouching owls, or bend a foot to gain a yard.”

“Moreover, the bearing of the great worthies is most unrefined, their savor most plain. Serene and self-content, tranquil and unknowing, they are known by few and grieve not; though the age cannot employ them, they chafe not. Though they go two days without a grain of pulse, they would not trade it for the unrighteous feast of the grand sacrifice; though their wadded robes cannot last the year, they would not rejoice in the lawless white fox-fur. Alone they may loosen their hair and pillow high, guarding what they already possess, never bowing low or knitting their brows to seek what they do not yet need.

They do not consort where virtue is thin and rank exalted; they do not draw near where name and reality part; they do not pursue glory and frantic chase; they do not engage with the powerful and the cunning; they do not respond to vulgar chatter and petty quibbles; they do not follow where shoulders are shrugged in compliance.

Their mien is dull yet their aspiration distant; their face is grimed yet their conduct pure. Steadfast as Mount Song and Mount Tai, they cannot be measured by the scales; vast as the blue sea, they cannot be gauged by the bushel. Towering in their fathomless height, they conceal the wealth of a hundred offices. They regard those who pilfer and skulk as mere weeds. Their integrity transcends the world, soaring beyond autumn clouds; their spirit forsakes the common, dwelling far at the edge of the Nine Mysteries.

Though poor and lowly, they cannot be coerced by might; though distressed and suffering, they cannot be swayed by profit.

Their pursuits and esteem may be heard of but never exhausted; their convictions and adherence may be seen but never debated. Thus, those who detest them raise a chorus of sidelong glances; those who love them are few and feeble, of no avail. It is like a handful of earth that cannot dam a bursting river, or a ladle of water that cannot quench a prairie fire.

Thereupon, war drums stifle the sound of thunder, rattles and drums indulge in the clamor of great drums. Fragrant orchids are cut down, while stinking salted fish are worn as pendants. Dark millet wine is poured out in shame, and thin ale is lavished upon the round mound. Blood-sweating horses are driven away with drooping ears, while the lame and broken are galloped in the imperial carriage.

This is why the ancients embraced sand and carried stones, plunging into the current to be buried with the fish, unable to bear sharing the world with such as these. Alas! How lamentable!”

“Yet to cast black jades into the mire is not because the night-shining pearl is false, but because none recognize it; to discard the vermilion bowcase without bending the bow is not because the *Fanruo* lacks strength, but because none appreciate it. Thus, jade and jasper await a Jing He to reveal their price of linked cities; the *Wuhao* bow requires a Feng Men to display its power to breach the firmest armor; the *Feitu* steed depends on a Zi Yu to soar on the wind; and the outstanding man finds a true friend to unleash his strength.

If fine jade does not emerge from the deep mountains, if the mighty bow is not carved into a hundred layers, if the swift *Jilu* steed is not harnessed to the vermilion carriage, if the man of destiny does not tread the path of rank and power—who then would know their ability to radiate resplendent brilliance, to strike down cloud-scraping birds from a thousand fathoms, to gallop with wind-chasing speed, or to bring order to the myriad affairs of state?

Yet if they do not meet their moment, they at least avoid being mixed with worthless stones, being equated with rotten wood, being ranked among inferior steeds, or being lumped with the common and petty!

Alas! To draw a wide bow with thorn arrows and expect a master’s shot from a vast marsh, to whip a weary nag and seek to follow in the tracks of King Mu of Zhou, to lay aside the axe and adze and desire the dual craft of Ban and Mo, to neglect fine talent and hope for the proper ordering of constant norms—is this not difficult?

Though fame and reality may leak away in this generation, the echo of virtue may yet reach posterity.

To delight in Heaven and know fate—what worry, what sorrow?
To rest in the times and follow the natural order—what resentment, what sorrow?”

*Pure Mirror*, Scroll Twenty-One
*Baopuzi* says:

Those commonly deem men of unmatched valor and strength as material for supreme generals, and those well-versed in order and chaos as talents for the Three Ducal Ministers. Yet Zhang Fei and Guan Yu, each a match for ten thousand foes, both lost their heads and shamed their lords, their severed crowns falling where they ought not. Kong Rong and Bian Rang, whose learning and letters soared above the vulgar, both proved inept in governance, leaving ruin in their wake wherever they served. Deng Yu and Ma Yuan, mere scholars from the fields, excelled in the art of war; Xiao He and Cao Can, untouched by classical teachings, surpassed all as chief ministers. Thus it is clear that judging men is no easy task.

To test them only when proven is to risk toppling the tripod and spilling its feast; to judge by words and appearances is to risk mistaking semblance for truth, where the genuine and the false intermingle. Yet the world deems this easy—what passes the ear or meets the eye is thought to be thoroughly known. I remain deeply skeptical and dare not grant my assent.

To distinguish the worthy from the unworthy, to perceive the spirit through form, lies with the man himself and cannot be forced. Unless one’s brightness rivals sun and moon, unless one’s hearing and sight are free of distortion, one should proceed with clarity and caution, advancing gradually in employment, never granting full trust at once. To lightly lend the sharpest tools is to find their return exceedingly difficult, and the damage wrought already great.

One cannot guarantee the rest based on a single deed. Those who align with oneself are not necessarily fit for use; those who differ from oneself are not necessarily to be dismissed.

Some may object: "What hangs in the heavens bears its signs, and what lies on earth takes form. Thus, gazing at mountains and measuring rivers, one may infer their heights and depths; watching winds rise and clouds drift, one may trace fortune and misfortune. The wise man, seeing timber unmarred, perceives the fine jade hidden in the mountain; observing shores that never wither, he senses the luminous pearl sunken in the deep. When comets appear, he knows the sturgeon has just perished; when sun and moon are eclipsed, he recognizes the qilin locked in combat. The weight of huohuo grass need not be weighed to know its boundless heaviness; the volume of rivers need not be measured to confirm their unfathomable depth. The wings of the swan goose, the hooves of the swift steed—though they have not yet flown or run—guarantee their speed and agility. The swords of Hao Cao and the daggers of Xu Shi—though they have not yet struck—leave no doubt of their decisive edge. The young colt bears the capacity to swallow an ox; the osprey and eagle display the bearing of soaring raptors. Where plants flourish, the soil must be rich; where fish grow large, the waters must be vast. A tiger’s tail does not attach to a fox’s body; elephant tusks do not emerge from a rat’s mouth. Shu Yu’s insatiable greed was evident in his newborn visage; the omens of my clan’s destruction were inscribed at the moment of my birth. Shen Tong recognized the wife-stealing sorcerer in Wu Chen; Zhang Fu foresaw Chen Ping’s future nobility. Fan Li vanished into the Five Lakes because of Goujian’s wasp-like eyes and beak-like mouth. The people of Zhao ceased their rivalry because of Bai Qi’s sharp brow and piercing gaze. When King Wen met Lü Shang, before the mulberry’s shadow had shifted, he knew this man was worthy of being his teacher. When Xuande beheld Kongming, before the sun’s shadow had changed, he had already entrusted him with his heart. Guo Tai, though of middling talent, still knew men well: in Yingchuan, he befriended Li Yuanli; in Chenliu, he allied with Fu Weiming; in Waihuang, he grew close to Han Zizhu; in Puheng, he took Qiu Jizhi as his master; in the academy, he gathered Wei Degong; watching the plowman, he singled out Mao Jiwei; he marveled at Meng Min’s burden-bearing and warned of Yuan Ai’s inevitable downfall. In the end, his words proved entirely accurate, without a single error. Surely one can discern the keen from the dull by their outward signs, assess urgency or composure through their voice and breath, gauge brightness or obscurity in their actions, detect purity or corruption in their wealth and desires, observe propriety in their giving and taking, judge truth or falsehood in their words and deeds, examine their conduct within the inner chambers, compare their beginnings and ends through trust and results. Is not the verification of worthiness thus an easy matter?"

The Master of Simplicity replies: "I do not claim that people are entirely unknowable or that discerning others lacks all principle. Yet this art resides in the radiance of great clarity—something not every man can claim for himself. Nevertheless, men of the world each assert their own competence, which is why I have spoken thus, to caution those who would entrust responsibilities lightly.

For a majestic appearance does not guarantee worthiness, nor does a frail frame ensure foolishness; the roaring are not necessarily brave, nor are the gentle necessarily timid. Some may share outward signs yet differ in intent; others may vary in temperament yet align in purpose. This is not like the heavens and earth, which follow constant patterns, or mountains and rivers, which have fixed places.

Indeed, some things are easy to know from afar yet difficult to judge up close—just as the eye can survey the vast heavens yet cannot examine the nape of the neck; the ear can hear thunder yet cannot distinguish the sounds of insects and lice. Tang Lü and Fan Xu were skilled in reading appearances, able to foretell lifespan, wealth, poverty, rank, and status, yet they could not discern the breadth or narrowness of a man’s nature, the rise or fall of his aspirations. If even the wise find this difficult, how much more so the common man!

Yet you cite examples of judging by form and challenge the discourse on discerning the spirit. Without cultivating the root, you have likely missed the point."

"The arrow that misses its mark may yet pierce the autumn down, yet one cannot call the archer skilled for this. The mother of Shu Xiang and the son of the Shen clan may have made a single accurate judgment, yet they could not do so consistently. Yao of Tang studied antiquity yet misjudged his appointments; the Duke of Zhou was reverent and wise yet erred in his grants. Confucius discerned the rise and fall of states long before signs appeared, yet failed to recognize Zhan Tai by his appearance. Yan Zhou could judge purity and impurity across a thousand years, yet was blind to extraordinary men right before his eyes. The difficulty of knowing others is thus extreme.

What Guo Tai discussed—was this not the case of men surpassing even sages in their errors? What they succeeded in were the obvious and easily recognized; what they failed in, others could not record.

What is truly valuable is to perceive outstanding talent among the nameless, to spot fleet-footed steeds on the slopes of Wu, to pluck pearl-bearing oysters from the depths of the Nine Abysses, to point out luminous treasures amid piles of stone. Like Bojie, who recognized an instrument of unmatched sound amid the ashes of destruction, or Pingzi, who selected bamboo of exceptional resonance before it was used.

When the six armies gather or the marketplace assembles, a single glance without confusion, probing their hidden intentions, determining the course of their conduct from beginning to end—this is the untransmitted marvel of unique insight. If, as in the previous discussion, one must wait to examine the entirety of their conduct, observe the quality of their deeds, this would be like weighing silk threads only after they have been measured, or assessing the length of cloth only after it has been cut—tasks anyone can perform. Why then would one need a sage?"

Gehong said: To emulate the azure and yellow of Heaven and Earth’s covering and sustaining, to raise the bright well to manifest the subtle—those whose literary brilliance shines like the sun at noon yet whose forms remain complete, whose solitary clarity attains the divine, are sages. Those endowed with lofty integrity’s purity, who stand aloft with towering standards far above the vulgar, whose empty spirit seems like folly yet who never repeat faults nor flatter or fawn, are worthies. Those who dwell in the stillness of non-action, who tread the straight path and uphold the level, are Daoists. Those who exhaust the rites of sacrifice for the living and the dead, who preserve their bodies and hair to glorify their names, are filial persons. Those who extend compassion to all living beings, who constantly forgive themselves in dealing with others, are humane persons. Those who align their lives with the state’s demands, who endure peril and hardship with unwavering integrity, are loyal persons. Those who perceive subtle principles in what is hard to discern, who foresee the hidden turns of fortune, are perspicacious persons. Those who measure order and chaos to expand or contract, who weigh departure and approach to preserve themselves, are wise persons. Those who remain constant in both success and obstruction, who follow their nature and destiny without hindrance, are enlightened persons. Those who do not bend a foot to straighten a fathom, who do not debase themselves for mere compliance, are refined persons. Those who regulate action and stillness by bodily measure, who remain clear and deliberate without regret, are steadfast persons. Those who embody the purity of ice and frost, who remain unstained by power or profit, are pure persons. Those who uphold constancy through cold and heat, who remain unsuspecting even in peril, are righteous persons. Those who keep a single word through long-standing agreements, who remain unchanged through decline, are trustworthy persons. Those who display keen ornamentation in their speech, whose words are radiant, lush, and perfectly clear, are literary persons. Those who rouse the valor of resolute heroism, who charge with weapons to quell disorder, are martial persons. Those who fathom the profound depths of the *Classic of Mountains and Seas* and the *Tangled Thicket*, who master ancient words to exhaust principle, are Confucian persons. Those who sharpen their minds on refined meaning, who begrudge every moment not spent advancing virtue, are improving persons. Those who recognize the transience of abundant wealth, who face emolument and gain as if they were mere trifles, are incorruptible persons. Those who do not alter their conduct in gain or loss, who do not incline their will toward desire, are steadfast persons. Those who relieve urgent distress and forget their toil, who take the worries of others as their own duty, are devoted persons. Those who maintain unsullied integrity to the end, who do not evade or seek escape through compromise, are principled persons. Those who soar with the brilliance of pure wit, whose words are concise yet clear and decisive, are eloquent persons. Those who always remain humble and yield credit, who grow ever more reverent even in prosperity, are modest persons. Those who honor harmony among the nine relations, who invariably stand upright to follow principle, are compliant persons. Those who can decide amid hesitation, who wield the marking line without partiality, are capable persons. Those who discern the true from the false amid confusion, who resolve hesitation with perfect appropriateness, are judicious persons. Those who track the waxing and waning of the seven luminaries, who deduce the patterns of rise and fall, are divinatory persons. Those who rush toward naked blades without thought of life, who confront rhinoceroses and tigers in the wilds, are brave persons. Those who array dignified bearing to command the multitude, who rely on laws and standards without deviation, are stern persons. Those who devise ingenious mechanisms to aid utility, who master tones and numbers with equal precision, are skilled persons. Those who defy the powerful without fear, who remain undaunted even in peril, are cunning persons. Those who maintain unremitting diligence day and night, who forget toil and exhaustion in deep endeavor, are diligent persons. Those who endure slander and vilification with equanimity, who remain unshaken by fear, are resolute persons. Those who hear praise without elation, who face hardship without change, are composed persons. Those who act decisively when a matter is right, who do not hesitate amid general doubt, are decisive persons. Those who follow the marking line in advance and retreat, who do not seek illicit gain through luck, are prudent persons. Those who serve rites and standards with trepidation, who remain blameless even among kin, are good persons. Those who tread the plain Way without desire, who remain unchanged though times shift, are unadorned persons. Those in whom none of these qualities are found, yet who do not rise to the traces of the good, are base persons.

Disciples asked: “We have heard the categories of the good person’s conduct; now we wish to know the deeds of the wicked that may serve as warnings to the world—may you set them forth in writing.”

Gehong said: Those who fail to nurture their parents, who harm the Way and endanger themselves, are perverse persons. Those who harbor deceit and falsehood to steal glory, who prioritize self-interest and forget their lives, are treacherous persons. Those who turn from the upright path of benevolence and righteousness, who recklessly endanger others to secure themselves, are vicious persons. Those who love contention and are never sated, who specialize in vilifying the upright and harming the honest, are evil persons. Those who wield the marking line to wound and carve, whose hearts delight in killing and endure cruelty, are cruel persons. Those who adorn false doctrines to insinuate themselves, who fabricate slander to entangle the loyal and pure, are slanderous persons. Those whose words are artful yet whose deeds contradict them, who truly tread filth yet feign purity, are obsequious persons. Those who do not examine the roots of right and wrong, who recklessly seek victory and vent anger, are violent persons. Those who display minor virtues to gain trust, yet secretly harbor malice and have no attachments, are treacherous persons. Those who follow the wind to ingratiate themselves, who gauge their lord’s intentions to support wrongdoing, are flattering persons. Those whose words cannot be relied upon in reversal, who readily make promises yet lack substance, are hollow persons. Those who see profit and forget righteousness, who abandon integrity and shame for mere gain, are greedy persons. Those who gaze upon beauty and are stirred in heart, who adorn themselves in fine silks and entertain wicked thoughts, are lustful persons. Those who see accomplished matters and doubt, whose actions miscalculate and bring much regret, are dull persons. Those who turn from the classics and rely on themselves, who are ashamed to seek guidance from those better than themselves, are harmful persons. Those who know what is good yet cannot achieve it, who do much yet accomplish nothing, are inferior persons. Those who neglect virtue and conduct, who serve power and influence to curry favor, are corrupt persons. Those who tread crooked paths for quick success, who push bribes to compete for passage, are depraved persons. Those who are arrogant and rude without propriety, who delight in humiliating those better than themselves, are fierce persons.

Those who, when oppressed or wronged, falsely accuse themselves, and tremble in fear though no hardship befalls them, are cowardly persons. Those who, in crowded assemblies, fuss over trivial distinctions and speak unreservedly to those unworthy of their words, are shallow persons. Those who, though they aspire to it, cannot discern the right course in obscure matters, are obstinate persons. Those who, knowing their actions are wrong, refuse to change, and upon hearing good counsel only grow worse, are deluded persons. Those who lack a compassionate heart, and lightly sever ties with kin and old friends, are callous persons. Those who resent what they cannot attain, and rejoice in the misfortunes of others, are envious persons. Those who hoard wealth and grain while scorning righteousness, and watch others suffer want without relief, are miserly persons. Those who court extreme danger for the sake of luck, and plant the seeds of disaster without remorse, are foolish persons. Those whose affections are narrow and partisan, whose minds are set only on profit, are petty persons. Those who gallop hounds and hawks across the plains in pursuit of game, and indulge in gambling without end, are infatuated persons. Those who forget the distinctions of rank and status, and delight in ostentatious finery, are extravagant persons. Those who wallow in music and sensuality at feasts, and neglect the rites of celebration and mourning, are dissolute persons. Those who have no inclination for self-cultivation, and neglect their family duties, are slothful persons. Those who lack dignified restraint, and act carelessly without thought, are frivolous persons. Those who regard the Dao and virtue as though drunk, and are stirred to agitation by the prospect of wealth, are sordid persons. Those who rely on shallow, flawed judgment, and cannot discern the right path from the wrong, are dull-witted persons. Those who despise the worthy and hold them in no esteem, and turn deaf ears to lofty words, are clamorous persons. Those who cannot distinguish between the noble and the base, and remain unmoved even when admonished, are benighted persons. Those who hesitate and falter in defiance of the Dao and virtue, and recklessly disregard ritual and law, are disorderly persons. Those whose every action invites ridicule, and whose words, once spoken, defy reason, are clumsy persons. Those who serve the powerful like slaves, yet turn their backs on the weak who once showed them kindness, are treacherous persons. Those who abandon old friends in poverty and low station, and treat scholars with arrogance, are haughty persons. Those who spurn aging companions in pursuit of endless desires, and wander far without purpose or study, are dissolute persons. Those who lack steadfast loyalty and trustworthiness, and forsake their benefactors for profit, are traitorous persons. Those who feign agreement to your face, yet slander you when parted, are deceitful persons. Those who cultivate aggression and assert their will, and reject loyal advice without heeding it, are contentious persons.

The *Baopuzi* says: "Human character is not easily known; truth and falsehood may resemble one another."

Scholars may possess refined and elegant appearances, with graceful and composed demeanors that dazzle the eye upon first sight and bring comfort upon closer acquaintance. Their bearing resembles that of dragons and tigers, their movements forming perfect propriety. Yet their minds are obscured, their spirits stifled, and their talents prove utterly inadequate. All they possess remains but skin-deep. Their mouths cannot utter a single marvel, their brushes cannot compose half a phrase. In office, they cannot govern the people; in war, they cannot command troops. In administration, affairs fall to ruin; in missions, they bring shame upon their charge. Their actions are never fitting, their paths never sound. These are the first of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may appear plain and haggard, their countenances coarse and unrefined, their voices weak and timid, their movements stiff and awkward. Yet they harbor hidden brilliance and treasured wisdom, their learning profound and conduct lofty. Their abilities surpass those of the finest ministers, their writings flourish like spring forests. In civil office, they bring order and prosperity; in military command, they secure unmatched victory. These are the second of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may possess strategies deep as abysses, their tactical skill divine, their wisdom encompassing success and failure, their thoughts penetrating the obscure and mysterious. Their talents suffice for every task, their minds equal to any challenge. Yet their mouths cannot express their thoughts, their brushes cannot convey their intent. In casual encounters, they appear no different from the common and mediocre. These are the third of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may be quick-witted and sharp, their words artful and dazzling, their analogies flowing like deep springs and cutting like fierce winds. Yet what they speak, they themselves cannot practice. Skilled in understanding the past, they falter in managing the present. In governance, they bring chaos; in shepherding the people, they provoke resentment. These are the fourth of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may appear outwardly respectful, their demeanor reverent and speech deferential, yet their spirits are distant, their minds careless, their inner selves scattered and unrestrained. Entrusted with tasks, they show no concern; placed in positions of responsibility, they fail to govern. These are the fifth of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may draw bows with unerring accuracy, strike with empty hands to land precise blows, ride backward with ease, and master all five weapons. Yet their bodies are light, their thoughts shallow, their hands swift but their hearts timid. In practice, they face no equal, but in real combat, they prove ineffective. At the sight of dust from fleeing enemies, they turn and flee; at the mere rumor of foes, they lose their nerve. These are the sixth of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may be forthright and unhurried, their speech sparse and manner unadorned, their minor conduct flawed, lacking even small acts of courage. Their words are restrained, their backs bent, their claws sheathed, their wings folded—seemingly weak and yielding. Yet their courage is steadfast, their resolve unyielding. Unafraid of the strong and overbearing, they regard death as a return when righteousness demands it. Even if dismembered or cut to pieces, they do not abandon their principles. These are the seventh of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may be filial, fraternal, gentle, and kind, their demeanor modest and composed, their conduct faithful and obedient. They tread only where propriety allows, their aspirations pure even in hardship, their integrity as unblemished as ice and frost. Yet they are slow, impractical, and out of touch, failing to grasp the essentials of affairs. They see opportunities but do not act, achieving nothing in their endeavors. In their own households, they are like pillars, but entrusted with tasks, they cannot fulfill them. These are the eighth of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may conduct themselves with lofty simplicity, their character stern and unyielding. They whistle and strut, aloof and disdainful, scorning their peers and defying convention. They disregard restraint, overlook minor faults, follow their inclinations, and act as if no one else exists. Their cliques slander others, and their words bring shared ruin. Scholars and friends do not draw near, and their reputations are left behind. Yet in court, they stand with unflinching integrity, knowing no hesitation in action. Loyal in serving their superiors, they govern their subordinates with clarity. These are the ninth of the hard-to-discern.

Scholars may embody magnanimity and generosity, humbling themselves to embrace all things. They conceal faults and flaws, their demeanor warm and reverent, their conduct modest and pure. They labor with humility, yield with restraint, rescue the imperiled, and uphold trust. They entrust their lives without doubt, and their guardianship of orphans is secure. Yet their goodness lacks discernment, their kindness lacks decisiveness. They cannot reward the good nor bear to punish the wicked. Their loyalty and integrity are abundant, but their practical ability is lacking. Wielding the axe of judgment, they hesitate; abandoning laws, they imitate wrongs. Right and wrong become entangled, and in the end, they bring failure upon themselves. These are the tenth of the hard-to-discern.

Things may appear one way yet be another; they may seem thus but are not. To assess them without confusion, to perceive their essence beyond form—even sages may falter in this, let alone ordinary people. Thus, in employing talent and selecting scholars, in fostering closeness and forming friendships, one cannot but choose with precision and test with care.

Moreover, the transformations of character and conduct—beginning upright but ending in depravity—are beyond the foresight of even the moderately capable. Take Wang Mang, who at first seemed finer than Yi and Huo, yet in the end proved more vicious than Zhao Gao. If scholars were as easily distinguished as the wren from the swan, the fox from the dragon, then the Four Fiends would not have held office in Yao’s court, Guan and Cai would not have nearly toppled the Zhou, Confucius would not have erred with Zhan Tai, Yanling would not have regretted discarding his gold, Yi Yin would not have labored seventy times, and Xiang Yu would not have resented Fan Zeng.

The trouble lies in their resemblance to true jade yet being mere stone, like the *jiaomiao* mimicking the phoenix, frozen ice resembling crystal, or smoke imitating clouds. Thus, those who avoid error are few. If even the emperor finds this difficult, how much more so for those near at hand!

Only the supremely enlightened, with profound insight into the subtle and obscure, with divine judgment to weigh all things, with thoughts piercing the hidden and dim, who can recognize uncut jade in the mountains and discern pearls in the rivers—only they can, amidst these hard-to-discern scholars, make choices without regret. They distinguish the worthy from the unworthy, and their praise and censure are perfectly just. Thus, Wu Ding and King Wen of Zhou did not govern alone, and Fu Yue and Lü Shang were not forever cast aside. Gao Yao and Yi Yin did not succeed in their villainy, and Hong Gong and Shi Xian found no refuge for their deceit.

Such are the broad outlines of selecting scholars and the general principles of careful choice. The subtleties of precise discernment rest with the individual, and cannot be fully detailed by brush and ink.

*Chapter Twenty-Three: On Quelling Litigation*
Master Guzi, Liu Junshi, remarked: "The human order begins with husband and wife, their union mirroring the Two Modes. Thus, the rites of grand marriage were held in great esteem by the ancients. They sought to unite two families in harmony, to continue the ancestral line. The host bowed in welcome at the gate, received the command in the temple, presented dark and light silks as gifts, and personally handed the reins to the bride. The groom observed three years of mourning for his parents-in-law, conveyed his intent to the bride’s family, and the bride’s family consented without daring to alter it."

Once the grand mourning rites have concluded, the bride’s family petitions the groom for consent, and only after the groom declines may she be wed to another. This is to honor reverence and deference. How could there be a practice where litigation precedes marriage? Yet in this degenerate age, disrespect and moral decay prevail: obligations are neglected, promises made and broken, disputes and slander foul the courts, and official bureaus are clogged with grievances. It would be fitting to decree that in all contested betrothals, before the couple shares the wedding feast, the engagement may be dissolved at will, with the bride-price doubled and returned along with the betrothal gifts. For those who have already dissolved two engagements, the penalty shall be twice the bride-price; for three dissolutions, four times the bride-price. Thus, those who dissolve engagements will have no cause for litigation, and the greedy and miserly will find no advantage in repeated betrothals. This is an essential technique of royal governance, an unchanging and enduring law.”

Master Baopu replied: “Master Liu grieves the decline of virtue and deference and laments how civil disputes harm the moral order. Though litigation may swiftly implicate me, even the loss of household harmony is of little consequence when weighed against the value of harmony itself, which risks being utterly lost. He proposes bold measures to reclaim what the world has forsaken and devises excellent strategies to rescue it from its downward course. Through this, the clamorous customs of the age may yet be transformed, and the shameless winds of the time may shift. Yet to fully address the complexities of human nature and deceit is no easy task. Truly, this is an enduring law for governing the state and a most beneficial discourse. Though I am unworthy and lack understanding of the highest principles, in this hasty response to your inquiry, I venture to express my doubts.

The bond of marriage is formed without coercion: one party selects and seeks, while the other consents only if pleased. To make a frivolous promise and later regret it is the fault of the bride’s family; to break one’s word and abandon trust, granting or withholding at whim—even strict prohibitions and severe penalties have failed to curb this. Now, if we indulge such behavior and merely impose a penalty of doubled bride-price, only the poor will be deterred, while those of ample means will achieve their desires. The subsequent suitor may grow wealthy and willingly assist in paying the penalty, content to do so. Then the first family must remain silent, unable to voice their grievance.
