Examining the matter by its true nature, how can they not harbor resentment and sigh in despair? For those who refuse to submit, death is as nothing; thus, bloodshed and slaughter will surely arise. If we惜惜 spare them the minor disgrace of litigation only to provoke their unbearable resentment, it is like begrudging the trouble of hiring a carriage while forgetting the devastation of a ruined journey.

When one buys goods at the market, if another raises the price and seizes them, few can endure it without anger—how much more so when it is a wife, betrothed and awaiting the wedding, who is taken away! If this law is enacted, those entering marriage will, even as they pay respects and personally welcome the bride, still harbor the fear of her being seized. Master Liu’s argument hinges on the wedding feast as the decisive moment, and this is sound. Yet if the bride’s family, though they have received the betrothal gifts for years, constantly wield the threat of dissolution, relying on the possibility of repeated seizures, they will inevitably grow careless in selecting a groom. If the groom is even slightly displeased, he may change his mind, leading to enmity and disaster—nothing could be worse.

The laws of former times were designed with foresight for the entire process, blocking the path at its inception to prevent trouble from arising. Their precision is evident, yet they did not leave the door open to arbitrary seizure, instead establishing the system of written contracts as the decisive measure. Is this not a settled principle? Suppose a woman possesses peerless beauty, a face that could topple cities, and she encounters a powerful and wealthy official who, captivated by her allure, forgets all propriety. With wealth amassed in the thousands, he spares no expense. Even a tenfold repayment of the bride-price would not deter him—how much less a single penalty? The House of Hua did not hesitate to kill Kong Fu and take his wife; the people of Chu, when arranging a bride for their lord’s son, took her for themselves upon seeing her beauty. By this reasoning, who would hesitate to exhaust their family’s wealth to assist the bride’s family in repaying the first suitor? Petty and fickle men, quick to take offense and nurse grudges, delight in abandoning the weak to pursue the strong, favoring the cold to chase the hot. If this law is implemented, the rich and powerful will surely seize brides from the poor and lowly. I wonder, Master, what measures you propose to prevent such abuses?

Some suggest: Let the bride’s family receive the bride-price without regard to its amount, but require an immediate written contract. Later, have witnesses—no fewer than ten, including those who have traveled far or died—sign their names on a separate document. Also, require the bride’s father, brothers, or uncles to respond to the groom’s family in their own handwriting. If there is a breach of promise with clear evidence, the bride’s parents and brothers shall all be subject to criminal penalties. In this way, perhaps litigation can be avoided.”

*Chapter Twenty-Four: On Wine Admonitions*

Bao Puzi says: What the eye delights in must not be indulged; what the ear takes pleasure in must not be followed; what the nose favors must not be yielded to; what the mouth craves must not be pursued; what the heart desires must not be gratified. Thus, what deludes the eye is surely alluring beauty and vibrant adornments; what deludes the ear is surely sweet sounds and wanton music; what deludes the nose is surely the fragrance of orchids and basil; what deludes the mouth is surely rare delicacies and fine flavors; what deludes the heart is surely power, profit, and fame. When all five are indulged, the calamities that follow and the harm they bring to the self—is this not indeed true?

Therefore, the wise man imposes strict restraints upon his nature, does not recklessly expend his spirit in pursuit of external things, governs himself with tranquility and joy, and augments his resolve with long-term planning. In restraining his emotions, he is more vigilant than one guarding against a breached dike; in mastering his nature, he is more cautious than one riding a galloping steed with worn reins. Thus, he preserves his years within and avoids entanglements without.

Hunger and cold are hard to endure, yet the man of pure integrity will not accept grain or silk obtained through unrighteous means. Poverty and lowliness are hard to bear, yet the man of lofty ideals will not dwell in the perilous honors of a chaotic age. For when calculation prevails, the heart that can endure remains whole; when the Dao triumphs, the deeds that harm one’s nature are cast aside.

Wine and sweet liquor, though pleasant to the taste, are poisons that breed illness. They offer not the slightest benefit yet bring ruin as great as mountains. The nobleman is undone by them in virtue, the common man hastened by them to crime. Those who indulge and become infatuated with them seldom escape disaster.

The scholars of this world know this well, yet while none can entirely abstain, neither are they willing to moderate. They give free rein to the immediate desires of heart and mouth, lightly summoning the root of calamity—like one parched with thirst who indulges in cold drink, gratifying the self while endangering the body. From minor disorders to great collapses, is there any that is not caused by wine?

Yet the common run of men revel and wallow in it. At the outset of a feast, all is solemn and orderly, words are few and demeanor composed. They chant the *Zhan Lu*’s “gentle and serene,” sing the *Zai Gao*’s “joyful and harmonious,” raise the cup of “ten thousand years,” and cultivate the virtue of “warmth and restraint.” Before the sun has shifted its shadow, their bodies grow light and their ears burn hot. Vessels of glass and conch shells are used together, and the rules of full cups and forfeits grow ever stricter. Drunkenness is not halted; they pull up the linchpin and cast it into the well.

Then mouths spew and noses drip, soaking heads and causing chaos. They dance again and again, abandoning their seats; they shout and clamor, like boiling water or seething broth. Some vie in eloquence to outdo others; some laugh soundlessly to themselves; some speak without a listener; some vomit across the mats; some seize upon a fine singer; some lose their caps and loosen their sashes. The upright and honorable are swept into lewd glances; the timid and weak mimic the agility of Qing Ji; the slow and heavy whirl like tumbleweeds in the wind; the solemn and dignified prance like deer and leap like fish. Those tongue-tied in cold or heat now wave their hands and chant; the humble and unassuming now puff themselves up to curry favor. The decorum of modesty and shame is destroyed, and the sickness of dissipation erupts; the base nature of the petty is exposed, and the arrogance of the haughty emerges. Spirit is befouled and mind confounded; right and wrong are inverted.

Some race chariots and gallop horses, rushing toward ravines without fear, treating the Nine Bends’ steep slopes as mere anthills. Some climb perilous heights and tread crumbling edges, unaware of their falls, mistaking the depths of Lü Liang for a cow’s hoofprint. Some vent their fury upon objects; some rage drunkenly at wives and children. They heap cruelty upon servants, wield sharp blades against livestock, set fires blazing in their halls, cast treasures into deep waters, redirect their wrath toward passersby, and inflict violence upon friends.

There are those who insult their lords with the treatment of barbarians and execution; those who provoke the fierce and suffer entrapment. Their words, though adorned with fine phrases, are vexatious and contrary to reason; their bows and prostrations, though many, are labored and sorrowful, not respectful. Subjects and children commit improprieties before their lords and parents; the young and lowly act insolently toward their elders in their seats. They take candid speech as slander, loyal admonition as an affront to themselves.

Thus, blades are drawn and thoughts of peril forgotten; staffs and cudgels are brandished without regard for what lies ahead. They provoke enmities that draw blood, invite capital punishment. To slight one’s elders is to incur the heavy censure of the village; to insult another’s father or brother is to invite the vengeance of his sons and brothers; to expose another’s secrets is to provoke the wrath of the strong; to reckon accounts with harsh exactitude is to leave the sober unable to forgive.

In an instant, a multitude of woes arise; in the depths of dissipation, a hundred ailments take root. A team of four horses cannot outrun the regrets of the past, and though one may wish to reform, there is no path to return. This is what the wise deeply guard against, yet what the foolish cannot avoid. The disasters and defeats it brings cannot be fully recounted.

Yet when men gather in revelry, none will set it aside. Cups are raised to the ears, and no one considers whether they are capable. They calculate the lingering drops of rain to the last moment, taking delay as a slight. They pour out their full measure to those they respect, only for earnestness to turn to indifference. To urge restraint is futile; to supervise is ineffective. Resentful looks and ugly words arise from this.

When wind penetrates the organs, it leaves one disoriented; in severe cases, it brings self-injury and self-dread. Those afflicted by such illness are all anxious and fearful, swallowing bitterness and enduring pain in hopes of swift recovery. Yet the sickness of drunkenness differs not at all from this. While they seclude themselves to avoid the wind, they cannot sever their desires to moderate their drinking.

If one feared wine as one fears the wind, loathed drunkenness as one loathes illness, then the faults of dissipation would be blocked, and the errors of indulgence would cease. For while the illness of wind can still be treated with gradual remedies, the transformations wrought by wine lie in a single breath. When confusion and disorder take hold, one hovers between existence and oblivion. They see Mount Tai as a pellet, the vast sea as a basin. They wail that the heavens are falling above, cry that the earth is collapsing below. They lie in wait for tigers and wolves, leap into wells and rush into fire, yet do not call it evil.

The use of one’s body in such a manner—how can one still cherish the rites of reverence and respect, or guard against the faults of joy and anger? Of old, when Yi Di was cast aside, Great Yu rose to power. With mounds of grain dregs and pools of wine, Jie and Zhou met their ruin. Lord Feng incurred guilt by carrying a goblet in his bosom. Jing Sheng’s dissipation led to his downfall through the three ceremonial cups. Liu Song’s intestines rotted from his summer-evading drink. Guo Zhen went mad from drinking without a day’s respite. The untimely death of the Lord of Xinling, the misrule of Xiangzi, the loss of the people’s trust by Zhao Wu, the execution of Zifan, the shortened life of Emperor Hui of Han, the extermination of Guan Fu’s clan, the harm that befell Chen Zun, the banishment of Ji Bu, the demotion of Zijian, and the silencing of Xu Miao—all were caused by this same thing. Though many in the world delight in and love it, few caution against or fear it. Outnumbered as I am, where can my earnest admonitions find purchase? I only wish that you would moderate it.

In former times, when years were lean and grain dear, men killed one another in drunkenness. The regional governors thereupon imposed bans on wine, issuing strict orders and repeatedly emphasizing them. Officials conducted searches, seizing and parading those caught, subjecting them to public humiliation. Some were flogged to death, more than half perishing. Yet the stricter the prohibitions grew, the more violations occurred. Men dug cellars to brew, carried wine in oil bladders. The people’s devotion to this thing may truly be called steadfast.

Though I, a humble commoner, set forth these empty words in writing, what can I do? Moreover, those who govern the people, though they enact such laws, cannot themselves abstain from this thing. They relax their own restraints while tightening others’, issue commands yet do not follow them, and fail to lead by example—how can the common people trust them? With such teaching, how can instruction take hold? With such prohibitions, how can restraint be achieved?

Those who sell wine, if they abandon their trade, fall into hardship. Thus they embellish their wares, offer bribes, and rely on the powerful. The officials under their jurisdiction dare not intervene. Only the powerless desist, while the influential monopolize the market. They set up furnaces to monopolize profits, doubling their prices. Those who buy from them do so openly, without fear. The law is light, the profit heavy—how can they escape?

Some may object: “The downfall of Jie of Xia and Zhou of Yin, the ruin of the Lord of Xinling and Emperor Hui of Han—were these due solely to wine? Were not excesses in sensual pleasures also to blame? To cite ancient calamities and impose prohibitions today is like blaming Bao Si for the fall of Zhou and demanding that rulers abandon their six palaces, or attributing the peril of Qin to the Epang Palace and requiring kings to dwell in thatched huts.

Moreover, I have heard that the Azure Heavens display the constellation of the Wine Banner, and the Earth Spirit brought forth the transformation of the hollow mulberry. Sacrificial fires blaze on round mounds, offerings are buried in marshlands, libations and fragrant wine fill the ritual vessels, and spirits descend—wine is part of ritual. A thousand goblets and a hundred cups were the drinking of Yao and Shun. ‘Only with wine is there no limit’—this was Confucius’ capacity. The Duke of Zhou never removed the wine and dishes, and thus he was able to create rites and music. The Han founder danced in drunken revelry, and thus he slew the serpent and rallied his troops. Lord Yu drained a full *hu* and judged cases with greater clarity. Guan Lu downed three *dou* and spoke with brilliant eloquence. Yang Xiong never let wine leave his lips, yet his *Tai Xuan* was completed. Ziyu drank to oblivion, yet his hegemonic achievements were realized. A single jar of unstrained wine was poured, and the three armies rejoiced. The cup that dispels poison was passed, and the horse thieves were moved. It dispels sorrow and perfects rites, records merit and seals covenants, summons spirits and unites men—nothing else can take its place. Within, it hastens the respect due to elders; without, it welcomes honored guests. Like the Huai and Meng rivers, it is prized in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. From this, how can one admonish against it?”

The Master of Plainness replied: “That the Wine Banner is a constellation is true. It is like the celestial bodies that shine brightly—nothing surpasses the sun and moon. The origins of water and fire lie therein. Yet when used with moderation, they nurture life and establish merit; when misused, they burn and drown to death. Can one rely on the celestial bodies in the sky and claim that water and fire do not kill?

The foremost necessity for sustaining life is food; yet overeating causes lumps and masses. How much more so with the poisonous things that are wine and sweet liquor! If Jie of Xia, Zhou of Yin, the Lord of Xinling, and Emperor Hui of Han had been lost in the decadent sounds of ruined states, drowned in the chaos of ravishing beauties, it was all because wine inflamed their natures and drunkenness created the conditions for their excesses. Thus they reached the utmost in reckless indulgence and forgot the art of self-restraint. You focus on the branches, while I address the root. If not for the calamity of wine, whence would these disasters arise?

You know only that a fierce rain soaks your clothes, but not that it arises from the clouds. You fret over flying dust in your eyes, but do not perceive the whirlwind that stirs it. The tales of a thousand goblets and a hundred *hu* are baseless words, impossible deeds—no wise man would believe them. The sages’ distinction lies in their talent and wisdom, not in their physical forms, which are no taller than seven feet or three *zhang*, nor a myriad times larger than others. How could one drink such quantities in a single day?

Confucius feared the alteration of his nature and dared not reach the point of disorder. The Duke of Zhou, though he bowed a hundred times a day, kept his dishes dry and his wine clear. Even the most sagely trembled thus—how much more so ordinary men, who cannot avoid regret?

The Han founder answered Heaven’s call and succeeded to the Mandate of Revolution. Even had he not been drunk, he would still have slain the serpent. Lord Yu was perceptive and clear in his judgments, weighing matters great and small with equity, never erring in right or wrong. Thus his punishments were never excessive, and the world knew no resentful people. His heavy drinking did not impede his duties. But if we speak of great drunkenness, it too leads to oblivion. What use is wine in rendering judgments? I have never heard that Gao Yao, Fu Hou, or Zichan needed to be drunk to decide cases.

Guan Lu was young and rarely engaged in serious discourse, so he relied on wine to bolster his courage. Yet if he exceeded his limit, he too would have been confused. When he discerned the finest details in the lines of hexagrams, sought the transformations of ghosts and spirits, divined fortunes by observing complexions, foretold the future by listening to birdsong, predicted good and ill by watching the winds and clouds, or read omens in the cypress and pine—did he require wine to do so?”

Yang Yun was a man of profound understanding, his talent lofty and thoughts far-reaching. His abundance of brilliance and foresight was endowed by Heaven—how could he rely on external things to aid his writings? When he drank several times, it was merely due to a passing fondness, or perhaps illness that called for the potency of medicine. Ziyu gave free rein to his ambitions, which had long been firmly established; even without intoxication, the outcome would have been the same. The parable of the jar of wine delighting the masses is but a metaphor. If one can truly ensure that rewards and punishments are just, authority and favor aptly bestowed, long-term strategies deployed with flexibility, and responses to circumstances boundless, then warriors will be resolute in thought and men glad to risk their lives. If not, even rivers of wine would do nothing to alter victory or defeat.

Duke Mu of Qin once drank with a thief—such hasty indulgence, forsaking the law to indulge vice, is hardly worthy of praise! How could it compare to caution?

*On Errors and Delusions*, Scroll Twenty-Five

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: As worldly affairs continue unbroken, rites and teachings gradually decline. Reverence and deference are no longer esteemed, while arrogance and insolence become the norm. At gatherings of peers, some squat or sit cross-legged. In the scorching heat of summer, they bare their heads and strip their bodies. Their chief pursuits lie in gambling, tossing dice, or playing *tanqi*. Their discussions never stray from sensual pleasures. They never lift their feet from the side of embroidered silks and fine trousers, nor do their steps ever leave the doors of power-brokers and wine-guzzling guests. They never hear words of pure discourse or Daoist teachings, but instead prioritize coarse jests and mockery. Those who act thus are deemed refined and lofty, while those who do not are dismissed as dull and rustic.

Thus, the reckless commoners and vulgar men of the age, admiring them like moths to a flame or learning from them like light feathers in a whirlwind, rush to emulate their ways. Their jests and taunts may target ancestors above or women below. Those who initiate such talk strive to make it as cutting as possible, while those who respond fear their retorts will not be weighty enough. The instigator does not consider the consequences of retaliation, and the respondent feels ashamed if his words fail to fully avenge the slight. Like reaping Zhou’s millet or harvesting Wen’s wheat, it is all driven by the inability to let go of grudges.

Those with sharp tongues ally with the strong and side with the powerful, while the glib borrow the momentum of their words to strike like a shield’s boss. To fail to respond is deemed clumsy and inferior; to be the first to cease is seen as defeat. In such an environment, how can hostile exchanges ever fall silent?

Those with talent and wit, when engaging in such behavior, are adept at seizing opportunities, modeling their approach on established patterns, drawing parallels between past and present, speaking subtly yet covering all principles, being elegant yet amusing, hitting the mark without causing harm, avoiding what others taboo, and steering clear of what others cherish. But those who are clumsy at it twist right and wrong to fit their ends, leaving others stunned. Whether their words are beautiful or ugly, they are equally inappropriate—how could this bring anything but harm?

Then there are the drunken brawlers, those with unyielding tempers who cannot endure provocation. They fling their robes aside, bare their fists, and come to blows, hurling vile words at their superiors. Joy turns to enmity, friendships rupture, reputations are ruined, and feuds lead to disaster. Some hurl cups and bowls at each other; others expose each other’s secrets. In the past, Chen Ling was struck by an arrow, and the Guan clan was wiped out—not by divine decree, but by the power of words.

The trigger of a crossbow determines honor or disgrace; the warning of the two seals was no lie! A clap of thunder cannot retrieve a careless word, nor can a grindstone erase the stain of a spoken blunder. Though one may not always weigh one’s words thrice before speaking, one can still curb jesting to prevent the sprouting of disaster.

Respect your speech and honor your bearing, ensuring that your words are free of error and your demeanor free of arrogance. Be someone worthy of emulation, observation, awe, and affection—this is the finest method to avoid disgrace and the essential path to preserving friendships. For those who insult others do not love their own kin, and those who engage in reckless quarrels do not value their own bodies. Both are lapses into unfilial conduct—can one not reflect deeply on this?

Yet the deluded lack the clarity to see themselves, and those driven by passion reject advice that grates on their ears. They suffer from beauty’s ailment but lack the acupuncture of forthright counsel; they are a confused multitude without a compass to guide their reflection. Flatterers and sycophants laugh and praise their virtues, while yes-men clap their hands and extol their achievements. This only deepens the delusion of the misguided, who believe themselves to possess Yanzi’s quick wit and surpassing eloquence, never realizing that such behavior is a banner summoning disaster, a talisman beckoning harm, a dispatch from ruin, a chariot hurtling toward self-destruction.

Is this merely about diminishing one’s reputation in the annals of history or tarnishing one’s posthumous renown? Even lifelong prudence cannot undo a single morning’s mistake, nor can the wealth of Tao Zhu compensate for a single misplaced word. A tiny error can lead to a thousand *li* of deviation; a wounding remark can inflict the pain of swords and halberds. Minute faults accumulate into glaring flaws, shallow transgressions deepen into grave offenses. Thus, a swan’s feather can sink a dragon boat, and many light burdens can break a sturdy axle. A gust of wind no wider than a hand can burn a towering mansion, and a tiny termite can topple a tree of immense girth.

Why were the ancients so restrained in speech and so reverent in bearing, while modern men are so reckless, arrogant, and dissolute? Thus, men of noble character turn away at the mere sight of such dust, while the frivolous and shallow flock to it like echoes to sound or shadows to form. Those who plot affairs lack the aid of the wise, and those in peril receive no benefit from mutual counsel. Good historians hold their brushes in vain, for there are no worthy deeds to record; orators purse their lips, for there is no beauty to transmit. Their reputations are unremarkable, their infamy spreads far and wide, leaving behind only ruin and bequeathing ridicule to future generations. They begin with nothing worthy of emulation and end with nothing worthy of remembrance—this is the shame of the aspiring scholar. How can one bear to act thus?

To err and not correct it is to willingly tread the path of thorns and brambles, to forsake sweet nectar and swallow the poison of *gouwen*. Is this not what is meant by deeming small virtues insignificant and thus not practicing them, dismissing small vices as harmless and thus not ceasing them, until evil accumulates beyond concealment and sins grow too great to absolve?

I urge the people of this world to amend their unchecked conduct, to rid themselves of the faults of arrogance and stinginess, to cast off the malady of boastfulness and self-aggrandizement, and to cease all mockery and improper speech. Then the gates of Zhao Sheng shall see no departing guests, and the cudgels of Huang Zu shall find no use.

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* says: Some do not cultivate pure virtue to earn respect but rely on brute force to command fear. When they enter a gathering, they stand stiffly and refuse to sit, vying for the most prominent place. They scowl and raise their voices, forcing others to yield for their own comfort. When displeased, they seethe with resentment and refuse to withdraw. When they travel abroad, they scorn yielding even in narrow paths, whipping their steeds to charge forward and shoving others into peril. Should anyone fail to make way, they heap abuse upon them. Alas, how far this is from the ancients! Those of old, though humble, were never to be surpassed—they yielded shade and made way for others, humbled themselves before scholars, and never contended with the world. They stood as though too frail to bear their robes, walked as though their bodies could scarcely fit through doors. How utterly unlike these modern men!

When virtue flourishes and conduct is pure, even if one humbles oneself profoundly, others still hold one in esteem. But when one’s steps are not lofty, even violent arrogance will not earn respect. If one wears the outward form of a human yet loses the heart of one, they will be despised, not revered. Of old, when Zhuangzi had not yet eaten, King Zhao of Zhao stood at his side; when Zou Yan entered the border, the Lord of Yan swept the ground before him; in the village of Kangcheng, even barbarian invaders bowed from afar; in the courtyard of Lin Zong, none failed to show reverence and awe. This was not submission to force.

To amass wealth through plunder and theft, though one may grow immensely rich, is not worthy of praise. To coerce others through vicious conduct, though one may inspire dread, is not worthy of honor. Yet the common folk still pursue such ways without shame. Those who hear their words recoil as though a vulture’s cry has pierced their ears; those who see their faces shrink as though confronted by a demon’s form. Wherever they go, it is as though a malevolent spirit has descended; when they travel the roads, they are like a pack of tigers. Fools emulate them, priding themselves on their boldness; petty men cite them as stepping stones to tyranny. Disorder knows no bounds—this is its true cause.

Yet those who dare act thus are not necessarily incorrigibly wicked. Most come from families of high rank and powerful connections, having long feigned good deeds to steal hollow reputations. Once their names are vaguely established, their true natures are unleashed. Some bribe the powerful with wealth and beauty, others exploit the times to seize glory and position, still others marry into noble families or manipulate praise and blame to curry favor with those in power. Their vessels overflow, their ambitions swell, their mannerisms grow diseased, their factions expand, and their paths ascend. The judgments of the wise can no longer restrain them, nor can the rule of law rein them in. Thus they become the hawks’ nooses and the temple walls’ rats. Those they have not yet reached, they greet with lowered brows and sweeping obeisance. Those beneath them, they lord over with tyranny and caprice.

Those superior to them dare not be mentioned—even if heard of, they feign ignorance. Those inferior to them dare not speak—even if they do, no prohibition follows. Just as pests that harm the grain perish with the first frost, so too do flatterers and bullies meet their downfall in stern times. For those who would keep their own conduct pure, the only recourse is to refuse to serve them or follow their example. But with an axe and no handle, what can be done?

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* says: The *Book of Songs* praises the *suijiu* bird for its distinction. In the rites, men and women do not meet without a go-between, do not sit together, do not exchange messages, do not share clothing, and may not hand objects directly to one another. When sisters marry and return home, brothers do not sit with them on the same mat. Matters of the outside are not brought in, nor are those of the inside taken out. Women do not go beyond the door to see guests off or welcome them, and when they travel, they must cover their faces. On the road, men keep to the left, women to the right—these are the sagely regulations for emphasizing separation and preventing impropriety at its source.

Even between husband and wife, who may be called intimate, a man does not stay indoors during the day unless ill, nor does he die in a woman’s hands when his end comes—how much more so for others! Of old, the woman of Lu did not seclude herself deeply enough, leading to the scandal of *Taohu*; Kong’s wife did not guard her household closely enough, bringing about Hua Du’s disaster. Shi Ji failed to take precautions, inviting the shame of *Han Zhong*; Wangsun’s lack of strictness led to the humiliation of being barred from his own gate.

Nowadays, common women abandon their silkworm-weaving and spinning, neglect their dark tassels and black ribbons, and cease their hemp-work, reveling instead in the marketplace. They forsake their duties of managing the household, pursuing instead the frivolities of social calls. They visit relatives in droves, setting out by starlight and returning by torchlight, their journeys unceasing. With large retinues of attendants, their splendid processions fill the roads—maidservants, stewards, and foot soldiers mingling like a bustling market. Along the way, they engage in vulgar banter, their behavior detestable and repulsive. Some lodge at others’ gates, others venture out at night and return under cover of darkness. They roam Buddhist temples, watch fishing and hunting, climb heights, gaze upon waters, attend celebrations and funerals beyond their own domains, ride in open carriages with curtains drawn, and wander through cities in disarray. They drink and toast along the road, strum strings and sing as they go, each emulating the other in their pursuit of refinement, turning wrong into custom. They create opportunities for entanglement, refusing nothing. This is the wellspring of licentiousness, an urgent and grave matter. When the primary wife is properly guided, the household and state are set right. I urge all gentlemen to curb and eradicate such practices. Women should have no affairs outside the home—this is how to guard against subtle improprieties.

The *Baopuzi* states: Frivolous men, though their conduct may touch upon lofty and profound matters, form bonds through financial gain, and their reputations and ranks may roughly align, soon turn their backs on ritual and sever teachings, claiming to act with spontaneity. Lacking talent beyond their peers, they force themselves into unbridled abandon, regarding arrogance and lack of restraint as magnanimity, and those who cherish integrity as petty and dull. Thus, during the winter drumming festivals, even the most worthless of men, flushed with wine and warmth, band together in groups, roaming without regard for company. Eminent scholars and learned recluses may be ignored beyond the fence, while reckless behavior knows no bounds—though distant, they will surely arrive, hand in hand, sleeve linked to sleeve, to wander and gather. They enter others’ halls and observe their women, pointing out flaws and virtues, debating beauty and ugliness. What purpose do such men serve? Some, without announcing themselves to the host, barge forward together. If the household is not yet prepared, they pay no heed, breaking through gates, scaling walls, and slipping through cracks like bandits on a raid. If concubines or maidservants hide in haste, they search out secluded corners, dragging them forth—truly monstrous behavior. Even in a gentleman’s own home, he does not conceal his family’s unpreparedness; thus, he announces himself upon entering and lowers his gaze when ascending the hall. How, then, can such intrusion into another’s home be justified?

Yet dissolute men, lacking backbone and eager to follow the crowd, regard such behavior as intimacy and reject those who resist as discourteous. Truly, in this age, one cannot but conform. Thus, they gather in noisy throngs, enter homes to ogle wives, sit knee-to-knee in cramped quarters, pass cups at arm’s length, and play lewd, seductive melodies, their words foreshadowing the stirring of Wenjun’s heart. They howl and clamor, indulging in vulgar and obscene jests, sinking to the depths of depravity. In doing so, they mock the great principles of male and female propriety, trampling the decorum decried in *The Rat*. Jie’s downfall, Zhou’s ruin, the fall of Zhou, and the demise of Chen—all stemmed from a lack of ritual. How much more so for common folk! If trust does not arise from within, repeated oaths are of no avail. When the mind is at ease and the spirit attains its peak, even physical form may be forgotten.

The bonds of the noble are forged through the Dao and virtue, their closeness rooted in shared aspirations—thus, their friendship endures in simplicity. The ties of the petty are bound by power and profit, their intimacy built on familiarity and carelessness—thus, their relationships collapse in sweetness. Why must one gather in private chambers or host feasts with wives and concubines to express sincerity or deepen affection? The ancients, discerning the subtle defenses against licentiousness and stemming the beginnings of waywardness, were truly exemplary. Those who cultivate such principles are gentlemen; those who abandon them are sinners. Yet when prohibitions are lax, even the inner palace harbors thieves who tunnel through walls, and when nets are loose, even in the mulberry groves, women elope in secret. To indulge and unleash such behavior is like setting a raging fire in the Yunmeng Marsh or releasing pent-up waters from a thousand-yard cliff—can it be extinguished with a broom or dammed with a handful of earth?

Yet custom has become habit, and all say: “This is what the nobles and scions of the capital do.” I refute them, saying: The Central States are the wellspring of ritual. How could ritual permit this? Such conduct arises from decline and chaos—it is not the old custom of a well-ordered age.

The Master Laozi, paragon of purity and emptiness, still dared not expose himself to desires lest his heart be thrown into chaos. If even one as steadfast as Liuxia Hui—whose lofty conduct was beyond reproach—were repeatedly subjected to intimate and wanton encounters, could he not but allow passions to stir within and manifest upon his countenance? How much more so for those whose dispositions are but faintly tempered, of whom one in ten thousand may be found, and those who restrain their emotions, who are scarcely to be met with? Such affairs—how can they be deemed enduring?

Though impoverished scholars may recognize that these customs are unworthy of emulation, lacking both renown and influence, how can they rectify them? I have often lamented this, and thus earned the enmity of such factions, who deride me as a rustic and uncouth man, incapable of adapting to the times. Yet I remain true to myself—how could I, for the sake of conformity, follow what others deem acceptable when I know it is not?

Alas, such grievances are not few, and so it has ever been. Enough! What can I do? Those who have been corrupted by depraved customs and sunk into ruin—how could they heed candid words that grate upon the ear, or retrace their steps from paths so far astray?

The *Baopuzi* says: Among the common folk, there exists a vulgar practice of humiliating women—amidst crowds and before kin, they are assailed with lewd questions and forced into shameless replies. Such indecency is too vile to recount. Some are flogged until they wince, others bound and hung upside down. Drunken revelers, unrestrained in their excess, may even cause bloodshed, or leave their victims with broken limbs and maimed bodies. How lamentable!

The ancients, moved by parting, would not extinguish the lamp; grieving the loss of kin, they would not perform music. By ritual, the bride was received with shame, not celebration. Yet today, unable to uphold these ancient norms, even those whose virtue commands the respect of their village, whose words are trusted by scholars, ought to sternly rebuke such conduct. Why, then, do they instead drift with the current, perpetuating these corrupt customs?

For these practices have taken root among the people for so long that none perceive their wrongness. Even earnest discourse cannot curb them; only severe punishment could halt their spread. Thus, they disparage the rites of Zhou and find fault with Confucius, calling defiance and detachment "transcendence of the world."

Some, seizing advantage in times of upheaval, usurp honor and rank; others, relying on powerful patrons, soar above the rest. Thereupon, they grow haughty and boastful, their qi towering over all things. Striding with lofty steps and distant gaze, they regard themselves with disdain, looking down upon those who lag behind or lose their way. Though these may be truly exceptional, they are dismissed as mere weeds.

Some recline to receive guests; others feign illness to turn them away. They demand that scholars stand at their gates like a forest, that cavalry choke the lanes, all crying, "This is what the noble and exalted must do!" Yet by the measure of power and rank, the Duke of Zhou was diligent in receiving and grasping hands; by the standard of renown, Confucius was gentle and skillful in instruction. Both made humility and toil their duty, never prizing arrogance and indolence.

By the late Han, however, all had changed. Hair disheveled, sashes undone, some received guests in disheveled robes, others sat bare-chested with legs splayed. At gatherings of friends or feasts of connoisseurs, none earnestly cultivated virtue, diligently refined their conduct, corrected faults, or admonished transgressions. They did not expound the Dao or delve into its profound principles.

When they met, they no longer inquired after long partings or asked after well-being. Guests entered and called for servants; hosts, spying visitors, summoned their dogs. Those who did not act thus were deemed unworthy of intimacy, cast aside and excluded from their circles. At their revels, they crouched like foxes and drank like oxen, vying for food and tearing at meat. They wrested and snatched, stripped and broke, utterly devoid of shame. To conform was deemed excellence; to refuse, inferiority.

All day, not a word of moral substance; all night, no benefit of admonition. They falsely invoked Laozi and Zhuangzi, prizing unbridled spontaneity. "The great man disregards petty rites; the perfected one is unbound by restraints." Whistling and roaming freely, they called it embodying the Dao.

Alas! How sorrowful! Is this not a tragedy? Thus, they mocked and ridiculed to foster camaraderie, indulged in excess to forge bonds of intimacy.

Those who loll and stretch their legs are hailed as enchanting paragons of beauty, while those of upright and solemn demeanor are dismissed as rustic plow-horses. Those who gesticulate wildly with raised fingers are praised for their vigor and fresh allure, while those whose speech is measured and articulate are condemned as abrupt and brash. Such frivolous and fickle men, though they flatter and fawn to curry favor, though they amass followers and form cliques, though they laud one another to hasten their rise—all are but sleek and cunning on the surface, hollow and empty within. They resemble the gourd-vessel of the Shu people: their breasts hold not a single page of learning, and what they know extends no further than wine and roasted meat. These are the ones whom the *Book of Documents* calls "arrogant and cruel, scorning bright virtue," who "follow the deaf and blind," who "covet wealth and glut themselves on food"—the "worthless sons" recorded by Zuo Qiuming.

Should one inquire of them concerning the profound words of the *Canon* and *Index*, the nature of ghosts and spirits, the transformations of the myriad things, the wonders of distant lands, the grand rites of court and ancestral temple, the protocols of suburban sacrifices and grand offerings, the origins of the Three Norms and Four Beginnings, the principles of yin-yang and the measures of pitch-pipes and calendars, the standards of military and state, or the continuities and changes of antiquity and the present—they would grow flustered and dismayed, mute and stammering, bewildered and lost. Though inwardly they sense their ignorance as if facing a wall, outwardly they conceal their deficiencies, refusing to quiet themselves. Instead, they forcefully expound at length, declaring: "Trivial anecdotes and fragmentary lore are the province of pedantic scholars from back alleys, who chant over withered bamboo slips and grovel to guard their yellowed scrolls. Such things are unworthy of our concern."

They know full well the harm of ignorance and the value of great scholars; they recognize the error of their own ways and the folly of their disdain. Yet they persist in their delusion and do not turn back, for their wantonness brings no hindrance to their advancement. Were a man of lofty virtue to rebuke them with stern admonitions, they would not fear authority nor cease their wickedness. But should their names and ranks be demoted, they would surely tremble like melting ice, reform their ways, and flee like those who turn eastward in haste.

**Mockery and Delusion, Chapter Twenty-Six**
*Master Embracing Simplicity says:*
When clarity and turbidity first parted and the myriad things took form, even the beasts of the field could respond with understanding, and the creatures with fur could speak with wisdom. The ape knows the past; the tortoise foresees the end. The dark-feathered bird grasps yin and yang; the worm, though a worm, is not far from the flowing spring. Neither milfoil nor tortoiseshell surpass it; Gan and Shi cannot rival it.

Yet because man lacks innate propriety, in the remotest antiquity, the people knew no hierarchy. The Lord on High lamented their crude confusion and pitied their wretched dwellings in nests and caves. Thus, He raised halls and houses to separate them from the flocks of birds and beasts, and established rites and ranks to distinguish the degrees of dignity. He taught them the art of measured movement, instructed them in the grace of yielding, so that standing, they bent like a chime-stone; bowing, they clasped their hands like a drum. The rhythms of advancing and retreating, ascending and descending, the bearing of gaze and response—these numbered three thousand. Such are the dikes that restrain excess, the urgent necessities of human order.

Thus, solemnity crowns the *Records of Propriety*; demeanor leads the Five Constants. Stepping out, one meets guests with reverence; at rest, one heeds the caution of solitary vigilance. Yan Hui prepared his bearing with nocturnal ablutions; Zhong You, facing death, tied his cap-strings. Should respectful composure lapse even briefly, indolence and sloth follow. To pacify the realm and govern the people, nothing suffices but this.

Man’s possession of rites is like a fish’s possession of water. Though a fish may briefly survive without water, its desiccation and ruin are certain. Though a man who abandons rites may yet appear whole, he stands upon the threshold of disaster. Lu, upholding the Zhou rites, repelled violent armies; Wei, honoring Ganku, turned back the sharpest helmets. Mighty Chu, with a million armored troops, knew the chill of withered stalks in moonlight; fierce Qin, with its impregnable passes, lacked the steadfastness of a willow’s bend. Was this not the consequence of forsaking the Three Roots and severing the very foundation?

How much worse in times of ease and indulgence, when disorder has endured long, when the winds of teaching have withered and the pages of instruction decayed, when the rites of restraint have fallen away and the customs of carelessness have taken hold! The common people are ensnared by corrupt governance; the mediocre are entangled in the broken net of the Dao. They are like fish escaping the watery net, like beasts breaking free of the land’s snare.

Since the onset of chaos, affairs and things have changed repeatedly. Hats and shoes, robes and sleeves—all have altered in cut and measure, shifting with each day and month, never fixed. Now long, now short; now wide, now narrow; now high, now low; now coarse, now fine—their adornments are ever inconstant, delighting in uniformity. The eager imitators change morning and night, as the saying goes: "In the capital, they prize full brows; in distant lands, all half their foreheads." I, a mere mortal, am clumsy at following custom. My attire and belongings, though often urged to change, remain unaltered, for nothing is lost thereby. Though I am mocked and ridiculed, I pay no heed. It is not that I stubbornly defy the crowd, but truly, such things are not urgent.

The Central States excel in many matters, yet some are not to be emulated. The noble man practices rites without seeking to alter customs, for even in a foreign land, he does not abandon the ways of his homeland. How much less should one, in the land of one’s parents, cast aside the old and forcibly adopt new ways!

As for Wu’s mastery of calligraphy, there are Huang Xiang, Liu Zuan, Cen Boran, and Zhu Jiping—all unrivaled in their generation. Like Zhong Yu of the Central States, Hu Kongming, Zhang Zhi, and Suo Jing—each a master in their own realm—they employ ancient styles, each sufficient for their purpose. I say: to abandon a familiar method and toil anew to learn the script of the Central States is unnecessary. How much more so to alter one’s speech to mimic northern accents, achieving neither grace nor skill, inviting only shame and mockery—like one who fails to learn the steps of Handan and earns only the scorn of crawling!

This is but a minor matter. Yet there are those who, in mourning, imitate the wailing of the Central States, suddenly bereft of all genuine grief.

Of old, Zhong Yi and Zhuang Xi never forgot their native accents—this the ancients praised. Confucius said: "One who mourns a parent is like an infant who has lost its mother." What regulated tones could such wailing possess? Better that grief overflow while ritual falls short, that weeping vent sorrow—what place is there for artifice or clumsiness? Yet some refine and adorn their voices, which is no true expression of anguish.

I have heard that when men of high station are in deep mourning, some fall ill, yet take mineral powders with every meal to stimulate the drug’s potency, and drink wine as if it were life itself. When illness grows grave and they cannot endure the wind’s chill, they retreat behind curtains and layered bedding, letting comfort dictate their rest. Thus even petty men of means no longer remain in the mourning chamber, but stay in separate quarters, on lofty beds with thick quilts, eating fine foods and drinking deeply. Some even entertain close companions, draining cups and tossing them aside until they sink into drunkenness, saying, "This is the way of the capital!" How lamentable!

In my hometown, men of former virtue faced great sorrow—some even bore it in old age. Though ritual required only sackcloth and hemp, and they were not expected to waste away in grief, all still mourned beyond measure, taking only gruel, never tasting sweetness. Even the unworthy of the time strove to emulate them. Yet now men choose such conduct for themselves—how vast the distance between then and now!

Moreover, some ignorant people claim that those in the Central States who observe mourning are mostly extravagant. This is far from true. I have heard that the four emperors of Jin—Xuan, Jing, Wen, and Wu—all wasted away beyond ritual bounds in mourning their parents. They did not follow the Wang clan’s twenty-five-month observance, but wore mourning for only seven months. At the time, all under heaven who faced deep sorrow took these four emperors as their model. How is it that people today remain unaware of this, yet falsely slander the noble? Are they not deluded?

**Censure of Arrogance (Chapter 27)**
*Baopuzi says:* Born into a family of wealth and prestige, basking in the blaze of power, most men do not seek arrogance—yet arrogance comes of its own accord. Only those of surpassing capacity can avoid the excess of overweening pride.

For humility and self-effacement draw many to one’s side, while arrogance and haughtiness drive them away. Many drawn near bring the blessing of stability; many driven off foretell danger. The pivot of survival or ruin lies here. To treat it lightly is indeed blindness!

Some rise from lowly origins, advancing from obscurity to prominence, merely by hunching their shoulders, shrinking their steps, bowing low, and bending their knees to ingratiate themselves with the powerful. Through connections and opportunity, they leap ranks, their feathers grown and wings spread, shedding their former selves like cicadas molting from the earth. Suddenly they strut, eyes fixed above, steps never touching the ground, full of self-satisfaction, regarding others as mere dust. In private gatherings, music and revelry fill the air, guests crowd the gates, yet they no longer deign to receive them. Or among those who seek their favor, they may single out a few, though not necessarily the most worthy. They merely respond to those who pursue them most diligently, whose gifts arrive in an unbroken stream, whose flasks and jugs are never empty. This is what Mencius called "loving without respecting"—treating others like livestock. Yet many practice this, claiming it is the way of self-respect.

The way of self-respect lies in humbling oneself before the lowly and cultivating modesty—not this! Such is the corrupt custom of a declining age, a chronic malady of the heart. That so many embrace it is truly pitiable.

As for men of greatness and vast capacity, whose measure is boundless and spirit far-reaching, who tread high paths alone, serene and self-contained—whose bodies dwell amid the world’s currents, yet whose spirits soar beyond the nine heavens, whose inner Way is full while all else is cast aside, whose caps are tattered and sandals broken, whose robes are patched and belts frayed—how could they deign to compete with common men in seeking petty advantages, or cultivate the flattering demeanor of sycophants? How could they imitate the babbling miser of the Shanglin Park or become the buzzing flies and summer cicadas that grate on the ear?

If judged by appearance, measured by superficial beauty, the vulgar see only their rough and simple exteriors, unable to perceive the depth and vastness of their spirits. They focus on the surface, never considering the heart and mind. Though such men harbor exceptional talents and surpass the age, though their actions could enlighten the world’s ways and their words resolve the doubts of past and present, though they contain brilliance within and speak only of the Dao, their thatched huts cannot convey the resonance of their mighty voices, nor can dwarves appreciate the grandeur of towering peaks. Thus the world scorns them as ordinary. Yet without the people of the Han River’s banks, who could recognize a pearl in the mouth of a mud-dwelling clam? Without those who weep blood, who could discern the radiance of luminous jade buried in dark cliffs?

In the swarms of moths and midges, men mock the vast Peng that soars across the heavens; the inch-long minnow swimming in ox-hoof puddles scorns the great scales that traverse the ocean. Thus their paths and practices cannot intersect, nor can their wisdom and insight reach one another. The principle is inherently discordant—no cause for surprise.

Yet those who revile them cannot remain silent, for they wish that gentlemen in office would not judge men by appearance, but instead encourage humility and self-restraint, thereby preserving the celestial order.

The *Baopuzi* says: The world hears of Dai Shuluan and Ruan Sizong, who defied convention and roamed freely, and calls them men of great tolerance, without measuring whether their talents and strength matched the boldness of their lives—yet many emulate them. Some dishevel their hair and go bareheaded, some strip and squat like barbarians, some wash their feet in crowds, some relieve themselves before others, some eat alone while guests wait, some pour wine only for their intimates. These are the deeds of those who wear their robes left-lapped, not the refined customs of the Central States. Even with the learning and talent of Dai and Ruan, their indulgence and arrogance brought them harm, and their gains and losses scarcely balanced. Had these two men conducted themselves with reverence and restraint, treating others with earnestness and managing affairs with diligence, how far might they have risen—yet they did not! How much more perilous for those far beneath them who imitate their ways: swift calamity and mortal danger will not wait for the turning of a shadow. What is at stake is not merely failing to be esteemed for one’s pure virtue!

Once, Xishi lay ill by the roadside, her beauty radiant, her fragrance like orchid and musk. Those who saw her admired her loveliness and pitied her suffering; none could pass without lingering. Then a neighboring woman, envying her, feigned illness and lay in the same place. But her appearance was ugly, and her stench unbearable. Passersby loathed her face and reviled her odor, turning away to cover their noses and hurrying past. Today, men who lack the natural gifts of Dai and Ruan yet mimic their arrogance are no different from that ugly woman who failed to know herself.

Even emperors observe the rites of younger kin when addressing the Three Elders and Five More Venerables, leading the people by example in reverence. A man without propriety is deeply flawed. He who treats others with contempt will not honor his own kin. If one wishes to be respected, one must first show respect. To neglect good deeds is to become wicked; to disregard great matters is to become base. Zhou, though lawless, was called the "Lone Man"; Confucius, though a minister’s minister, was named the "Uncrowned King." Thus the noble man does not depend on wealth and rank.

Now, to commit acts of impropriety yet resent hearing reproaches of sudden death is like a man carrying a pig who hates being told of its stench, or one who throws himself into mud yet resents being called filthy. Long ago, Xin You saw men with unbound hair making sacrifices and knew the Rong would soon flourish. I observe that in the era of decay and disorder, the customs exalted arrogance and disrespect, and the Yi and Di acted as they pleased. Later, the Qiang and Hu invaded and plundered the capital—only then did men realize that these were the omens of earlier portents.

Today, the world trends toward peace, and signs of revival appear. How can we not together correct past errors and cultivate the beauty of solemn dignity? Only when among tigers and wolves does one recognize the valor of Meng Ben and Xia Yu; only in a society where rites have collapsed does one appreciate the constancy of the refined. As the Dao and its teachings decline and degenerate, and the current flows ever onward, scholars and gentlemen should grieve together. They must examine their hearts, share a common resolve, and rectify what is crooked. If their strength is insufficient, so be it—but at the very least, they should emulate the bamboo and pine, whose integrity remains unchanged even in the cold of winter. How can they instead rush headlong to compete with the drunken and frivolous, seeking favor with such men? The distance from the Way grows ever greater—this is truly cause for bitter lament.

Yet some stand tall in their integrity, steadfast and unyielding, neither swaying with the crowd like tumbleweed nor altering their refinement to adopt the vulgar. Though none can hate them and all recognize their goodness, those who differ from themselves treat them as enemies and hold them in contempt. Alas! Can such corruption truly be the way of things? The noble man can maintain his lofty integrity, unswayed by the crowd, stirring clear waves to rouse the turbid current, wielding a sturdy arrow to correct the crooked. He may not be tolerated or gain wealth and rank, but if the celestial nobility remains within his person, then to stand alone and unrecognized—what hardship or regret is there in that? Instead, to sever one’s roots and patch together disparate parts, to feed on dregs and grasp at mud, to cut one’s feet to fit the shoes, to destroy the square to fit the round—is this not extreme?

The man of principle cannot compel others to respect him, yet his resolve cannot be taken; he cannot prevent others from hating him, yet his Dao cannot be bent; he cannot stop others from insulting him, yet his honor remains his own; he cannot prevent others from rejecting him, yet his conduct cannot be altered. Thus, with his course set and his mind made up, neither encouragement nor discouragement can sway him. Content with Heaven and knowing his fate, neither sorrow nor fear can enter. Hardship only strengthens him; adversity does not make him regret. One who truly applies his heart in this way—how could he ever bow like grass before the wind, seek ill-fitting joints, or imitate the deeds of those rejected by propriety?

The *Baopuzi* says: I have heard that in the late Han, men of no conduct ranked and graded one another, and the proud and arrogant who ignored the Dao and discipline were hailed as leaders and heroes, "well-connected in all directions." All turned their backs on ritual and teaching, indulging in perversity and depravity, slandering the upright, wounding the innocent, speaking foul words, and committing shameful deeds. Their every action was too vile to recount.

The ancients spoke of "understanding" as comprehending the Dao and virtue, and "attainment" as mastering benevolence and righteousness. How could they mean understanding debauchery and attaining depravity? It is like Robber Zhi claiming to possess five of the Sage’s virtues. Such customs tear at the fabric of human relations more severely than bandits and thieves, and their damage cannot last long—how could it be merely a matter of ruining one’s reputation?

As for the scions of noble houses and men in office who disregard the laws and appear bareheaded and half-naked before guests, not only do they disgrace their high positions, but they also corrupt the common people. Later generations, seeing that some of these men have already gained high rank or stolen empty fame, imitate their behavior. The vulgar then believe that this is how one establishes oneself in the world—nothing is more admirable than this!

Those who uphold ritual restraints find it bitter and arduous, and most of them remain poor and lowly; those who indulge in arrogance and abandon find it joyous and easy, and all who do so swiftly rise to prominence. Thus, the common people cast aside the former and embrace the latter. In the world, there are those who, from youth, lack pure and unsullied conduct, yet in adulthood gain wealth and nobility through the purchase of office. Some possess knowledge sufficient to adorn themselves, or factions sufficient to promote one another. The dissolute then point to them as proof, saying: *"They gave free rein to their passions and desires yet suffered no hindrance to their brilliance, while those who disciplined themselves and walked the Way could not escape poverty and lowliness."* They do not realize that those who achieve glory and prominence do so by fortune, while those who suddenly fall do so by misfortune—neither outcome stems from their conduct.

Yet those so-called "thoroughfares in all eight directions" attract flatterers who cling to them. Their shoes are not yet tied, their belts not yet fastened, yet they are already led by the hand into the hall, entering the chamber sleeve to sleeve. When they go out, they sit knee to knee; when they request a meeting, their wish is immediately granted. What they bestow is abundant, what they entrust is always heeded, what they desire is invariably fulfilled, what they say is always indulged. If they face trouble, they are rescued; if they recommend someone, even a lame donkey commands the price of a dragon steed; if they slander someone, even a filial son like Zeng Shen is maligned like Prince Shangchen. Thus, the rush of petty men toward such figures is like breaching a thousand-fathom dike to release pent-up waters, or setting ablaze the withered grasses of Yunmeng. To hope for solemn order and reverent conduct, or that the young will have worthy models, is like scorching ice to dry it or piling ashes to kindle flame.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: The Hundred Li**
*Baopuzi* says: The Three Ducal Ministers and the Nine Chamberlains sit and discourse on the Dao; the governors of provinces and commanderies grasp the net and lift its cords. The higher their office, the lighter their duties—except for the burdens that converge upon the hundred *li*. All corvée labor issues from this office, and all exactions rush toward it. Even if a governor or prefect is worthy, if the magistrate of the hundred *li* is incapable, state affairs will not be carried out, the ten thousand mechanisms will falter, and the damage will not be confined to a single region. Thus, the magistrate of the hundred *li* must be especially capable—more urgently so than the officials of the central courts.

If the wrong person is appointed, the reason is no other than this: the spirit of utmost impartiality is not put into practice, and the intent to favor private interests is not abandoned. Some are the sons or younger brothers of the powerful, appointed on the strength of their family’s reputation; some are recommended by influential figures, yet mediocrities are promoted for their incompetence; some are long-held favorites, or relatives with no other connection. Those who know such men are unfit still appoint them. Occasionally, there may be a competent one, for not all are entirely unworthy—but the majority are unrefined. Few are those who strive to prove themselves, diligently cultivate frugality and integrity, labor day and night in service of the public, seek the people’s praise, fear that their achievements will not be fine, and feel shame if their patrons’ judgment is mistaken.

The mediocre and base, with their petty capacities and narrow ambitions, are obsessed with bribes and wealth, seeking only to enrich themselves. They indulge their passions and desires without restraint or limit. The officials who oversee them, knowing their rapacity, rely on their superiors to impeach and expose them. Yet when the time comes for their removal, fearing they will make enemies, they instead suffer slander and dare not offend them, allowing their greed and cruelty to run unchecked. In this way, how can the common people avoid suffering and division? When the divided grow numerous, they cannot but gather and become bandits.

A hall a hundred *xun* in height burns from a spark the size of a fingertip; a thousand-*zhang* embankment collapses from the hole of a single ant. How can one not guard against this deeply? How can one not reform the system?

Those who wield the scales may abandon the balance and act on whim; those who grasp the axe may bend the inked line to favor their own. Those who select officials do not choose men for office, while those who seek office never admit their own unfitness. Thus, when they assume governance, governance falls into neglect; when they shepherd the people, the people scatter. Some are filthy, arrogant, and extravagant, oppressing the common folk; some are harsh, cruel, and brutal, breeding resentment and division. Some are dull, blocked, and withdrawn, throwing all affairs into chaos; some are slovenly, careless, and sluggish, leading to decay and ruin. Some delight in launching unnecessary projects, exhausting the people’s strength; some harbor fugitives and act with violence and tyranny. Some, ignorant of laws and decrees, are deceived and manipulated; some, indulging in music, wine, and sensual pleasures, fall into dissipation. Some abandon governance for *weiqi* and *chupu*; some forget their duties in hunting, feasting, and revelry; some neglect lawsuits and petitions, throwing the penal system into disorder.

The people, unable to endure, rise as bandits and rebels. When their crimes are exposed and reported, they are cast into the thorny prison, tarnishing the sovereign’s clarity and multiplying the complexity of the penal code. The suffering of the people has already run deep!

Appointing the wrong person is like harnessing a wooden horse with ornate reins—how can it gallop to chase the wind or trample dragons in the clouds? How can it shine its radiance on the celestial thoroughfare? If those who hold the reins of state and issue the sovereign’s decrees scrutinize the glance of a true remedy, do not allow the lame and stumbling to mingle with the swift *qilu* horses, and do not permit the reckless and unthinking—those blind to their own limits—to stumble midway, nor yoke the weak and inferior to the phoenix’s crossbar, then what worry is there that the myriad affairs will not flourish? What fear is there that the Four Fiends will not retreat? The Three August Ones would not be surpassed by four, nor the Five Emperors by six!

*Jie Shu*, Scroll 29
*Baopuzi* says: When the exceptional encounter great illumination, the shade of the mulberry has not yet shifted, yet the bond of golden orchids is already firm. When great talent meets profound insight, there is no need to await trial—mutual understanding is already clear. Soaring like a swan riding a mighty wind, fluttering like a dragon scaling startled clouds. If the suppressed could be ignored, then Jiang Gong would not have served the Zhou; if the distant and lowly could be rejected, then Mao Sheng would not have been valued by Zhao. If only those with long-standing virtue were entrusted with governance, then Ning Qi would not have risen in Qi; if only those with established reputations were appointed, then Chen Ping and Han Xin would not have been employed by Han. The wise overlook minor flaws, neither envious nor covetous, and thus establish authority, secure achievements, and bring balance to heaven and earth. How could they deign to weigh firewood before cooking or count grains before boiling rice? To discard the jade because of a blemish or pluck feathers to seek flaws—how absurd!

*Jun Shi*, Scroll 30
Someone said: "The authors of ancient texts were of great talent and profound thought, so their writings are subtle and difficult to understand. People today are shallow in intent and limited in strength, so their works are overt and easy to grasp. Comparing this ease to that obscurity is like comparing ditches and streams to the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, or molehills and ant mounds to Mount Song and Mount Tai. If water does not issue from the Kunlun Mountains, it cannot surge into mighty currents and flow eastward; if writings do not come from the exceptional, they cannot achieve the far-reaching resonance of great harmony."

Gezhi Zi replied: "Those who discuss mere burrows cannot be questioned about the boundless Nine Heavens; those accustomed to confinement cannot be urged to surpassing insight. The scholars of antiquity were neither ghosts nor spirits—their forms may have perished long ago, yet their spirits remain spread across bamboo and silk. Their emotions manifest in their words, and their intent may be discerned. Moreover, the obscurity of ancient texts does not necessarily mean the ancients deliberately sought to make them difficult to understand. Some arose from changes in language over time, or differences in regional speech. With the passage of ages, wars, and chaos, texts were buried and lost, their bamboo slips rotting away, leaving many gaps. Some were pieced together from fragments, others had entire passages missing—thus their difficulty, which only *seems* profound.

Take the *Book of Documents*: it is a collection of governance, yet it does not match the refined elegance and richness of modern imperial edicts, military dispatches, or memorials. The *Mao Odes* are florid in expression, yet they do not compare to the vast, sweeping grandeur of the *Shanglin*, *Yulie*, *Two Capitals*, or *Three Metropolises*. Yet ancient philosophical works surpass modern ones—why? Because those who cling to the past, mumbling over their texts with ears but no eyes, would never admit it. They deem the ancients’ works divine and modern writings shallow, prizing the distant and scorning the near—this bias has long existed.

Thus, a new sword gains value from fraudulent engravings, and a worn prescription becomes treasured through false attribution. Ancient texts, though plain, are called ‘heavenly’ by vulgar scholars; modern works, though like gold and jade, are dismissed as mere rubble. Ancient texts, though numerous, are not all flawless—they should serve as mountains and seas for scholars, allowing writers to quarry and hunt within them. Yet like the forests of Dong’ou or the groves of Changzhou, though rich in catalpa and elm, they cannot compare to the grandeur of a great hall or the splendor of a magnificent palace. Or like the marshes of Yunmeng and Mengzhu, teeming with fish and game, yet not equal to the sumptuous feasts of *Jianhuo* or the exquisite flavors of *Yudi*.

Ancient and modern poems both contain meaning, yet differ in beauty. Like scholars, all may possess virtue, but one may excel in literature—this does not mean they are the same. Like women, all may be of peerless beauty, yet one may master a hundred arts—this does not make them identical. If we compare writings on palaces, how does the *Song of Xisi’s Audience Hall* compare to Wang Sheng’s *Rhapsody on the Lingguang Palace*? If we speak of hunting, how do the poems of Shu Tun and Lu Ling compare to Sima Xiangru’s *Shanglin Fu*? If we praise sacrifices, how do the *Qingmiao* and *Yunhan* compare to Guo’s *Southern Suburban Hymns*? If we discuss campaigns, how do the *Chuche* and *Liuyue* compare to Chen Lin’s *Military Dispatch*? From these examples, the pattern becomes clear.

Recently, Xiahou Zhan and Pan Anren both composed *Supplementing the Lost Odes*—*White Flowers*, *Southern Shore*, *Hua She*, and others. Eminent scholars and literary connoisseurs all agree that the *Three Hundred Poems* of old cannot match the works of these two masters.

Moreover, the ancients lived in simplicity, while today all things are adorned—this is the natural course of changing times. Brocade, though ornate, is stronger than straw raincoats; carriages, though elegant, are sturdier than crude carts. Writing is like speech: if one speaks in dialect, even those from the same land may not understand, let alone those from distant regions. If such words were used for instruction, who would comprehend them? If clarity were the measure of eloquence, why would texts be praised for their difficulty? Just as boats and carriages replaced wading and walking, and writing replaced knotted cords, later inventions surpass earlier ones by ten thousandfold—this is too vast to enumerate. The world already knows this and rejoices in progress, so why should literature alone lag behind antiquity?"

*On Reducing Burdens*, Scroll 31

Gezhi Zi said: "For governing the people and stabilizing the state, nothing surpasses ritual—it encompasses human affairs in exhaustive detail. Yet why must the rites of caps, marriages, banquets, and archery be so excessively intricate? Though human relations are rightly valued through ritual, it should suffice to distinguish rank and express respect. Why insist on endless bowing, rising, and prostrations?

In earlier times, when the realm was at peace and the four quarters tranquil, officials fond of antiquity sometimes revived these rites. They would lecture and test for months, enforcing discipline with the rod, demanding day and night practice at the expense of sleep and meals. After years of study, they would be tested in a single day—scroll in hand, following the text in every gesture. Mistakes brought demotion, yet errors still occurred, and the true meaning remained elusive. To make this the constant practice of the people would be nearly impossible. This is what Mozi meant when he said, ‘Generations cannot exhaust its study, nor a single year complete its mastery.’

The ancients consulted even the words of woodcutters and children’s rhymes, selecting from the sayings of madmen. Mozi’s arguments cannot be dismissed outright. Though he overemphasized punishments, opened paths, and exhaustively detailed governance, he did not elaborate in full. Yet his critiques of lavish burials and excessive ritual cannot be discarded. After the Jian’an era, the rulers of Wei—Cao Cao and Cao Pi—enacted frugal funeral rites, which align with Mozi’s principles and are worth adopting.

Now that chaos has subsided and the court and countryside are at ease, with the ruler following the ancients, I propose appointing learned and widely versed scholars, capable of discernment and free from rigid folly, to revise the *Three Ritual Classics*. They should excise what is unnecessary, trace the origins of each rite, consolidate their purposes, and categorize them systematically."

Let those verbose and repetitive persuasions—where words differ but meanings align—be preserved, while those impractical for regular practice be removed. Where no harm is done, condense and simplify, lest they sink into obscurity or stagnate unresolved.

For ceremonial implements—such as *zu*, *dou*, *gu*, and *zhi*—and for the regulations of robes, caps, carriages, and attire; the distinctions of banners and colors; the gradations of palaces and halls; the protocols of court audiences and guest receptions; the variations of sacrifices, mourning, and burials; the rites of suburban sacrifices, *di*, and *xia*; and the ceremonies for altars of soil and grain and mountains and rivers—all may be streamlined to ensure frugality. For frugality eases compliance, and simplicity reduces expenditure; ease of compliance prevents vexation, and minimal cost lightens burdens. Without vexation, those who administer affairs commit no errors; with lightened burdens, those who levy taxes impose no harshness.

The gestures of bowing, resting, and yielding; the postures of ascending, descending, and circling—let these suffice to convey matters without fragmentation. Arrange each category separately for ease of reference.

Today, the *Five Rituals* are entangled, adorned with杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余杂余, their branches scattered and leaves dispersed, with repetitions and cross-references. Even seasoned scholars, when consulting the old texts, often falter; disputes and objections proliferate endlessly, with divergent interpretations and competing explanations growing year by year. Without the keenest discernment, none escape confusion. Hesitating at the crossroads of doubt, laboring in the thickets of uncertainty, they exhaust their spirits and drain their thoughts, collating and adjudicating precedents, sometimes toiling for years without resolution. The toil of governance is arduous, and the space for resolving doubts is nonexistent. They groan as they parse, wither as they debate, their temples graying before their work is done, wasting days and months, neglecting other pursuits, and exhausting the young—truly endless.

Exegeses now outnumber the original texts. If we were to harmonize with prevailing customs, order them like millet and wheat, excise the nonessential, elevate the essential principles, and refine their decrees, they would shine as brightly as the sun and moon, as distinctly as the five colors. This would spare scholars ten thousandfold labor and quell the disputes of the learned. Future generations, looking back, would praise it as we now praise the Zhou.

This is akin to abandoning stone-boiling for cooking or blood sacrifices for sustenance—nothing to fear, yet some cling to old attachments, as if reluctant to leave cave dwellings. Yet when those wedded to convention hear this, they will surely startle, calling it the ravings of a madman.

The Three Kings did not inherit one another’s music, nor did the Five Emperors replicate one another’s rites, yet their transformations of customs and governance of the people achieved the same ends. Some reforms, some continuities—each adjusted and improved as needed. Why must one ride a boat to climb a mountain, spur a horse to cross a river, don armor to ascend the ancestral temple, or wear heavy furs in sweltering heat?

If ancient practices were truly immutable, then coffins should never have replaced burial in firewood, nor should clothing have supplanted nakedness.

*Extensive Learning*, Scroll Thirty-Two
The *Master Embracing Simplicity* says: The orthodox classics are the deep sea of the Dao and virtue; the masters’ writings are the rivers that deepen them.

Looking up to compare them, they are like the bright stars that assist the Three Luminaries; looking down to liken them, they are like the thickets and undergrowth that complement Mount Song. Though their paths diverge, they converge in the cultivation of virtue; though their steps may differ, they unite in advancing transformation. Thus, the master of comprehensive learning traces origins to encompass branches, grasping the guiding threads to attain unity.

The ancients sighed over the scarcity of talent, saying that a hundred generations follow in one another’s footsteps. Yet they did not discard the luminous jewel of the night because its ore was not from Kun Mountain, nor did they dismiss instructive words because their authors were not sages.
