Thus, clumsy poems from the lanes and alleys, military oaths from the ranks—though their language be coarse and their metaphors crude, though their length not exceed ten lines—were still compiled and ranked alongside the canonical pronouncements. The words of the Hundred Schools share the same measure of goodness. It is like those who carry water: though their vessels differ, they all serve to extinguish fire. It is like acupuncture and moxibustion: though their techniques vary, they equally attack illness.

Since the Han and Wei dynasties, the profusion of writings has grown ever more vast. Though their meaning delves into the abyss of mystery and their diction surges like waves, their application can bring auspicious omens from the heavens, evoke propitious signs from the earth, summon ring-necked pheasants from beyond the wilds, and secure the realm within the Four Seas. They quell the proximate causes of calamity and disorder, and distantly bequeath blessings for ages to come. Yet the age lacks sages to discern their eloquence, so they cannot gallop the tracks of swift steeds across a thousand *li*, nor append the Dao of recent times to the end of the Three Canons.

Those bound by narrow constraints, shackled within the fetters of shallow learning, clinging to the teachings of the *qieping*—they scorn the extraordinary and despise the unfamiliar, deeming them unworthy of attention. Some say minor teachings are unworthy of study; others claim that broad learning confuses the mind. They fail to see that the accumulation of minute grains can equal the weight of mountains, that the gathering of hundreds and tens can reach the sum of billions and trillions. When many colors converge, the imperial robes shine resplendent; when myriad sounds blend, the *Shao* and *Hu* harmonize.

Some prize and cherish shallow, trivial writings like the *Book of Songs*, while dismissing the profound, rich, and expansive works of the masters. They mistake the most incisive words for dullness, and regard hollow, florid sophistry as refinement. Truth and falsehood are inverted, jade and stone confounded. They equate grand music with the tunes of Sangjian, and compare dragon robes to common garments. How lamentable, how deplorable that all drift along in this way!

Someone asks: “Though writings abound, they serve only to display rhetorical flourish and adorn language, offering no remedy for success or failure. They are not as good as the wordless instruction of virtue and conduct. Thus, Yan and Min rank highest, while You and Xia come after. The four categories of worthies place learning as the foundation and conduct as its culmination. Then is not writing merely a secondary pursuit? Yet you do not exalt its source but instead prize its branches—is this right?”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replies: “Virtue and conduct are tangible matters, their excellence or inferiority easily seen. Writing is subtle and wondrous, its essence difficult to discern. What is easily seen is coarse; what is difficult to discern is refined. Precisely because it is coarse, its assessment can be fixed; precisely because it is refined, its appraisal is hard to unify. Thus, I set aside the easily seen coarseness to discuss the difficult-to-discern refinement—is this not fitting?”

Someone asks: “Virtue and conduct are the root; writing is the branch. Thus, in the sequence of the four categories, literature does not occupy the highest place. Then are writings on paper merely the dregs of secondary matters? Are what can be transmitted but straw dogs after the sacrifice? The hierarchy of high and low is clear. May we hear of the essential nature of writing?”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* says: “One may discard the fishing basket only after the fish is caught; one may abandon writing only after the Dao is realized. As for the vast or concise scope of literary style, the density or sparsity of diction and allusions, the length or brevity of origins and influences, the depth or shallowness of implication and allusion—their extremes are so distant that even the space beyond the heavens or within a hair’s breadth cannot compare to their remoteness. Their mutual distinctions are so minute that even the radiance of the Three Luminaries cannot match their subtlety. The sharpness of Longyuan swords and the bluntness of lead rods cannot illustrate their keenness or dullness; the weight of swan feathers and heaps of gold cannot compare to their lightness or heaviness. Their clarity or turbidity, their gradations, arise from inherent endowment. Brightness and obscurity belong to different categories; strength and weakness arise from distinct qi. Yet vulgar scholars, seeing only those who can dip their brushes in ink and write on paper, lump them all together. This is why Boya forever longed for Zhong Ziqi, and why the man of Ying’s axe failed to move. Carvers may stand shoulder to shoulder, yet Ban and Di alone bear the title of unmatched masters. Those who pluck the zither are countless, yet Kui and Xiang alone are known for the difficulty of finding a true connoisseur. Among a thousand teams of horses in the stables, only the *qiji* commands a price beyond the herd. Among ten thousand beauties, only Wei and Shi possess transcendent allure. Truly, there are those who far surpass the multitude.

“Moreover, writing and virtue are like ten feet to a *zhang*—to call it a secondary matter is unheard of. The celestial signs that Heaven hangs, the praises earned by Yao and Shun, the tiger’s brilliance of great men, the leopard’s luster of noble scholars, the single character by which Chang Dan and Ding Sheng bestowed posthumous titles, the flourishing of Confucius’s teachings in the Zhou—none of these are without writing. The *Eight Trigrams* gave rise to the spread of eagles and falcons; the *Six Jia* emerged from the burden of the sacred tortoise. Where writing resides, even the lowly become noble; the hides of dogs and sheep cannot compare. Furthermore, the root is not always precious, nor is the branch always base. It is like brocade woven upon plain cloth, pearls and jade nestled in oyster and stone. Clouds and rain arise from a hand’s breadth; rivers and seas begin within a foot’s span. Thus, though writing may be the younger sibling of virtue, it cannot be called a secondary matter.”

Someone says: “The achievements of the present age mostly fall short of antiquity. Is writing and authorship not also the same?”

“Is this due to the decline of the qi and the natural order of things?”

*Baopuzi* replied: “Though the writings of the hundred schools may have arisen later, they all emerged from the thoughts of great scholars and the hands of accomplished talents. Compared to the ancients, they are not necessarily inferior. Some possess vast and profound vision, aligning perfectly with the authors’ intent—delving into unfathomable depths within and spreading inexhaustible currents without. Their origins are lofty, their unraveling exquisite. Their transformations are not bound by the square and circle of rules, nor does their expansiveness congeal in the narrowness of a single path. Thus, those who favor only sour or salty cannot know their true flavor, and those whose thoughts are limited cannot grasp their essence.

When the soaring dragon ascends with measured grace, gazing down upon the clouds; when the *hanxue* steed ambles yet breathes a thousand *li*—how could the ant marvel at its height without steps, or the plodding nag resent its surpassing swiftness? Yet those who gallop through poetry and discourse, who circle within the bounds of commentaries and records, who measure the extraordinary with common sentiment, gauge the boundless with narrow capacity, seek the profound with the crude, and fathom the deep with the shallow—even if they began as children and persisted until their hair turned white, they would still fail to comprehend.

Those who delight in something must praise it as good, while those who cannot understand it must condemn it as bad—this is the natural order. Thus, they dismiss what they do not grasp as absurd, sincerely believing it to be so, not out of malice to harm others.

Moreover,世俗 invariably reveres antiquity and disdains the present. Though there be steeds swift as the wind, they say they cannot match the chariots of Zaofu; though there be treasures worth cities, they say they cannot equal the tears of the man of Chu; though there be swords that sever doubt, they say they cannot rival the blades of Ouye; though there be medicines to raise the dead, they say they cannot compare to the compounds of He and Que; though there be men of surpassing talent, they say they cannot match those recorded in bamboo and silk; though there be books that benefit the age, they say they cannot approach the writings left by former generations. Thus, Confucius was not esteemed in his own time, and the *Tai Xuan* was mocked by his contemporaries.

The vulgar often claim: today’s mountains are not as high as the ancient mountains, today’s seas not as vast as the ancient seas, today’s sun not as scorching as the ancient sun, today’s moon not as bright as the ancient moon—how could they ever admit that the talents of today might not yield to the dry bones of antiquity? Valuing what is heard and belittling what is seen has been the affliction of more than one age. Truly, there was reason for the breaking of lutes and the cutting of strings in the past!

*Baopuzi* said: Surveying the records of the past down to recent times, no era saw the Dao so diminished and customs so corrupted as the end of the Han. Those who held power—eunuchs and their ilk—wielded the sacred regalia and grasped the reins of state. They cast aside the upright and promoted the perverse, ravaged benevolence and harmed righteousness. They trampled the virtuous and embraced the despised, followed the deaf and blind, and formed cliques of shared wickedness, drawing in factions of treachery.

They hoarded wealth without limit yet could not spare a single *zhu* to relieve the poor and upright. Promotions came only to those with great wealth; lawsuits were judged only for those with deep pockets. Though their rank was high and their power sufficient to elevate talent, they could not utter a word to advance the most outstanding of their time. Their appointments extended no further than the relatives of their wives and concubines; their favors reached no one beyond their close and petty attendants. They heeded not the censure of Zang Wen for usurping office, nor did they recall the impartiality of Xie Hu. They distributed emoluments to rival Wang Lin and entrusted affairs to men like Fang Hui.

Thus, though recluses filled the streets, none received the aid of an Zheng Yang; though men of noble integrity gathered, none encountered the recommendation of a Bao Sheng. They suppressed the independent and promoted the sycophantic—this is why Fan Ji covered her mouth in dismay, and why Feng Tang sighed forever.

Those in power were all idle drones, concealing virtue and obscuring worth. They envied merit and sought to endanger it, detested purity and strove to expel it, shunned loyal counsel and entrapped it, despised independence and cast it out. The fawning received protection and adornment, while the upright faced slander and abandonment. They nurtured jackals and wolves while slaughtering unicorns and tigers, cultivated brambles and thorns while pruning pepper and cassia.

Thus, the arrogant and unrestrained, who rolled like pellets on the water’s surface, were called magnanimous and broad-minded; the petty and treacherous, who harbored venom and stings, were called just and upright; the obsequious and clever, who had the appearance but not the heart, were called keen and perspicacious; the glib and artful, who curried favor with clever words, were called refined and elegant; the reckless and haughty, who were insolent and frivolous, were called towering and magnificent; the dissolute and debauched, who were dissolute and shameless, were called uninhibited and unfeigned; the greedy and rapacious, who seized without restraint, were called generous and far-reaching in virtue; the disheveled and slovenly, who consorted with the unworthy, were called broadly virtuous and all-embracing; those who defied the classics and distorted the sages, who followed falsehoods and boasted of their learning, were called disciples of Zhuangzi and Laozi; those who mocked and ridiculed, who scorned refinement and indulged in arrogance, were called carefree and refined; those who abandoned principle to ingratiate themselves, who echoed others’ words, were called unparalleled talents; those who relied on the powerful, who trod the paths of bribery, were called adept at seizing opportunity; those who shunned documents and signed names without reading were called great in ambition and lofty in purpose; those who depended on powerful relatives and held positions beyond their ability were called peers of the Four Lords; those who bribed influential families to purchase titles and ranks were called generous and noble; those who formed cliques and feigned praise, whose deeds contradicted their words, were called gathering friends through culture; those who practiced heterodox arts and invoked ghosts and spirits were called spiritually attuned sages; those who deceived with divination and embellished fortune and misfortune were called masters of foresight; those who displayed mere individual bravery in horse-archery and spear-throwing were called supreme generals of the first rank..."

Those who cobbled together hearsay and echoed vulgar opinions were called brilliant talents and eminent scholars.

As for those of upright conduct and lofty bearing, whose spirits were pure and capacities vast—who neither flattered with false smiles to curry favor nor bent their words to betray their hearts; who endured frost and trod snow, unswerving in righteousness, never stooping to base compromise; who upheld the Dao and applied its principles, standing aloof and unmatched; whose branches did not bend though the wind raged, whose integrity remained unshaken though their bodies suffered hardship; who, when advancing, spoke forthrightly in correct discourse, attacking faults and admonishing failings, and when retreating, maintained sincerity and rejected self-interest, acting without reservation—these were called the steadfast and unyielding.

Those who rose early and retired late, who took their meals only after public duties were done, who labored tirelessly for the state’s benefit, exhausting their strength in governance—these were called petty and vulgar officials.

Thus the wise and discerning grew wary and withdrew into seclusion; the noble and outstanding held their tongues and feigned foolishness. The lowly and obscure soared upward to choose their perches, while the constrained and bound bent to circumstance and served in hidden reclusion. The wise refused to reveal their secret strategies; the brave would not display their resolve. Loyal and forthright men were driven into retreat, while the treacherous and wicked prevailed. The tide of deceit swelled beyond restraint, and the path of falsehood widened beyond closure.

This led to the subversion of superiors and the abandonment of subordinates, to rampant banditry and theft. Eunuchs usurped the sovereign’s authority, and the Three Excellencies and Nine Ministers perished at the hands of base underlings. Loyal and worthy scholars were branded as factionalists, imprisoned, executed, and eradicated, drawing lamentations from all under heaven. The innocent and blameless, who shut their doors, met with disaster. A wisp of smoke rose from within the palace walls, yet the whirlwind’s flames scorched the entire cosmos; a shallow crack spread from a hair’s breadth, yet its waves and billows surged to the four extremities. The impregnable fortress was overrun by common bandits; the boiling moat was crossed by a single reed. The keenest blades melted like ice at the sight of dust; conscripted soldiers turned their weapons and fled in rout. Flying blades rained upon doorways and chambers; barbarian garb was seized within the palace precincts. Millet and sorghum sprouted in the ancestral halls; brambles and weeds flourished upon the jade steps. Cloud-capped towers became dens for foxes and hares; the imperial gateways turned into paths for tigers and leopards. The eastern halls were reduced to ashes; the common people were scorched by fire and drowned in abyssal flames. The wicked households that harmed the state were recorded in bamboo and silk as guilty, yet the annals of good historians contained no words of praise, and the inscriptions on bronze and stone bore no virtuous echoes. How could this be, if not for the nature of men?

*Wu Shi Juan*, Chapter Thirty-Four

The *Baopuzi* states: The final years of Wu suffered the same afflictions as distant eras. Though they recognized the failures of the past, they could not change course in the present. Though the lessons of chaos and collapse were not far removed, they followed the ruts of overturned chariots. They witnessed the fight over the mother rhinoceros’s grass and forgot the shared peril to their own bodies; they laughed at the ease of lice and fleas, unaware that circumstances differed but the calamity was the same. They saw the sinking of boats in competitive crossings yet failed to grasp that different paths led to the same drowning.

I was born in the Jin era and did not witness these events firsthand. My teacher, Master Zheng, personally experienced them in full and often instructed me thus: The late years of Wu suffered from particularly severe maladies. The worthy went unused; the filthy and corrupt were cast aside without order. The framework of governance collapsed into chaos; even vessels that could swallow boats leaked. Those who offered bribes took precedence in tribute and selection; those with powerful factions were favored in official appointments. Without wealth or influence, one toiled for years without hope. Those of pure virtue and lofty conduct harbored their talents in seclusion; those with ability and strength rode the clouds to official rank. The ruler above was benighted; the ministers below deceived. Without factional ties, one could not advance; without competition, one could not rise. The custom of betraying the public grew ever more severe; the path of uprightness was utterly ruined.

Thus the sparrow, startled by the wind, soared to the heavens; the rotting boat, borne by swift waves, raced like lightning. The phoenix and simurgh furled their six wings among thorns; the swiftest vessels stagnated in stagnant pools, never to ascend. The aides who upheld the reins of governance, the officials who shepherded the people—none were kin to the empress dowager, but all were flatterers and sycophants. In office, they offered no loyal counsel to correct faults or fill gaps; in retirement, they lacked the competence to hear lawsuits. In speech, their words were as cold as ice and frost; in conduct, they were fouler than mud and mire. They felt no shame at the censure of unearned salaries, nor fear of the disaster of foreign invasion. They wove reputations like silkworms spun threads, and replaced farming with the wielding of power. Their carriages and robes gleamed so brightly they could serve as mirrors; their grand mansions were so vast that flocks of birds nested within. Their shouts were swifter than thunder; their infliction of fortune and misfortune was faster than spirits. Their influence and profit overshadowed the lords of the land; their accumulated wealth surpassed that of the state treasury. When going out, they were escorted by the retinues of Dukes of Wei and Huang; when returning, they wandered among the ornate rafters of jade-rooted halls. Their servants formed armies; their gates became markets. Cattle and sheep covered the plains and lowlands; fields and ponds stretched for a thousand *li*. They feigned the frugality of Yuandi’s washed robes to steal the reputation of Zhao Xuanping, while inwardly they emulated the greed of Tao Kan and Wen Xin, in truth possessing the corruption of Anchang, Dong, and Deng. Though they never bathed worthy guests in kind hospitality, nor offered even a handful of grain to starving scholars, their halls overflowed with gold and jade, their chambers teemed with singing girls and concubines. A thousand merchant ships plied their trade; ten thousand granaries held rotting grain. Their gardens and parks rivaled the Shanglin; their mansions and halls usurped the grandeur of the Taiji. Meat and fine food were left to rot for dogs and horses; treasures piled up in the treasury until they were buried. In their dealings with scholars, they lacked even the slightest familial connection; in their self-indulgence, they exceeded all bounds of propriety.

Some there were who had never opened a single volume of law or statute yet usurped the seat of the Grand Judge; who knew not where the desk stood yet occupied posts of critical importance; who could not name the Five Classics yet drew the salary of a Confucian official; who had never endured the heat and cold of a single sheet of paper yet sat in the office of composition. Their brushes were neither wild nor terse, yet they enjoyed the honor of drafting memorials; with lowered brows and drooping wings, they filled the ranks of impeachment officials. They could not distinguish fine men from coarse yet were entrusted with the governance of literary evaluation; they knew nothing of the three powers’ military formations yet strutted beneath the banners of high command. Repeatedly humiliated as fleeing generals, they never lost their prestigious titles at the vanguard; unable to tell wheat from weeds, they shamelessly clung to positions of close counsel.

Such men were like fish with dragon markings—appearing right yet fundamentally wrong. They rejoiced in water but wept at the sight of an otter. Though threatened with the axe’s might or tempted by treasures of a city’s worth, they could not exchange their leaden blades for rhinoceros horns, nor could their lame nags keep pace with the wind. Not that they feared severe punishment, nor that they disdained rich rewards—it was simply beyond their nature, and nothing could be done. Yet to entrust them with harmonizing the hundred offices and managing great affairs was like placing ten thousand *jun* upon a foot-long boat, or seeking a thousand *zhong* in a mere *sheng*. To tether straw dogs and demand the skill of a hunting hawk, or to assemble chicken-like crossbows and expect the prowess of a soaring eagle—how could such men be of use? The folly was all too clear.

The Lord of Wu gave no thought to this, nor did he remain vigilant by night. Instead, he entrusted great responsibilities to flatterers and mediocrities. Peril loomed sharper than a drawn bow, and signs of ruin shone as bright as the sun and moon, yet he fancied himself as secure as a towering peak, aspiring to the heights of Yao and Shun. His eyes wearied of fine silks but never examined the successes and failures of state affairs; his ears were dulled by lewd music but never heeded the right and wrong of loyal counsel. Grain and silk were squandered in wasteful excess, yet he did not relieve the hunger and cold of his soldiers. His heart delighted in flattery but never pondered the weighty principles of survival or ruin. Truly, he treated the sources of rise and fall with levity and neglected the gravity of the ancestral temples.

Master Zheng once recounted that his teacher, Master Zuo, dwelled in reclusion at Mount Tianzhu. He sought neither office nor profit, nor did he befriend lords, yet his heart longed for peace and secretly grieved for his homeland. One day, beneath his thatched roof, he sighed deeply and said to his disciples: “The Han will surely be eclipsed, as the Yellow Essence rises anew. It will bind the pivot at the Supreme Culmen and turn the Purple Canopy toward the Quail’s Head. It will unite heaven and govern all things, illuminating the eastern lands. Its benevolent winds will reach beyond the borders, and its dark blessings will suffuse the realm. Distant envoys will tread in succession, their tribute of *hu* wood filling the court. Vast and majestic, it will reach both high and low. To maintain peace and uphold tradition would be effortless, yet the five-stringed zither falls silent, and the *Southern Wind* is left unsung. Above, there is no ease of self-restraint; below, no songs of well-being are heard. The flying dragon soars but does not alight; the deep leviathan coils but does not leap. The *zouyu* hides in obscurity, and the vermilion blossoms remain budding and unopened. Yin and yang clash, cold and heat fall out of rhythm. The seven celestial bodies portend misfortune; mountains and valleys shift their places. The thunder chariot rumbles ominously. In the month when dragons lie hidden, frost congeals and the killing force descends upon the season of summer’s glory. The Jade Candle does not shine; the sweet spring does not well forth. The suburban altars stand in ruins; fine crops do not flourish. Is this due to any other cause? Truly, it is because the Four Fiends were not cast out, the great worthies were not promoted, the employed were unworthy, and the worthy were not employed.

“Yet those of lofty resolve and far-reaching vision, who clothe themselves in coarse garb while harboring jade within, who guard their purity and still their ambitions, desiring nothing from the world—who hide in deep pools and secluded marshes, finding contentment in leaving the world behind, who move only when propriety demands and appear only at the proper time, who endure hardship without distress and poverty without regret, who delight in heaven’s will and accept fate, merging honor and disgrace into one, showing no joy in advancement nor sorrow in retreat—such men may well die in obscurity beneath tiled roofs. How could they deign to peddle their priceless treasures, competing for the sale of worthless pebbles? The ways of Confucius and Mozi once went unheeded; Mencius and Yang Xiong also faced hardship and adversity. To possess virtue without the right time—this has always been so.

“The world lacks men like Li Zhu, and black and white blur together. The age is without men like Guan Qing, and fine steeds mix with lame nags. Pebbles fill the golden coffers; jade and fine gems lie discarded in ditches. The master craftsman has vanished into the distance, and the finest timber goes unrecognized. Alas! My life has come neither too early nor too late. I shall witness the land of Wu transformed into Jin territory, and the people of the south become northern subjects.”

These words still rang in the ears when the Sun clan surrendered their coffin. The *Baopuzi* heard them and said: “The words of these two masters may serve as a warning for later ages.” Thus, I have recorded them in this chapter, that future generations may know that Wu’s loss of its state was not decreed by heaven.

If one were to conceal a state’s faults, refusing even the slightest criticism, then Dong Hu’s straight brush would be of no value, and Jia Yi would be mocked for his *Over the Qin*.

**On Guarding Reclusion, Chapter Thirty-Five**

The *Baopuzi* said: Among my friends was a gentleman who dwelled in reclusion. Admiring the unclaimed lands of Qinshui, he built his hut upon barren soil. He sharpened his mind on the arts and literature, disdaining the study of farming. Years passed, and hunger etched itself upon his face. Someone challenged him, saying: “Heaven knows that propriety resides in a full granary, and generosity stems from abundant wealth. Exaltation arises from surplus, while frugality is born of scarcity. Thus, the poet praised ten thousand *qian*, and the *Eight Policies* placed food and goods first. To till the soil oneself is the foundation of matching the worthy; to plow in pairs brings unchanging joy. The dwelling of an extraordinary man—when he advances, he soars with the phoenix; when he retreats, he joins the governance of Tao and Bai. In office, he must become a hegemon or king; in reclusion, he must possess a thousand gold. Thus, the ancients divided fertile lands to share their bounty, toiled with their limbs in planting and harvesting, sowing the vast fields with beans and millet, nurturing fine vegetables to flourish. They gathered thousands of granaries of barley and black millet, amassing countless *yu* of grain. They rode forth with mounted escorts to hunt, and returned to feast on jade-like delicacies.”

The former kings who dwelt in this land faced dire straits: when scorching yang prevailed, grain withered and dust flew; when dense yin gathered, floods drowned hills and mounds. The fields bore no sprouting seedlings, the waters yielded no ripened ears. Chaff and coarse grain lay abandoned in granaries, firewood and hearths stood idle in kitchens. Yet you cling to the folly of waiting for hares by the stump, complacent and without a plan for advance or retreat. I fear the fate of Boyi and Shuqi on Shouyang Mountain will befall us now, and the hardship of the phoenix on Dan Mountain will soon arrive. My heart grows cold for your sake—why do you remain so untroubled, so at ease?

He who sees the moment yet does not act cannot be called wise. To cling to one’s land without moving is the way of the common folk—how could one who delights in the stench of salted fish forget the fragrance of orchids, or one deeply lost alter his nature? Why have you, Master, remained so long in ignorance?

This humble one is perplexed and does not grasp your meaning. Ceremonial caps and robes are not armor against arrows and blades; classics and decrees are not tools to stave off hunger and cold. Why not survey the fertile fields beyond the four suburbs, take up the good work of the plowman, set aside the impractical Six Arts, and gather a thousand granaries to relieve the needy?

The Recluse replied: “The deaf cannot be urged to distinguish refined music from vulgar tunes; the blind cannot be tasked with telling cinnabar from lacquer. The frog in the well cannot be told of the vast sea, nor can the vulgar be persuaded by the teachings of the classics. You may know the small gains of the old farmer, but you do not yet understand the great folly of one who faces a wall. What difference is there between picking up grains of sand while discarding a priceless pearl, or turning toward a flickering candle while turning one’s back on the bright sun?

Tastes and preferences cannot be leveled with a single stroke; choices and rejections cannot be swapped between you and me. He who aspires to ascend the heights of Langfeng or scale the peaks of Song and Hua will not linger on anthills. One whose heart is set on sailing the Southern Sea or drifting across the boundless ocean will not idle in stagnant ponds. Thus, he who lends his ear to the *Nine Shao* finds the tunes of the Ba people grating; he who feasts on the finest meats from a banquet table finds the taste of bitter herbs and smartweed unpalatable.

The roc soars to the crimson skies, the wagtail flits proudly through the reeds—high and low, each follows its own path, yet both reach the limits of flight. The span of a month is fleeting to the morning mushroom; the mayfly darts about in a moment’s time, while the wild horse of summer rests only after six months. The minnows sport on the waves, their scales glinting, while the divine dragon remains hidden in unfathomable depths. With such divergence in paths and deeds, how can one grasp the intent?

Though my coarse meals may not fill my belly, I am content with the fare of kings. Thus, Liezi did not covet the salary of Zheng Yang despite his poverty, and Zeng Shen did not trade his humble state for the riches of Jin or Chu.

To preserve subtle words on the verge of being lost was the distant legacy of Zhou and Confucius. To toil tirelessly for profit is the fault of which Mengzi’s followers stand accused. He who seeks the far reaches cannot master every fork in the road; he who acts cannot pursue both farming and learning. When the body is weary but the spirit at ease, what harm is there in living simply? Yet to dwell in barren lands is to labor, and labor leaves no room for learning—purity comes only through purity. To live in abundance is to grow idle, and idleness leaves no room for learning—extravagance comes only through extravagance.

Purity is where fortune gathers; extravagance is where misfortune rushes in. When fortune gathers, the humble may rise, and the declining may flourish. When misfortune strikes, the strong may weaken, and the living may perish. This is what comes unbidden and unsummoned.

Thus, the noble man who wishes to correct what is amiss must first rectify its root; to stem the flow, he must first dam the source. When the work of the Dao and virtue is established, the gates of excess and waste will close.

Jiang Wang, of utmost virtue, no longer tilled the fields; Chong Hua, the great sage, fished without casting nets. Yet the jade tablet marked the prosperity of Yingqiu, and the great merit of Yu rose to twenty *zhang*—why mock them for laziness or scrutinize their talents as if judging men?

When two men divide wealth, taking less is called integrity. I now yield the world’s abundance, dwelling in this remote and barren land. I forsake the rich fields of Anchang for the desireless outskirts of the north. Truly, all things may be small, yet where is there not enough?

The North Star is revered because it does not change; the Five Peaks are honored because they do not shift. The cricket, though it moves often, is not prized; birds and fish that glut themselves in deep waters meet with disaster. I shall now clear the overgrowth from the Nine Classics, sow the fine grains of the Six Virtues. My fields will surpass the ranks of the highest scholars, and my harvest will fill the space between heaven and earth. Why must I make plowing and sowing my sole concern?

Of old, Pi Yi discarded wealth to stop thieves; the Geng family used parables to curb greed. Liu Guang scattered his gold to avert disaster for his descendants; Shu Ao chose barren land to dispel the worries of desire. Niu Que’s treasures brought bandits upon him; Tao Gu’s hoarding invited calamity. The contrast between gain and loss is clear—can one not take heed?


+++++ src/site/notes/10000 Reading Notes/10100 Classical Chinese Texts/周易注 王弼 (Wang Bi's Commentary on the Zhouyi).md
---
{"dg-publish":true,"permalink":"/10000-reading-notes/10100-classical-chinese-texts//","dgHomeLink":false,"dgPassFrontmatter":false}
---

### Bibliographic Data
**Title:** 周易注 (Wang Bi's Commentary on the *Zhouyi*)
**Author:** 王弼 (Wang Bi, 226–249 CE)
**Category:** 儒家 (Confucianism), 易学 (Study of the *Yijing*)
**Edition:** Based on the *Zhonghua Shuju* annotated edition (中华书局, 1980), collated with the *Sibu Beiyao* (四部备要) version.

---

### **Translation**

#### **乾卦第一 (Qian, The Creative)**
**卦辞 (Hexagram Statement):**
「乾，元亨利贞。」
*Qian: Originating, flourishing, beneficial, and steadfast.*

**王弼注 (Wang Bi’s Commentary):**
「夫易者，象也。 象之所生，生於化也。 化之所始，始於无也。 故乾坤之始，以无为本。 无，故能运物而不匮，无形，故能化物而不倦。 夫无不可以无明，必因於有，故受名於乾坤。 乾坤，其易之蕴邪？ 乾坤成列，而易立乎其中矣。 乾坤毁，则无以见易；易不可见，则乾坤或几乎息矣。 是故形而上者谓之道，形而下者谓之器。 化而裁之谓之变，推而行之谓之通，举而措之天下之民谓之事业。」
*Now, the Changes are images. The arising of images arises from transformation. The beginning of transformation begins with the formless. Thus, the beginning of Qian and Kun takes the formless as its root. Being formless, it can set things in motion without exhaustion; being without shape, it can transform things without weariness. The formless cannot be illuminated by the formless—it must rely on the formed, and thus receives its name from Qian and Kun. Are Qian and Kun not the essence of the Changes? When Qian and Kun take their places, the Changes are established between them. If Qian and Kun were destroyed, there would be no way to perceive the Changes; if the Changes were not visible, Qian and Kun would nearly cease to exist. Therefore, that which is above form is called the Dao; that which is below form is called the vessel. To transform and regulate it is called change; to extend and enact it is called penetration. To lift it and apply it to the people of the world is called the work of affairs.*

**爻辞 (Line Statements) & 注 (Commentary):**
1. **初九：潜龙勿用。**
   *Nine at the beginning: The hidden dragon. Do not act.*
   「潜龙勿用，阳在下也。 阳气潜藏，未可施用，故曰勿用。」
   *The hidden dragon does not act because yang is below. The yang qi is concealed and hidden, not yet ready to be employed—thus it says, “Do not act.”*

2. **九二：见龙在田，利见大人。**
   *Nine in the second: The dragon appears in the field. It is beneficial to see the great man.*
   「见龙在田，德施普也。 君德也，未得位而应於二，故曰利见大人。」
   *The dragon appears in the field—its virtue is universally bestowed. This is the virtue of a ruler. Though it has not yet attained its position, it responds to the second [line], thus it says, “It is beneficial to see the great man.”*

3. **九三：君子终日乾乾，夕惕若，厉无咎。**
   *Nine in the third: The noble one is ceaselessly active all day, vigilant in the evening as if in danger. There is no blame.*
   「终日乾乾，反复道也。 虽危无咎，得中道也。」
   *Ceaselessly active all day—this is the way of returning and repeating. Though in danger, there is no blame—this is attaining the central way.*

4. **九四：或跃在渊，无咎。**
   *Nine in the fourth: Perhaps leaping in the abyss. No blame.*
   「或跃在渊，进无咎也。 处下体之极，上体之下，故或之。」
   *Perhaps leaping in the abyss—advancing without blame. It is at the limit of the lower body and below the upper body—thus it says “perhaps.”*

5. **九五：飞龙在天，利见大人。**
   *Nine in the fifth: The flying dragon is in the sky. It is beneficial to see the great man.*
   「飞龙在天，上治也。 德备天位，治成也。」
   *The flying dragon is in the sky—this is governing above. Its virtue is complete in the position of heaven—governance is achieved.*

6. **上九：亢龙有悔。**
   *Nine at the top: The dragon soars to its limit. There is regret.*
   「亢龙有悔，盈不可久也。」
   *The dragon soars to its limit—there is regret. Fullness cannot last long.*

**用九：见群龙无首，吉。**
*Use the nine: See the flock of dragons without a leader. Auspicious.*
「用九，天德不可为首也。 无首，故吉。」
*Use the nine—the virtue of heaven cannot be made the leader. Without a leader, thus auspicious.*

---

#### **坤卦第二 (Kun, The Receptive)**
**卦辞 (Hexagram Statement):**
「坤，元亨，利牝马之贞。 君子有攸往，先迷后得主。 利西南得朋，东北丧朋。 安贞吉。」
*Kun: Originating and flourishing. Beneficial is the steadfastness of the mare. The noble one has somewhere to go: if he goes first, he will be lost; if he follows, he will find a master. Beneficial to gain companions in the southwest, to lose companions in the northeast. Steadfastness in tranquility is auspicious.*

**王弼注 (Wang Bi’s Commentary):**
「坤者，地也，万物皆致养焉，故曰致役乎坤。 牝马，地类，行地无疆，柔顺利贞，君子攸行，先迷失道，后顺得常。 西南，地也，故曰得朋；东北，山也，故曰丧朋。 阴之为道，卑顺不盈，安贞者也。」
*Kun is earth, to which all things come for nourishment—thus it says, “to be employed by Kun.” The mare is of the earth’s kind, roaming the earth without bounds. Gentle, yielding, beneficial, and steadfast—when the noble one travels, if he goes first, he will lose the way; if he follows, he will attain constancy. The southwest is earth—thus it says, “gain companions”; the northeast is mountains—thus it says, “lose companions.” The way of yin is humble, yielding, and not full—this is steadfastness in tranquility.*

**爻辞 (Line Statements) & 注 (Commentary):**
1. **初六：履霜，坚冰至。**
   *Six at the beginning: Tread on frost—solid ice is coming.*
   「履霜坚冰，阴始凝也。 驯致其道，至坚冰也。」
   *Tread on frost—solid ice: yin begins to congeal. Following its way to the end, it reaches solid ice.*

2. **六二：直方大，不习无不利。**
   *Six in the second: Straight, square, and great. Without practice, nothing is not beneficial.*
   「直其正也，方其义也。 君子敬以直内，义以方外，故不习无不利。」
   *Straight is its correctness; square is its righteousness. The noble one is reverent within to be straight, righteous without to be square—thus, without practice, nothing is not beneficial.*

3. **六三：含章可贞。 或从王事，无成有终。**
   *Six in the third: Containing brilliance, it may be steadfast. Perhaps following the king’s affairs—without accomplishment, there is an end.*
   「含章可贞，以时发也。 或从王事，知光大也。」
   *Containing brilliance, it may be steadfast—this is to manifest in due time. Perhaps following the king’s affairs—this is to know brilliance and greatness.*

4. **六四：括囊，无咎无誉。**
   *Six in the fourth: Tie up the sack. No blame, no praise.*
   「括囊无咎，慎不害也。」
   *Tie up the sack—no blame: caution does no harm.*

5. **六五：黄裳，元吉。**
   *Six in the fifth: Yellow lower garment. Greatly auspicious.*
   「黄裳元吉，文在中也。」
   *Yellow lower garment—greatly auspicious: pattern is within.*

6. **上六：龙战于野，其血玄黄。**
   *Six at the top: Dragons battle in the wild. Their blood is dark and yellow.*
   「龙战于野，阴阳相伤也。」
   *Dragons battle in the wild—yin and yang harm each other.*

**用六：利永贞。**
*Use the six: Beneficial is eternal steadfastness.*
「用六永贞，以大终也。」
*Use the six—eternal steadfastness: to end in greatness.*

---

#### **屯卦第三 (Zhun, Difficulty at the Beginning)**
**卦辞 (Hexagram Statement):**
「屯，元亨利贞。 勿用有攸往，利建侯。」
*Zhun: Originating, flourishing, beneficial, and steadfast. Do not act on what you have somewhere to go. Beneficial to establish a lord.*

**王弼注 (Wang Bi’s Commentary):**
「屯者，物之始生也。 物之始生，困於无阳。 故屯乃大亨，而利贞也。 勿用有攸往，刚柔始交，未有应援，故勿用有攸往也。 利建侯者，刚柔始交，则须建侯也。」
*Zhun is the beginning of things’ birth. At the beginning of things’ birth, they are hindered by the absence of yang. Thus, Zhun is greatly flourishing and beneficial in steadfastness. “Do not act on what you have somewhere to go”: hardness and softness have just met, and there is no response or support—thus, “do not act on what you have somewhere to go.” “Beneficial to establish a lord”: when hardness and softness have just met, there is a need to establish a lord.*

---

*(Note: Due to length, this sample covers the first three hexagrams. The full translation would continue in the same style, preserving Wang Bi’s philosophical emphasis on the interplay of *you* 有 (being) and *wu* 无 (non-being), the centrality of the Dao, and the dynamic balance of yin and yang.)*

---

###

The questioner then fell silent, his mouth agape yet unable to speak, his head bowed and unable to lift. With a sigh of lament, he at last realized: those who establish words of enduring legacy do not muddy their harmony with worldly possessions; those who pursue scholarly achievement behind lowered curtains do not let their gaze wander to the gardens. You call the phoenix with the sweetness of a fledgling’s flesh, and lure the tiger with the cunning of a crab—how vulgar! How base, your belated understanding!

**Chapter Thirty-Six: Embracing Poverty**

*Baopuzi* said: In the past, the Han’s fire dimmed, dragons warred and tigers contended, the Nine Provinces split asunder, and the Three Families stood like tripods. There was a Master of Enjoying Heaven who, fleeing turmoil, drifted like tumbleweed, wandering through Min and Yi. At first, he dwelled in intimacy with Wen Xiu, and in the end, he was known by Kongming. Yet his words were lofty and his conduct square; he stood alone, apart from the crowd. The people of his time feared him, and none dared associate with him. Though the power of these two lords could not defy the masses, they allowed this scholar to sink into obscurity beneath a thatched roof, his hair turning white as the mulberry and elm, yet his plain cloth unchanged. The ruler of the time sought worthy men but neither heard nor knew of him; those in power neither recommended nor presented him. He hid in the humble lanes of Wu, concealed himself in a hut with a rope latch, abandoned the affairs of governing the world, and forgot the means of sustaining life. His meals of wild herbs and pears often left his belly empty, and he did not know where his next meal would come from.

Then a worldly young gentleman visited and questioned him, saying: “I have heard that those with the talent of Yi and Lü do not long remain in poverty and lowliness; those who possess the methods of Yi Dun do not suffer hunger and cold for long. The discerning value their ability to adapt; the wise test themselves by whether they lack nothing. Thus, when Fan Li emerged, he destroyed Wu and made Yue hegemonic, becoming a counselor for the ages; when he withdrew, he engaged in trade and amassed wealth of ten thousand gold. Heaven’s poverty lies among the Six Extremes, while wealth resides in the Five Blessings. The *Book of Songs* praises ‘what can be added,’ and the *Book of Changes* values ‘gathering people.’ When fragrant bait is cast, eels and sturgeons come; when generous rewards are offered, the resolute are roused. Thus, Sima Xiangru shed his apron and held the vermilion tassel; Chen Ping left his humble door and received a fief of land. Lü Buwei was enfeoffed with a city of ten thousand households, and Zhang Liang was lifted from the depths of prison. Therefore, the man of Yue who was frugal later met with misfortune, while the drifting old woman who was generous received a thousand gold in return.

You, sir, possess no extraordinary strategies like Shao Bo, yet you focus your keen thoughts solely on the Six Classics. You ignore the real disaster of cutting off the supply of grain, and instead covet the empty fame of immortality. You are ashamed to seek office through devious means, and disdain to flaunt your talents for glory. You hope for a royal hunt like that of King Wen, and wait for the Yellow River to run clear. You endure the emaciated look of Liezi, abandoning the body to preserve the spirit—how is this different from painting a picture of a fine steed to replace the toil of walking, or pointing to the distant sea to quench a parched mouth?”

Atop the sheerest cliffs, one casts fishing nets; from the tips of towering trees, one dangles hooks—though one may deem this the right place, it remains but a futile and impractical endeavor. No deed yields benefit beyond one’s lifetime, no thing flourishes against the season. Now the realm lies sundered like melons, heroes vie in might, rebels swell like floods to drown the land, and traitors cast off all restraint. The slow and the swift race side by side, jackals and wolves bare their fangs in mutual strife. Those who block the path raise their sleeves to protest injustice, while scholars of purity gather dust upon the road. Pure Confucians set aside the royal Dao to practice the arts of the Five Hegemons; eminent scholars abandon the Four Disciplines to heed the monthly critiques of the age. Those whose baskets and chests brim with goods rise from the wilds; those lacking wealth and rank withdraw from court. Those who grasp gold and silver push through the golden gates to ascend the jade halls, while those who recite the classics find themselves bound by世 enmity and mired in mud. Those whose gifts are lavish see tiles and stones transformed into jade tablets; those whose petitions are meager see dragons and steeds abandoned in the wilds. Those with many allies ride the whirlwind to soar above the clouds; those with few connections consort with turtles and crabs to sink and swim. A pellet of clay cannot dam the boiling torrent of Pengli, nor can a lone worthy reverse the course of a deviant age.

Now you, sir, possess no store of grain even to fill a picul, no bundle of dried meat to offer in tribute. Yet you bear latent brilliance like the dragon and phoenix, adorned in patterns like the tiger and leopard. Your words surge forth like waves, your discourse unfolds like brocade—yet you freeze and starve within four bare walls. What plan could be more pitiable? Why not launch a light skiff to ride the swift currents, hoist a flying sail to journey far? Trade in rare goods from north to south, gather gold and jade from the Nine Doubts. Follow in the distant footsteps of Cui Lie, and bind yourself to noble rank in this pure age. Why exhaust yourself in writing and composition—when will you ever shed this shell like the cicada? To wear out the body for the sake of fame is but the mockery of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi!”

Master Letian replied: “The Six Arts I have studied exhaustively, the Eight Compilations I have mastered in full—this is wealth indeed. With brush I unfurl my literary splendor, my virtuous voice knows no end—this is nobility indeed. Seek benevolence, and benevolence arrives; what more is there to desire? Dwelling deep in the abyss to nurture the spirit, I remain untouched by the myriad things and find contentment within. I leave the dust and peril of the world behind, purifying my spirit in profound silence. Without gazing through the window, I survey the distant; I discern the subtlest words and remain unconfused. Though offered the three ducal seats, I would stand firm and unyielding. How could I deign to fret over poverty and vie for profit with merchants, or lament hardship and compete for success with the vulgar?

If you know that trading can amass treasures and farming can stave off hunger, you fail to see that the deer-chaser heeds not the hare, and the distant traveler arrives late. When the venerable Lü Shang still wielded his cleaver, his white-haired head had already conceived his marvels. A weight of ten thousand jun cannot be shifted by the fiercest gale; before the *Xiao Shao* melody reaches its ninth movement, the numinous birds have already gathered in formation. Thus, those who await the whirlwind to ascend the azure heavens do not scorn the humble perch of the thatched hut; those who spur their sinews to climb sixty thousand *li* do not vie for the road with a lame donkey’s herd.”

The great filial son must fear the peril of bringing humiliation upon his kin, thus Zichun trembled in dread at descending the hall. The supremely wise do not prize hard-won wealth, thus Yao and Shun cast away gold and cast aside parables. The enlightened sage averts disaster before it arrives; the discerning scholar, upon hearing of profit, weighs its harm. Yet you, sir, speak of common servants setting sail on boats, diligently adorning their halls, urging the slinging of a Sui pearl to strike a sparrow, seizing meat from a tiger’s maw, lightly risking one’s body in the unfathomable, rushing headlong into grave danger for distant ends, forgetting the clear warning of skin and hair, and seeking profit in the face of hopeless odds.

When timber is felled and cliffs collapse, when trees are uprooted and stones take flight, when the River Earl rears like a mountain and towering waves rise like peaks, then light skiffs drift like dust, strength and will part ways, and though one weeps and wails, it is too late—only then does one grasp the foresight of the truly discerning.

Of old, Yan Yuan was esteemed for his purity and austerity; Chen Ping escaped peril by lacking gold; Guang Han perished for his love of profit; Niu Que was reduced to ashes for carrying treasure. The common man dies wrongfully for holding jade; the plump fox invites disaster with its fine pelt. Now you urge me toward a trade that invites theft, press me with schemes that summon bandits, proffer poisoned wine as a toast, and call it a loyal boon to nurture life. The scholar treasures the *Three Canons* as gold and jade, the *Five Classics* as zither and zheng, discourse and study as bells and drums, the Hundred Schools as reed pipes and flutes—letting those who savor the Dao feast on its words, those drunk on virtue awaken through righteousness, rise above the vulgar with lofty steps, and outshine ages past with resounding fame. Then, driving forward alone, why would one sully one’s nature with mere goods?

He who hoards much loses abundance; he who favors modesty shuns fullness. He who conceals a night-shining pearl hastens its exposure; he who follows the path of an overturned cart will surely overturn. The overloaded ship sinks; he who seeks to prevail destroys his life. These are the concerns of lesser minds, not the pursuits of the highest virtue.

Thereupon, the questioner was struck with dismay, humbly requesting to be counted among the last of the disciples, forever treasuring the true formula for long life.

---

*Baopuzi* says: My disciples debated the precedence of benevolence and wisdom, each holding to their views, and so consulted me. I told them:

“The three luminaries and celestial images belong to Qian; the boundless, nurturing earth belongs to Kun. Qian possesses benevolence and also wisdom; Kun possesses benevolence but lacks wisdom. How could the order of high and low be so distant? Only the sage aligns his virtue with Heaven. Thus, Tang Yao crowned his canons with reverence and wisdom, and Confucius placed ‘wisdom and righteousness’ at the head of his teachings. ‘Radiant and wise’ is the honorific for the sovereign; ‘the wise preserve themselves’ is the supreme path of the *Greater Odes*. Even insects, birds, and creeping things possess benevolence—their intent to nurture is vast, their sorrow at suffering is evident. Yet they rush into pits without suspicion, enter nets without awareness. Possessing benevolence but lacking wisdom, they hasten toward disaster and suffer great loss, seeking shade to escape the sun, building halls to avoid nests, choosing grains to replace poison, crafting garments to end nakedness, fashioning boats to cross impassable waters, harnessing oxen and horses to cease bearing burdens, ordering ranks to quell chaos, forging tools to guard against the unforeseen, creating writing to govern officials, and establishing rites and laws to refine customs. These are the works of great wisdom, not the discernment of partial men.

If the heart does not stray from benevolence yet wisdom does not govern the state, the calamity of peril and ruin cannot be averted—this is self-evident. To gauge rise and fall before signs appear, to probe subtle affairs before they take shape, to point out hidden dangers beyond reason, and to resist insidious influences at their root—these are the works of wisdom. To extend compassion to insects, though wronged yet not retaliating, to change the sacrificial victim upon seeing its trembling, to avoid trampling the tender reeds—these are the acts of benevolence. Thus, wisdom is talent, while action is conduct. The conduct of sacrificing oneself to perfect benevolence can be striven for, but the wisdom to fathom the profound and perceive the obscure cannot be borrowed. The distinction between refinement and coarseness is plainly evident.

If one embodies the benevolence that cannot bear suffering yet lacks the wisdom to discern right and wrong, the heart will be confused by truth and falsehood, the spirit will mistake red for purple, calculations will blur, and good and evil will go unrecognized. Failing to foresee safety and danger, one cannot even preserve oneself—how then could one stand to aid others? Of old, the Duke of Zhou was not without fraternal love, yet he wept and punished his kin; Shi Shilun was not without natural affection, yet he severed private ties for the public good. Clearly perceiving the nature of affairs and not drowning in immediate sentiment, they became pure ministers. Cutting off affection for the sake of righteousness, setting aside benevolence to employ wisdom, using strategy to restrain benevolence—benevolence may be set aside at times, but wisdom cannot be absent.

Tang and Wu seized power by force and governed with virtue—this was truly not benevolent. Yet they responded to Heaven’s mandate and revolutionized the age through their wisdom. When Xu Yan cultivated benevolence to unify the court, outwardly abandoning the defense of cities and walls, inwardly lacking weapons and armor, he lost his state and ruined his family—this was the calamity of lacking wisdom.”

The disciples said: “Confucius sighed that benevolence is a heavy burden and the Way is distant, and also said, ‘If a man lacks benevolence, what can he do with ritual?’ Of the sage and benevolence, how dare I presume! Mencius said, ‘Benevolence is the dwelling; righteousness is the path.’ A man without a compassionate heart is not benevolent. The Three Dynasties gained the world through benevolence and lost it through lack of benevolence. These are the maxims of sages and worthies, the clear evidence of bamboo and silk. Yet you, Master, prize wisdom—we have not seen the classical basis for this. We are benighted and humbly confess our confusion.”

*Baopuzi* replied: “The ancients said, ‘To love benevolence but not love learning—its flaw is stupidity.’ You are close to this. In the past, the Six States devoured one another, jackals and tigers vied in strength, exalted power and deceit while despising virtue, prizing slaughter and abandoning yielding. Mencius then sought to curb the rapacious and cruel, to exalt and uphold benevolence and righteousness. How could he not earnestly and repeatedly extol benevolence alone? Yet he never uttered a single word saying that benevolence surpasses wisdom.”

Just as in times of plague and pestilence, physicians and shamans are held in high esteem, their voices unanimous in discussing only medicines and stones—could one then claim that the art of acupuncture and moxibustion surpasses the Dao of long life and enduring vision? Moreover, I hold that the matters of benevolence and wisdom are spread across records and bamboo slips, aiming only to illustrate essential principles and convey the broad spirit by举ing a single corner. Yet you remain unaware in daily use, asserting that wisdom lacks classical basis!

The *Qian* hexagram speaks of "great brightness completing the beginning," its six positions timely formed—this establishes Heaven through illumination, encompassing all. The *Kun* hexagram says, "Supreme indeed! The myriad things rely on it for birth," for Earth’s virtue is benevolence, nothing more than compliance and submission. The principle of precedence and succession—is it not resplendently clear?

The *Book of Songs* states:
*"Bright, bright is the High Heaven,*
*Illuminating the lands below.*
*Bright, bright is the Son of Heaven,*
*His good renown unceasing."*

The *Book of Changes* says, "When the king’s brightness is pervasive, all receive its blessing." To darkly assist the numinous and make it spiritually luminous—this is where brightness unites with the divine, something pure benevolence can never attain or emulate.

Confucius spoke of "keen perception, wisdom, spiritual martiality," not "keen benevolence." He also said, "Of old, enlightened kings governed the world," not "benevolent kings." The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states, "Virtue is bright as a chime stone," not "benevolent virtue." The *Book of Documents* says, "The sovereign’s head is enlightened indeed," not "benevolent indeed."

Laozi praised the highest scholar as "understanding and penetrating the four directions," while of decline he said, "Lose the Dao, then virtue; lose virtue, then benevolence." The *Book of Changes* says, "The king faces south toward brightness," not "toward benevolence."

"I desire benevolence, and benevolence arrives." It also says, "Benevolence comes from oneself"—thus, all may practice it. But as for keen perception and wisdom, how can one compel them? Hence Mencius said, "Whenever people see a child about to fall into a well, none fail to rush to save them." From this, we see that all possess a heart of benevolence; only in its depth or shallowness do the distinctions of perception and wisdom arise at times.

Formerly, when Cui Zhu did not kill Yan Ying, Yan Ying called him greatly unbenevolent yet possessing a small benevolence. Thus, even treacherous ministers and villainous sons may yet have benevolence.

A disciple further said, "The *Book of Changes* speaks of establishing the human Dao as benevolence and righteousness—then nothing is greater than benevolence."

*Baopuzi* replied: "The reason I say this is that benevolence lies in action, which can be striven for through effort, whereas wisdom enters the realm of the spirit, requiring Heaven-bestowed talent—not something to be taught to the ignorant."

*Baopuzi* said: A weight of ten thousand jun must begin with a single zhu; towering heights piercing the clouds must start from a hair’s breadth. Thus, trickling streams gather to form the Southern Sea’s boundless vastness, and common measures accumulate to raise the Dark Garden to heaven’s utmost heights.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who gallop swiftly may leave the sun behind without toil; those who ride the wind and cleave the waves may cross peril without capsizing. Thus, the great ministers divided their duties, and the achievements of emulating Heaven were accomplished; Yi and Lü departed, yet the work of revolution was achieved.

*Baopuzi* said: A boat of jade and ropes of precious stones are useless for crossing rivers; a bow of gold and strings of jade lack the power to launch arrows.

Thus, those pure and aloof yet devoid of governance are no instruments for quelling chaos; those refined and learned yet lacking in administrative strategy are no talents for assisting bright rule.

*Baopuzi* said: The towers of Langfeng and the gardens of Xuanpu owe no height to anthills; the gems Xuanli and Jielü need no comparison to fine jade to be seen. Thus, the extraordinary and unmatched startle the fragrance of hidden orchids; the lofty and independent resound like flocks of birds taking flight.

*Baopuzi* said: Ice and burning coals do not flaunt their powers of cold and heat, nor do flawless gems prove their worth to be recognized. Thus, the noble man, self-composed, need not fret over solitude; the perfected one, dwelling in stillness, leaves his heart beyond praise or blame.

*Baopuzi* said: The whirlwind may topple mountains yet cannot exert force to pluck a hair; fire may melt metal and stone yet cannot kindle dampness with its blaze. Thus, Han Xin excelled in warfare and defense yet fumbled in the strategies of governance; the Marquis of Jiang secured the altars of state yet lacked the ready wit for courtly exchange.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who chase fame do not find risking their lives difficult; those who value their persons do not let fleeting desires burden their hearts. Thus, Ji Xin gladly became ashes and porridge without regret, and Yang Zhu equated a single hair to a city of linked jade.

*Baopuzi* said: The minnow does not grasp the far-reaching designs of the divine dragon; the teal and wigeon know not the gulf between themselves and the swan. Thus, plowmates mocked Chen Sheng for casting aside his plowshare, and the shallow-minded sneered at Kongming for clasping his knees in thought.

*Baopuzi* said: The edge of the *Chun Jun* blade is proven on rhinoceros and buffalo; the excellence of a compassionate steed is tested in clear trials. Thus, in idleness, the great ministers differ not from petty officials; in service, the swiftest steeds are no different from worn-out nags.

*Baopuzi* said: A vessel unfit for the ancestral temple will advance swiftly but retreat just as fast; one whose capacity rivals Yi or Lü may start late yet arrive early. Thus, the wren, though weary of flight, does not stray beyond the tip of the thistle; the fledgling phoenix, though rising slowly, gazes back before reaching the azure heavens.

*Baopuzi* said: After adversity comes prosperity; after deepest darkness, the morning’s radiance shines. Thus, the horse that once strained up Wu’s slopes now gallops a thousand *li* with ease; the fish that once coiled in the ninefold abyss now soars above the rainbow’s arc.

*Baopuzi* said: Nine breaks and four joints—this is how waterweeds display their grace; shattered boughs and crushed leaves—this is how orchids and angelicas enhance their fragrance. Thus, Guan Zhong emerged from shackles to forge a legacy of unity; the Marquis of Ying endured humiliation to inscribe his deeds in Qin.

*Baopuzi* said: When petty disputes are heard, profit aligns and enmity takes root; when good and ill diverge, affairs differ and knots form. Thus, the homely Momu, bearing her tumor, loathed the sight of Xishi’s beauty; the young princes Shangchen and Xiaobai once heard of Yanling’s retired plowman.

*Baopuzi* said: When purity and impurity mingle, the declining feel shame and regret; when strength and weakness are ranked apart, the mighty are envied. Thus, Han Xin’s sudden rise left common laborers sullen and resentful of his ascent; Wu’an’s towering merit drew Fan Sui’s artful words to undo his work.

*Baopuzi* said: For a fatal illness, no medicine is too bitter; for rotting wood, no carving is fitting. Thus, Bi Gan, unswerving in loyalty, had his heart carved out for his devotion; Tian Feng, perceiving the subtle signs, was executed for his forthright words.

*Baopuzi* said: The lone paulownia of Yiyang cannot stir mournful notes without strings; the solitary bamboo of Great Xia cannot sound its pure tones without a player. Thus, those of low rank, like Hou Ji and Li, cannot bring order to the commonwealth; those of meager authority, like Yi and Zhou, cannot attain great peace.

*Baopuzi* said: For the climber, peril lies in scaling too high; for the voyager, disaster arises from a heavy boat. Thus, in the west, Qin had Li Si, who rued his rise from Shangcai; in the east, Yue had Wen Zhong, who regretted his overreaching pride.

*Baopuzi* said: Hard and soft possess unchanging natures; integrity and flexibility have their innate qualities. Thus, though refined a hundred times, southern gold does not lose its essence; though beset by peril, the steadfast man does not forsake his principles.

*Baopuzi* said: Without the Dao, wealth and honor are not worth dwelling in; forsaking benevolence and abandoning righteousness, even a long life is not to be cherished. Thus, Bian Sui cast himself into the depths with a stone, and Zhong You met the blade without flinching.

*Baopuzi* said: High and low cannot be leveled with a single measure; rations and granaries cannot sway the transformed. Thus, Hui Shi fretted over the fewness of his followers’ carts, while Zhuang Zhou lamented the abundance of ways to catch fish.

*Baopuzi* said: Withdrawal and engagement differ as ice and coals; restlessness and stillness diverge as flight and sinking. Thus, Mo Di wore his calloused feet with a joyful face, while the recluse of Ji delighted in leaving the world behind.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who suit the heart grow close though their acquaintance be brief; those who offend the spirit drift apart though they meet for a lifetime. Thus, shared voices bring intimacy beneath the same canopy; divergent paths breed indifference even after a hundred years.

*Baopuzi* said: The grand ship with its kingfisher prow is a fine vessel for crossing rivers, yet if steered by a northern barbarian, it founders in the waves. The swift *Pushao* and *Hanxue* are fleet-footed steeds, yet without a Zaofu to drive them, they stumble on treacherous paths. The *Qingping* and *Haocao* are blades of the sharpest edge, yet without a master from Yue to wield them, they bring self-inflicted harm. Mighty soldiers and keen troops are divine tools for quelling chaos, yet without a wise leader, they hasten their own destruction.

*Baopuzi* said: Heaven’s order has an unchanging hierarchy of honor; those who violate rites face swift and severe punishment. Thus, the birds and beasts of the dark isles, though able to speak, may not join the ranks of sacred sacrifices; the *qiongqiong* insect, though bearing its burden, may not be called benevolent or righteous.

*Baopuzi* said: Slander and false words cannot be dispelled with clever speech; true resentment cannot be dissolved with empty gestures. To dispel them by improper means is like carrying ice to banish cold, piling furnaces to repel heat, chasing light to escape shadows, or boring a boat to stop a leak.

*Baopuzi* said: A wise ruler appoints officials without exceeding their capacities; a loyal minister holds office without overstepping his measure. To grant rank beyond one’s talent or entrust duties beyond one’s competence is like filling an ice bowl with boiling water, wrapping fire in reed floss, fastening ten thousand *jun* to a rotten rope, or loading double cargo onto a skiff.

*Baopuzi* said: A leopard-fur robe is not made for firewood carriers; the nine movements and six transformations of music are not composed for the deaf; lofty songs and distant harmonies are not sung for the dull-witted; and acts of selfless courage are not performed for those of meager virtue.

*Baopuzi* said: When the people’s wealth is exhausted yet demands never cease, when their strength is spent yet labor never ends, to expect no grievance or lament, to hope for lasting peace—this is like grafting branches by severing roots, patching the belly by cutting the back, broadening vision by gouging the eyes, or sharpening hearing by scooping out the ears.

*Baopuzi* said: Laws have no fixed form, yet one admires the flexibility of adapting to the times; achievements do not double those of the past, yet one delights in constant change to suit the vulgar—this is like trimming a tall horse to fit a low carriage, shaving the ankles to wear mismatched shoes, shortening a long sword to fit a narrow scabbard, or cutting a foot-wide jade disk to fit a cramped box.

*Baopuzi* said: The scaled fish that stills waves does not emerge from a narrow ravine; the towering tree where phoenixes perch does not flourish in a lowly mound; the maxims of the Nine Categories are not spoken by the vulgar; the lofty calculations of golden tablets do not arise from the minds of common folk. Seeing the branches of a hundred-*bao* tree, one knows its roots are not slender; beholding the vast and profound patterns of writing, one perceives the depth of its author.

*Baopuzi* said: A dense forest of mulberries cannot relieve the chill of a cypress; fertile fields near the city walls cannot lift the sleeve of a starving beggar. Yet silk cocoons and fine floss, when drawn out, yield endless bales of silk. Thus, those who see far value the root, while those who see near attend to the branches.

*Baopuzi* said: The coarse-bodied cling to form; the keen-witted grasp the spirit. Those who trace beginnings to ends find a thread to follow; those who grasp things before their signs appear need no external cause. Thus, seeing heaven and earth by day is not called true brightness; discerning the finest details at night is deemed unparalleled.

*Baopuzi* said: Ornate spring blossoms cannot long retain their freshness once severed from the branch; fish that swallow boats cannot survive once parted from water. Therefore, those whose reputation outstrips their substance will lack steadfast integrity; those whose rank exceeds their capacity will not escape the ruin of banditry.

*Baopuzi* said: One endures the pain of medicine and stone to cure life-threatening illness; one bears the weight and chill of armor to ward off arrows and blades; one endures the constraints of pure conduct to complete an outstanding career; one accepts heart-straining admonitions to avoid the confusion of changing course.

*Baopuzi* said: When phoenixes vie for grain in the courtyard, they are disdained by chickens and ducks; when dragons and *qilin* mingle with fodder, they are treated like the six livestock. Thus, Shang Lao dwelled in lofty seclusion to preserve his transcendent integrity, and Bian Sui plunged into the depths to safeguard his reputation beyond the world.

*Baopuzi* said: A well not dredged grows ever more muddy and clogged; fine grain not weeded is overrun by tares. Learning without reflection leads to abundant obstruction; teaching without precision results in lingering confusion and wasted effort.

*Baopuzi* said: Hoarding ten thousand gold in chests, though frugal and unused, differs little from poverty. Treasuring refined writings in the heart yet never committing them to paper differs little from the vulgar.

*Baopuzi* said: Nan Wei and Qing Qin, paragons of beauty, still required adornment to enhance their allure; Hui and You, though brilliantly gifted by nature, still needed the *Book of Documents* and *Announcements* to broaden their wisdom.

*Baopuzi* said: Crimson curtains and layered silk canopies obscure plain features; red lacquer and dazzling embellishments conceal withered wood. Thus, mastery of the Six Arts transforms the base into gentlemen, and the gathering of praise elevates the obscure to noble circles.

*Baopuzi* said: Where dense forests cast deep shade, feathered flocks gather like clouds; where dark abysses stretch vast, scaled creatures vie to arrive. When virtue flourishes and merit abounds, many take refuge; when flaws are overlooked and talents employed, distant hearts draw near.

*Baopuzi* said: Those with legs swift as fleeing shadows cannot gallop freely at Lü Liang; creatures that glide through waves and dive into depths cannot scale peaks or climb cliffs. Thus, Li Zhu could split autumn down at a hundred paces yet could not distinguish the elegance of the eight sounds; Zi Ye could harmonize with the most ethereal music yet could not point out black and white at a hand’s span.

*Baopuzi* said: When the four faculties of hearing are widely opened, even Xihe receives the light; when one empties oneself to a height of ten thousand *ren*, even the shallowest streams converge. Thus, the way of vast discernment expands to gather all uncommon knowledge; the brilliance of courtyard torches draws forth extraordinary scholars; the tree for slander ensures that faults are known; the drum for forthright admonition guarantees that candid words are offered.

*Baopuzi* said: None fail to praise Yao, yet Yao’s governance was not entirely flawless; all condemn Jie, yet Jie’s deeds were not entirely wrong. Thus, a single withered branch does not diminish the lushness of a dense forest; winter wheat sprouting in summer does not dispel the autumn’s severity. Xi Shi’s flaws could not lessen her beauty, for her beauty was abundant; Mo Mu’s virtues could not redeem her ugliness, for her ugliness was profound.

*Baopuzi* said: Body and reputation rarely both thrive; achievement and spirit seldom both endure. Those who fragment their virtue suffer but find security; those who adapt to the world enjoy pleasure but face many dangers.

Thus, birds of prey are trapped when they strike out from confinement; songbirds are cherished for their intelligence; jade and fine stones are cut and judged by their markings; the three metals are refined and shaped by their rarity; orchids and angelica are cut down for their fragrance; and patterned catalpa is felled for its resonant timber. For this reason, the kingfisher, seeing the alchemical elixir, soars into the black clouds; the divine phoenix, encountering Meng Xi, returns to the cinnabar cave; Zi Yong laments the grandeur of heavenly principles; and Zhuangzi mourns the sacrificial ox adorned in embroidery.

*Baopuzi* said: When ten thousand elk gather, even fierce tigers sheath their fangs; when a thousand birds and beasts assemble, birds of prey clench their talons. Thus, when slander spreads through the four lands, the Duke of Zhou cannot suppress it; when critics fill the roads, Zichan has no means to silence them.

*Baopuzi* said: The beauty of Wei Shi is such that no powder or rouge can enhance it; the qi of the solstices is such that no breath can augment it. Therefore, those who possess a noble and reclusive disposition do not strive to display their character to stand apart; those who embody a nature transcending the common do not trouble themselves with petty praise to conform.

*Baopuzi* said: A single phoenix halting in its majesty is lamented for its rarity; the cries of foxes and the calls of owls are feared for their abundance. Thus, when the talented fill the court, the search for worthies never ceases; when slanderers and flatterers wield power, the loyal and steadfast gnash their teeth.

*Baopuzi* said: Great strength need not belong to Meng Ben or Wu Huo; surpassing beauty need not be limited to Zheng Dan or Mao Qiang; swiftness need not be the sole domain of Hua Liu or Su Shuang; decisive judgment need not be unique to Shen Lü or Gan Jiang. Therefore, those who establish the legacy of the Uncrowned King need not be Qiu of Eastern Lu; those who perfect the virtue of reviving the withered need not be Chang of the Western Neighbor.

*Baopuzi* said: When the divine phoenix sings at dawn, though it brings no tangible benefit, none fail to listen raptly from below; when the owl perches at night upon the eaves, though it causes no harm, none fail to cover their ears and aim their arrows. Thus, kind words travel far and bring joy; harsh words, no matter how near, provoke offense. Like the sun and moon, which do not falter in their steadfast brightness, or the errant arrow, forgotten once it has flown.

*Baopuzi* said: A shadow does not deviate from the form; a name does not depart from reality. Thus, water that turns from its source cannot flow far to the east; untimely blossoms cannot preserve their splendor amid frost and snow.

*Baopuzi* said: Beasts with jagged fangs, though they crouch low, are still feared; insects that wield axes, though they cease their biting, retain no awe. Thus, the noble man in coarse cloth, though impoverished, cannot be slighted; the petty man in fine robes, though prosperous, is not to be esteemed.

*Baopuzi* said: The untamed qilin roams beyond the great wilderness, free from the peril of traps and snares; the divine roc soars above the peaks of the Dark Garden, avoiding the threat of nets and snares. Why must one dwell in crooked burrows, forever anxious? Why must one clutch reeds in the beak, ever fearful in countenance? Thus, those destined for the butcher’s block are surely fond of fodder; those meant for the cauldron’s fire are surely content to stand in the courtyard.

*Baopuzi* said: The wise treasure the ability to discern the echoes of events from a thousand years past and uncover the traces of rise and fall. The discerning prize the skill to recognize extraordinary talent amid hardship and draw forth those who can save the age. To seek counsel on intricate music from a mediocre craftsman is not the keen hearing of Yan Zhou; to consult common folk about far-reaching talent is not the singular vision of a true judge. Thus, discussing the use of exceptional tools with those who do not prize them is like asking the blind to name the five colors; plotting to promote the fiercely virtuous with those who envy their betters is like asking a fox to mend a fur coat.

*Baopuzi* said: The *lübo* bird endures hardship on perilous cliffs, unwilling to serve as a mere mouth guard; the *jiguang* beast hungers and thirsts in frozen wastes, unwilling to accept the savor of sacrificial meat; Guzhu would not trade fasting for the riches of Lutai; Zilian would not barter hardship for the abundance of Tongshan.

*Baopuzi* said: Those whose aspirations align do not regard mountains and seas as distant; those whose Dao diverges do not consider a footstep near. Thus, some traverse great distances to gather, while others remain close yet never meet.

*Baopuzi* said: Resplendent court robes are not the work of a single dye; the towering heights of Mount Song and Mount Tai are not the accumulation of a single basket. Thus, when the Nine Ministers were employed, the achievements of Kang and Ning flourished; when the Four and Seven were entrusted, the meritorious deeds of their aides shone forth.

*Baopuzi* said: The azure dragon soars to the heavens without wings; the *tenshe* serpent darts like lightning without feet; the turtle, though earless, hears keenly at the pass; the earthworm, though mouthless, projects its voice.
