Thus, Gao Yao, though mute, equaled the eloquent in achievement; Jin Ye, though blind, matched Li Zhu in clarity.

*Baopuzi* said: Those in high office do not necessarily possess talent fitting their rank; those with fine reputations do not necessarily live up to their names. Thus, saw teeth cannot chew; the tongue of a winnowing basket cannot distinguish flavors; the ears of a pot cannot discern sound; the nose of a corpse cannot detect scents; the eyes of a cauldron cannot perceive the moon’s light; the legs of a bed cannot traverse a short distance.

*Baopuzi* said: The common traveler cannot draw a strong bow and recognize the archery of Yang You; Yanzi cannot control the reins and whip and foresee the failure of Dongye. Thus, some cannot play chess yet recognize victory and defeat at a glance; some cannot pluck strings yet understand Zheng and Ya melodies by ear.

*Baopuzi* said: The tree that casts shade over ten thousand *mu* must grow from a towering peak; the flood that drowns the heavens must surge from the source of the Ban and Tong rivers. Extraordinary merit in an age apart must arise from a vessel beyond compare; the strategy to steady a tottering state must spring from a mind above the common. Thus, the nests of the *chongjiao* and *ming* insects lack wings to ride the wind; the streams and ditches hold no luminous jade.

*Baopuzi* said: The whirlwind fans the flames, causing the wildfire to burn brighter, yet the firefly and candle, encountering it, are extinguished; sweet rain and nourishing moisture cause fine plants to flourish, yet withered trees, receiving them, hasten to decay.

Crimson wheels and ornate hubs are the great treasures of the worthy, yet the unfit who usurp them invite disaster; feasts of ten thousand *zhong* are the ample reward for meritorious service, yet bestowing them upon the mediocre leads to ruin and starvation.

*Baopuzi* said: Rhinoceros hide is carved into armor for the exclusive use of generals on campaign; kingfisher plumes are woven into ornaments for the crowns of empresses and consorts. Though they leave the deep valley to ascend the towering tree, in the reckoning of these two creatures, it would have been better to remain hidden in the thickets, preserving life among the brambles. Thus, the sacred tortoise would rather drag its tail through the mud than be treasured in a casket; the marsh pheasant delights in pecking ten paces at a time, avoiding the calamity of barnyard fowl.

*Baopuzi* said: Partial talent is insufficient to meet all needs; a single strength cannot compensate for many weaknesses. Thus, the rooster knows the approach of dawn but cannot fathom the calculations of yin and yang; the swan recognizes midnight but cannot grasp the principles of sundials and shadows. The mountain turtledove foresees fair weather or rain but cannot comprehend astronomy; serpents and insects may know the dwelling of hidden springs but cannot master geography.

*Baopuzi* said: When prohibitions are unclear, severe punishments are imposed to quell disorder; when temple strategies are imprecise, armies are exhausted to invade neighbors. This is like reaping grain to eradicate locusts, felling trees to kill borers, ingesting poison to target fleas and lice, or demolishing a house to drive out sparrows and rats.

*Baopuzi* said: A sharp blade is born from dull stone; bright fire blazes from dark wood; precious pearls emerge from lowly clams; fine jade is hewn from ugly ore. Thus, one cannot measure the worth of Shun by his parents, nor gauge the merit of the Wei and Huo clans by their ancestors.

*Baopuzi* said: When aspirations are fulfilled, the countenance is joyful; when intentions fail, the face grows sorrowful. If the root is rotten, the branches wither; if the source is shallow, the flow is brief. What lies within must manifest without; what arises near must be evident afar.

*Baopuzi* said: Lovely features and alluring beauty vary in form and hue, yet delight the heart equally; strings and bells, metal and stone produce diverse tones, yet please the ear alike. Casting lines or sinking hooks, raising or lowering nets—all yield the same catch; establishing merit or leaving words, pursuing different paths—all share the same esteem.

*Baopuzi* said: Abundant profit invites grave harm; fine quality beckons disaster. Thus, southern birds are slaughtered for their patterned plumage; cave leopards die for their patterned hides. Sturgeon and carp accumulate until the dark abyss runs dry; deer and elk gather until the dense forest burns. Gold and jade are prized, yet bandits and thieves arrive; fame and rank rise, yet cares and reproaches accumulate.

*Baopuzi* said: When the autumn wind howls at night, fine gauze fans are discarded; when climbing perilous heights, light boats are abandoned. When weapons and war clouds gather, scholars of letters retreat; when mourning and chaos are pacified, warriors are demoted.

*Baopuzi* said: What is valued at ten thousand gold need not be seen to distinguish its worth; a branch spanning an armful need not be measured to judge its girth. Thus, beholding the vast waves surging to the heavens, one knows they did not arise from a stagnant puddle; observing the boundless expanse of a soaring goose, one knows it did not emerge from pedants of mere phrases.

*Baopuzi* said: Vermilion blossoms and green grass are not confined to crooked and withered stalks; purple mushrooms and fragrant herbs are not limited to saline and alkaline soil. Thus, those who receive the dark jade tablet to announce success are born from the gates of the Four Condemned; those who inherit the Mandate from the ancestral temple emerge from stubborn and clamorous households.

*Baopuzi* said: Good words about one’s dwelling resonate even from afar; crookedness and straightness misaligned repel even the near. Thus, in the wilds of Song, Mars retreated; in the Shang court, intimate kin fled. The Four Encirclers came from beyond the smallest breadth; deer and horses transformed within the palace walls.

*Baopuzi* said: Jing Ke and Zhu Hai did not display courage among the timid; Meng Ben and Feng Fu did not brandish halberds among rustic warriors. Eminent scholars and great minds do not adorn petty arguments for the shallow; enlightened and distinguished persons do not alter their daily conduct among the vulgar.

*Baopuzi* said: Bowing and yielding are not the demeanor to repel bandits; donning armor and helmets is not the adornment for the ancestral temple. Dangling sashes and jingling pendants cannot wield blades in combat; pacing with measured steps cannot rescue from fire or drowning.

*Baopuzi* said: Heaven and Earth nurture all things, yet the multitude does not recognize their kindness, for their benefits are boundless; the great person transforms through spirit, yet the masses do not perceive their influence, for they govern before things arise. Thus, what can be known is small; what is easy to predict is little.

*Baopuzi* said: E Huang, Ren Si, and Tai Ren were not chiefly praised for sericulture and weaving; Tang, Wu, and Han Gaozu were not celebrated for trivial virtues. Thus, those who gaze clearly at the Three Luminaries do not hasten to bend over well or valley; those who listen purely to the *Shao* and *Hu* do not deign to incline their ears to *Sangjian*.

*Baopuzi* said: The surface may not reveal what lies within; appearance may not reflect ability. Confucius resembled a stray dog; the Duke of Zhou seemed like rough-hewn timber; Gao Yao’s face was like a masked demon; Yi Yin’s form was like a withered corpse. Yet Longyang and Song Chao were like clay figurines crowned with luminous pearls; Jie Ru and Dong Zhong were like brocade and fine silk wrapped around dust.

*Baopuzi* said: Shun and Yu could not transform the utterly foolish, so their teachings failed among their sons and younger brothers; Jie and Zhou could not corrupt the supremely wise, so their evil did not taint the Three Benevolent Ones.

*Baopuzi* said: The utmost greatness has what it cannot alter; the extreme minuteness has what it cannot strip away. Thus, frost and chill cannot wither the lushness of beans and wheat; scorching heat and oppressive humidity cannot melt the snows of the Kunlun Mountains. Whirlwinds churn the sea but cannot make hidden springs surge; spring rains nourish all things but cannot make withered plants bloom.

*Baopuzi* said: The gem that weeps blood relies on *jianshi* and *zhushi* to cast its brilliance; the swords *Chenluan* and *Menglao* require Chu whetstones to temper their edges. The swift steed *Liuma* awaits Wangsun to achieve great distances; the fine timber *Lingzhi* depends on steaming and bending to perfect its virtue.

*Baopuzi* said: The perching phoenix and the soaring *yu* bird, though starving and parched, would not deign to be caged in the kitchen of the royal butcher; the *cheng-huang* and the celestial deer, though famished in confinement, would not delight in fodder and grain within the Dragon-Washing Stables. Thus, the old man who plucked the cicada forgot all things in the fragrant groves; the youth who angled with his line scorned the jade tablet of southern Chu.

*Baopuzi* said: Square and round, though mismatched in form, diverge in their courses and destinations. Thus, the celestial sphere is honored for its ceaseless motion, while the Earthly Sovereign is esteemed for her tranquil constancy. The seven luminaries and the four seasons accomplish their revolutions through cyclical flow; the Five Peaks and the Six Pillars stand as steadfast anchors through their towering stillness. Thus, Song Xing and Mo Di of Chu preserved their states through swift chariots; Gan Mu and Hu Ming repelled invasions through nonaction.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who find contentment in hills and gardens may suffer bodily want yet enjoy spiritual peace; those who exhaust themselves for the sake of others may appear at ease in form yet labor in heart. Thus, the man who waters his garden with an urn delights more than the lord of the terrace; those who dine on ferns and pulse find it finer than the feast of tripods; the one who staff in hand departed from Bin may seem as lean as the moon in late winter; those who wait through the night for dawn wear themselves with toil and shorten their lives.

*Baopuzi* said: Between benevolence and cruelty lies a chasm as vast as heaven and abyss; between good and evil, the difference is as stark as being and nonbeing. The *zouyu* treads lightly enough to walk on air, while jackals and wolves hunt in packs to harm the living. Yu Qing cast aside his ministerial seal to aid the destitute; Hua Gong relinquished his three offices to yield to the worthy. Li Si, resentful of surpassing talent, put Han Fei to death; Pang Juan, vexed by inferiority, mutilated Sun Bin.

*Baopuzi* said: When one employs another’s strengths, no talent is ever discarded; when one fixates on another’s flaws, nothing in the world is without fault.

Thus, sheer silken gauze and mist-like chiffon are the height of elegant attire, yet cannot withstand the flight of arrows; the *Shenlü* and *Juque* swords are peerless for cleaving and slaying, yet cannot be used to pick thorns from the foot.

*Baopuzi* said: Minor flaws do not mar a great vessel, nor do petty ailments burden a towering talent. The sun and moon bear the blemish of birds and insects, yet this does not mar their radiance across the heavens; the Yellow River carries the turbidity of silt, yet this does not hinder its torrents from surmounting mountains. A tree blocking the path cannot justify abandoning Guan Zhong; seizing fields cannot justify despising Xiao He; stealing a wife cannot justify dismissing Xiangru; accepting gold cannot justify casting out Chen Ping.

*Baopuzi* said: Tigers and leopards cannot rend and tear amidst the waves; winged serpents cannot soar on days without mist. When seizing pheasants and hares, even phoenixes cannot match hawks and falcons; when drawing the plow, even dragons and unicorns cannot rival a pair of oxen. Thus, warriors and brave men are of no use in an age of tranquility; towering scholars and untethered talents are not prized in times of strife and competition.

*Baopuzi* said: When both are hobbled by the neck, the swift *qilu* is no different from a lame donkey; when deprived of forests and confined to cages, apes and gibbons are no better than badgers and raccoon dogs. When sheathed and never unsheathed, the *Longquan* blade is no different from a leaden knife; when great talent is given petty tasks, the most brilliant are no better than mediocre kings. To seek the tracks of a thousand *li* from a tethered steed, or demand the achievements of a master craftsman from a petty artisan—if one calls this discernment, I do not believe it.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who discard bitter herbs to chew on mugwort must lack a palate for sweetness; those who cast aside jade to gather pebbles must lack the eye to discern treasures. To scorn the *Nine Shao* and delight in rustic tunes is to know such a one cannot commune with spirits or emulate the soaring phoenix. To forsake the exceptional and rely on the common is to know such a one cannot order the proper relations of heaven and earth or attain great peace.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who grasp the ultimate principle of openness and obstruction do not fret over adversity; those who understand that fate is of nature do not rejoice in the success of their Dao. Thus, though mired in the deepest abyss, they cast off resentment and vexation; though clad in the robes of the imperial chamber, they show no sign of pride. Such was the man who thrice took office and thrice resigned.

*Baopuzi* said: Prosperity and decline depend on fate; success and failure cannot measure a scholar’s worth. Gain and loss lie in suitability; glory and disgrace cannot gauge talent. Timing and destiny cannot be sought by force; encounters cannot be defied by cunning. Thus, the Venerable Father was a husband abandoned by an old woman; Han Xin was a starving beggar; Lord Xiao was a petty clerk; Qing Bu was a fugitive convict.

When the dragon’s bearing moves among vipers and lizards, when the phoenix’s wings fold among sparrows and swallows, even those dragons to come will mistake them for their own kind.

*Baopuzi* said: The Four Spirits, though hidden in obscurity, become omens of great peace; the reclusive man, though dwelling in seclusion, becomes the treasure of a kingdom. Why must one crow at dawn or champ the bit, fettered by the anxieties of duty? There is the use of others’ usefulness, and there is the use of my own usefulness. Those who follow their nature do not let fame disturb their harmony; those who cultivate life do not let things burden themselves.

*Baopuzi* said: He who measures talent before bestowing office does not seek achievement beyond the vessel’s capacity; he who gauges ability before accepting duty does not bear responsibility beyond his strength. Thus, one who extinguishes a firefly’s glow need not trouble the vast sea; one who lifts a few pounds need not call upon Wu Huo. To haul firewood or transport salt is no task for the hooves of a fine steed; to assign trifling duties is no way to employ a man of singular excellence.

*Baopuzi* said: The streams of fields and ditches cannot bear the weight of a great ship; a vessel of a sheng or a he cannot hold a thousand zhong. A flickering light cannot reveal the shadow of the faintest glimmer; mediocre talent cannot distinguish the vessel of surpassing worth. For what Heaven lends is rooted in the source of wisdom and brightness.

*Baopuzi* said: The people of Ying praise the lewd croaking of frogs in the streets and belittle the harmonious tones of the *liujing*; the common man delights in flattering words that please the ear and despises sound counsel that benefits conduct. Thus, Song Yu abandoned the pure music of *Yanling*, and the wise man seeks out far-reaching plans born of unique insight.

*Baopuzi* said: Though jade and stone pile up like mountains, there will still be flawed vessels among them; though the Deng Forest stretches a thousand *li*, there will still be crooked and withered trees. When judging treasures, one must not discard great beauty because of minor blemishes; when speaking of greatness, one must not abandon the many because of a few faults. The rebel against his lord was Li Guang and Chen Ping, yet they devised six stratagems to secure the throne; the bandit leader was Peng Yue, yet he rendered great merit in aiding the founding of a dynasty.

*Baopuzi* said: The Five Peaks stand towering, yet their hidden flaws do not diminish their soaring heights; the vast sea stretches boundless, yet the filth it contains does not lessen its immeasurable breadth. Thus, the Nine Virtues esteem magnanimity to win the people, and Confucius embraced all with universal love to advance the worthy.

*Baopuzi* said in *Guang Pi*, Chapter Thirty-Nine: To establish virtue and fulfill one’s word, to act with integrity and keep one’s conduct pure—this is wealth. What need is there for heaps of jade and silk? To hold one’s aspirations high, neither yielding nor debasing oneself—this is nobility. What need is there for the purple sash of high office? The common folk cannot recognize such a man’s measure; the mediocre cannot fathom his scale. This is true loftiness. What need is there to tread upon clouds and walk upon rainbows? None can plumb the depths of his abyss; none who seek find any lack in him. This is true profundity. What need is there for the Yangtze or the sea? If the Four Seas are well-provided, though one’s home holds only a hanging chime of poverty, one may still be without envy of those who smelt mountains and boil the sea. Though one dwells among birds and beasts, one may still be without thirst for crimson wheels and ornate chariots.

*Baopuzi* said: The hidden spirit awaits auspicious clouds to soar; the soaring swan mounts the strong wind to rise into the void. The white fish awaits the maiden’s hair to leap; the white pheasant comes when the Duke of Zhou appears. Jiang Ziya met King Wen and cast his fishing line at Panxi; Han Xin and Ying Bu met Emperor Gao and unleashed their talents to restore order.

*Baopuzi* said: He who clarifies his spirit in the Mysterious Unity may forget the form of his body; he who extends his noble integrity beyond external things may leave wealth and honor behind. Thus, the crippled man of *Zhili* delighted in the workings of creation; the old man of Ji and the elder of Song dwelt in the peaks of Mount Song and found contentment.

*Baopuzi* said: Coarse patterns cannot encompass perfection; skilled work cannot embrace all tasks. Thus, the celestial bodies, though bright, may be obscured; mountains and rivers, though vast, may shift. Gold and jade, though hard, may be softened; solid ice, though dense, may be parted. The Duke of Zhou could not climb the perilous peaks with Bo Shi; Confucius could not compete in skill with the swimmer of Lüliang in the hundred-*ren* gorge.

*Baopuzi* said: The crashing thunder cannot refine its sound to match the harmony of bells and stones; the sun and moon cannot withhold their radiance to alter the favor of partial illumination. The great rivers cannot hasten their banks to suit the urgency of those who cross; the Five Peaks cannot level their heights to gratify the wishes of climbers. Thus, the broad carriage cannot narrow its wheels to squeeze through a narrow path; the lofty scholar cannot suppress his integrity to conform to a cramped world.

*Baopuzi* said: Yin and yang employ their vastness to nurture all things; the Three Luminaries use their universal light to illuminate all. Mount Song and Mount Hua conceal their flaws to remain vast; the Northern Sea contains its filth to be called great. The great scholar advances the Dao through inclusiveness; the far-reaching teaching embraces the multitude with broad love.

*Baopuzi* said: The shell of the spirit turtle need not be used in battle; the horn of the qilin and the claws of the phoenix need not be employed in combat. Thus, the outstanding man does not lay aside his sword in times of peace; the watchman does not cease his vigilance in times of security.

*Baopuzi* said: Fine gold from the south does not devalue itself for dwelling in obscurity; jade and fine stone do not cease to be pure for residing in the depths. He who is devoted to the Dao does not alter his course because of adversity; he who upholds integrity does not compromise for lack of reward.

*Baopuzi* said: He who ascends the Mysterious Garden perceives the lowliness of hills and mounds; he who floats upon the vast sea recognizes the narrowness of ponds and marshes. Only when one peruses the Nine Classics does one realize the deep obscurity of those who see no farther than a wall; only when one hears the supreme Dao does one know the many delusions of the vulgar.

*Baopuzi* said: The source of primordial chaos has no clear and limpid streams; the root of a hair’s breadth has no branches that embrace; the ember of an inch has no far-reaching heat; the space within a crack has no radiant assembly; the crooked hook has no straight shadow; the uneven surface has no level base.

*Baopuzi* said: Without beholding the brilliance of jade and fine stone, one does not perceive the worthlessness of tiles and pebbles; without witnessing the splendor of tigers and leopards, one does not know the plainness of dogs and sheep.

Listening to the nine movements of the "White Snow," one then realizes the extreme vulgarity of the "Barbarian Songs"; recognizing the vastness of refined elegance, one then laments the stubborn crudeness of the unlearned.

*Baopuzi* said: A jade bowl without utility is not as good as a whole vessel of clay; a shred of brocade embroidery is not as fine as sturdy, intact hempen cloth. Thus, the impropriety of Xia Ji is not as pure as the solitary exile’s integrity; the many sins of the rich and noble are not as virtuous as the poverty-stricken who tread the Way.

*Baopuzi* said: Fierce beasts do not strike beyond their measure, nor do hawks and falcons recklessly spread their wings to attack. If one’s inner calculations do not gauge virtue, and one’s outward ambitions do not measure one’s strength, it is like a light feather sinking into a raging furnace, flying snow melting in a boiling cauldron, morning mushrooms testing the *Ganjiang* sword, or lambs and calves challenging a mighty tiger.

*Baopuzi* said: When the three luminaries are obscured in the heavens, the clear light darkens on earth; when roots and grasses stumble here, branches and twigs wither there. When the Way is lost nearby, calamity reaches afar; when governance errs above, the people suffer below.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who focus on the distant may lose sight of what is near; those who govern the exterior may suffer ills arising within. One who covers the head does not necessarily keep the feet dry; one who shields the belly does not necessarily protect the back. Thus, when the First Emperor of Qin began building the Great Wall to block the Hu, disaster arose within his inner chambers; when Emperor Wu of Han raised banners across ten thousand *li*, turmoil erupted behind his palace walls.

*Baopuzi* said: Human talent has no fixed value, and the use of tools follows no constant principle. The straightforward consider adapting to the world as extraordinary; those who serve and guide deem aligning with the times as mastery. Thus, when black ice forms, the five luminaries are cast aside; when scorching summer blazes, fur robes and stoves retreat. When high-flying birds gather, fine bows are drawn; when cunning hares abound, swift hounds run. When weapons are raised, warriors rise; when the *Shao* and *Xia* music plays, scholars of letters emerge.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who stir swift currents to hasten their journey to the sea cannot turn their backs on the Five Cities and leap over Mount Jishi; those who unfurl emerald leaves and sprout crimson blossoms cannot abandon the lush grasses and depart from flourishing branches. To lose the source and forsake the root is seldom not to wither; to rebel against the sages and violate the classics is not to achieve great success.

*Baopuzi* said: The four great rivers distinguish their sources, the five rivers divide their flows, rushing toward the lowlands and pouring into the sea—different paths, same destination. Colors differ yet all are radiant; sounds vary yet all are mournful; fragrances are not uniform yet all are sweet; flavors are unequal yet all are delicious.

*Baopuzi* said: Things are valued for their utility, not their adornments; customs are transformed by virtue, not by words that miss the essence. Thus, plain cloth can ward off the cold—there is no need for sable and fox; unadorned simplicity can craft things—there is no need for ornate eloquence.

*Baopuzi* said: When the rushing wind stills and the air calms, tumbleweeds halt and mountains stand firm; when the great net is spread, a myriad eyes align in order. Thus, there has never been a ruler who cherished humility yet whose subjects grew insolent, nor one who despised treasures yet whose people remained impoverished.

*Baopuzi* said: Affairs may begin subtly yet become manifest; things may be governed nearby yet extend afar. Thus, tending a small pond can draw deep-swimming fish from rivers and seas; enriching the woods on the sunlit side can attract divine birds from the cinnabar caves. Setting images in basins and bowls can summon azure dragons from the dark skies; casting ashes into a foot of water can transform the moon’s charioteer across the cosmos. Therefore, when Duke Wen of Jin turned his chariot for the brave worm, stalwart warriors flocked to him; when Goujian of Yue bowed to the enraged frog, his soldiers scorned death. When the Nine-Nine multiplication table was displayed, the hidden talents of the herdsmen arrived; when the bones of the dead were buried, the benevolence of the Three Kingdoms spread.

*Baopuzi* said: Fertile soil lies in the grassroots, yet withered leaves bear blossoms; leading the people by example transforms them without words. Thus, the Tang dynasty achieved great peace with deer-skin robes; Duke Huan of Qi curbed extravagance by discarding purple. The construction of Zhanghua Palace led to the excess of grand mansions; the abandonment of the Terrace of Dew spread the wind of serene silence.

*Baopuzi* said: The wise discern the rise and fall in the fading echoes of the past; the enlightened perceive the workings of fate in the unseen subtleties of the yet-unformed. Thus, the people of Yue saw the signs of Duke Huan of Qi’s decline in his unnoticed illness; Jizi recognized the disaster of King Zhou’s Deer Terrace at the first appearance of ivory chopsticks.

*Baopuzi* said: Heaven and earth cannot abandon spring and autumn to complete the year; a wise ruler cannot forsake punishment and virtue to achieve order. Thus, executing the noble establishes authority; rewarding the lowly encourages goodness. When punishment reaches the high, treacherous sprouts are crushed—this is not the work of the weak; when benevolence extends to the lowly, distant people are drawn—this is not something stinginess can achieve.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who sail the vast seas must carefully observe the winds and tides to secure the blessing of safe passage. Those who govern well must tremble at the thought of gains and losses to enjoy enduring fortune. Thus, what a benighted ruler dismisses is precisely what a wise ruler values; what a doomed state discards is what a well-ordered age practices.

*Baopuzi* said: A hair’s breadth of error in the mechanism throws off the target by a *xun* and a *chang*; a misstep in giving and taking here confuses right and wrong there. When good and evil are indistinguishable, the constant norms are disrupted; when merit and fault are unmeasured, all achievements collapse. Thus, a wise ruler’s rewards are like spring rain—without the excess of prolonged downpours; his punishments are like autumn frost—without the severity of untimely cold.

*Baopuzi* said: The one who wields the bright scale cannot be deceived in what is valued; the one who relies on the law cannot favor what is loved. Thus, those who gain others first gain themselves; those who lose others first lose themselves. There has never been one who gained themselves yet lost others, nor one who lost themselves yet gained others.

*Baopuzi* said: A wise ruler personally wields authority and favor, never entrusting others with the instruments of power; a benighted ruler inverts the grip of shield and spear, though in name exalted, his power is gone. Thus, controlling rewards and punishments to win the people is how Tian Chang seized Qi; monopolizing authority and power to dominate the court is how Wang Mang usurped the Han.

*Baopuzi* said: Constant systems cannot await change; a single path cannot respond to boundless circumstances. One cannot carve a mark on a boat to seek a lost sword, nor glue a zither’s peg to harmonize a pure note. Thus, jade-green canopies are not raised in clear skies, nor crimson wheels employed in crossing rivers. When flavor is bland, salt is added; when a pot boils over, water is poured and fire reduced.

*Baopuzi* said: Cinnabar-inscribed iron charters, sacrificial oaths sworn in blood—these cannot remedy the harm of broken promises, and so one cannot rely on knotted cords for governance. The five punishments and nine expeditions, the terror of exterminating clans—these are insufficient to halt the treachery of covetous men, and thus one cannot transform the realm with shield dances. Therefore, the *Book of Documents* contains texts for successive ages, and the *Book of Changes* teaches adaptation to the times.

*Baopuzi* said: Those with the clarity to discern truth cannot be deceived by falsehood; those with the wisdom to fathom depths cannot be misled by shallowness. Otherwise, one might mistake vipers for winged dragons, or foxes and owls for qilin and phoenixes.

*Baopuzi* said: The world may praise in unison, yet the praised are not necessarily worthy; the crowd may clamor in condemnation, yet the condemned are not necessarily evil. Thus, those who flatter and assent are not as discerning as those who examine deeds and test utility; those who resist and reject are not as wise as those who heed words and assess results. Then flatterers will not advance on empty talk, nor will true ability be set aside for its solitude. The slow and clumsy will not aspire to the grand chariot, while the swift and spirited will gallop like lightning. How then can great deeds remain unachieved? How can the distant Way remain unattained?

*Baopuzi* said: Rotten wood cannot withstand the gale of a collapsing mountain; a cliff with fissures cannot endure the surge of a sky-reaching wave. Thus, the seven centuries of Zhou’s mandate and the thirty generations of its rule were not solely the work of Mu Ye’s victory; the defeats of turning spears and the fall of Lu Tai were not born in the dawn of Jiazi. Their strength had long endured; their ruin had long been foretold.

*Baopuzi* said: To esteem the distant and despise the near is the common affliction of men; to trust hearsay and doubt what is seen is the perennial flaw of ages. Thus, the King of Qin sighed over Han Fei’s writings and longed to know the man, while Emperor Wu of Han was moved by Sima Xiangru’s prose and felt as though they lived in different eras. Yet when they obtained them, they could not elevate them—one accepted slander and executed him, the other left him in idle obscurity. This is like the man who loved the painted dragon but paled at the sight of the real one.

*Baopuzi* said: If a luminous pearl does not shine at night, it differs not from desert gravel; if the transformed *kun* does not soar to the heavens, it is no different from the sparrow. If the swift steed *Mianju* swallows its voice, it mingles with the mute; if exceptional talent is suppressed, it dwells among the mediocre. Thus, the loach and minnow defile the crimson *jiao* in stagnant pools, and the sluggish nag pollutes the swift *lu* in the fields—these fail to recognize that in stillness they are the same, but in motion they differ.

*Baopuzi* said: Cast gold and jade upon the road, and travelers will halt; abandon brocade in the mire, and onlookers will gasp. Yet if one exiles a man of transcendent worth to the company of the vulgar, or discards a tool of statecraft in a place of stagnation, critics do not protest his humiliation, nor do the enlightened rescue him from his plight. Some prize his writings yet neglect his person; others use his strategies yet forget his merit. This malady has endured for ages.

*Baopuzi* said: If a spring does not plumb the depths, it cannot yield a river to embrace mountains; if a peak does not scrape the heavens, it cannot bear clouds to blanket the sky. If wealth is not abundant, its generosity cannot be vast; if talent is not far-reaching, its words cannot be rich. Thus, seeing a tusk a yard long, one knows it does not come from a mouth of inches; observing branches a hundred fathoms long, one knows they do not sprout from a twig.

*Baopuzi* said: The numinous phoenix rises at dawn from the cinnabar cave and by dusk gathers on the sacred mound. Before the sun shifts its shadow, it traverses the nine realms, riding the wind and treading the clouds without pause or hindrance—all by virtue of its six mighty pinions. Great talent is likewise the six pinions of the state.

*Baopuzi* said: The waters of the Qi and Wei, though one forgets to return, cannot surge far without strings; the sound of vibrating dust cannot stir sorrow without an instrument. Thus, virtues that surpass the common and rise above the crowd cannot establish merit in times that have not yet arrived.

*Baopuzi* said: Vermilion and green adornments do not bloom on withered branches; torrents from collapsing mountains do not arise from dried-up springs. The flickering flame of a firefly cannot reveal the forms of all things; a mind consumed by profit and power cannot spread a fragrant breeze through the ages.

*Baopuzi* said: If a deep abyss does not pierce the earth, it cannot contain the *chilong* or disgorge a boat-swallowing fish; if a towering mountain does not scrape the heavens, it cannot conceal jade or summon clouds and rain. If one’s virtue does not transcend the common, one cannot gather a fine reputation or achieve solid substance; if one’s resolve does not stand apart from the crowd, one cannot attain success or inscribe great deeds. Yet the common man performs a cicada’s-wing good in the morning and expects the benefit of hills by evening—like planting millet and expecting a bountiful harvest while seated.

*Baopuzi* said: One who bears no mark of transcending the common yet seeks a reputation above the world, or who lacks the foundation of the Dao and arts yet schemes for cliques and factions—such a one hopes to win noble esteem in the present age and spread a virtuous name to posterity. This is like hiking up one’s robes to cross the sea or standing on tiptoe to leap to the nine heavens.

*Baopuzi* said: A clay dragon, though adorned with brilliant colors, cannot answer the call of auspicious clouds; a carved bird, though inlaid with black and yellow, cannot undertake the flight of riding the wind; a straw dog, though decorated with gold and kingfisher feathers, cannot tread the light to sudden speed; mediocre talent, though laden with favor and emolument, cannot make the heavens clear and the earth level.

*Baopuzi* said: When weak poison is spread, even the rats at its edge are rotted through; when a bright torch is raised at night, even the insects below are drawn to their deaths.

When lavish offerings of fodder and grain are prepared, the cauldrons and platters await them; when petty talent is tasked with great responsibility, tears of blood flow like streams. The warnings of Sang Hongyang and the lessons of Fan Li and Shuxiang are clear indeed.

*Baopuzi* said: The surging waves of a vast sea, though they rise for ten thousand *li*, cannot sweep away the dust of a mountain peak; the howling wind that fells trees of a thousand *ren* cannot uproot the frailest blade of grass. The might of the *zhiqu* and the ferocity of the tiger cannot intimidate mosquitoes and gadflies; talent that surpasses the age cannot align with the vulgar crowd.

*Baopuzi* said: A steadfast will is the master of merit and fame; unyielding diligence is the teacher of all virtues. One who does not halt before the perilous and steep will surely reach the towering ridges; one who does not resent hardship while accumulating goodness will surely perpetuate a noble reputation.

*Baopuzi* said: Though the magpie does not live long, acupuncture and stone needles cannot be called anything but instruments that save lives; though scholars often suffer poverty and low status, the classics cannot be called anything but tools for cultivating virtue. There are times when sowing yields no harvest, yet farming cannot be abandoned; there are times when benevolence and righteousness meet with misfortune, yet one’s conduct and deeds must not slacken.

*Baopuzi* said: A heavy load that does not cease will sink my boat; advancing blindly while forgetting to retreat will endanger my person. When scorpions swarm to attack the root, though the branch may seem secure for now, its collapse is inevitable.

*Baopuzi* said: Dark clouds gather for the dragon’s ascent—no lizard or skink can summon them; the gale roars for the tiger’s charge—no fox or raccoon dog can conjure it. Thus, when a great person receives the Mandate, scholars of extraordinary talent gather; when jade and silk are sought in seclusion, the hidden worthies of hills and gardens emerge.

*Baopuzi* said: Metal breaks because of its hardness, water endures because of its softness; mountains collapse because of their height, valleys remain secure because of their lowliness. Thus, one who holds to the feminine principle avoids the calamity of contending for dominance; one who exalts others excessively invites the trouble of resentment.

*Baopuzi* said: Han Xin concealed his valor beneath the crotch of a ruffian, yet this did not diminish his dragon-like leaps or tiger-like gaze; Fan Ju concealed his brilliance while drowning in a latrine basket, yet this did not hinder his phoenix-like soaring. Some became kings facing south, others governed the realm as chief ministers. Thus, the light swan soars through the void by rising and dipping; the truly talented bide their time by bending and stretching.

*Baopuzi* said: The lowly firefly refuses to cry to the heavens like a rat-swallower; the pure cicada, though starving, disdains the filthy fullness of the dung beetle. Thus, Yu Gou rejected the gifts of Zheng Yang, and Zeng Shen found no beauty in the treasures of Jin and Chu.

*Baopuzi* said: A gentle breeze cannot stir the waves of a great sea; the faintest flicker cannot move a bell of ten thousand *jun*. Thus, Zhuangzi of Qiyuan lamented the loss of his axe; Bo Shi grieved for his zither, mourning the severed strings. A brief tune cannot summon a grand and resplendent harmony; mere force cannot sway a heart devoted to tranquility.

*Baopuzi* said: The bear and brown bear do not compete in agility with the fox; the golden eagle does not contend in combat with the small sparrowhawk. Thus, Zhang Er concealed his strength as a gatekeeper, and Zhu Hai hid his courage as a butcher.

*Baopuzi* said: The fish is deceived by fragrant bait and hangs suspended; the tiger is trapped by the fox’s cage and dies within. What cannot be lured by fishing lines must be a horned dragon; what cannot be ensnared by traps must be a unicorn or *lin*.*

*Baopuzi* said: Those who soar in the clouds know not the filth of dwelling in mud; those who occupy high rank seldom empathize with the toil of the masses. Yet when roots rot, even the finest timber cannot preserve its thousand days of flourishing; when the people resent, even Yao and Shun cannot rely on the blessings of an enduring reign.

*Baopuzi* said: Though a tree takes root on a sacred mountain, Master Carpenter Shi will still lay his axe to rest upon it; though a small fish dwells in the Dragon Pool, the fisherman will still cast his nets. When mosquitoes gather on an eagle’s head, the *jiluan* bird dare not peck; when a rat lurks beside a tiger, the wildcat and hound dare not glare.

*Baopuzi* said: The sacred turtle remains silent, yet good and ill fortune are revealed without form; the spring frog croaks endlessly, yet its ugly noise is a nuisance to the ear. Thus, a rare sound produces a mighty echo; few words carry unmistakable sincerity.

*Baopuzi* said: The customs of those who sit with legs sprawled despise the refined bearing of propriety; the lands of the disheveled hair abhor the caps and robes of civilization. Thus, the loyal and upright are cast out in an age ruled by slander; the refined cannot be tolerated in a world that hates integrity.

*Baopuzi* said: Pouring water cannot quench the burning grass of a marsh; a handful of earth cannot dam the boiling rapids of the Stone Pillar; a single blade cannot fell the forests of a long isle; a lone voice cannot halt the wrongs of cliques and factions.

*Baopuzi* said: A thousand sheep cannot withstand a single tiger; ten thousand sparrows cannot resist one hawk. The clustered torches of the courtyard cannot match the last rays of Xihe; a hundred drums beaten together cannot equal the lingering rumble of thunder.

Thus, when mediocre men fill the halls of enlightenment, they cannot bring the constant norms into proper order; yet a single man of talent and resolve suffices to root all affairs in enduring growth.

*Baopuzi* said: Unmerited success is like the winter blossoms of forest plants; steadfast adherence to the Dao in adversity is like bamboo and pine braving the frost. Therefore, those who discern fortune and misfortune through solitary insight—though pressed by sharp blades—will not lose their rectitude nor alter their path. How could they fawn and smile to conform to vulgar ways? Those who embody square integrity and dwell in uprightness—though tempted by enfeoffed lands—will not betray their nature to chase the times. How could they tread crooked paths to seek acceptance?

*Baopuzi* said: The rumbling of thunderous chariots cannot produce sound in deaf ears; the radiant brilliance of the heavens cannot cast its light into secluded valleys; the bitter chill of congealed ice cannot wither the blossoms of winter plants; the scorching winds that melt stone cannot fell the trees of barren hills. Thus, the highest virtue cannot be swayed.

*Baopuzi* said: A bow drawn taut, a crossbow’s trigger poised, an arrow nocked and string drawn—these are most to be feared, yet the bold pheasant rushes forward without suspicion. A benighted government in a disordered state, where integrity is despised and talent envied—this is most inscrutable, yet the greedy vie for it without hesitation. Thus, flying blades strike unawares, and ruin arrives suddenly without recovery. Therefore, what delights the foolish is what the enlightened lament; what the mediocre pursue is what the truly wise abandon.

*Baopuzi* said: When the wind does not cease, fans are not used; when the sun does not set, candles do not shine; when blossoms do not fall, fruit does not form; when riverbanks do not erode, valleys do not fill. When the Nine Provinces are at peace, the merits of Han and Bai do not stand out; when wise rulers succeed one another, the achievements of Yi and Huo cannot be realized. Thus, only in times of illness is the skilled physician valued; only in an age of chaos is loyalty and integrity prized.

*Baopuzi* said: Those who crave glory thus desire abundant praise, while those who fear disgrace are quick to resent slander. But those who penetrate the essence of the One, whose fate aligns with creation, who blend fullness and emptiness into a single strand, and equalize gain and loss with a single finger—love and hate have never bound them, poverty and success cannot disturb their harmony.

*Baopuzi* said: Those whose spirit is not muddied by gain or loss are the most refined; those whose harmony is unstained by advantage or harm are the purest. Vast and boundless, they cannot be measured by gourds or cups, nor traced by half-steps. The rarest sounds are thus the greatest, and harmony in solitude exalts one’s worth. Though the cosmos stretches far and wide, it does not diminish their vastness. Life and death are great indeed, yet they do not alter their steadfastness. Why trouble over trifling distinctions?

*Baopuzi* said: Where trees grow dense, the carpenter enters; where pearls gleam bright, insects rend them. Stones containing gold are smelted; herbs bearing medicine are uprooted. Sharp blades dull first, and mournful strings snap quickly. Used to suit oneself, they are the treasures of the perfected; talents that meet the world’s demands bring disaster upon their possessors.

*Baopuzi* said: When targets are set, arrows fly toward them; when fair names arise, slander and defamation attack. Hoarded treasures, though not sought, invite resentment; overflowing vessels and arrogant ambitions, though not summoned, bring calamity.

*Baopuzi* said: Treasures worth a thousand cities cannot be purchased by the poor and humble; vessels of transcendent worth cannot be recognized by the shallow and vulgar. Yet a jewel of a foot’s length does not dim its luster for lack of appreciation; men of surpassing virtue do not diminish their integrity for lack of opportunity. Rejoicing in Heaven’s decree and submitting to fate—what cause for resentment or blame?

*Baopuzi* said: The great roc has no use for dawn’s vigilance; the mighty elephant has no talent for swift pursuit. Thus, Jiang Wan failed in a hundred-mile march yet became a paragon of the Three Excellencies; Chen Ping struggled in managing his household yet harbored the strategies of the Six Ruses.

*Baopuzi* said: Clarity and obscurity are matters of talent, natural and unadorned; poverty and success are matters of timing, contingent on circumstances and beyond human effort. Lü Shang was not early-obscured and late-wise, yet he cast aside his fishing line and barely met his moment; Han Xin was not initially timid and later brave, yet he rose from peril and hardship to greatness.

*Baopuzi* said: A galloping steed cannot retrieve an arrow already loosed; a thousand pieces of gold cannot erase a single careless word. Thus, preventing small faults is better than lavishing remedies; restraining speech is wiser than tireless pursuit.

*Baopuzi* said: The ardent knight loves his country as his family and serves his lord as his parent—thus, disloyal acts are not among his faults. The benevolent man regards others as himself and treats the distant as the close—thus, unforgiving grudges are not his burden.

*Baopuzi* said: If thick ice does not form and white snow does not accumulate, the verdant pine’s luxuriance goes unnoticed; if customs do not decay and moral teachings do not decline, the purity of steadfast conduct remains indistinct. Dwelling in a troubled state in lowly obscurity, thus Zhuang and Lai upheld their lofty disdain for renown; living in a disordered realm amid hunger and cold, thus Zeng and Lie spread their fame for forgetting wealth.

*Baopuzi* said: Heaven dwells on high yet gazes below, thus its net, though wide-meshed, does not let slip; the divine is keen and upright, thus its Dao rewards the true and punishes the false. Therefore, when kindness and harmony spread through the Nine Regions, the seven luminaries align in the dark heavens; when cruelty and harm afflict all things, the two qi falter in the four seasons.

*Baopuzi* said: Heaven’s ranks possess boundless dignity; human titles hold no honor that defies virtue. Thus, though Confucius was but a commoner, he is worshipped through a hundred generations; though Xin and Gui were emperors, even servants disdain to be compared to them. The Elder Shang’s body grew more lowly, yet his name grew more noble; You and Li’s positions grew more exalted, yet their crimes grew more glaring. Thus, the life of the King of Qi does not match the tomb of Liu Hui; the palace of the King of Qin does not equal the lane of Kangcheng.

*Baopuzi* said: Echoes and shadows cannot manifest without form and sound; lingering blessings cannot be summoned without virtue.

Thus, when Yao of Tang governed for over seventy years, only then did auspicious stars shine forth; when Lord Yang accumulated virtuous deeds without wearying even in old age, only then did golden rain descend. Those who travel far arrive late; those who bestow belatedly receive delayed recompense.

*Baopuzi* said: One cannot demand excess where principle is exhausted; one cannot seek universal salvation from a single arrival. Thus, the last ripples of a great wave cannot stir floating duckweed; the aftermath of a gale cannot lift light dust; the spent force of a mighty crossbow cannot pierce misty silk; the fading glow of the setting sun cannot illuminate the mountains to the east.

*Baopuzi* said: Though celestial bodies may dim, they cannot be likened to the steadfast radiance of fireflies; though the Yellow River may run turbid, it cannot be compared to the clarity of a pool’s waters. Though mountains may crumble, they remain loftier than mounds; though tigers may grow emaciated, they remain fiercer than jackals.

*Baopuzi* said: Had Shennong not suffered nine illnesses, the Way of the *Four Classics* would not have been bequeathed; had Yu the Great not endured calloused hands and feet, the blessings of the dark jade tablet would not have gathered. Thus, prolonged sorrow is the root of profound joy; momentary toil is the beginning of eternal ease.

*Baopuzi* said: Though golden hooks and cassia bait may be precious, they cannot ensnare the deep-dwelling scaled creatures of the nine abysses; though illustrious favor and abundant emoluments may be noble, they cannot entice the reclusive man of no desires. Thus, by the Lüliang cliffs stands a man like a crane, and by the banks of the Yellow River dwell those who hew sandalwood. Jade and silk merely gather in the lane of Master Ziling; carriages with rushes return empty from the gate of Master Xu.

*Baopuzi* said: Sights and sounds differ in appeal; love and hatred are seldom shared. Birds, upon seeing Xi Shi, take flight in alarm; fish and turtles, upon hearing the *Nine Shao*, sink into the depths. Thus, the radiant splendor of waterweeds cannot please the eyes of the naked tribes; the pure tones of the *Bianling* cannot delight the ears of Chu’s convict laborers. The benevolence of the Ancient Duke could not enlighten the land-greedy Di; the eloquence of Duanmu could not free the horse-tethering servant.

*Baopuzi* said: The rites of the turning lathe are despised in the land of the bare-legged and squatting; the carpenter’s inked line is resented in the workshop of crooked timber. The greedy and gluttonous detest the unblemished whiteness of raw silk; the cliquish and scheming abhor the solitary integrity of lofty conduct. Like the merchant’s disdain for shared profit or the ugly woman’s envy of a beauty, so too do they despise.

*Baopuzi* said: When the noble man ascends, he promotes the worthy and disperses emoluments; when the mediocre man gains power, he vaunts his status and scorns scholars. He lavishes favors upon flatterers, spends wealth on petty kindnesses, does not share peace with the wise yet expects rescue in peril, does not rejoice with extraordinary men yet desires consolation in sorrow.

Like wildfire blazing across the heavens, one then begs for rain from the famed mountains; like floodwaters surging to the skies, one then hews boats at the eastern gate—is this not too late?

*On Rhetoric and Meaning*, Scroll Forty
Someone asked: "Heaven and earth are round and square not by the work of compass and square; the Three Luminaries scatter their light not by the power of polishing and grinding. Spring blossoms shine resplendent without the art of gradual dyeing; orchid and iris exude fragrance without borrowing from external qi. Knowing this perfection of the truest, one values what is natural."

"Meaning is prized for its rarity, rhetoric for its uncommonness. Yet surveying the writers of past and present, few can bring forth untrammeled beauty at their brush-tips, most instead ladling from the words of those before. Why is this?"

*Baopuzi* said: "Pure sound is prized for its refined harmony; writing is treasured for its discernment of subtleties and dissection of principle. Thus the eight timbres differ in form yet share the same tuning; court robes and ritual vessels vary in pattern yet unite in the five colors. Idle harshness has its place as guest, beauty and ugliness their measured steps. Therefore, the grand compositions have no fixed melodies, the great kitchens no set flavors. Piles of catalpa and cedar cannot become fine craft without the master carpenter; boundless books cannot yield their essence without a genius to gather it. Why must one seek timber from a thousand *li* to build a great hall? Why must one await the words of spirits to compose an essay?"

*Baopuzi* said: "Talents vary in purity, thoughts in length. Though all engage in writing, their levels differ by ten thousand grades. Some flow boundlessly yet lack profound depth; some grasp matters yet their words are dull. To craft fine prose while defying the principles of things is but a partial excellence, not the talent of one who comprehends all. Blind to their own limits, they force themselves to master all, misapplying their abilities—no wonder they invite ridicule."

*Baopuzi* said: "Five flavors clash yet all are sweet; myriad hues diverge yet all are lovely. The common mind loves what is like itself and hates what differs, prizing what aligns with its own views and scorning what takes another path. The nature of writing is especially hard to appreciate fully. If one takes what pleases the ear as fine and what suits the heart as satisfying, few know the forgetting of taste in the *Nine Songs* or the elegance of the *Ya* and *Song*. This is like judging the salt and plum’s sourness while ignorant of the great broth’s seasoning, admiring the fluttering’s delicate craft while blind to the profound depths of the abyss. The brilliant and transcendent soar beyond the bounds of the patterned and plain; the constrained and petty remain shackled within cages and nets. Wings have their sharpness or dullness, so flight and perching have their heights and lows; tracks have their swiftness or slowness, so advance and retreat have their distances and proximities. The slow and the swift cannot be tuned to a fixed pitch. Writing values richness and abundance—why must all praise come from a single mouth? If it cannot stem the drift of customs, the decline of the world’s ways, open the path for the doubtful, or relieve the needy, it is no different from spring blossoms that cannot serve as food or orchid and iris that cannot warm against the cold. Ancient poems critiqued faults, so they were beneficial and prized; today’s poems are pure empty praise, so they are harmful and despised."

*Baopuzi* said: "Those who take up the brush each have their flaws. The deeper ones err in excessive analogies, redundant admonitions, and overabundant explanations—wishing to discard them yet loath to do so, they unwittingly grow verbose. The shallower ones err in beauty without substance, lacking evidence or support, their skin lustrous yet their bones frail. When floridness shines like the sun, it outshines even the seven luminaries; when subtlety sinks into mystery, it rivals the unfathomable abyss. No human affair is too small to be thoroughly understood; no aspect of the kingly Way is too minute to be exhausted. Thus one’s person may be lowly, yet one’s words precious—resounding ever more brightly through a thousand years."

*Following the Root*, Scroll Forty-One
*Baopuzi* said: "The profound, empty, and tranquil are the root of spirit and illumination; the yielding and firm of yin and yang are the root of the Two Modes; towering peaks and deep valleys are the root of mountains and ridges; virtue, conduct, and learning are the root of the noble man. None can stand without a root. Thus, to reach the heights, one must strengthen the foundation; to flourish at the branches, one must deepen the roots. If one’s village and kin are not in harmony, yet one busies oneself seeking from afar; if one’s official post brings no renown, yet one demands extraordinary prominence—this is like chasing fleeting fame: even as the reckless blossom blooms in frost to cast its glow, it withers before the morning is done. Though one may seize the great treasure without preparation, riding the dust as if borne by a carriage, it is like the minnow clinging to the waves to soar high—glancing back, one is already a withered stump on perilous land. The sages and worthies toiled ceaselessly, striving so earnestly, yet the shallow and near dismiss them so lightly. Habit makes one forget the stench of the tannery; the bare-legged tribes do not sense the shame of baring their forms. Unless one flees the world without distress or equalizes things in success and failure, how can one abandon the near and easy to seek the distant and arduous? To remedy this ill, the method is no other: simply elevate the people from their mountain retreats and employ talent without calculation."

*Responding to Ridicule*, Scroll Forty-Two
*Baopuzi* said: A guest ridiculed me, saying: "Sir, you nurture the One and conceal your brilliance in the abyss of spirit, dwelling apart from the world in lofty solitude. Your plans are decided yet you hesitate, unsettled in your heart’s resolve; your lot is fixed yet worldly burdens do not bind your breast. Laozi took the Dao and virtue as his first concern; Zhuangzi crowned his work with *Free and Easy Wandering*. Thus they set their lofty standards in the nine skies and spread their fragrant legacy without end. Yet you, sir, hold lofty principles yet do not serve; your body does not attend to duties, yet you write books on the Way of the ruler and the duty of the minister. You do not engage with the world, yet compose critiques of customs and discourses on saving lives. You cherish your bones and flesh, yet compile methods of warfare, defense, and battle. You do not seek advancement, yet write on the examination of the worthy, the poor, and the successful. I am utterly perplexed."

Bao Puzi replied:
"The greatness of lord and minister is second only to Heaven and Earth. To delight in the Dao and act with sincerity—whether one withdraws or emerges—is but a single sentiment. Concealment or prominence depends on the times; what constraint lies in words? The noble person adapts to changing circumstances. Laozi embraced non-action, and Guiguzi remained in reclusion, yet both composed works that discussed worldly affairs. Must one occupy an office before speaking of its matters?

A vessel need not be of jade for the Chu musician to weep; a creature need not be a hidden dragon for wind and clouds to gather. My talents are meager, my virtue slight, and my abilities unfit for governance. Whether I advance or retreat, my conduct remains consistent. Must one be a high official to discuss statecraft or serve the ruler? Otherwise, is one barred from debating order and chaos?

I have long regretted Zhuangzi’s self-promoting words and deeds, which shackled the world’s endeavors. Dwelling in the Lacquer Garden, he indulged in wild talk, fond of painting ghosts and demons while despising images of dogs and horses. He belittled loyalty and integrity, disparaged benevolence and righteousness. Such carving of tigers and painting of dragons cannot summon wind and clouds; empty ledgers of millions cannot relieve poverty."

The child’s bamboo horse cannot spare his feet from blisters; the scholar’s platters piled high upon his desk do no good for an empty belly.”

Another objected: “Yet what you write, sir, strikes at prevailing customs—your words are bitter, your phrases blunt. I fear this will only incur the hatred of those in power and bring about your banishment in these times. It is no way to spread your name, gain renown, or secure esteem.”

Master Baopu replied: “The maker of tools values their use in urgent need, not their adornment or outward form. The writer of words prizes their aid to teaching, not their flattery of custom or pursuit of empty praise. If one merely flatters and fawns, offering false praise and concealing faults, how can one correct errors, rectify deviations, awaken the deluded, or amend mistakes? To abandon the *White Snow* melody for lack of harmonious voices, or to scorn the *Liancheng* gem for its difficulty in finding a buyer—such choices hold no appeal for me.

It is not that I cannot weave ornate, pleasing phrases, nor that I am unaware of the many troubles that forthright speech brings. Yet I cannot bear to distort truth or falsify facts with a crooked brush. I wish for my heart and words to align, to face my shadow without shame, hoping that a kindred spirit may yet come after.

Prosperity and adversity are decreed by fate; success and obstruction are left to Heaven. Must one’s writings be read and words be heeded for glory to touch one’s own time?

When the noble man opens his mouth or moves his brush, he must guard against delusion and bias, striving to correct the inclinations of the crowd and polish away the hidden filth of evasion. Yet those who write books often do no more than adorn and display florid language, flaunting impractical breadth. They string together unverifiable, useless phrases, crafting ornate yet hollow beauties—like the writings on *firmness and whiteness* or the sophistry of Gongsun’s *names and forms*. Though they may encompass all beyond heaven and earth, penetrate the infinitesimal, untie linked rings, separate the identical, or unite the distinct; though they claim the bird’s shadow never moves, the chicken’s egg has feet, dogs can become sheep, and speak of giant turtles and long serpents—such words merely display cleverness and parade novelty to deceive the world. How are they different from painting the *Ao Granary* to stave off hunger or gazing at the *Milky Way* to quench thirst?

To speak of Kun Mountain’s abundance of jade cannot relieve Yuan Xian’s poverty; to examine the ledgers of an herbalist’s storehouse cannot cure a critical illness.”

Mozi carved a wooden rooster to implore Heaven, yet it was less useful than a three-inch axle pin; Guan Qing cast a golden steed in bronze, yet it was less serviceable than a nag. Words that soar to the autumn sky yet cannot be applied—I would not exchange them for even a single grain.”

**Metaphorical Obstructions, Chapter 43**
*Baopuzi* states: I have always held that Wang Zhongren’s *Balanced Discourses*, in over eighty chapters, stands as the foremost work of unparalleled talent. A fellow disciple, Lu, challenged me, saying: “Jade is prized for its rarity, while gravel is scorned for its abundance. Thus, Fuxi’s trigrams, though fewer than ten, encompassed the Two Modes; Laozi’s words, though under ten thousand, fully expounded the Dao and virtue. Wang Chong’s writings, however, fill trunks and pile scrolls, veering in and out of schools—now Confucian, now Mohist—with uneven prose and flawed reasoning. They are like the weeds of a marshy plain, far inferior to the millet and sorghum of measured steps.”

*Baopuzi* replied: “The creator is called a sage, the transmitter a worthy. Yet we see only the ranks of creation and transmission, never a limit on their quantity. What you describe is like one who dwells in a sunken burrow, unaware of the boundless Eight Extremities; who clings to the flicker of a lamp, blind to the radiance of the Three Luminaries; who wanders a shallow, stagnant pool, never knowing the vastness of the Southern Sea; who lingers on a molehill’s slope, never grasping the towering heights of Mount Song or Mount Tai. The Two Modes are called great because they embrace the Eight Wastes, their expanse without end; mountains and seas are called rich because they encompass vast spaces, containing all things in profusion. If, as you argue, rarity is prized and abundance scorned, then the vault of heaven would have no use for its boundless expanse, and the earth’s breadth would be no more valued than a narrow strip.

In a puddle’s midst, no leviathan swims; on a twig’s tip, no wings span the sky; atop an anthill’s crest, no Fusang trees rise; from a stagnant pool’s source, no floodwaters surge. Yet the giant turtle’s head crowns Yingzhou, its waves soaring to Fangzhang; the colossal peach spans Duling, the towering *jian* tree stands in Duguan; Kun the whale drifts across the Celestial Pond, Peng the roc soars to the constellations. Moreover, thunder’s roar cannot be softened to a whisper; the Yellow River’s torrent cannot be confined to a channel; the swiftest steed cannot match the wind’s trace; the soaring swan cannot humble its flight. Thick clouds bring fierce rain; a taut bow sends arrows far. Wang’s learning was vast, his talent great—how could you fathom it?

You say, ‘Jade is valued for its scarcity, stone scorned for its abundance.’ Yet beneath the Dark Garden, atop Jinghua’s peak, in the Ninefold Marsh, by the Square Abyss, piles of jade form mountains, night-shining pearls blaze like the heavens—would you still call them worthless? You cite Fuxi’s few writings, but the Duke of Zhou expanded the *Book of Changes* and added rites and music; Confucius composed the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and supplemented it with ten chapters. Both surpassed Fuxi and exceeded Laozi—should they too be disparaged? Few words may fail to encompass the perfect principle; sparse phrases cannot fully express the myriad affairs. Thus, scrolls must accumulate, chapters must amass, so that the guiding threads may be raised.

The sun charioteer ascends to usher in dawn; the moon maiden shines to illuminate the night. The Five Materials arise together yet serve different ends; the hundred herbs bloom in profusion yet heal distinct ailments. The four seasons converge to complete the year’s work; the five colors gather to adorn brocade’s splendor. The eight sounds harmonize to perfect the *Xiaoshao* melody; myriad words unite to distinguish the arts of the Dao. To hoard fine timber yet use little—how is this different from Yuan Xian, who possessed a vessel’s capacity yet penned meager, crude writings? What sets him apart from the petty and trivial? Music is treasured by those who understand; books are transmitted by those who recognize their worth. The melodies of the blind musician Kuang need not be appreciated in his own time; lofty words and refined prose—should they be diminished for lack of admirers?

The filth of rivers and seas is beyond counting, yet it does not diminish their depth; the crooked trees of the Five Peaks are beyond measure, yet they do not detract from their heights. The Xia jade, though flawed by the slightest blemish, still gleams with radiant luster; its brilliance compensates for the flaw. A work of tens of thousands of words, though some phrases may lack luster, is redeemed by its lofty principles and profound meaning. Thus, the turbid waters of the Four Seas cannot compare to the clarity of a jar’s contents; the emaciated giant elephant is not the same as a plump lamb.

You further criticize Wang for ‘veering in and out, now Confucian, now Mohist.’ Speech is uttered from the mouth; writing is set down on paper. Writing serves to convey speech; speech serves to record affairs. If the brush must not record diverse matters, then discourse must always adhere to a single subject. Yet when the feudal lords sought counsel or disciples asked about benevolence, Confucius answered each with different words. He adapted his guidance to the matter at hand, responding to the urgency of the moment—like the thousand remedies for illness, where acupuncture points vary, warming dispels cold, cooling relieves heat, all aimed at saving life and preserving the body. How could one insist that every traveler follow the same road as those bound for Qi or Chu?

Tao Zhu and Bai Gui amassed wealth through diverse goods—this was their abundance; Yunmeng and Mengzhu gave rise to myriad things—this was their vastness. Thus, the *Grand Clarity of Huainan* begins with *Tracing the Dao* and *The Primal Truth*, yet also includes *Military Strategy* and *The Art of Rulership*. Zhuangzi’s writings treat life and death as one, yet also speak of fearing the sacrificial ox, admiring the tortoise, begging for grain to stave off hunger. If we discard a work because its words are not pure, it is like gouging out an eye to cure a speck in the vision, amputating a foot to treat damp paralysis, uprooting grain to rid a field of weeds, or felling a tree to remove a withered branch.”

**The Hundred Schools, Chapter 44**
*Baopuzi* states: The words of the Hundred Schools, though not all refined in style or resplendent in grandeur, are the expressions of scholars, the concentrated thoughts of solitary minds.

The *Classic Scriptures* are the profound sea of Dao and virtue, while the *Masters’ Texts* are the deepening rivers that flow into it. Looking up to compare them, they are like the auspicious stars that assist the Three Luminaries; looking down to measure them, they are like the thickets and undergrowth that complement Mount Song. Yet scholars cling to a single discipline, wandering a well while ignoring the sea, stumbling in the mire and stagnating in unyielding confinement.

The *Masters’ Texts* unfold with boundless vastness, their depths distant and obscure, gathering the fathomless source and raising currents that leave nothing behind. Their transformations are not bound by the compass and square of rules, their lateral connections do not sink into deviant paths that stray from the upright. Their style is lofty and stern, towering so high it cannot be exhausted. Thus, those who favor only sour or sweet cannot appreciate their flavor; those whose thoughts are limited cannot discern their spirit.

The ancients sighed over the rarity of talent, so for a hundred generations, scholars have followed in one another’s footsteps. They do not discard the luminous treasure of day and night simply because uncut jade does not emerge from the ridges of Ban and Tong; nor do they dismiss the words that aid teaching merely because books did not issue from the gates of Zhou or Confucius. It is like those who carry water: though their vessels differ, they all serve to extinguish fire. It is like acupuncture and moxibustion: though their methods vary, they equally attack illness.

Yet those of narrow vision, stubbornly clinging to one thing, reject breadth and precision, thinking without understanding. They weigh mere ounces and deem them equal to mountains, or gather hundreds and thousands to reach billions and trillions. Bewildered by the trivialities of poetry and rhapsodies, they overlook the profound and beautiful words of the *Masters’ Texts*, inverting truth and falsehood, mixing jade with stone. They equate grand music with the tunes of Sangjian, or rank the dragon’s pattern with plain silk—how lamentable, how grievous! Is this not a matter of many strands?

**On Literature and Conduct, Chapter 45**

Some say: “Virtue and conduct are the root; literature is the branch. Thus, in the sequence of the Four Subjects, literature does not take precedence. Then what is written on paper is but the dregs of the essential; what is transmitted is like the straw dog after the sacrifice. The hierarchy of high and low is indeed open to ridicule.”

Bao Puzi replied: “One may discard the fish trap only after the fish is caught; one may abandon literature only after the Way is practiced. If the brush’s reach and the breadth of its resonance, the beauty or coarseness of its phrasing and parallel meanings, the length of its source and flow, the depth of its implication and allure—if these disparities are so vast that even the space beyond the heavens or the tip of a hair cannot compare to their remoteness, and even the three luminaries’ radiance cannot match their magnitude or minuteness; if the sharpness or dullness of Longyuan’s lead and iron cannot serve as their metaphor, nor the weight of swan feathers or heaped gold measure their lightness or heaviness—yet vulgar scholars, seeing only the ability to dip the brush and mark paper, lump all together as one. This is why Bo Shi endlessly pondered Zhong Zi, and why the man of Ying’s axe failed to move.

Though hewers of wood stand shoulder to shoulder, Ban and Di alone bear the name of unmatched skill; though players of the zither are many, Kui and Xiang alone hold the title of pure sound. Among a thousand teams of stable horses, Qiji and Liuma command a price far above the herd; among ten thousand beauties, Weishi alone possesses a transcendent allure—so far do they surpass the multitude.

Moreover, literature and virtue are as ten feet to a single zhang; to call it a mere surplus is something I have never heard. The Eight Trigrams arose from the flight of hawks and falcons; the Six Jia emerged from the burden of the sacred tortoise. Where literature resides, though it may be deemed secondary, the root is not necessarily shallow, nor the branch necessarily trifling. It is like brocade woven upon plain cloth, or pearls and jade borne by insects and stone; clouds and rain arise from a hand’s breadth, and rivers begin within a foot’s span. If the principle is truly thus, then the refined critique is flawed.”

He further said: “The ascending dragon rises slowly, yet with a single glance soars above the clouds.”

The blood-sweating steed treads gently, yet with a single breath covers a thousand li. Thus do ants and beetles marvel at heights attained without steps, and sluggish nags startle at surpassing themselves without gradual ascent.

As for those who gallop through the realm of poetry and discourse, or circle within the bounds of a single classic—gauging the extraordinary with common sentiment, measuring the boundless with narrow capacity—from childhood to hoary age, they shall never grasp it.

Moreover, the vulgar invariably exalt antiquity and disparage the present, revering what they have heard while scorning what they see. Though there be steeds swifter than the wind, outshining all others, they still claim none match the chariot of Bole. Though there be uncut jade worth two cities, they still claim none rival the tears of the Chu lapidary. Though there be swords that sever horses and carve dragons, they still claim none surpass the forging of Ouye. Though there be medicines that revive the withered and restore the decayed, they still claim none equal the compounds of He and Que. Though there be men who stand above the multitude in solitary eminence, they still claim none compare to the ancients.

Bao Puzi said: Ji Sheng held that Taiyuan’s Guo Linzhong, though he ultimately declined the summons of the Three Dukes, had mastered every branch of learning and won renown surpassing all past ages. Moreover, his discernment of men marked him as a sage in wisdom—truly a vessel second only to the sages. Yet in an age of decline, he wandered restless and anxious, never lingering long enough to warm his seat, his heart set on correcting abuses and enacting the Way, much like Confucius.

I replied: “Wisdom and folly often turn on a single word; a flaw in the pivot can mar a flawless jade. To call him ‘second only to the sages’ is no light claim. For what is meant by ‘second to the sages’? It must denote one whose substance is complete though in lesser measure, a figure of transcendent distinction, leaving no room between himself and the Zhou or Kong. How could such a title apply to this man? Linzhong was indeed outstanding, his judgment keen and penetrating—far above the common run of men, and thus often elevated by their praise. Yet in truth, he still fell short. He possessed eloquence and poise, knew well how to assert himself and seize opportunities, and had partisans who spread his fame to the four quarters. Thus he rode their acclaim to prominence in a disordered age, winning the admiration of those who hear without verifying. A single word of his praise carried more weight than a thousand gold pieces; wherever he traveled, wise and foolish alike were swept along, as though a dragon or phoenix had alighted, or some wondrous omen appeared. His every utterance left echoes to be emulated; his every step left traces to be imitated. He might be called one who strikes the war drum to raise the sun and moon—yet not a true recluse.

For when he wished to serve in court, the world was already in chaos; when he sought to withdraw, he found confinement unbearable. To leap forward was to fear calamity; to stand firm was to find no peace. Hesitating and unsettled, he rode in carriages by day and the moon by night, while the world pursued his splendor without examining his substance, admired his form without plumbing his spirit. Thus even when his headdress was drenched by rain, people still took him as a model, blind to his shortcomings—such is the way of the crowd. The common folk chase after fame to this extreme. Hence, though his flaws were plain, none dared point them out.”

The scholar Lin Zong’s learning encompassed the discernment of men—this was no empty claim. Yet he could not escape the reputation of one who sees through falsehoods, for he was blind to his own measure. When urged to enter official service, Lin Zong replied: “By day I observe the affairs of men; by night I study the celestial signs. What Heaven has cast aside cannot be propped up. The present age corresponds to the *Ming Yi* hexagram, occupying the position of *Wu Yong*—a time for lingering in seclusion, not for the celestial favor of public display. Though one may stand firm as the plains, one still fears the surging tides of the sea. I am but a fish—how could I dare to brave the gales and ride the rushing waves? Far better to nurture the spirit among the cliffs, delight the heart with Peng and Lao, and wander at ease, passing the years in leisure.”

Yet Lin Zong’s words reveal his clear understanding that the Han could not be saved—this was no failure of his own talent. By right, he should have gazed up to the heights of Shang and Luo, drifted down the Five Lakes, pursued Master Chao amid the towering peaks, or sought the Fisherman on the Canglang’s waves. If he could not bind his steps to the mountain recluses and wander alone, he ought to have concealed his light in the deep abyss, sealed his scales, and tied his talons in a sack. Instead, he journeyed from west to east, his mat never warmed, aspiring to emulate the ceaseless labors of Confucius and Mozi. Even the sages, who grieved for the world and traversed the four quarters, were mocked by recluses—how much more so one like Lin Zong, whose talent was not of the age, whose capacity was not peerless? In public, he could neither pacify the ruler nor govern the people, nor transform customs; in private, he could neither wield the brush to compose nor trace the Six Arts. His conduct was self-aggrandizing, his excesses glaring, his fame resounding, his rewards abundant.
