Yet in the end, his advancement brought no remedy to disorder, his retreat left no mark on bamboo and silk. His gaze was askew, his steps erratic—no different from the common man. For no reason, he plunged into the waves, turned his sandals in the dust, roamed the capital, and mingled with the noble and powerful. His wheels wore thin, his chests grew shabby, with no respite to rest. Thus his reputation blazed, the barbarians of Qin and Hu flocked to his light, crimson carriages clogged the lanes, red-robed guests filled his halls, light chariots crowded the streets, and memorials arrived in an unbroken stream. Truly, he was a knight-errant, not one who met the standards of reclusion. Had he lived in an age of the Dao, he would still have fallen short of the ranks of the lofty and pure, unfit to join the hidden worthies of the secret hills. How much less could he be praised in this perilous and chaotic time!

Who would not say he was blind to the waxing and waning of Heaven and man, deluded about his own strengths and weaknesses? In vain did he turn from the path of tranquility and silence, achieving no benefit from his labors. That he escaped disaster was mere luck. To claim such a one as a savior of the world, aspiring to the stature of the Uncrowned King, is like a lame man chasing a dragon-steed, a sparrow pursuing a swan, a firefly vying with the roc, or a shrew comparing itself to an elephant.

Thus Lin Zong may be said to have possessed talent for dazzling the world but lacked the steadfastness of true reclusion. He saw all with clarity, nearly fit for great use, his eloquence flowing outward while his spirit remained hollow within. He could not endure vexation, his words and deeds contradicting each other. Though he spoke of quiet retreat, his heart craved glory and profit. He never became the nesting bird of the Xuanpu gardens or the hidden spirit of the Nine Abysses. Self-promotion and self-advertisement are the shame of scholars and women alike. Knowing the impossible, he yet imitated those he should not have followed—where, then, was his capacity to rival the sages?

To foresee the beginning and end like the sun and moon is a task even the sage-kings Yao and Shun found difficult, a burden that troubled Confucius. Even they sometimes erred and could not always hit the mark. How much less could Lin Zong, with his firefly’s light, half-grasping success and failure, claim more? Yet his name weighed heavily in his time, and his praises resounded after his death. What he gained was transmitted by the world, while what he lost went unnoticed.

Though he did discern nameless scholars in the wilds and point out uncut jade in the hills, he never advanced loyal and resolute men to the court, nor placed skilled generals on the frontier. He did not relieve the desperate plight of the people or break the ambitions of rebellious schemers. Like Bao Shuya, who recommended Guan Zhong, or Yanzi, who recognized Tian Rangju, Lin Zong’s name shook the court and commanded respect in his day. The high ministers all revered him, his strength sufficient to elevate talent, his words enough to rouse the stagnant. Yet he merely nursed his illness in the capital, gathering guests, achieving nothing to rescue the realm from peril. He could recognize men but refused to recommend them—what difference is there between this and knowing fertile soil fit for fine grain or straight timber suited for beams, yet never plowing to sow or felling to build? How does this relieve famine or shelter the homeless?

His refusal of office was indeed a lofty principle, but his ceaseless rushing was also his affliction.

Ji Sheng further remarked, “While Lin Zong lived, he was the model for his age; after his death, his fragrant legacy spread far. Yet no eminent scholar or brilliant talent has ever pointed out his flaws—why then do I alone critique his shortcomings? Will I not be ridiculed by posterity?”

Master Baopu replied, “Why should it be so? If my words are just, they should be entrusted to later generations. What care do those of discernment in the future have for scant agreement? Moreover, many former worthies have also criticized him—only Huang Sheng praised him excessively. Thus, the Grand Tutor Zhuge Wuxun likewise said: ‘Lin Zong neither concealed himself in reclusion nor benefited his time when he emerged. In truth, he sought only to propagate his name and cultivate his reputation. Street gossip and alley chatter he deemed eloquence; slandering superiors and maligning governance he took for loftiness. The customs of the age prized such things, and the people followed like sheep—just as in former times they flocked to Guo Jie and Yuan She. Those who admire his fame among later scholars have not examined the canons of the sage-kings or weighed his conduct against the deeds of the ancient worthies. They are merely bewitched by empty renown, all competing to take him as their standard. Those who study him do so as if they could never learn enough; those who discuss him fill the air with his praises. Even men of middling judgment fail to perceive the truth—how could the ignorant and young possibly understand?’

Thus, Yin Bojing, Prefect of Lingling and a man of towering talent and incisive discourse, also said: ‘Lin Zong entered the company of generals and ministers, traveled among the feudal lords, and exalted private judgments to sway the masses, linking praise and blame to the court. Those he favored saw their reputations soar like wind and rain, their worth transformed and their bearing altered; those he despised were crushed and ruined, sunk into oblivion, and shunned by scholars.’”

“Yet even among those renowned as worthies, when chaos drove them into reclusion, they concealed their brilliance and hid their light—this was hardly excessive.

The noble man practices the Way to correct his lord and rectify customs. In an age when the lord could not be corrected and customs could not be rectified, Lin Zong still lingered, engaging in pure discourse among the lanes and alleys, offering no remedy for the decline of the world’s Dao, no relief for the emaciation of the people’s lives.”

Moreover, the late Gentleman Zhou of the Secretariat, Gongyuan—a man of heroic stature and renowned scholarship—also said: “When one encounters order and assists it, this is called delighting in the Way; when one encounters chaos and rescues it, this is called grieving for the Way; when chaos cannot be rescued and one withdraws, this is called preserving the Way. Yu Shun delighted in the Way; Confucius grieved for the Way; the Viscount of Wei preserved the Way.

In the Han dynasty’s final years, as the world teetered and social pursuits turned to frivolous exchanges, Lin Zong ought to have roused himself with an empty heart, sought out like-minded noble men, and joined them in correcting the age. Instead, he bustled about as their leader—hardly the proper course for one who would save the world. At that time, even the eunuchs of the Yellow Gate were but beasts lodged in human form.

Though men like Chen Fan and Dou Wu, though they held the highest offices of state, all revered Lin Zong, trusted his words, and let his judgments decide praise or blame—could one not have hoped for some aid in rescuing peril and reforming customs? Yet Lin Zong neither recommended men of action nor achieved even the slightest benefit. Those who fled office he likened to Chao Fu and Xu You; those who neglected their duties to entertain guests he compared to the Duke of Zhou; those who gathered disciples to evade service he equated with Confucius.”

Those who abandoned their kin to attach themselves to the powerful, he equated with Ziyou and Zixia. Thus the world was dazzled by names and realities, and great disorder grew ever more severe.

If one says Lin Zong did not know, then he cannot be called perceptive; if one says he knew yet did not change, then he cannot be said to have grieved for the Dao. In ancient times, the Four Nobles resembled the Duke of Zhou but could not become the Duke of Zhou; now Lin Zong resembled Confucius but could not be Confucius.”

Thereupon the questioner sighed in lament and said, “Then this man was but one who fled disorder, not a lofty hermit of complete reclusion.”

*Dian Mi*, Scroll 47

Master Baopu said: At the end of the Han, there was a man named Mi Heng, twenty-three years of age. Kong Wenju was past the age of fifty, held one of the Nine Ministerial Posts, and his literary renown surpassed all others. Young and old alike praised him, and his fame and position were unmatched. Yet he befriended Heng while he was still a commoner and later memorialized the Han court, recommending that he be appointed as a Gentleman of the Palace. He wrote:

“Only when mountains send down spirits do extraordinary men appear together. Whatever his eyes once see, he recites from memory; whatever his ears fleetingly hear, he does not forget. His nature accords with the Dao, and his thoughts seem divinely inspired.”

Such was his admiration.

Heng traveled to Xuchang, and from the highest ministers and state scholars downward, he never addressed them by their offices, calling them all “Ah So-and-so,” or addressing them by their surnames as “So-and-so’s boy.” He called Kong Rong “Big Boy” and Yang Xiu “Little Boy.” Only Xun Yu was barely worth speaking to; beyond him, all were mere wooden effigies or clay puppets—human in form but devoid of human *qi*, nothing more than wine jars or drinking sacks.

At a grand assembly of officials, Heng was present when he suddenly furrowed his brow, grew mournful, and sighed with indignation. Someone reproached him, saying, “This gathering of heroes and worthies is no occasion for sorrow.” Heng cast his gaze over the crowded hall and replied, “Amid these heaps of corpses and rows of coffins, how can a man of benevolence not grieve?”

Cao Cao once gnashed his teeth, wishing to kill him, but Heng had committed no crime under the law that warranted execution. Moreover, Cao feared the reputation of killing a scholar. Thus he demoted Heng to the post of drum officer. Heng showed no regret or shame; he tied the drum’s horns to a pillar, placed his mouth to it, and blew, producing strange sounds. He also shook the drum’s omen-sticks and struck the drum, so that those who heard could not tell it was the work of one man.

His criticisms grew even more severe, with no regard for consequences. Soon after, he fled to Liu Biao, Governor of Jingzhou. Liu wished to write a letter to Sun Quan—who by then had firmly established himself in the east, commanding a million armored troops—to forge an alliance of mutual support and jointly resist the Central States. He had his scholars draft the letter, but none could capture his intent. He then showed it to Heng. Heng read it and said, “This is only fit for the knife-wielding boys at Sun’s side. If Zhang Zibu were to see this, it would be a great humiliation.” He immediately tore it up and threw it to the ground. Liu was dismayed and looked at him strangely, saying, “Is it so unworthy of the brush? What a pity.” Heng asked for paper and brush and rewrote it on the spot. Liu’s scholars had produced over ten drafts, which Heng merely glanced at before committing them to memory. He then wrote out the letter and returned it to Liu.

Liu compared Heng’s version with the original drafts and found not a single error. All were astonished. Liu then asked Heng to compose another, and Heng did so without pause, his hand never stopping. Liu thought it excellent and put it to use.

Heng’s arrogance grew even more extreme. The scholars of the entire province came to detest him, and Liu could no longer tolerate him, wishing to kill him. Someone advised him, saying, “Cao Cao is known for his severity, yet he endured Heng. Though Heng has but a slight reputation, if you kill him now, no wandering scholars will ever set foot in Jing-Chu again.” Liu thus sent him away.

Heng fled to Xiakou and took refuge with General Huang Zu, who treated him as an honored guest. Huang’s eldest son, Huang She, accompanied Heng on a journey. Passing by a grave, they both read the inscription on the stele and then departed. After some time, She said, “The inscription we saw earlier was excellent, but I regret not copying it down.” Heng replied, “You only remember its general content. I recall it all after a single reading.” He then wrote it from memory, but at the end, one character was unclear due to damage to the stone. Heng supplied half the character and said, “I suspect this should be such-and-such a character, but I am not certain.” She examined it... *(text incomplete)*

Though he spoke and acted with frivolous disregard for others, he still craved honor and renown. Thus, soaring high through the phoenix groves, he could not bear to lurk hidden in the weeds and brambles. Yet in cultivating himself, he was abrasive and cutting, blind to his own delusions—so that with every word he uttered, he drew hatred, and with every step he took, he trod into disaster. With such skills and cunning, how could he ever find a place in the world and meet a natural end? Like the owl’s cry or the fox’s howl, all shun them; though their voices remain unchanged, what good does it do to change their perch?

Xu was an ocean of talent and character. Wenju was its steward, and even his favor was enough to secure one’s position—yet Heng could not find peace there. This was already clear. Like a terminal illness, even if Yu Fu or Bian Que attended him, there was nothing to be done. Rotten wood and leaden blades—even Lu Ban or Ou Ye could not craft them into anything useful. And so he fled once more to the lands of Jing and Chu, only to plunge into utter ruin. This was the consequence of Heng’s blindness and delusion.

For he desired what he could not attain—not that he attained it and failed to use it. Alas, men of talent, take heed!

Ji Sheng said: “What perplexes me is Heng’s hollow reputation. What you have discussed are his real flaws. How could I not awaken to your guiding compass and cast my staff upon the balanced path?”

*Interrogating Bao, Scroll 48*

Bao Sheng Jingyan was fond of the books of Laozi and Zhuangzi, and he studied the art of incisive debate. He believed that the ancients, who had no rulers, were superior to the present age. Thus, in his treatise he wrote:

“The Confucians say: ‘Heaven gave birth to the multitudes and established rulers for them.’ Could it be that august Heaven, in its earnestness, would speak thus to justify desire? The strong oppress the weak, and the weak submit to them; the wise deceive the foolish, and the foolish serve them. Submission gives rise to the Way of ruler and subject; service allows the few in strength to be controlled. Thus, subjugation and domination arise from the contention of strength and the comparison of wit. Yet in truth, boundless Heaven is without concern. In the primal chaos, namelessness was prized; all living things rejoiced in attaining their desires. To strip the cinnamon and carve the lacquer is not the wish of the tree; to pluck the kingfisher and rend the kingfisher’s feathers is not the desire of the bird; to tighten the reins and force the bit is not the nature of the horse; to bear the cart’s weight and toil endlessly is not the joy of the ox. The sprouts of deceit and cunning, the exertion of force contrary to nature, sever the root of life to adorn what is useless. To capture flying birds for frivolous display, to pierce the nose of the unblemished, to fetter the hooves of those born to roam—this is not the intent of the myriad things coexisting in the world. To labor the multitudes for the sustenance of those in office, to enrich the noble with generous emoluments while the people suffer—this is folly. To escape death and gain life brings boundless joy, yet it would be better never to have faced death at all. To decline rank and refuse emoluments in pursuit of hollow fame is no better than never having had to decline. When the world is in chaos, loyalty and righteousness become manifest; when the six relations are discordant, filial piety and kindness are extolled.

In the distant past, there were no rulers, no subjects. People drank from wells they dug, ate from fields they tilled, rose with the sun and rested at dusk. Unfettered and at ease, they found contentment in their natural state, neither competing nor scheming, knowing neither glory nor shame. Mountains had no paths, marshes no boats or bridges. Rivers and valleys were unconnected, so none could annex another; people did not gather in great numbers, so none could wage war. High nests were left unplundered, deep abysses unsounded. Phoenixes and simurghs nested in courtyards, dragons and scaled creatures swam in garden pools. One could tread upon hungry tigers, grasp vipers and serpents, wade through marshes without startling the gulls, enter forests without alarming the foxes and hares. Ambition and profit did not arise, calamity and disorder did not occur. Weapons were unused, walls and moats were unbuilt. The myriad things were in profound harmony, forgetting one another in the Dao. Pestilence did not spread, and the people lived out their natural span. Purity resided in their hearts, and scheming did not arise. They ate when hungry and roamed with full bellies. Their words were unadorned, their conduct unembellished. How could they amass wealth to seize the people’s property? How could they impose harsh punishments to create pits and snares?

Yet as the ages declined, cunning and artifice arose. Virtue and the Dao waned, and hierarchy took form. Elaborate rites of ascent and descent, of increase and decrease, were devised. Robes of black and yellow, adorned with embroidered sashes, were fashioned. Towers of earth and timber soared to the clouds, their rafters painted in cinnabar and green. Mountains were overturned in search of treasure, abysses dived to discern pearls. Jade was amassed like forests, yet could not exhaust its transformations; gold was piled into mountains, yet could not suffice for its expenditures. Indulgence in excess and dissipation led them to betray the great origin of their beginnings. The farther they strayed from their source, the more they turned from simplicity. Exalting worthiness incited the people to vie for fame; prizing wealth gave rise to thieves and bandits. The sight of desire confounded the heart’s true integrity; the display of power and profit opened the path to plunder. Sharp weapons were forged, prolonging the scourge of invasion and division. Crossbows were feared for their lack of strength, armor for its lack of sturdiness, spears for their lack of sharpness, shields for their lack of thickness. Were there no tyrants to overawe, all these could be discarded.

Thus it is said: If white jade is not shattered, how can it become a ritual scepter? If the Dao and virtue are not abandoned, why would one seek benevolence and righteousness? It was the tyrants Jie and Zhou who were able to roast men alive, mince feudal lords, pickle regional lords, carve out hearts, and break shins, exhausting the evils of arrogance and dissipation, employing the cruelty of the *paoluo*. Had these men been mere commoners, though their natures were fierce and extravagant, how could they have acted thus? It was because they were rulers that they could indulge their cruelty and desires, slaughtering and carving up the world at will. Once the relationship of ruler and subject was established, the multitude of evils grew daily. Yet to seek to throw off one’s shackles amid such fetters, to exhaust oneself in toil and suffering—this is folly. The ruler trembles in fear upon his ancestral hall, while the common people are tormented in their hardship and distress. To restrain them with ritual and measure, to discipline them with punishments and penalties, is like trying to dam the source of a raging flood or stem the current of an unfathomable torrent with a handful of earth or the palm of one’s hand.”

The *Baopuzi* objected, saying: “I have heard that when the primordial obscurity parted, the turbid descended and the pure ascended. The vault of Heaven arched above, the expanse of Earth spread below. Heaven and Earth took their places, and above and below assumed their forms. Looking afar to things, we see that Heaven is noble and Earth base, which establishes the structure of human relations. Looking near to the body, we see the head and limbs, which exemplify the order of ruler and subject. The pattern of hierarchy has existed since antiquity.

As for the Great Ultimate in its undifferentiated chaos, the Two Modes without substance—this is not to be compared with the division of the dark and the yellow, the hanging of the Seven Luminaries, the molding of the myriad things by yin and yang. To speak of abundance, we know that birds flock and beasts scatter, nesting in trees and burrowing in dens, feeding on flesh and blood, clothing themselves in grass and leaves. They know no hierarchy of the six relations within, no gradations of rank and authority without. This is not to be compared with the shelter of spacious halls, the fine grains and delicacies, the embroidered robes and silken garments that shield against winter and summer. The enlightened ruler attends to things, the good minister governs the age. Offices are established and duties divided, and the cosmos is at peace. With noble and base clearly marked, people aspire to reward and fear punishment. When power is equal and strength balanced, contention and plunder know no restraint. Thus, sages arose, receiving their mandate from Heaven. Some wove nets to hunt and fish, some observed the stars to drill fire, some tasted plants to select grains, some built shelters to shield from above.”

Equipping the people with useful things and removing harm to promote benefit, the hundred clans rejoiced and upheld them, honoring them as their rulers—thus the Dao of lord and minister was born. How could there be any principle of deceiving the foolish or oppressing the weak? As the Three August Ones and Five Emperors succeeded one another, the teachings of the Dao flourished. Distinctions were clarified, encouragement and deterrence established, virtue flourished and punishments were pure. Songs of wise rulers arose, and the vast transformative influence was perfected. The Grand Stairway was level, the Seven Governors followed their courses. The phoenix sang in response to the morning sun, the qilin and *lin*-beasts appeared bearing auspicious signs. The turtle and dragon disgorged their patterns by the riverbanks, the ancient sages unfolded their radiance upon the celestial path. The imperial wind shook the Nine Regions, and weapons of war were stored away in armories. Thus, with ritual institutions in place, the lord was at peace; with music performed, punishments were set aside.

Yet when extravagance, lust, madness, and violence arise from the self and others, must there necessarily be a ruler for such things to occur? Why then does Master Bao single out the sins of degenerate ages while ignoring the principles of perfect governance?

"In remote antiquity, when simplicity and plainness had not yet changed, the people remained as innocent as children, their scheming minds unstirred—like infants whose wisdom and desires had not yet awakened. It was not that they knew and refrained, or desired and restrained. If person fought person over the profits of wild grasses, if household contended with household over the land of nests and burrows, with no officials above to redress wrongs and no factions below to reinforce their kind, then private strife would exceed public warfare, clubs and stones would be sharper than halberds and spears. Corpses would litter the fields, blood would dye the roads crimson. If this continued without a ruler, all living things would perish.

As for taming dragons and training phoenixes, the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* revelations, the qilin bearing a jade plaque in its mouth, the yellow fish surging in the waves, the red birds soaring to deliver their messages, or the whirlwind gathering thrice—all these occurred in ages with rulers, never in times without kings. Auspicious omens point to the farthest mysteries: some manifest the signs of dynastic renewal, others display the flourishing of perfect governance. If having a ruler were contrary to Heaven’s will, who would summon these auspicious responses?

If you consider primordial chaos beautiful, then Heaven and Earth should not have separated. If you deem namelessness lofty, then the Eight Trigrams should never have been drawn. Did creation err, or was Fuxi truly benighted? The refined discourse you uphold values only spontaneity. Yet let me ask: to recognize one’s mother but forget one’s father is the nature of all living things; prostration and reverence are the world’s later embellishments. But nature cannot be left to itself—one must honor the father; embellishments cannot be abolished—one must perform obeisance. If you abandon these, how can a child be at peace?

In ancient times, people lived without houses and died without burial. Rivers lacked boats and oars, land lacked carts and horses. They swallowed poisonous plants and perished, fell ill without medicine, and died unjustly in countless numbers. Later sages reformed these conditions and bequeathed their improvements. The people to this day rely on their great beneficence. The benefits of ingenuity and craft are not easily undone. Now, if you were to dwell again in the crudeness of nests and burrows, discard the dead in the wilds, cross waters by swimming, traverse mountains on foot bearing your loads, abandon cauldrons and tripods to eat raw, foul-smelling food, forsake acupuncture and stone needles to suffer natural illness, go naked as adornment, and take a woman you meet as your mate without matchmakers—you yourself would say, ‘This cannot be.’ How much less tolerable, then, is a world without rulers?

If the people of antiquity were like wood and stone, if they felt no cold when ice formed, no hunger when provisions ran out, then perhaps it would be possible. But if the desires for clothing and food reside in their hearts, then what they contend over need not be gold or jade, nor what they compete for necessarily rank and honor. Acorns and grasses can provoke strife; coarse greens are enough to incite plunder. The nature of desire sprouts at the moment of receiving *qi*; the impulse to enrich oneself is evident from the day of taking form. Usurpation and murder arise naturally—if there is no chaos, what principle explains it?

When a wise king sits above, the feudal lords all follow the rules. He rises before dawn and eats late into the night. He invites criticism to correct his faults, demands that his intimates admonish and oversee him, listens to street songs to reflect on his governance, and trembles at the thought of treading on a tiger’s tail. He spreads a pure wind to sweep away filth, sharpens autumn’s severity to discipline all things. His laws are strict and his nets tight; those who violate them are not pardoned. Executions punish minor crimes, the Nine Punishments suppress great rebels. Yet like wolves blocking the road, the disruption of proper order still occurs. He worries that wielding power may bring misfortune to his house, fears that treachery may harm the state. Thus, he appoints stern officials like soaring eagles to impeach wrongdoers, and valiant ministers bearing axes to the four quarters. Yet even so, the madness of the cunning never ceases in any age. If he were to abandon restraint and let them act without fear, then robber Zhi would rampage and slaughter, while the good and upright would fold their hands and await disaster. With no lord to appeal to, no strong authority to rely on, how could one hope that every household would be like Boyi and Shuqi, or every person like Liu Xiahui? It would be like carrying a pig and wishing it had no stench, leaning on a river and hoping not to get wet, driving a galloping horse without reins or bridle, or sailing a light boat without oars—such things have never been possible."

Master Bao further objected: "Heaven and Earth take their positions, the two *qi* shape all things. When they delight in yang, clouds fly; when they favor yin, rivers settle. Following softness and hardness, they follow their natures; adapting to the four seasons and eight nodes, they transform and generate. Each attaches to what it finds secure—there is no inherent hierarchy. Once lord and minister are established, transformations multiply. Where otters abound, fish are disturbed; where hawks multiply, birds are thrown into chaos. When officials are appointed, the hundred clans are burdened; when tribute to superiors is heavy, the common people grow poor. They hoard precious goods, adorn palaces and terraces, feast on tables laden with delicacies, and wear robes embroidered with dragons. Within, they gather idle women; without, they leave many men unmarried. They prize rare treasures, value strange and exotic things, craft useless implements, and indulge insatiable desires. If not by ghosts or spirits, from where do these resources come?

When grain and silk accumulate, the people suffer hunger and cold. When the bureaucracy is fully staffed, it squanders resources in idle support. Palace guards consume without labor, and the people feed those who do not work. The people lack clothing and food, barely sustaining themselves—how much worse when taxes and corvée are added, compounded by hard labor? The people cannot bear their fate, freezing and starving. Violating the law becomes rampant, and thus they fall into ruin."

The ruler toils and worries above, while his ministers and high officials frown and grieve below, ever treading on thin ice, ever fearing disaster’s approach. Fearing that wisdom and courage may go unused, he offers generous ranks and lavish emoluments to entice them; fearing that treachery and strife may arise unforeseen, he fortifies walls and deepens moats to guard against them. Yet he does not realize that lavish emoluments drain the people and embolden ministers, while fortified walls multiply corvée and refine the arts of assault.

Thus, when King Zhou’s gold from Deer Terrace was scattered and the grain from Juqiao Granary was distributed, none failed to rejoice. How much more, then, if gold had never been amassed and grain never exacted from the people? When cattle were rested in Peach Forest and horses released on Mount Hua, when weapons were sheathed and bows and arrows stored away, this was still deemed excessive. How much more if there had been no armies at all, no wars and no conscription?

Thatched roofs and earthen steps, abandoning weaving to pluck mallows, using coarse sacks for curtains, washing furs and wearing cloth blankets—concubines not clad in silk, horses not fed with grain—frugality set the example for all, becoming a tale of praise. This is like Robber Zhi dividing spoils, modestly taking the lesser share, or fish stranded on land, warming one another with foam.

When the body bears no public service, the household no taxes or levies, when one dwells content in one’s place, follows Heaven’s allotment and Earth’s divisions, when within there is enough for clothing and food, without no strife for power or profit, then wielding a staff to rob and plunder is not in human nature. Under the teaching of symbolic punishments, none among the people transgress; yet when laws and decrees proliferate, thieves and bandits abound. Is it that they lack a nature drawn to profit while only these are greedy and cruel? No—when the ruler is pure and tranquil, the people naturally align; when the people are exhausted and resentful, cunning and deceit arise.

Left to nature, one still fears oppression; driven without respite, plundered without end, fields lie fallow and granaries empty, the loom and shuttle stand idle. Food does not fill the mouth, clothing does not cover the body—how can one expect no disorder? Thus, measures meant to avert disaster only deepen it; strict prohibitions only fail to halt it. Checkpoints and bridges are meant to prevent wrongdoing, yet corrupt officials use them to commit it. Weights and measures are meant to detect fraud, yet deceitful men use them to deceive. Great ministers are meant to shore up peril, yet treacherous ministers fear their lord’s safety is not imperiled. Arms and armor are meant to quell strife, yet bandits and invaders use them to create it. All this is the consequence of having a ruler.

When the people see profit, they vie for it. For the wealthy and noble, the stakes are high. Yet the petty disputes of common folk are but trifles—when ordinary men test their strength, how far can it go? There is no territory to covet, no cities to seize, no gold or jewels to desire, no power or authority to contest. They lack the means to rally followers or the might to command strangers. How does this compare to a king’s wrathful display, mustering armies and marshaling hosts, driving guiltless people to attack blameless states, where the slain number in the tens of thousands, where blood flows so thick it reddens the fields and floats the oars?

Rulers without the Dao are never absent from any age. Indulging in tyranny and chaos, they leave the world without a single secure state. The loyal and good are harmed within, the common folk lie exposed without—is this merely the scourge of petty strife? Even to the point of displacing fatherly duty to serve the lord, abandoning filial piety for loyalty, issuing decrees as if there were no ruler—such things are commonplace.

In ancient times, houses sufficed to shelter from wind and rain; now they are adorned with vermilion and purple, embellished with gold and jade. In ancient times, clothing sufficed to cover the body; now it is dyed in dark and bright hues, embroidered with intricate patterns, woven from fine silks. In ancient times, music sufficed to settle the human heart; now it is filled with wanton sounds that startle the soul and disrupt harmony. In ancient times, food sufficed to fill hunger; now forests are burned, rivers drained, and all living things slaughtered. [...]

(Here follows the refutation of *Baopuzi* in response to objections:)

"Can one discard a practice entirely because it has been misused? When Yu reigned above, Ji and Xie assisted in governance—palaces were humble, taxes light, and the people labored in due season. Frugality was exalted as a pure virtue, while extravagance was sternly forbidden. Those who embraced simplicity were honored and promoted; those who corrupted customs and oppressed the people were demoted and punished. Then praise arose, and the common folk lived in peace. Why, then, fear fire and demolish all houses, or dread storms and fill the great rivers?"

*Baopuzi* replied: "Master Bao extols the age of antiquity when there were no rulers, a view I have already refuted. His subsequent responses are too lengthy to record in full, so I shall excerpt his arguments and present my rebuttals in turn."

Master Bao said: "Rulers covet rare treasures, amass strange curiosities, and adorn themselves with useless luxuries, never sated in their endless desires."

*Baopuzi* countered: "I ask: have all rulers across the ages sought rare treasures and strange curiosities? Some have not. I have heard that when Tang Yao ruled, he cast gold into the mountains; when Yu Shun abdicated, he discarded jade in the valleys. Their coarse food and plain garments were no different from those of a gatekeeper. Clearly, they did not drain the depths for pearls, cleave cliffs for jade, quarry stone for gold and silver, cross seas for kingfisher feathers, trap hawksbill in distant lands, or dig for cinnabar along the Min and Han rivers. If robes and insignia are indistinguishable, authority cannot be manifest; if ranks and titles are not distinct, rites and rituals lose their proper measure. Thus, the Duke of Zhou distinguished the differences between high and low, establishing regulations for palaces and dwellings—walls had their limits; for caps, carriages, banners, and flags—ornaments had their designs; for vehicles, robes, vessels, and utensils—quantities had their rules; for kitchens and banquets—dishes had their standards. In years of famine or disaster, these were reduced. Endless desires do not arise from the virtuous. What you describe may indict Jie and Zhou, but it cannot serve as proof for a sound argument."

Master Bao said: "When a ruler’s harem numbers three thousand, is this truly Heaven’s will? When grain and silk accumulate, the people suffer cold and hunger."

*Baopuzi* countered: "The number of a king’s consorts is established by the sages, who align their virtue with Heaven and Earth. How could this be mere indulgence of desire? It serves to assist the six palaces, regulate yin and yang, instruct in reverence for ancestral temples, uphold grand sacrifices, provide ceremonial robes, and expand the lineage of descendants. According to the *Zhou* records of the Nine Lands and the Han geographical texts, the number of women in the world exceeds that of men. How could the ruler’s harem deprive others of wives? The Duke of Zhou’s deliberations on this seem well-considered. Emperors lead the hundred officials in plowing the sacred field, while empresses and noblewomen tend to silkworms and weaving. Among the common people, farming quotas are fixed, diligence is rewarded, and idleness is punished. Taxes are set at one-tenth to support public needs. Households store provisions for famine, and the state accumulates nine years’ worth of supplies. Each follows Heaven’s allotment and Earth’s divisions, never encroaching on the seasons. With light taxes and few corvées, the people suffer neither hunger nor cold. When clothing and food are sufficient, courtesy and deference arise. In the reigns of Wen and Jing, the people devoted themselves to farming, households prospered, and granaries overflowed with grain so abundant it rotted in heaps. Yet scholars and commoners still dined from tripods and wore fine robes, while cattle and horses filled the marshes—all because taxes were moderate and did not harm the people. In later ages, officials abandoned farming quotas, private pursuits turned to frivolous trades, grain production dwindled, and idle mouths multiplied. Thus, both high and low erred, and transgressions grew common. Master Bao blames the existence of rulers. If one criticizes excessive selection of consorts, condemns lax farming quotas, rebukes the three thousand cattle-drinking in the palace, or reduces land taxes to less than half, and ensures the harem follows Zhou rites while taxes are not arbitrarily increased—this would suffice. Must there be no ruler at all? If one rises late even for a day, affairs fall into disorder. Without a forester, even a deer entering the forest is lost. Can this be sustained forever? Without a governing authority, the noble lose their model, the wicked achieve their aims, and even a loose net still catches some fish. Can there be no net at all?"

Master Bao said: "People already struggle to clothe and feed themselves. Why add taxes and corvées, compounding their burdens until hunger and cold converge, and the people can no longer endure, leading them to break laws and commit crimes?"

*Baopuzi* countered: "Spiders weave webs, yet fleas and lice do not starve. Humans, with their intelligence and skill, harness the myriad things to feed their mouths and clothe their bodies—how is this a struggle? The problem lies in the rich having no limit to their desires and the noble no end to their extravagance. Should one stumble once and abandon walking forever, or long for no ruler because of Jie and Zhou’s tyranny? To say that governance has expanded, taxes have grown heavier than in ancient times, the people’s strength is exhausted by frivolous tasks, and hunger and cold arise from these—such criticism is valid. But to claim that taxes and corvées cause national chaos—let me ask: In the age of Tang and Yu’s peace, or the virtuous eras of the Three Dynasties, were there no taxes or corvées to support the ruler, the ministers, or the sovereign’s own plowing for self-sufficiency? Master Bao sees only hunger and cold converging, with no one enduring hardship, yet ignores that when clothing and food are sufficient, the people know honor and shame!"

Master Bao said: "A ruler treads as if over deep abysses or upon a tiger’s tail—what danger could he not foresee? He sleeps lightly, rises before dawn, and eats late—what calamity could befall him?"

*Baopuzi* retorted: "If one truly acts thus, then he is a sage ruler."

The ruler’s maladies lie in arrogance and extravagance, in failing to employ the worthy and employing the unworthy. Jie of Xia pointed to the sun and swore by heaven to justify himself, while Qin Shi Huang fretted over sharing the same posthumous title for ten thousand generations—thus they invited collapse and became laughingstocks for posterity. If a ruler could remain vigilant by night and cautious by day, broadly accepting admonitions and seeking counsel, consulting even the humblest herdsman as if he were Yao and heeding the words of the aged, what worry would there be of affairs going awry? What fear of the hundred offices falling into disorder? For when one is ever wary, the constant norms are ordered; when one grows indolent and dissolute, treachery and villainy arise—how much more so without a ruler? Could there be no chaos?"

Master Bao said: "The ruler cherishes rare omens and beckons distant lands, seeking to exalt his virtue and surpass his majesty, to awe and dazzle the unsubdued. What benefit do white pheasants or jade rings bring to the common folk?"

*Baopuzi* retorted: "When the ruler’s virtue reaches Heaven, Heaven responds with omens; when it reaches Earth, Earth answers in kind. The radiant star unfurls its light to aid the glow of Wangshu; the solar halo cradles its splendor to mark the shadow of Xihe. Auspicious birds warble in the palace tower, golden images gleam in the clear pools—were these brought about by humble words or lavish gifts? Wang Mang, treacherous and cunning, harbored malice in his heart, feigning peace to deceive court and country, showering foreign lands with gifts to induce them to send auspicious objects. Can one claim that all ancient rulers acted thus? Seeing a tail a fathom long, one knows the beast is not a handspan in size; glimpsing tusks a cubit high, one knows the mouth is not a finger’s breadth. Thus, the Queen Mother of the West sent envoys, proving her dark transformation had reached the spirits, and no distance was too remote to embrace. The repeated translations of Yuechang’s tribute suffice to show that kindness had moistened distant lands, and grace extended without bound. Remote domains cannot be subdued by force, nor barbarians cowed by might—without perfect governance, how could this be so? Why does Master Bao claim such things are useless? The Zhou court was not lacking in jade yet prized the Queen Mother’s ring, nor was it parsimonious in its meals yet thirsted for Yuechang’s pheasants to fill its kitchens. What was truly valued was that these objects signified great peace: above, no harsh or oppressive governance; below, no people left destitute. From the tiniest flying insect to the lowliest creeping thing, all found joy. What greater glory could a state possess? Yet you say they are useless, of no benefit to the common folk. Such vast and profound matters are not easily perceived—no wonder Master Bao speaks as he does."

Master Bao said: "The ruler fears unforeseen treachery, so he fortifies his walls to prepare for it."

*Baopuzi* retorted: "The *Book of Changes* esteems lords who set up barriers—why fault the fortification of walls? When the Two Modes first parted and the myriad things took form, both good and evil arose. The sage knows that villainy is natural, that the foolish are hard to change—just as spring’s warmth cannot revive withered wood, nor summer’s heat melt metal or stone. Alluring beauty and careless concealment invite lust and theft, so one takes the hexagram *Kan* as a model and prepares before trouble sprouts. Double gates require the watchman’s clapper, arms are readied to repel sudden raids—yet you would abolish these. What logic is this? The rhinoceros’ horn, the phoenix’s spur—Heaven bestows them, but must one use them daily? Wasps and scorpions wield venom to defend themselves; clever birds carry reeds to ward off nets. Badgers curve their burrows to guard against sudden thrusts; water buffalo form ranks to repel the assaults of tigers and leopards. Yet Master Bao would discard armor to block blades, raze walls to halt charges. If armor is cast aside and blades do not cease, if walls are torn down and assaults still come—even Gongshu Ban and Mo Di could not save themselves. What plan does Master Bao propose?"

Someone said: "If there were no coveted objects, even without fortified walls, enemies would not come."

Bao Puzi replied: "What need is there for coveted objects to be gold or jade? Even the tip of an awl or the edge of a knife—foolish folk contend over them. The great war of the Yue people arose from the failure to hook a python; the armed conflict between Wu and Chu began over a single mulberry leaf. In times of famine, men devour one another—bare-handed, barefoot *(text missing below)*.

Far back, there were Fu Hou and Zigao; more recently, there was Yu Gong who released them—weighing emotions, scrutinizing punishments, splitting hairs and dividing the finest threads. Those who suffered execution swallowed their voices and sang of virtue; those who endured mutilation never uttered a word of resentment to their dying day. Yet none of these were times without rulers.

Of old, when a widower dwelt in low estate, the Four Peaks did not conceal him; when obscure worthies were openly commended, all the finest talents were raised up. Some cast aside their butcher’s knives to stride through the Golden Gate; some left their trowels and rammed earth to ascend the Jade Hall. Some abandoned their fodder and livestock to become ministers and chancellors; some, fleeing as outlaws, became supreme generals. Bo Liu avenged his foe; Xie Hu recommended his enemy. Fang Hui knocked his head to summon scholars; Qin Xi shattered his skull to advance the worthy. I ask you: were these times without rulers?

You also say: 'Fields lie fallow, granaries stand empty—all because of rulers.' But rulers are not the creeping weeds that choke the fields, nor are ministers the sparrows and rats that devour the grain. That fields lie fallow and granaries stand empty is ultimately due to the turning of fate, to floods and droughts, to epidemics and pestilence—how can this be blamed on taxes, making them the cause of such calamity?

As for the first of the Eight Regulations—food—it is called the people’s heaven. Hou Ji tilled the soil himself; the Lord of Yu plowed with his own hands. In years of plenty, millet and rice abound, our granaries overflow with countless bushels, the people eat of the old stores. The White Canal was opened, turning saline wastes into fertile soil; Shao Fu built the embankments of Yangling, piling grain like mountains. Shu Ao created the irrigation of Qisi, and households had rotting millet; Zhao Guo devised the ingenuity of the three-plow system, and the lands west of the Pass flourished. Ren Yan taught the people of Jiuzhen to farm, and the black-haired masses were sated and full. Were these times without rulers?"

Bao Puzi said: No prayer is greater than contentment with little, no blessing deeper than knowing when to stop. To embrace fullness while dwelling in emptiness is the surest calculation for preservation; to revel in ease and abundance is a peril hard to safeguard. Consider the followers of Shan Juan, Chao Fu, Guan, and Hu—all soared high on clouds and mist, traced the paths of dragons and phoenixes, discerned hidden blades amid fragrant bait, awoke to overturned chariots on the road ahead, shunned treacherous paths to cross afar, and thus evaded the hooks of Zhan He. Truly, they excelled at foreseeing faint shadows beyond form, glimpsing solid ice before frost, removing firewood and curving chimneys while flames still blazed, furling sails and stowing oars before the gale. Gazing at the harm in nine oxen, they sank deep; beholding dense thickets, they passed beyond. They did not nest on the tips of reeds, nor lie down beneath crumbling cliffs. All possessed vessels vast and measures broad, weighed opportunities and grasped subtleties, surpassed their peers in solitary resolve, and were not shackled by common desires. Their spirits joined the workings of creation, their minds left the myriad things behind. Temptations could not sting their purity, near-truths could not muddy their clarity.

If one lacks such innate wisdom, it is indeed difficult to follow in their transcendent tracks. Yet even the lesson that those who complete great deeds should withdraw, and those who bear great toil should not be rewarded, remains: when the cunning hare dies, the hunting hound is no longer needed; when the high-flying bird is gone, the fine bow is about to be discarded. Mirroring the clear example of Peng and Han, one recalls the art of withdrawing the hairpin; witnessing the hidden mechanism of Yue’s seeds, one recognizes the value of the golden elephant. Like Lord Fan, who drifted away in a boat to escape the limelight, or Scholar Xue, who fled chaos to preserve his integrity; like the Two Shu who resigned their seals at the height of success, or Tian Yu who relinquished his sash as the candle guttered—advancing, they avoided the fault of overreaching; retreating, they suffered no regret of wetting their tails. Their pure winds suffice to sweep away the dust of a thousand years, their virtuous echoes suffice to dispel the confusions of ages to come. Compared to mere treasures, how distant they are!

Yet some, though wise in small matters, fail in great designs; some, though strong in shafts, break under heavy loads; some, though alone in rightness, are ensnared by the crowd’s wrongs; some, though wholly loyal, are impeached in times of discord; some, though advocating lofty plans, suffer the fate of Chao Cuo; some, though exhausting heart and strength, meet the harm of Wu Qi. Thus, even those whose words are restrained and whose backs are thick may not escape. The banishment of the Duke of Zhou, the wanderings of Confucius, Jia Yi’s exile among lesser men, Sima Qian’s castration though innocent, Yue Yi’s conquest of Qi, Wu Zixu’s destruction of Chu, Bai Qi’s hundred victories expanding the realm, Wenzi’s nine strategies dominating Yue, Han Xin’s achievements covering the world, Qing Bu’s annihilation of his house to aid the mandate—glory did not last a day before disgrace overtook them. To not avoid such calamities—can this be the way of the wise?

Serving as a minister is no easy task, nor is there but one path for generals. In essence, the decision lies in choosing one’s lord. If I am not worthy of reliance, the proof is as clear as this. To resign, yield to the worthy, keep oneself pure and secure—what finer name, what richer reward, what greater blessing could there be? Yet among ten thousand, not one can cultivate this art. Fortune and misfortune are shaped by man—can one not reflect on this?

Words that grate on the ear are seldom welcomed; offering advice in hope of honor hastens one’s ruin. Even refuting slander is beyond one’s power—how can acceptance be assured? When arrows and snares fill the air, even mandarin ducklings fold their wings; when pits and traps line the path, even unicorns and tigers retreat. Emotions must not be pushed to extremes, desires must not be indulged to the full. The enlightened use the Dao to govern their feelings, use strategy to dispel their cravings. Even when advising others, one should act with loyalty—how much more so when devising plans for oneself!

For those who know contentment are ever content; those who know no contentment are ever without. Ever-content, blessing comes to them; ever-without, calamity is their lot. Excessive indulgence in life’s richness is the very death of life. The Song man who pulled at the seedlings, the man of Ying who stretched the hide—though they wished it to thrive, they hastened its withering and tearing. This is what is meant by advancing without thought of retreat.

Those who spur their steeds without cease are seldom spared from stumbling; those who tread the waves without rest are rarely saved from drowning.

Those who wield blades without rest invite wounds and stabs; those who strike and hew without cease court breakage and ruin. Fullness invites loss—such is the principle of nature. Did the vessel of the Zhou temple ever deceive us? Thus, when Yang You shot his arrows, passersby knew his bowstring would snap; when Dongye drove his chariot, Yanzi foresaw his imminent collapse. Beneath the heights of success, one cannot long abide.

Drinkers need not all descend into chaos, yet most who do are drunkards; the rich and noble are not all imperiled, yet peril finds some among them. The wise anticipate affairs beyond their twists and turns, fell trees at the first sprouting of their shoots. They do not boast in the midst of stacked chess pieces, nor debate fur-making amid a throng of foxes. The ancients feigned madness and played the fool—was this their delight? The times demanded it; they had no choice. Some fled deep into the wilds only to meet waves and billows; others hid in seclusion yet saw their waters engulfed in flames. Like Gong Sheng, who starved himself to death, or Li Ye, who swallowed poison in anguish—such fates arise from traces left behind, from shadows that do not fade. Had they walked as though treading ice, dwelled as though in shadow, moved without leaving tracks to follow, stilled themselves to merge with non-action—would such misfortunes have befallen them? How much more so for those who brandish sun and moon to conceal their forms, or beat war drums to parade their blades!

In prosperous times, one may excel in aiding the realm; in perilous ages, one may barely preserve oneself. This eternal lament is not confined to a single scholar. I have heard that no flame burns unquenched, no overflow goes unspilled; brilliance bears the omen of cold ashes, spring grasses foretell autumn’s withering. The sun declines after noon, the moon wanes after fullness; the seasons turn, and those who succeed must retreat. Look far to things: build too high, too steep, and collapse becomes the only fear. Look near to oneself: indulge too freely in fine foods and rich wine, and illness and harm to one’s nature follow. How much more so for those who pile their loftiness to the clouds without cease, whose might shakes rulers and whose honors mount without end!

A gnat or fly that falls from a mountain may yet soar; a rhinoceros or tiger that tumbles is shattered to dust. This speaks of great things that cannot lose their place. Moreover, to confront wrongdoing with unyielding integrity, to walk the straight path, to strike at those who violate the law without heeding enmity—such actions deepen resentment and accumulate hatred. Yet if one abandons the law to indulge transgressions, responds to favor like an echo, spews forth strength while swallowing weakness, and bends to the carpenter’s line—then loyalty and integrity are lost, and those who dwell in such a state do not escape toil.

Thus, those who preserve both body and name are rare, while those who stumble and overturn their bowls are many. Yet within, there are secluded chambers adorned with cinnabar, vermilion curtains and tasseled canopies, embroidered cushions spread across resplendent halls, radiant beauties gleaming at one’s side. Soft voices and tender forms, exquisite songs and enchanting dances—the craft of Song and Cai, the grace of Yang’e—mouths pour forth eloquent melodies like dew-laden vines, feet trace the steps of the *Green Water* and *Seven Plates*. Harmonies delight the ear, alluring figures gladden the heart; secret feasts follow one after another, fine wine never withdrawn. Gazing up from painted pavilions, looking down upon clear pools, wandering among crimson and emerald fruit groves, sporting in fragrant orchards of orchids—crimson-scaled fish glide and leap, vermilion birds soar and dip. Flying nets bring down swans from the clouds, sinking lines draw forth bream and carp. Rare treasures gather without seeking, luxuries and splendors arrive of their own accord.

Abroad, crimson wheels gleam on the roads, lofty canopies press close to axles, vermilion banners billow like clouds, pennants and staffs flash in brilliance. Golden mouths clamor, halberds and armor gleam in dazzling array. Triumphant, one rides in the rear carriage, fine delicacies fill the attendant vehicles. One exhausts the pleasures of sightseeing, the joys of the hunt and the catch. Praise for sagely brilliance fills the ears; flattering words pour forth in unison from every mouth.

At that time, one felt so loftily detached that the ancients seemed beneath regard—Yi, Lü, Guan, and Yan were deemed not even worth reckoning. How could one perceive the cyclical rise and fall of fortune, the swift alternation of sorrow and joy, and thus willingly relinquish glory and splendor, beg to retire, turn one’s back on vermilion gates, and return to the rustic fields?

As for those sagely enlightened rulers above, with great worthies aiding their governance—where the hundred offices would fall into disarray without me, and the myriad people would find no deliverance without me—those who stand high yet do not fear peril, who abound yet do not dread excess, such matters need not be discussed.

**On Success and Failure**
Someone asked: *"Men of the same talent may meet with either success or failure—why is this? Does the swift or the shackled feel regret?"*

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: *"When abilities are equal yet some are suppressed while others rise, it is due to the lack of a true patron. Thus, adversity and prosperity depend on the times, obstruction and opportunity on fate. Those who discern the times—why resent obscurity? Those who understand fate—why lament lowly toil? The deep-pool leviathan, finest of sturdy creatures, if never unleashed, cannot even sever morning mushrooms; the pearl of *yaohua* and jade of *lǜlǜ*, treasures beyond price, if cast into mire, will be buried beneath shards and pebbles. Thus, what is precious need not always be prized, what is useful need not always be employed. The vulgar, blind to distinctions, cannot tell crimson from violet, beans from wheat; they deem the successful wise, unaware that success may come from mere luck, and brand the struggling inferior, without considering that failure may stem from steadfast integrity. The celestial bodies, if not fixed in the heavens, cannot radiate their boundless brilliance; Mounts Song and Tai, if not rooted in the earth, cannot soar to the clouds. The hare’s swiftness is proven on level ground, the dragon-prowed boat’s speed on rushing currents; fire’s glow would not blaze without Suiren, Chu’s metal would not gleam without Ouye; lush blossoms await spring’s call to display their splendor, soaring swans wait for the gale to take flight. If the Four Mountains do not commend, even the worthy remain unappointed; if Shuya does not recommend, Yiwu never rises to greatness. Rangju relied on Pingzhong to ascend, Han Xin on Xiao He to soar like a hawk; Junsheng was elevated by Shengzhi’s counsel, Qu Ni by Wu Zhi’s endorsement; Yuanzhi was roused by Kongming’s dragon-like entanglements, Gongjin presented Xingba’s crouching tiger. Thus their illustrious names endure in imperial annals, their grand deeds shine in their age.

Yet in the late Han, the final years of Wu, it was otherwise. In selecting officials, one favored only those who fawned; in choosing men, one trusted only those with many partisans. The fawning were not necessarily worthy of promotion—one kept them simply because they agreed; the partisans were not necessarily outstanding talents—one trusted them merely because many voices praised them. Some, swayed by these mediocrities, could not set aside personal attachments; others, recognizing true excellence, could not judge with perfect impartiality. Thus, they abandoned the scales of merit and weighed men by familiarity; discarded fair measures and counted heads by factional ties. At that time, those so-called refined men of lofty bearing, who held the reins of state, decided promotions and demotions at will, and dispensed praise or blame with a word—few escaped this corruption. How much worse for those whose hearts harbored hatred for the independent, envy for rivals, and disdain for the humble! Alas! The unconventional, the unaligned—how countless they were, some stifled in marshes, others expelled from court. Cultivating virtue yet finding the Way blocked, honing their talents yet meeting no opportunity; some waited for the Yellow River to clear until their teeth fell out, others exhausted loyalty and toil yet gained no recognition. Their far-reaching deeds never spanned an age, their meritorious blessings never reached the people. Treasures fit for the banquet table lay mired in filth, talents to aid the world found no outlet. They wielded trowels yet never met King Wu Ding, held fishing poles yet never encountered the Earl of the West. From ancient times to now, how boundless such cases—why single out one for sorrow? Is this not shallow? Gazing at the distant path yet ashamed to walk it, knowing the great Dao is obstructed yet refusing to change course—those who equate success and failure, entrust honor and disgrace to nature, how could they harbor resentment or sigh endlessly at time’s passage? To suspect they feel regret is to misunderstand the mind of the perfected. The short-lived cannot comprehend the long-lived, the well-frog cannot fathom the sea—this has always been so.

**Further Remarks**
The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: Among my friends was one called Master Tranquil Depth. In his youth, he had already mastered the Six Arts, delved into the River Chart and Luo Scripture, racing the sun’s last rays by day, chasing the moon’s lingering glow by night. No Dao was too distant for his inquiry, no word too subtle for his discernment. He wielded Confucianism and Mohism as ramparts, his wit and spirit as weapons—thus, debaters could only trail dust and offer jade in surrender, scholars avert their eyes and lay down their brushes. Yet soon he awoke to the wisdom of silence, realized the blamelessness of brevity. When his mind was at ease, he saw the *Quan Ti* as discardable; when the Dao diverged, he understood that lofty words find few echoes. Thus, he heeded Laozi’s warning of many defeats, pondered the Golden Man’s three seals of silence. He sheathed his sharp tongue as if dull, polished his brush on the red tube, holding gold within and jade without, restraining eloquence and brilliance. From dawn to dusk, he might not utter a word.

His disciples approached and said: *"Master, your silence makes us, your students, no different from the common crowd. We humbly suggest: if a bell does not ring, it is no better than a heap of bronze; if a floating chime falls silent, it is no different from a pile of stones."*

Master Tranquil Depth replied: *"I seek only to gather a distant reputation for ages to come, to find a true patron in the future. How could I vie for recognition today, set my standards by the moment? A wine vessel, though fine, cannot reveal its intoxicating power if never filled..."*

A lowly station with lofty speech earns no trust. To curl the tongue and exhaust the voice—what remedy is there for wayward drift? The ancients at sixty laughed at fifty-nine, for they saw how near one may stray before awakening to truth. The hardness of jade, the temper of gold, the chill of ice, the heat of fire—do these require self-proclamation to be known?

Moreover, the eight tones and nine suites cannot escape flaws of length or brevity; even Yang You’s hundred shots must falter once. Those who lean on rivers drown in their currents; those who relish heated debate often slip in speech. Bo Ya guarded his strings with care, thus avoiding the burden of tangled fingers; scholars revered their words, thus escaping the shame of misplaced wit.

The shallow and vulgar act otherwise. They debate the insubstantial and urgent, squander words on trifles, expound on the vast and impractical theories of "hard and white," recite the heterodox texts of the Hundred Schools—condemning the sages and overcorrecting the norm. They claim confusion as profundity, abandon the true classics, and vie to master perverse doctrines. Some match wits with the dim-sighted, vying for victory in lip and tongue; others regale the ignorant with refined discourse, answering those who cannot grasp it. Facing no worthy opponent, with no discerning audience at hand—is this not like performing court music beside wooden effigies, or displaying brocade before clay idols?

In vain do their mouths wither and their qi flag; they pound tables and slap thighs, axes dull and knots unbroken. Their faces flush with rage, yet their errors grow more distant. Thus do anger shape their features, foul words spill from their lips, and the shift to pettiness arise between them. Once stained by such folly, no polishing can restore them. Better to embrace the unstruck note, where the great sound remains incomplete, and speak sparingly, awaiting those who truly understand.

*Self-Narrative, Scroll Fifty*

The Master Embracing Simplicity, surnamed Ge, named Hong, styled Zhichuan, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. His ancestors descended from the Ge Tian clan, who once ruled all under Heaven in ancient times. Later, their domain diminished to a feudal state, and thus they took Ge as their surname. Hong’s great-grandfather served as Inspector of Jingzhou. When Wang Mang usurped the throne, the elder scorned to serve a traitorous regime, resigned his post, and returned home. Together with Zhai Yi, Governor of Dongjun, he raised an army to punish Wang Mang. Defeated by Mang’s forces, he was later pardoned and escaped disaster. Claiming illness, he severed all ties with the world. Fearing the strength of his clan, Wang Mang suspected future rebellion and relocated him to Langye.

His son, Ge Pulu, raised troops to aid Emperor Guangwu, rendering great service. Upon Guangwu’s ascension, Pulu was appointed General of Chariots and Cavalry, later promoted to General of Agile Cavalry, enfeoffed as Marquis of Tong County in Xiapi, with a fief of five thousand households. In the early years of the dynasty, the marquis’s younger brother, Ge Wen, followed him on campaigns, achieving repeated victories. The marquis repeatedly memorialized the throne to plead for Wen’s merits, but officials dismissed the claims, arguing that Wen had privately accompanied his elder brother without formal military appointment. Thus, no reward was granted. The marquis declared, “My brother and I shared the same arrows and stones, our bodies covered in wounds, his right eye lost in battle—yet he receives not even the smallest recompense. Meanwhile, I am laden with gold and purple seals. How can my heart be at ease?” He then personally memorialized the throne, requesting that his own enfeoffment be transferred to his brother. The memorial was submitted, and the Han court, wishing to honor his noble virtue, granted the request. Wen declined but was compelled to accept. Upon receiving his title, he retired to his estate, where the General of Agile Cavalry had a residence built for him in Bowang Ward. To this day, its foundations and stone pillars remain. He also divided his stipend to provide for the officers and soldiers, matching the provisions of the two marquises. Though the General of Agile Cavalry urged him to desist, he would not comply.

The General of Agile Cavalry said, “This would only further burden the people of the state—how can I accept such deference?” He then feigned other business and crossed the Yangtze southward to settle in Jurong. There, his sons and younger brothers tilled the land themselves, finding joy in the classics. Wen repeatedly sent envoys to welcome the General back, but he never returned. He also ordered the residence in Bowang Ward to be maintained, hoping the General might one day return—yet for generations, no one lived there.

Hong’s grandfather was learned in all fields, delving deeply into the subtlest principles. His literary and artistic attainments were unmatched in his time. Possessing the talent to govern a state, he brought benevolence to Wu, serving successively as magistrate of Haiyan, Lin’an, and Shanyin counties. Later, he was appointed Gentleman of the Ministry of Personnel, Palace Aide to the Censor-in-Chief, Prefect of Luling, Minister of Personnel, Junior Tutor to the Crown Prince, President of the Secretariat, Grand Master of Ceremonies, Palace Attendant, Superintendent of the Palace Guard, General Who Assists Wu, and was enfeoffed as Marquis of Wushou County.

Hong’s father was renowned for his filial piety and fraternal devotion, serving as a model for scholars. He exhaustively studied all records in the archives. In Wu, he held the posts of Gentleman of the Five Offices, Impartial and Just, Magistrate of Jiancheng and Nanchang counties, Gentleman of the Secretariat, Commandant of Justice, Protector of the Army, and Prefect of Kuaiji. Before he could resign, the Jin forces advanced downriver, and the western frontier fell. With his mastery of civil and military arts, Hong was unanimously recommended by court and common opinion. He was thus transferred to command the five commanderies in response to the crisis. Appointed Grand Commander-in-Chief, he was granted five thousand personal troops to lead the expeditionary forces and defend the borderlands. Yet what Heaven had decreed could not be withstood by man. Thus, when the lord submitted in reverence and the Nine Provinces became guests of the realm, Hong, retaining his former rank, was appointed Gentleman of the Palace. Gradually promoted, he became Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, then Grand Impartial and Just, and later Magistrate of Feixiang County. With twenty thousand households under his jurisdiction, his governance was the finest in the province. His virtue transformed the people, his kindness suffused the land, and his administration was just. The countryside resounded with praise, the roads bore no trace of wrongdoing, and even those who did not cultivate public fields traded across borders as in a marketplace. Gifts as small as autumn hairs were not accepted at his gate, while paper and brushes for official use were supplied from his own wealth. Punishments were set but never enforced, and transformation spread without a word. Resigning due to illness, he was later summoned by imperial edict to serve as Chamberlain to the King of Wu. With unyielding integrity, he corrected errors, recommending the worthy and impeaching the corrupt. The military and state were ordered and harmonious. Later promoted to Prefect of Shaoling, he died in office.

Hong was the third son of the lord’s younger brother. Born late, he was indulged by both parents and not early disciplined in books and histories. At thirteen, he lost his kind father. Bereft of paternal instruction, he endured hunger, cold, and hardship, personally tending the plow and following the stars through the grass, diligently tilling the fields. Repeatedly ravaged by war and fire, his family’s classical texts were utterly lost. With no books to read during farming respites, he carried a satchel and walked to borrow them. Yet even these were often incomplete, so he split firewood by day to afford paper and brushes, writing by the light of kindling in his garden hut. For this reason, he could not early engage with arts and letters. Often lacking paper, he wrote on both sides of each sheet, leaving few able to read his work.

At sixteen, he began studying the *Classic of Filial Piety*, the *Analects*, the *Book of Songs*, and the *Book of Changes*. Too poor to seek distant teachers or friends, his learning was shallow, his thoughts limited, and he grasped little of the great principles. Yet he craved broad reading, and of the many books he perused—from the orthodox classics and histories to the hundred schools’ teachings, down to miscellaneous short works—nearly ten thousand volumes—he could recite them all from memory with precision. Still, his nature was dull and forgetful, his literary skill meager, his will unfocused. What he retained was scant, and he often fell into confusion. Yet in his writings, he could still draw upon them, though he never became a pure Confucian or a master fit to transmit teachings. As for the *River Chart* and *Luo Writing* apocrypha, he glanced at them and set them aside, never giving them serious attention. He disliked astrological texts, calculations, the Nine Palaces, Three Boards, *Taiyi* flying tally, and the like, entirely ignoring them. For he was a man of hardship, lacking such inclinations.

Later, he studied *fengjiao* divination, *wangqi* observation, the Three Origins, *dunjia*, *liuren*, and *Taiyi* methods, gaining rough familiarity with their principles but never delving deeply. He judged such arts as tools for others’ use, born of worldly desires, and saw no need to exhaust himself with them. Better to spare oneself and study works of true benefit—so he abandoned them again. Consulting the *Separate Record* and *Treatise on Arts and Writings*, he found over thirteen thousand two hundred and ninety-nine volumes listed. Since the Wei era, however, writings had multiplied, doubling those of earlier times. Thus he knew how much he had yet to see. Books in the south were incomplete, and though he once wished to journey to the capital to seek rare works, war forced him to turn back halfway. He often sighed in regret.

Now nearing forty, his youthful ambitions had faded. He sought only to diminish and diminish again, to act through non-action, to dwell in marshes and thickets, merely preserving his life. His broad learning waned day by day. As for Hong’s character—(lacuna) he was simple and uncouth, slow-witted and inarticulate, his appearance coarse and unrefined, yet he never sought to embellish or boast. His hat and shoes were filthy and worn, his clothes sometimes ragged, yet he felt no shame. As fashions changed—now wide collars and broad sashes, now tight sleeves and long hems, now trailing robes, now garments too short to cover the feet—Hong clung to constancy, refusing to follow the world’s shifts. His speech was blunt and truthful, free of jest or mockery. Without a suitable companion, he remained silent all day. Thus his countrymen called him a man of unhewn simplicity, and so he took this as his sobriquet when writing.

Hong was frail by nature, further weakened by illness. Poor and without carriage or horse, he could not bear to walk, nor did he enjoy it. Disdaining the corrupt customs of the age—its neglect of fundamentals, its excessive socializing—he took up his brush in seclusion, guarding quietude behind his humble gate, with no place to rush to. Even among the powerful, though some lived nearby, none knew him. His clothes did not ward off the cold, his roof leaked, his food did not fill his stomach, his name did not leave his door—yet he felt no distress. Without servants, his fences collapsed, brambles choked his courtyard, weeds overran his steps, and thorns barred his path. Critics saw him as one who scorned the near while aspiring to the distant, unyielding in his poverty.

He did not know how to pay respects—(lacuna)—and thus never cultivated visits to officials. Yet when attending grand funerals or visiting the sick, he forced himself to go, though illness often left him unable to fulfill even these duties, drawing reproach from critics. Hong accepted the blame without concern. If his intentions were pure but illness thwarted him, he felt no shame before himself—why should he care if others failed to understand? Only those of discernment forgave his steadfast simplicity, not mistaking it for cultivated aloofness.

Most people cherished the pleasures of ease and intimacy, prizing even the secrecy of dark chambers. Hong believed knowing others was no easy task, even for the sages. Superficial friendships, where words aligned but hearts diverged, brought no benefit, only harm. Though he could not sever all ties like Zhu Gongshu, he insisted on clarity and thorough understanding before placing trust. For this, he was hated by many, yet he did not change.

Those who chased fleeting success or fawned at the gates of the powerful despised Hong for differing from them, slandering him as arrogant and scornful of custom. Yet Hong acted with sincerity, deaf to both praise and blame. He loathed those who, relying on their strengths, belittled others’ weaknesses. Though but a humble follower of the Confucians, when speaking with others, he gauged their knowledge and discussed only what they could grasp, never forcing them toward what they had not heard. When debating with scholars, he addressed the essentials. If they struggled to grasp his meaning, he roughly conveyed the direction of his thought, just enough to enlighten them, without pressing the argument so far that they could not respond.

Those of tranquil mind, when they reflect in detail, often awaken and attain understanding on their own. When Hong judged someone unfit for discourse, even if they asked a question, he would routinely decline with an "I do not know," to avoid the excess of wasted words. By nature, Hong deeply disliked importuning officials. From youth to old age, he had rescued several friends from oppression, and when compelled, he would speak to those in power—yet those he aided never knew it was Hong who had shown them solicitude. Unable to bear seeing others trapped in injustice, he would quietly arrange matters himself. As for the rest, even if they were close kin, when they held office and wielded power, if they shared no bond with Hong, he would never burden them with so much as a word or half a character.

When his provisions ran desperately low and he needed to prepare medicine, he would call upon friends and acquaintances, and if they aided him, he would not refuse. When he received another’s kindness, he would gradually repay it over time, never letting them notice. He would not rashly accept gifts from those outside his circle. If Hong had ten days’ worth of food in store, he would share it to relieve others’ want; if he himself was barely sufficient, he would not deprive himself. He did not pursue ostentatious minor virtues nor cultivate meticulous petty integrity.

Among the common folk of village and town, those who were deemed good and virtuous would, on occasion, bring wine and food to visit Hong. Though they were not his peers, he would not refuse them. Later, if he returned the gesture, he would not do so immediately. Hong once remarked that Shi Yun refused to eat with his brothers and that Hua Sheng maintained fastidious purity with his close associates—such were pretentious acts to court fame, not the magnanimity befitting the halls of state.

Hong particularly detested those without principle who neglected the fundamental work of farming and sericulture to covet illicit gains.
