Those who controlled local opinion sold offices to collect bribes; those with power and influence exploited their positions to extort wealth. Some took bribes from criminals, others wronged households with just claims. Some became havens for fugitives, sheltering outlaws; others conscripted laborers to obstruct public service. Some hoarded goods to inflate prices; others monopolized markets to seize the people’s profit. Some seized others’ fields and plundered the property of the orphaned and weak. Lurking in the gaps of officialdom, they sought opportunities for extortion, flaunting their spoils before their wives and concubines while outwardly seeking fame and rank. Hong would have no dealings with such men. Thus, the vulgar despised Hong for loathing them, and naturally kept their distance. No carriage tracks marked the lane before his home, no guests of divergent minds sat in his hall. His courtyard could have been set with bird snares, and dust gathered on his tables and mats.

From the time he first gained understanding until he neared old age, Hong never spoke of others’ faults, never gossiped about their private affairs—this came naturally to him. Even with servants and subordinates, if they had shortcomings or shameful matters, he would not jest about them. He never discussed or judged the strengths and weaknesses of others, nor did he delight in criticizing the likes and dislikes of others’ relationships. If pressed by a superior and unable to decline, when he spoke of someone, he would only mention the finest qualities within them. When discussing writing, he would highlight what he found excellent, never pointing out its flaws. Thus, he incurred no resentment from praise or blame.

When powerful men occasionally asked about officials or commoners—who was superior, who inferior—if they were pure, lofty, and capable, Hong would recount their admirable deeds; if they were greedy, violent, dull, or corrupt, he would reply that he happened not to know them well.

Hong thus faced frequent reproach for being overly cautious, unable to clearly distinguish right from wrong and separate black from white—but he never dared alter his ways. Whenever he saw others fond of evaluating people, drawing comparisons and ranking them, their judgments were not necessarily fair, and their praise or censure often missed the mark. Those who received praise took it as their due, though their virtue might not be genuine; those who were slighted bore resentment to the bone, deeper than blood feuds. This only strengthened Hong’s resolve to avoid speaking of others at all. Even when it came to disciples from his own clan, he entrusted their appraisal entirely to their communities, refusing to lightly assess their worth.

Some mocked him for this, to which Hong replied: “My own self belongs to me—it should be easiest to know. Yet if someone asked me to compare myself to the ancients or to my contemporaries, to rank myself among them, I truly could not say with whom I might be matched. How then, for those who are not me, can I presume to judge and evaluate them? In the corrupt customs of the late Han, factions divided into cliques, and men like Xu Zijiang used their tongues to sow warnings. Disputes and debates turned clans into enemies. Thus, the scholars of Runan had no fixed reputation, only the monthly critiques of ‘moonrise evaluations.’ Even Emperor Wu of Wei deeply despised this, seeking their heads—so they fled in panic, nearly to the point of annihilation. The lesson is not distant; one may well take it as a teacher.

Moreover, people are not easily known. Even fathers and elder brothers cannot fully understand their sons and younger brothers. Those who agree with me are not necessarily right, nor are those who differ necessarily wrong. Some begin well but end poorly. Even Yao, the Duke of Zhou, Confucius, and Jizha all had regrets over incomplete understanding. How can ordinary men trust their narrow, flickering candlelight insight and lightly judge others? Would this not be selling short the greatest sages and worthies?”

During the *Da’an* era, when Shi Bing rebelled, six provinces fell into chaos, their branches and leaves withering as they turned from the right path to join the rebels. The Grand Commander of the Righteous Army summoned Hong to serve as Commander of the Army. Pressed repeatedly, he could not refuse—his homeland was threatened, the disaster grave, the peril immense. The ancients spoke of the duty to act in times of urgent crisis, and fearing military law, he dared not follow his own inclinations. Thus, he recruited several hundred men and advanced with the other armies. Once, when attacking a rebel lieutenant, they crushed the enemy, and treasures piled like mountains, jewels and fine goods covering the ground. The other armies all released their soldiers to gather spoils, their carts laden in an unbroken line. Hong alone ordered his troops not to stray from their ranks. When a soldier was caught looting, Hong immediately executed him as an example. Thus, none dared abandon their posts. Soon after, hundreds of hidden rebels emerged, wounding many in the other armies. Those armies, now scattered, had no formation—men and horses burdened with plunder, their will to fight gone. They fell into chaos, dead and wounded strewn about, nearly beyond recovery. Only Hong’s army remained in orderly ranks, their carts aligned, suffering no losses. For rescuing the other armies from total collapse, Hong’s contribution was decisive.

Later, in a separate battle, he slew a minor rebel commander, capturing many suits of armor and heads, which he presented to the headquarters. The Grand Commander then appointed Hong General Who Calms the Waves and awarded him a hundred bolts of cloth by regulation. Many generals kept their rewards sealed or sent them home, but Hong distributed his among his officers and soldiers, as well as to impoverished acquaintances. Of the ten bolts remaining, he immediately used them to buy meat and wine to feast his officers and men. For a time, this became the finest tale of the day.

When the rebellion was quelled, Hong cast aside his weapons and armor and headed straight for Luoyang, seeking to broaden his search for rare books. He made no mention of his military achievements, secretly admiring the principle of Lu Lian, who refused the gold of Liaocheng, and Wu Zixu, who declined the rewards for preserving Chu—those who succeed yet do not dwell on their success. Just then, the capital fell into great turmoil, and the northern roads were blocked. Chen Min had also rebelled in the Jiangdong region, cutting off his return route.

Just then, an old friend, Ji Jundao of Qiao Commandery, was appointed Inspector of Guangzhou. He submitted a memorial requesting Hong to serve as his military advisor. Though this was not what Hong desired, the opportunity allowed him to take refuge in the south, so he reluctantly accepted. He was sent ahead to muster troops, but Jundao was later assassinated en route, leaving Hong stranded in Guangzhou. Repeatedly invited by regional commanders to serve, he declined each time.

He reflected: wealth and rank may be gradually attained but cannot be suddenly grasped—yet even in their pursuit, the petty details are enough to weary a man. Moreover, glory, position, and profit are like lodgings for a traveler: neither permanent nor something one can cling to. What rises high must fall, what blazes bright must fade, like spring blossoms that wither in an instant. To rejoice in their attainment or grieve over their loss is to invite endless regret, anxiety, and fear—suffering beyond words. Such things are not worth pursuing.

He also assessed his own nature: deeply indolent and of meager talent. Even if he were to bow and scrape, rushing about in the dust, he would still fail to secure great renown or avoid trouble—how much less could he succeed without such efforts? Better to cultivate the Way of Master Redpine and Wang Qiao, which depends on oneself alone, not others. He would ascend famous mountains, practice dietary regimens, and nurture his nature. This was no neglect of duty, but rather an acknowledgment that one cannot serve all things at once. Without renouncing worldly affairs entirely, how could one cultivate the profound and tranquil?

True understanding is difficult to attain, and he could not bear to withhold inquiry or discuss it with others. Thus, his carriage and horse never passed through the domains of the powerful, and not a single letter was exchanged with those in office. Though he could not entirely withdraw from the literati, he found visitors a distraction, preventing him from focusing wholeheartedly. He sighed: "There is no Dao in the mountains and forests. Yet the ancients who cultivated the Dao invariably entered such places, for they truly sought to distance themselves from clamor and keep their minds undisturbed. Now I shall fulfill my original aspiration: leave my homeland, journey to Mount Song, and follow the path of Lord Fangping and Liang."

He had already devoted effort to his philosophical writings, the *Inner* and *Outer Chapters*, and now merely arranged them further to present to posterity. At fifteen or sixteen, he had composed poems, rhapsodies, and miscellaneous essays, believing them worthy of his time. But upon reviewing them in his twentieth year, he found most unsatisfactory. Not that his talent had diminished—rather, his reading had broadened, and he now discerned the fine from the coarse. Thus, he discarded all but one in ten of his earlier works. Excluding his philosophical writings, he still had over a hundred scrolls of miscellaneous pieces, though many remained unrefined and brimmed with melancholy, leaving no time for further revision.

Others might declare their work complete and feel satisfied, but Hong’s dull wit and slow thought made this impossible. Each revision of his writing improved it, yet his laziness and the sheer volume of his work prevented frequent review. In his twenties, he calculated that composing minor, trivial pieces wasted time better spent establishing a school of thought. Thus, he began drafting his philosophical writings. But war and upheaval scattered him across the land, and some works were lost. For over a decade, he did not take up his brush again. Only in the Jianwu era did he finalize his corpus: *Inner Chapters* in twenty scrolls, *Outer Chapters* in fifty, a hundred scrolls of stele inscriptions, eulogies, poems, and rhapsodies, thirty scrolls of military dispatches, proclamations, memorials, letters, and notes. He also compiled works not included in common catalogs: *Biographies of Divine Transcendents* in ten scrolls, *Biographies of Recluses* in ten scrolls, and an anthology of excerpts from the Five Classics, Seven Histories, and the Hundred Schools, along with military treatises, arts and techniques, and miscellaneous essentials—totaling three hundred and ten scrolls, with a separate catalog.

The *Inner Chapters* discuss divine transcendents, alchemical recipes, ghosts and transformations, life-nurturing and longevity, and averting misfortune—matters pertaining to the Daoist school. The *Outer Chapters* address the gains and losses of the human world, the right and wrong of worldly affairs—matters pertaining to the Confucian school.

Hong had read Emperor Wen of Wei’s *Treatise on Literature*, which omitted mention of board games and swordplay. Though he intended to briefly recount his own skills, he had few proficiencies and could not falsely claim expertise. Now he would enumerate what he had not mastered.

By nature, Hong was dull and had few hobbies. From childhood, he had never joined other boys in throwing tiles or playing with clay balls. He had never raced chickens or dogs, nor ridden horses. When others gambled, he paid no attention; if dragged to watch, he remained indifferent, as if daydreaming. To this day, he did not know how many spaces were on a chessboard or the names of dice in *chupu*. He considered such pastimes trivial, distracting the mind and wasting time. For officials, they hindered governance; for scholars, they disrupted study; for commoners, they led to neglect of farming; for merchants, they caused financial loss. Before the outcome was decided, players quarreled in the streets, hearts burning with anger, faces twisted in distress. What was called "amusement" was in truth torment, eroding integrity and breeding strife. Gambling led to heavy wagers, secret grudges, and even bloodshed. In ancient times, Duke Min of Song and the Crown Prince of Wu met violent ends because of such games, sparking rebellion and nearly toppling dynasties. The lessons were clear for all ages.

Whenever he observed players, he saw them grow increasingly enraged, exchanging blows and insults, severing friendships. Resentment, whether great or small, brought endless regret—such things were not worth pursuing. Confucius had warned against napping in the daytime, but compared to Hong, even that seemed preferable. Napping was merely unproductive, whereas gambling invited hatred and litigation. The sage had worn out bamboo strips studying the classics—how could ordinary men, with their limited talents, afford such distractions? All games were inferior to even a single line of a book. Thus, he had never taken interest in them, though this set him apart from common folk.

In my youth, I once studied archery, but my strength was insufficient to draw a strong bow—only one like Yan Gao’s. I believed archery was among the Six Arts and could be used to repel bandits, fend off raids, and hunt birds and beasts, so I practiced it. During my time in the military, I once shot at pursuing cavalry, striking them down as the arrows flew, killing two enemies and one horse, which allowed me to escape death. I also trained in the saber and shield, as well as the single saber and double halberd, all of which had oral formulas and essential techniques for subduing opponents. There were even secret methods so refined they seemed divine. When facing an untrained opponent with such skills, one could prevail alone, unstoppable in any direction. Later, I learned the seven-foot staff technique, which could be used to disarm blades and seize great halberds—though this, too, was but an impractical fringe skill. Knowing it was like possessing the horn of a qilin or the spur of a phoenix: what need was there to employ it beyond necessity? Few would ever understand.

From a young age, I resolved never to seek office. Whenever I read the biographies of Chao Fu, Xu You, the recluse of Stone Gate, the two Jiangs, the two Yuans, Fa Zhen, or Zilong, I would lay down my book and lean forward in admiration of their character. I aspired to master the Five Classics, compose a philosophical work, and leave posterity with the knowledge that I was but a scholar of letters. Later, when the province and the General of Chariots and Cavalry summoned me, I declined. Though recommended to the Prime Minister’s office of the Prince of Langye, when the rebel forces rose, I joined the righteous army. After the rebels were pacified, I never sought recognition or presented myself to the authorities to claim merit. Thus, I held no hope of reward. When the King of Jin answered heaven’s call and aligned with the people’s will, restoring order and reviving the fading imperial lineage, reviving the neglected ancestral sacrifices, and acknowledging the unrecognized merits of the previous dynasty, I followed the general amnesty. In the year *gengyin*, an edict granted me the title of Marquis of Guanzhong, with a fief of two hundred households in Jurong. Privately, I thought: suppressing rebels to save my homeland was labor unworthy of record, and the honor of a noble rank was never my original intent. I had hoped to emulate Lu Lian from afar and Tian Chou from near, submitting a memorial to decline and thus fulfilling my humble aspiration. Yet a general decree allowed no exceptions. In the past, Zhong You declined a reward to encourage virtue, but the barbarians were not yet subdued, the realm was still troubled, and the state sought to clarify rewards and punishments to uphold the law. How could I, a mere scholar, stubbornly cling to my petty, cowardly resolve and defy the great system of public order? Thus, I set aside my objections and respectfully accepted the imperial command.

After composing my "Self-Narration," some questioned me: "In the past, Wang Chong, at sixty, saw his path blocked and his hopes dashed. Fearing his name and deeds would perish with him, he wrote his own chronicle. But you, in the prime of life, live in an era of order, poised to receive the silken gifts of Shen Gong or the rush-wheeled carriage of Mu Sheng, to shine in the splendor of the Nine Five, and to surpass the renown of Kunwu. Why lament the failure to spread your fragrance and instead busy yourself with the pursuits of an old pedant?"

I replied: "Heaven and earth stretch boundless, yet man dwells as a transient guest. The morning mushroom’s brief bloom fades before the day is done; the spring blossom’s fleeting glory withers within ten days. Even the whirlwind’s night-long rush or the lightning’s sudden flash are not swifter. The span of life races like a shooting star through smoke, and old age passes like an arrow through a gap. How much more so for those who perish before their time, like bamboo shoots cut down before autumn or plants withering against the season? Thus, Xiang Zi sighed over the unharvested grain, and Yang Wu mourned his early death. Surveying the distant past, men of exceptional talent have soared like dragons through literary arts or crouched like tigers through martial deeds. Their great merits were recorded in the archives of state, their virtuous names echoed in music and verse. Though their bodies have long turned to dust, their brilliance endures in the depths of the earth, their praises carried on the wind, day after day. Thus, even after a thousand generations, their glory remains as if present.

I, however, am mediocre and obscure, my steps faltering, my conduct at odds with the world. My voice rings discordant with the vulgar, my footsteps clash with the crowd. Within, I lack the patronage of the Jin and Zhang clans; without, I have no friends to brush off their caps in anticipation. Though the road ahead is smooth, my feet are not those of a swift steed; though the six directions stretch wide, my wings are not those of the great peng. Above, I cannot soar like an eagle to aid the state; below, I cannot glorify my parents or leave a name. My virtues will not be entrusted to a worthy historian, nor will my fame be inscribed on bells and tripods. Thus, in the margins of my writings, I have composed this "Self-Narration." Though it does nothing to alter my fate, it may yet rely on those of the future to carry on its telling."
